Human Perfection is Nothing Before God's Perfection
Imam Razi concludes that human perfection is negligible compared to God's perfection and cites a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that human perfection is as nothing compared to God's perfection, and that the prophetic hadith illustrates the overwhelming nature of divine glory.

Core Idea
Human perfection is as nothing when placed beside God's limitless perfection.

Imam Razi concludes his discussion by driving home the absolute gap between human and divine perfection. Even the greatest human soul cannot bear the lowest glimpse of God's glory. He anchors this in a powerful hadith that speaks of the burning effect of God's 'face' on creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Imam Razi uses a very emphatic style here.
2
Identify the main conclusion
Find the sentence where Imam Razi states his conclusion about human perfection relative to God's perfection. Notice the phrase 'أولى بأن يقال إنه كالعدم'.
3
Understand the supporting argument
Imam Razi gives a reason: the human soul alone cannot bear to receive or even observe the least of God's perfection. Consider what this implies about the vastness of divine perfection.
The word 'أدنى' means 'lowest' or 'least'.
4
Connect to the hadith
Read the hadith quotation. Ask yourself how it reinforces the point. The 'glories of His face' burning everything indicates the overwhelming power of God's attributes.
This hadith is often cited to show that direct vision of God's glory in this world is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيظهر من هذا : أن كمال الإنسان المعين بالنسبة إلى كمال الله تعالى : أولى بأن يقال : إنه كالعدم . ولاشك أن روح الإنسان وحده ، لا تطيق قبول أدنى ذلك الكمال ، ولا يمكنه مطالعته . بل الأرواح البشرية تضمحل في [أدنى؟] مرتبة من مراتب تلك الكمالات . فهذا هو المراد بقوله عليه السلام : لو كشفها لأحرقت سبحات وجهه كل شيء ، أدركه بصره ،
So it appears from this: that the perfection of a particular human being relative to the perfection of God, Most High, is more deserving of being described as non-existence. And there is no doubt that the human soul alone cannot bear to receive the least of that perfection, nor can it observe it. Rather, human spirits vanish at the [lowest?] level of those perfections. So this is the intended meaning of his statement, peace be upon him: 'If He uncovered them, the glories of His face would burn everything that His sight reaches.'
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a concluding argument from Imam Razi. He begins by stating a conclusion ('so it appears from this') that the perfection of a particular human being in comparison to God's perfection is more appropriately described as nothing. He then supports this with a certainty: the human soul alone cannot bear to receive the lowest degree of that perfection nor can it even observe it. He further emphasizes that human spirits vanish at a [likely lowest] level of those perfections. Finally, he cites the prophetic hadith as evidence: 'If He uncovered them, the glories of His face would burn everything that His sight reaches,' indicating that even the sight of God's glory is overwhelming.

Key Terms
كمال الإنسان
كمال الله
سبحات وجهه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that humans can attain or comprehend divine perfection.
Correction: Imam Razi argues that human perfection is like non-existence compared to God's, and the human soul cannot even bear the least of it.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi say about human perfection compared to God's perfection?
A) It is like non-existence
B) It is a small part
C) It is equal
D) It is greater

Key Takeaway
Human perfection is essentially nothing when measured against God's perfection, and even the highest human soul cannot bear the least of God's glory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the achievements, skills, and virtues you are most proud of. How does Imam Razi's statement that your perfection is 'like non-existence' before God's perfection affect your sense of self-importance? Can you honestly say you feel humbled rather than defensive? What would change if you truly believed this?
?
The hadith says the glories of God's face would burn everything His sight reaches. Think about a moment in your life when you felt overwhelmed by something magnificent (a sunset, a mountain, a moment of awe). How does that compare to the idea of being completely consumed by God's glory? What prevents you from longing for that encounter despite its intensity?
?
Imam Razi writes that the human soul cannot bear even the least of God's perfection. How does this truth affect your prayers and worship? Are you approaching God with the awareness of your utter incapacity, or do you rely on your own strength? What step can you take today to humble yourself before God's absolute perfection?

Divine Nearness: Nearness of Mercy, Not Space
Imam Razi defines divine nearness as nearness of mercy and explains a variant hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran and hadith references to God's nearness refer to nearness of mercy and favor, not spatial proximity, and how a variant narration is interpreted to maintain consistency.

Core Idea
God's nearness is not spatial but a nearness of mercy and protection.

Imam Razi opens this chapter by addressing a crucial question: When the Quran and hadith speak of God being 'near', what does that mean? He clarifies that the intended meaning is the nearness of His mercy and favor, not any physical closeness. He then examines a variant narration to show that even if a literal reading might suggest something else, scholars interpret it in line with divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the evidence from Quran and hadith
Start by reading the Quranic verse (Qaf 50:16) and the prophetic hadith (qudsi) about drawing near to God. Notice the language of nearness used.
These texts are the basis for the discussion.
2
Understand the interpretation: nearness of mercy
After citing the evidence, Razi states the key interpretive principle: 'the intended meaning of His nearness is the nearness of His mercy.' Reflect on why this is necessary to affirm divine transcendence.
This is the core theological move.
3
Examine the variant hadith and its resolution
Read the hadith narrated by Ibn Furak about the believer on the Day of Judgment, and then Razi's explanation of the phrase 'placing His protection'. Then consider the variant with 'shoulder' and how it is dismissed as a corruption (tashif) and interpreted if authentic.
This shows how scholars handle apparent problematic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن في ( القرب ) قال الله تعالى : ونحن أقرب إليه من حبل الوريد (١) ، وقال عليه السلام ) حكاية عن الله) (۲) من تقرب إلى شبراً تقربت إليه ذراعا ، ومن تقرب إلى ذراعا ، تقربت إليه باعا ، ومن أتاني يمشى ، أتيته هرولة، وروى الأستاذ ابن فورك رحمه الله - في كتاب « المتشابهات ، عن عن ابن عمر رضى الله عنهما ـ عن رسول الله أنه قال : . يدنو المؤمن من ربه ( يوم القيامة) (۳) حتى يضع الجبار كنفه عليه ، فيقر بذنوبه ، فيقول : اعرف - ثلاث مرات - فيقول تعالى : إنى سترتها عليك في الدنيا . وإنى أغفرها لك ، فيعطى صحيفة حسناته ، و أما الكفار والمنافقون ، فينادى بهم على رؤوس الأشهاد . هؤلاء الذين كذبوا على : وبهم (٤) واعلم : أن المراد من قربه و من دنوه : قرب رحمته ودنوها من العبد . وأما قوله : « فيضع الجبار كنفه عليه ، فهو أيضا مستفاد من قرب الرحمة . يقال : أنا في كنف فلان، أى فى إنعامه وأما ما رواه بعضهم فيضع الجبار كتفه ، فاتفقوا على أنه تصحيف ، والرواة ضبطوها بالنون ثم إن صحت تلك الرواية فهى محمولة على التقريب والغفران ) والله أعلم) (۰)
Chapter Eight on Nearness. God Most High said: 'And We are nearer to him than the jugular vein' (1). And he (peace be upon him) said (as a narration from God) (2): 'Whoever draws near to Me by a span, I draw near to him by a cubit; and whoever draws near to Me by a cubit, I draw near to him by a fathom; and whoever comes to Me walking, I come to him running.' And the master Ibn Furak (may God have mercy on him) narrated in the book 'al-Mutashabihat' from Ibn Umar (may God be pleased with them both) from the Messenger of God that he said: 'The believer will approach his Lord (on the Day of Resurrection) (3) until the Almighty places His protection over him, and he confesses his sins. He says: "Recognize" - three times - then God says: "Indeed I concealed them for you in the world, and I forgive you for them." Then he is given the scroll of his good deeds. As for the disbelievers and hypocrites, they will be called out before the witnesses: "These are the ones who lied about" (4) [their Lord]. And know: that what is meant by His nearness and His closeness is the nearness of His mercy and its closeness to the servant. As for His saying: 'the Almighty places His protection over him,' it is also derived from the nearness of mercy. It is said: 'I am in the protection of so-and-so,' meaning in his favor. And as for what some narrated: 'the Almighty places His shoulder over him,' they agreed that it is a corruption, and the narrators recorded it with the letter noon. Then if that narration is authentic, it is interpreted as drawing near and forgiveness. And God knows best. (0)
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage is a chapter titled 'On Nearness'. Imam Razi begins by citing Quranic and prophetic evidence that God is near. He then narrates a hadith about the believer's nearness on the Day of Judgment. After these proofs, he provides the theological interpretation: the intended meaning of God's nearness and closeness is the nearness of His mercy and its closeness to the servant, not spatial or physical proximity. He explains the phrase 'places His protection over him' as also derived from mercy, giving a linguistic example. Finally, he addresses a variant narration that replaces 'protection' with 'shoulder', stating that scholars agree it is a corruption, but if it were authentic, it would be interpreted as drawing near and forgiveness. He concludes with 'And God knows best'.

Key Terms
القرب
قرب الرحمة
الكنف
التصحيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's nearness in the Quran and hadith implies physical or spatial proximity.
Correction: Imam Razi explicitly states that the intended meaning is the nearness of God's mercy and favor, not any spatial or physical closeness.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi say is the intended meaning of God's nearness (القرب) in the Quran and hadith?
A) Spatial closeness
B) Nearness of mercy
C) Nearness of essence
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Divine nearness is always understood as nearness of mercy, not spatial closeness, and any variant readings are interpreted accordingly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel distant from Allah, do you assume He is far spatially or emotionally? How would your repentance change if you truly believed His mercy is closer to you than your own doubt?
?
The hadith says Allah concealed your sins and forgives you. Do you live as if you believe that, or do you still feel weighed down by past sins? What would it take for you to accept His forgiveness fully?
?
Imam Razi dismisses a variant reading as a corruption. Are you quick to reject ideas that challenge your understanding of Allah's transcendence? How can you be both humble in learning and firm in aqeedah?

Two Approaches to Anthropomorphic Verses and the First Proof of Transcendence
Imam Razi defines two types of discussion and gives the first proof of transcendence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's two-fold approach to interpreting anthropomorphic verses and the first rational proof establishing that Allah is transcendent of spatial movement ('coming and going').

Core Idea
Motion and change imply contingency; Allah, being eternal, cannot be described by them.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, a master of both kalam and tafsir, introduces a fundamental principle for handling verses that ascribe physical actions to Allah. He distinguishes two necessary steps: establishing transcendence through rational proofs, and then offering allegorical interpretations in line with that transcendence. In this lesson, we examine his first proof, which draws on a core kalam principle: anything subject to change is contingent, and the Eternal is beyond contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi divides the discussion and the logical flow of the proof.
Pay attention to the structure: two types, then first proof.
2
Identify the two types of discussion
Observe that Razi mentions two approaches: first, proving transcendence by conclusive proofs; second, offering allegorical interpretations. Understand why he prioritizes the first.
This shows his methodology: reason then scripture.
3
Trace the logical steps of the proof
Follow the argument: coming/going implies never free from contingent; whatever is never free from contingent is itself contingent; therefore, it is created. Then apply to Allah: He is eternal, so cannot be subject to coming/going.
This is a classic kalam argument against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم: أن الكلام في قوله: « هل ينظرون إلا أن يأتيهم الله في ظلل من الغمام»، من نوعين: الأول: أن نبين بالدلائل القاهرة أنه سبحانه وتعالى منزه عن المجيء والذهاب. والثاني: أن تذكر التأويلات في هذه الآيات. أما النوع الأول: فنقول: الذي يدل على امتناع المجيء والذهاب على الله سبحانه وتعالى وجوه: الأول: ما ثبت في علم الأصول: أن كل ما يصح عليه المجيء والذهاب، فإنه لا ينفك عن المحدث. وما لا ينفك عن المحدث، فهو محدث. فيلزم: أن كل ما يصح عليه المجيء والذهاب، وجب: أن يكون محدثا مخلوقا. والإله القديم يستحيل أن يكون كذلك.
And know: that the discussion regarding His statement: 'Do they wait except that Allah should come to them in canopies of clouds?' is of two types: the first: that we prove by conclusive proofs that He, glorified and exalted, is transcendent of coming and going. And the second: that we mention the allegorical interpretations of these verses. As for the first type, we say: that which indicates the impossibility of coming and going for Allah, glorified and exalted, are several aspects: the first: what is established in the principles of theology: that everything to which coming and going are applicable is never free from being contingent. And whatever is never free from the contingent is itself contingent. Therefore, it follows that everything to which coming and going are applicable must necessarily be contingent, created. And the eternal God is impossible to be like that.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a methodological statement followed by a logical proof. Imam al-Razi begins by explaining that the discussion of the verse 'Do they wait except that Allah should come to them in canopies of clouds?' (Quran 2:210) and similar anthropomorphic expressions has two aspects. The first is to demonstrate by definitive arguments that Allah is completely beyond any notion of physical movement or spatial change ('coming and going'—al-maji' wa al-dhahab). The second is to provide allegorical interpretations (ta'wil) of these verses, so they are understood in a manner befitting Allah's transcendence.

Then he proceeds to the first type, noting that there are multiple proofs. He presents the first proof, which is based on a foundational principle of theology (usul al-din): anything that can be described as 'coming' or 'going' is necessarily connected to temporal events (muhdath). In other words, motion and displacement are accidents that inhere in bodies and are entailed by change. Whatever is never free from being connected to the contingent (i.e., cannot exist without such contingent attributes) is itself contingent. Since the attribute of 'coming and going' is inseparable from being contingent, anything that possesses this attribute must be contingent and created. The Eternal Being (al-ilah al-qadim) cannot be described as contingent or created; therefore, it is impossible for Him to come or go. This is a classic kalam argument: change implies temporality, and temporality implies need for a creator. Allah, being eternal and self-sufficient, cannot be subject to change.

Key Terms
المجيء والذهاب
المحدث
القديم
التأويل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular because it assumes that coming/going implies contingency without proving that God cannot have such attributes.
Correction: The proof relies on an independently established principle of kalam: any change or motion is a mark of contingency (huduth). This principle is accepted by both Muslims and rational theologians, so the argument is not circular.

Checkpoint
According to Imam al-Razi's first proof, what is the relationship between being subject to 'coming and going' and being created?
A) They are unrelated.
B) Everything subject to coming and going is created.
C) Only things subject to coming and going are eternal.
D) Coming and going is a sign of divine power.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi establishes that the rational principle linking change to contingency proves that Allah, being eternal, cannot be subject to physical movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that 'Allah comes' in the Quran, do you inwardly struggle to avoid thinking of a physical movement, or have you never considered the problem? What does Imam al-Razi's proof demand of your mind right now?
?
Have you ever settled for a comfortable, literal reading of an anthropomorphic verse because it felt safer than the intellectual effort of tanzih? How does this lesson expose that laziness in your heart?
?
If the logical proof for God's transcendence requires you to reject a literal understanding of 'coming', what else in your faith might you be holding onto that is actually incompatible with God's perfection? Are you willing to let reason purify your belief?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
15 min
Four proofs that Allah is above coming and going
Razi provides four rational proofs against the possibility of motion for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four rational proofs from Imam Razi demonstrating why Allah cannot be characterized by motion or change.

Core Idea
Allah's transcendence is proven by the impossibility of change in the divine.

Imam Razi presents four rational arguments that refute the attribution of coming and going to Allah. These proofs draw on principles of contingency, limitation, and the need for a specifying agent. They also engage with the prophetic argument of Abraham. Understanding these proofs strengthens one's conviction in Allah's absolute transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four proofs
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the translation. Note that Razi lists four distinct proofs (wujuh) against motion for Allah.
Each proof begins with an ordinal: الأول, الثاني, الثالث, الرابع.
2
Understand the first proof (contingency)
The first proof argues that whatever is subject to coming and going cannot be free from contingency (huduth), and whatever is contingent is created. Therefore, Allah, being eternal, cannot be described with motion.
This proof relies on the principle that change entails dependence on an agent.
3
Analyze the second proof (limitation and specification)
The second proof shows that anything that moves from place to place is limited and finite, having a specific size. That size could have been different, so its actual size requires a specifier (mukhassis) and preponderator (murajjih), which is impossible for the self-sufficient God.
This proof uses the kalām principle of specification (takhsis).
4
Examine the third proof (distinction from idols)
The third proof states that if we allowed motion for an eternal god, we could not deny the divinity of the sun and moon, which also move. This undermines the uniqueness of Allah.
This proof is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the fourth proof (Abraham's argument)
The fourth proof cites the Quran: Abraham argued against celestial bodies because they set (afool), meaning they undergo absence and presence. If Allah could also undergo such change, Abraham's argument would fail, and God would be contradicting His own support of Abraham.
This proof combines rational and scriptural evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما النوع الأول : فنقول : الذي يدل على امتناع المجيء والذهاب على الله سبحانه وتعالى وجوه : الأول : ما ثبت في علم الأصول : أن كل ما يصح عليه المجيء والذهاب ، فإنه لا ينفك عن المحدث. ومالا ينفك عن المحدث ، فهو محدث . فيلزم : أن كل ما يصح عليه المجيء والذهاب ، وجب : أن يكون محدثا مخلوقا . والإله القديم يستحيل أن يكون كذلك . والثاني : إن كل ما يصح عليه الانتقال والمجيء ، من مكان إلى مكان ، فهو محدود متناه فيكون مختصا بمقدار معين ، مع أنه كان يجوز في العقل وقوعه على مقدار أزيد منه أو أنقص منه . وحينئذ يكون اختصاصه بذلك المقدار ، لأجل تخصيص مخصص ، وترجيح مرجح ، وذلك على الإله القديم محال . والثالث : و هو أنا لو جوزنا ، فيما يصح عليه المجيء والذهاب ، أن يكون إلها قديما أزليا، فحينئذ لا يمكننا أن نحكم بنفي إلهية الشمس والقمر . الرابع : إنه تعالى حكى عن الخليل عليه السلام : أنه طعن في إلهية الكواكب والقمر والشمس ، بقوله : لا أحب الآفلين ولا معنى للأفول إلا الغيبة والحضور . فمن جوز الغيبة والحضور على الإله تعالى : فقد طعن في دليل الخليل وكذب الله في تصديق الخليل في ذلك . حيث قال : وتلك حجتنا آتيناها إبراهيم على قومه .
As for the first type, we say: That which indicates the impossibility of coming and going for Allah, exalted and glorious, are aspects: The first: what is established in the science of principles: that everything to which coming and going are applicable is not free from the contingent. And whatever is not free from the contingent is contingent. Therefore, it necessarily follows that everything to which coming and going are applicable must be contingent and created. And the eternal God is impossible to be like that. The second: that everything to which transition and coming from place to place are applicable is limited and finite, thus it is particularized to a specific magnitude, while it was intellectually permissible for it to occur in a magnitude greater or lesser than that. In that case, its specification to that magnitude is due to the specification of a specifying agent and the preponderance of a preponderator, and that is impossible for the eternal God. The third: that if we were to allow, in that to which coming and going are applicable, that it could be an eternal, preexistent god, then we could not judge the denial of the divinity of the sun and the moon. The fourth: that He, exalted, narrated from al-Khalil (Abraham), peace be upon him, that he criticized the divinity of the planets, the moon, and the sun with his statement: 'I do not love those that set.' And there is no meaning to setting except absence and presence. So whoever permits absence and presence for God, exalted, has criticized the proof of al-Khalil and belied God in His affirmation of al-Khalil in that, where He said: 'And that is Our argument which We gave to Abraham against his people.'
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage is a series of four rational proofs (wujuh) demonstrating that Allah cannot be characterized by coming and going (maji' wa dhahab). 

Proof 1: From the principle that anything subject to coming and going cannot be free from contingency (al-muhdath), because movement entails change, and change indicates dependence on an agent. Whatever is contingent is created, and the eternal God cannot be created. So movement is impossible for Allah.

Proof 2: Everything that moves from place to place is limited and finite, having a specific size. But that size could have been greater or smaller, so its actual size requires a specifier (mukhassis) and a preponderator (murajjih). Such need for external determination is impossible for the eternal, self-sufficient God.

Proof 3: If we allowed that something subject to motion could be an eternal god, then we could not refute the divinity of the sun and moon, since they also move. This destroys the uniqueness of Allah.

Proof 4: Abraham (al-Khalil) argued against the divinity of celestial bodies because they set (afool), which means they undergo absence and presence. If we allow that Allah can be absent or present (as coming and going implies), then Abraham's argument collapses, and Allah would be accusing Himself of falsehood in supporting Abraham. This shows the absurdity of attributing motion to Allah.

Each proof is independent and builds on different premises: contingency, need for specification, loss of distinction from idols, and undermining prophetic arguments.

Key Terms
محدث
قديم
مخلوق
تخصيص
مرجح
مقدار
أفول
غيبة
حضور
دليل الخليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think these proofs are purely rational and do not rely on revelation.
Correction: The fourth proof explicitly cites the Quranic story of Abraham, showing that reason and revelation are aligned in affirming Allah's transcendence.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion of the third proof?
A) Allah is limited.
B) If motion were allowed for Allah, we could not deny the divinity of the sun and moon.
C) Motion implies being created.
D) Abraham's argument is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot be described by coming or going, as proven by reason and revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the knowledge that Allah is beyond all change affect your trust in His promises? Are you ever tempted to think that Allah might change His mind about you? What specific proof from this lesson counters that doubt, and how will you remind yourself of it this week?
?
The third proof shows that if we allow coming and going for Allah, we could not deny the divinity of the sun and moon. In your daily life, do you ever give created things—like status, wealth, or people—a kind of 'divine' trust or reliance? How does this proof expose that as a form of hidden shirk, and what is one step you can take to redirect your trust exclusively to Allah?
?
Abraham's argument against 'setting' was that anything that undergoes absence cannot be God. When you feel spiritually 'absent' or distant from Allah, do you mistakenly think Allah is absent from you? How does this lesson reshape your understanding of His constant presence, and what will you do the next time you feel that distance?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate

The Impossibility of Movement for Allah
Imam Razi presents the second rational proof against literal movement for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the principle of limitation and the need for a specifier to argue that literal movement is impossible for Allah.

Core Idea
Anything that moves is limited and needs a specifier; Allah is beyond such need.

Imam Razi presents his second rational proof that Allah cannot literally move or come from place to place. The argument hinges on a simple but powerful idea: anything that moves must be limited in space and quantity, and that limitation requires an external cause. Since Allah is self-sufficient and eternal, such dependence is impossible. Let's trace the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional phrasing: 'If... then...'.
2
Identify the two premises
The passage argues that (1) movement implies limitation, and (2) limitation implies specification to a particular measure. Find where each premise is stated.
3
Understand why a specifier is needed
The passage notes that the measure could have been different in reason. This shows that the actual measure is contingent, requiring a cause to choose it. This is the key step connecting contingency to the need for a specifier.
This reasoning is a standard kalām argument for a creator.
4
Connect to Allah
The conclusion says that such specification is impossible for the eternal God. Ask yourself: Why would it be impossible? Reflect on Allah's self-sufficiency and necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إن كل ما يصح عليه الانتقال والمجيء ، من مكان إلى مكان ، فهو محدود متناه فيكون مختصا بمقدار معين ، مع أنه كان يجوز في العقل وقوعه على مقدار أزيد منه أو أنقص منه . وحينئذ يكون اختصاصه بذلك المقدار ، لأجل تخصيص مخصص ، وترجيح مرجح ، وذلك على الإله القديم محال
The second: Everything that can undergo transition and coming from place to place is limited and finite, so it is specific to a particular measure, while it was possible in reason for it to occur in a greater or lesser measure. Then its being specific to that measure is due to specification by a specifier and preferment by a preferrer, and that is impossible for the eternal God.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is the second of several rational proofs (adillah) against taking literally the Quranic descriptions of Allah's 'coming' or 'movement'. The argument proceeds in a conditional form: (1) If something can undergo transition from one place to another, then it is limited (maḥdūd) and finite (mutanāhī). (2) Anything limited must be specific to a particular quantity or measure (miqdār muʿayyan). (3) But it is conceivable in reason that this quantity could have been greater or smaller; therefore, its being fixed at this particular quantity requires a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) and a preferrer (murajjiḥ) that chose this quantity over others. (4) However, the eternal God (al-ilāh al-qadīm) cannot depend on external causes for specification or preference, as He is self-sufficient and necessary. Hence, such transition is impossible for Allah. The proof is based on the principle of contingency (imkān) and the need for a cause for any particular determination, a foundational argument in kalām.

متناه
مقدار معين
تخصيص
مخصص
ترجيح
مرجح
إله قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'coming' or 'movement' in the Quran is literal for Allah and does not contradict His perfection.
Correction: This proof shows that literal movement implies limitation and need for a specifier, which contradicts Allah's self-sufficiency and eternality.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's second proof, anything that can undergo transition from place to place is:
A) eternal and self-sufficient.
B) limited and finite, requiring a specifier.
C) one of Allah's attributes.
D) proves Allah can move.

Key Takeaway
Literal movement for Allah is impossible because it entails limitation and dependence on an external specifier, contradicting His divine nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah's 'coming' on the Day of Judgment, do you picture a literal movement? How does this proof challenge that mental image—and what does that reveal about the limits of your own imagination when trying to conceive of the Divine?
?
If Allah cannot change or move, what does that mean for your daily supplications? Do you sometimes act as if your dua 'causes' God to become merciful, as if He were not already eternally and unchangeably merciful? How can you reconcile this proof with your prayer life?
?
The proof says Allah needs no 'specifier' to determine His attributes. Which of your own attributes—patience, generosity, humility—do you treat as if they were accidental and in need of external circumstances to exist? What would it take for you to embody a quality that is self-sufficient, like Allah’s?

The Third Proof: Movement Cannot Belong to the Eternal God
Imam Razi's third rational proof against literal movement for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why allowing literal movement for Allah leads to an unacceptable consequence—the inability to reject the divinity of celestial bodies.

Core Idea
If movement is possible for God, the sun and moon could be gods too.

Imam Razi now presents the third rational proof against anthropomorphism. He argues that if we allow that something subject to coming and going could be an eternal god, then we lose any rational basis for denying the divinity of the sun and moon. This proof shows why movement must be impossible for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice that it is a single conditional statement leading to a logical conclusion.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the premise and conclusion
Identify what Imam Razi assumes (the premise) and what he concludes (the absurdity). Ask yourself what the intended lesson is.
The premise is allowing movement for God; the conclusion is inability to deny divinity of celestial bodies.
3
Connect to previous proofs
Reflect on how this third proof builds on earlier arguments against anthropomorphism. Why does movement specifically lead to this problem?
This proof highlights the danger of attributing bodily characteristics to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : و هو أنا لوجوزنا ، فيما يصح عليه المجيء والذهاب ، أن يكون إلها قديما أزليا، فحينئذ لا يمكننا أن نحكم بنفى الهية الشمس والقمر .
The third: That if we allow, concerning that which can undergo coming and going, that it be an eternal ancient god, then we cannot judge the denial of the divinity of the sun and moon.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is a concise argument, the third in a series, that draws an absurd consequence from allowing literal movement for Allah. The reasoning is: (1) The sun and moon are observable celestial bodies that undergo coming and going (i.e., rising and setting, movement across the sky). (2) If we admit that something which can move (mā yaṣiḥḥu ʿalayhi al-majīʾ wa al-dhahāb) could be an eternal ancient god (ilāhan qadīman azaliyyan), then the sun and moon, which also move, could also qualify as gods. (3) But the Qurʾān and consensus deny the divinity of the sun and moon. (4) Therefore, if we accept that movement is compatible with divinity, we lose the rational basis for rejecting the worship of the sun and moon. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: the premise (that movement is possible for God) leads to an unacceptable conclusion, so the premise must be false.

Key Terms

ذهاب
إله قديم أزلي
نفى الهية
شمس
قمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Movement is a neutral attribute that does not affect God's divinity.
Correction: If movement were allowed for God, then any moving object, such as the sun and moon, could also be considered divine. This would undermine the distinction between Creator and creation, making it impossible to deny the divinity of celestial bodies.

Checkpoint

A) To prove the existence of God.
B) To deny the divinity of the sun and moon.
C) To believe in the afterlife.
D) To assert God's oneness.

Key Takeaway
If movement were possible for God, we could not deny the divinity of the sun and moon, so movement must be impossible for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Fourth Proof: Abraham's Argument Against Setting
Imam Razi uses Abraham's statement against setting as a proof that Allah transcends spatial change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abraham's statement 'I do not love those that set' serves as a scriptural proof that Allah cannot be subject to spatial movement or change of location.

Core Idea
Setting implies absence and presence—qualities impossible for the Eternal God.

This lesson presents Imam Razi's fourth proof, which is rooted in the Qur'anic story of Abraham. Abraham rejected the divinity of celestial bodies because they set—i.e., they disappear and reappear. Since setting entails change of location, it cannot apply to Allah. Razi argues that to allow absence or presence for Allah would undermine Abraham's proof and contradict Allah's own affirmation of it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the Qur'anic allusion and the logical flow of the argument.
The Arabic contains OCR errors; refer to the translation for clarity.
2
Understand the key term 'setting'
Setting (ufūl) means disappearance after being present—a change in spatial location. This is the basis of the argument.
Think of the sun disappearing below the horizon.
3
Grasp the logical implication
If setting implies absence/presence, then anything subject to that cannot be divine. Applying absence/presence to Allah would defeat Abraham's proof, which Allah Himself affirmed.
Consider why the same reasoning applies to any supposed divine being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنه تعالى حكى عن الحلميل عليه السلام : أنه طمن في إلهية الكواكب والقمر والشمس ، بقوله . . لا أحب الأفلين (۱۳) ولا معنى ولا للأفول إلا الغيبه والحضور . فمن جوز الغيبة والحضور على الإله تعالى : فقد طمن في دليل الخليل ( وكذب الله في تصديق الخليل) (١٤) في ذلك . حيث قال : د وتلك حجتنا آتيناها إبراهيم على قومه ،
The fourth: That He, exalted, narrated from al-Khalīl (Abraham), peace be upon him, that he cast doubt on the divinity of the planets, the moon, and the sun, by his saying: 'I do not love those that set' (Q 6:76), and there is no meaning for setting except absence and presence. So whoever allows absence and presence for God, exalted, has cast doubt on the proof of the Friend (al-Khalīl) and belied God in His confirming the Friend in that, where He said: 'And that is Our argument which We gave Abraham against his people' (Q 6:83).
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, which is scriptural-rational rather than purely rational. The author quotes the story of Abraham (peace be upon him) from Sūrat al-Anʿām: when the sun set, Abraham said, 'I do not love those that set' (lā uḥibbu al-āfilīn). The word 'setting' (ufūl) linguistically means disappearance after being present. So Abraham rejected as false gods anything that undergoes absence and presence (ghayba wa ḥuḍūr). The argument is: (1) Abraham's proof against his people's deities was based on the fact that they set, i.e., they are subject to change in location. (2) If someone allows that Allah can also be absent and present (i.e., change location), they have essentially undermined Abraham's argument, because then why could the sun and moon not also be gods? (3) Moreover, Allah Himself affirmed Abraham's argument as His own proof (Q 6:83). So to reject the implication of Abraham's proof is to contradict what Allah has approved. Thus, literal movement for Allah is impossible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'setting' only applies to pagan idols and has no bearing on Allah's attributes.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that setting entails absence/presence, which are inconsistent with the divine nature; the principle is universal.

Checkpoint

A) To show that Allah is greater than the sun and moon.
B) Because setting implies absence/presence, which cannot apply to God.
C) To prove that Abraham was a prophet.
D) To argue that the sun and moon are not eternal.

Key Takeaway
Abraham's proof establishes that whatever undergoes setting (absence after presence) cannot be divine, thereby negating spatial change for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously pictured Allah as being 'far away' when you feel distant from Him, or 'close' when you feel His presence? How does this lesson's insistence that Allah is not subject to spatial change challenge that way of thinking? Today, commit to reminding yourself that Allah is never absent, even when you don't feel Him – and ask yourself: what sin or neglect caused that feeling, not a change in Allah?
?
Abraham rejected anything that sets as a god because setting implies a lack of perfection. In your own life, are you still 'loving' or attaching yourself to things that 'set'—pleasures, status, relationships that inevitably fade? How does this lesson expose that as a form of hidden shirk? What concrete step will you take this week to direct your love only to the One who never sets?
?
If you truly believe that Allah affirmed Abraham's proof as His own, then do you act as if Allah's transcendence is non-negotiable? When you feel tempted to think of Allah as changing mood or location in response to your deeds, do you immediately reject that thought as false? Be honest: have you ever entertained the idea that Allah 'comes down' or 'moves' in a literal sense? What will you do the next time such a thought arises?

First Interpretation: Coming of Allah Means Coming of His Signs
Imam Razi explains the first ta'wil: the verse means 'coming of the signs of Allah', emphasizing intimidation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first interpretation of the verse about Allah's coming, which explains that it refers to the coming of His signs as a threat.

Core Idea
When Allah's 'coming' is mentioned, it is a metaphorical way to speak of His signs for emphasis.

Imam Razi begins his treatment of the controversial verse by offering a powerful linguistic and contextual interpretation. He argues that taking the verse literally leads to inconsistencies with its purpose as a warning. Instead, the verse should be understood as describing the coming of Allah's signs, magnified by attributing them to Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
The passage is part of a larger discussion on interpretations of a challenging verse.
2
Identify the linguistic principle
Notice the example of saying 'the king came' when his army comes. This shows that actions of subordinates can be attributed to the leader for magnification.
This principle is key to understanding how 'Allah comes' can mean 'His signs come'.
3
Examine the contextual proof
Look at the reference to the preceding verse (Q 2:209) which is about clear proofs and contains a threat. The verse about 'Allah comes' continues this theme of intimidation.
Imam Razi uses this to show that the verse must be a threat, not a neutral statement.
4
Reflect on the rational argument
Consider the argument: if Allah literally came, His presence could also bring mercy, not just threat. Since the verse is only about threat, it must imply something else (awe and subjugation).
This logical step removes doubt about anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النوع الثاني (وهو) في بيان التأويلات المذكورة في هذه الآية: فنقول: فيه وجوه (١٥): الأول : المراد : هل ينظرون إلا أن تأتيهم آيات الله. فجعل مجى آيات الله مجينا له . على التفخيم لشأن الآيات . كما يقال : جاء الملك . إذا جاء جيش عظيم من جهته ، والذي يدل على صحة هذا التأويل : أنه تعالى قال في الآية المتقدمة : « فإن زللتم من بعد ما جاءتكم البينات ، فاعلموا : أن الله عزيز حكيم (١٦) فذكر ذلك في معرض الزجر والتهديد . ثم إنه تعالى أكد ذلك بقوله : « هل ينظرون إلا أن يأتيهم الله ، ومن المعلوم : أن بتقدير أن يصح المجيء والذهاب على الله تعالى، لم يكن مجرد حضوره سببا للزجر والتهديد ، لأنه عند الحضور كما يزجر قوما ويعاقبهم ، فقد يشبب قوما ويكرمهم . فثبت أن مجرد الحضور ، لا يكون سببا للزجر والتهديد والوعيد ، ولما كان المقصود من الآية ، إنما هو التهديد ، وجب أن يضمر في الآية بجى، الهيبة والقهر والتهديد . ومى أضمرنا ذلك ، زالت الشبهة بالكلبة . وهذا تأويل حسن موافق لنظم الآيه
As for the second type (which is) concerning the explanation of the interpretations mentioned regarding this verse, we say: There are several aspects (wujūh). The first: The meaning is: 'Do they await except that the signs of Allah should come to them?' So He made the coming of the signs of Allah like His coming, to magnify the matter of the signs, just as it is said: 'The king came' when a great army from his side comes. And that which indicates the soundness of this interpretation is that He, exalted, said in the preceding verse: 'But if you deviate after the clear proofs have come to you, then know that Allah is Almighty, Wise' (Q 2:209). He mentioned that in the context of rebuke and threat. Then He, exalted, emphasized that by His saying: 'Do they await except that Allah should come to them?' And it is known that if we suppose that coming and going could apply to Allah, exalted, then just His presence would not be a cause for rebuke and threat, because when He is present, just as He rebukes some people and punishes them, He may also give good news to some people and honor them. So it is established that mere presence is not a cause for rebuke, threat, and warning. Since the intended meaning of the verse is only the threat, it is necessary that what is implied in the verse be [something indicating] awe, subjugation, and threat. And when we imply that, the doubt is completely removed. This is a good interpretation, in agreement with the order of the verse.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage is the first interpretation (al-wajh al-awwal) of the verse that mentions Allah's 'coming' (e.g., Q 2:210 or Q 89:22). The author classifies this as part of the 'second type' (al-nawʿ al-thānī), which deals with ta'wīl (interpretation) of the problematic verses. The interpretation is: the verse actually means the coming of Allah's signs (āyāt Allāh) — that is, His miraculous signs or portents of punishment. The author gives a linguistic principle: in Arabic, one may attribute an action to a leader when it is actually performed by his subordinates, as in saying 'the king came' when his army comes, for emphasis. He supports this interpretation with the immediate context: in the preceding verse (Q 2:209), Allah speaks about 'clear proofs' (al-bayyināt) coming, which is a warning. So the mention of Allah's coming in the next verse is a continuation of that threat. He then offers a rational argument: if we take 'coming' literally as Allah's physical presence, that does not necessarily imply threat, because presence could also bring mercy. But the verse is clearly about intimidation; therefore, something else must be intended — namely, the coming of His power and punishment. Thus, the implied meaning (muḍmar) is something like 'the coming of His awe, subjugation, and threat'. Once we understand that, the doubt about Allah's movement is removed.

Key Terms
تأويل
آيات الله
تفخيم
زجر
تهديد
إضمار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse should be taken literally as Allah physically coming.
Correction: The verse is actually a threat, and literal presence would not consistently imply threat, so the intended meaning is the coming of His signs, as supported by linguistic convention and context.

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation, what is the implied meaning of 'Allah comes' in the verse?
A) He moves into the world.
B) He sends His angels.
C) It implies awe, subjugation, and threat.
D) It means He appears in a form.

Key Takeaway
The verse about Allah's coming should be interpreted as the coming of His signs for the purpose of threat, as supported by linguistic convention and context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Imam Razi argues that mere presence is not necessarily threatening. In your relationship with Allah, do you seek only His comfort and mercy, while neglecting the weight of His majesty and justice? How can you this week intentionally remember both His hope and His threat in your worship?
?
If you were to apply the same method of contextual and purpose-driven interpretation to a comfortable belief you hold about Allah, what might you discover? Are you willing to let go of a literal understanding that makes you feel safe, if the overall message of the Quran demands a more awe-filled, accountable stance?

Second Interpretation: The Coming of Allah's Command
Imam Razi explains the second ta'wil: the verse means 'coming of Allah's command', with two confirmations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second ta'wil of Allah's 'coming' as the coming of His command, supported by linguistic principles and Quranic parallels.

Core Idea
When Allah's action is attributed to Him literally, if impossible, interpret it metaphorically.

Imam Razi presents the second interpretation of the verse on Allah's coming. He argues that the intended meaning is the coming of Allah's command, not a literal movement. This is based on a well-established principle in Arabic: an impossible literal attribution necessitates metaphorical interpretation. He supports this with Quranic examples like 'oppose Allah' meaning 'oppose His friends' and 'ask the town' meaning 'ask its people'. This lesson will unpack that principle and the confirmatory evidence from the Resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the entire passage once to get the flow of the argument. Note the key terms and examples.
2
Identify the key principle
Look for the sentence starting with 'ومدار الكلام' which states the rule: when an action is attributed to Allah and the literal meaning is impossible, ta'wil is necessary.
3
Examine the supporting examples
Notice how 'oppose Allah' and 'ask the town' are interpreted, establishing the pattern of 'حذف المضاف' (deletion of the ascribed).
This is a well-known metaphor in Arabic.
4
Review the first confirmation
The first confirmation connects the verse to other Resurrection verses, showing consistency across the Quran.
Only the first confirmation appears in the text; the second is not present in the OCR.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أن يكون المراد : هل ينظرون ، إلا أن يأتيهم أمر الله. ومدار الكلام فى هذا الباب : أنه تعالى إذا أضاف فعلا إلى شيء ، فإن كان ظاهر تلك الإضافة ممتنعاً ، فالواجب صرف ذلك الظاهر إلى التأويل . كما قال العلماء في قوله تعالى: «إن الذين يحادون الله (۱۷) والمراد : يحادون أولياءه ، وقد قال تعالى : ( واسئل القرية (۱۸) ، والمراد : أهل القرية . فكذا قوله تعالى : ويأتيهم الله (۱۹) ، أي يأتيهم أمر الله ، وليس فيه إلا حذف . المضاف ، وإقامة المضاف إليه مقامه ، وذلك مجاز مشهور . يقال : ضرب الأمير فلانا ، وأعطاه . والمراد : أنه أمر بذلك .
و الذي يؤكد صحة هذا التأويل وجهان :
الأول : إن قوله تعالى «يأتيهم الله، وقوله : دوجاء ربك (۲۰)، إخبار عن حال القيامة . ثم إن الله تعالى ذكر هذه الواقعة بعينها في سورة النحل
The second aspect: That the intended meaning be: 'Do they await except that the command of Allah should come to them?' The pivot of the discussion in this chapter is that when He, exalted, attributes an action to something, and the apparent meaning of that attribution is impossible, then it is necessary to turn that apparent meaning to interpretation (ta'wīl). Just as the scholars said concerning His saying: 'Indeed those who oppose Allah' (Q 58:20) – the intended meaning is 'oppose His friends', and He, exalted, said: 'And ask the town' (Q 12:82) – the intended meaning is 'the people of the town'. Similarly, His saying: 'And Allah comes to them' (Q 2:210) means 'His command comes to them'. There is nothing in it except the deletion of the ascribed entity and the standing of the ascribed-to in its place, and that is a well-known metaphor. It is said: 'The amīr struck so-and-so' or 'gave him' – the intended meaning is that he commanded that.
And that which confirms the soundness of this interpretation are two aspects:
The first: That His saying 'Allah comes to them' and His saying 'And your Lord comes' (Q 89:22) are informing about the state of the Resurrection. Then Allah, exalted, mentioned this same event in Sūrat al-Naḥl ...
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage presents the second interpretation (al-wajh al-thānī) of the verse. The author argues that the intended meaning is not a literal 'coming' but rather the coming of Allah's command (amr Allāh). This is based on a well-established principle: when an action is attributed to something in the apparent sense, but that attribution is impossible for the attributed subject (e.g., physical movement for Allah), then the apparent meaning must be interpreted metaphorically. The principle is supported by other Quranic examples: 'those who oppose Allah' actually means 'oppose His friends' (the attribute is transferred from the friends to Allah); 'ask the town' means 'ask the people of the town' (the attribute of asking is transferred from the people to the town). Similarly, 'Allah comes' means 'His command comes'. This is a case of ḥadhf al-muḍāf (deletion of the ascribed) and iqāmat al-muḍāf ilayhi maqāmahu (putting the ascribed-to in its place), a common metaphorical usage in Arabic. The author then mentions two confirmatory aspects (wajhān), but only the first appears in the text: that the verses about 'coming' (Q 2:210, Q 89:22) refer to the Day of Resurrection, and Allah describes the same event in Sūrat al-Naḥl without using 'coming' but rather describing His command. The implicit conclusion: if it were literal coming, it would have been mentioned in the same terms elsewhere, but instead it is described in terms of command.

Key Terms

حذف المضاف
إقامة المضاف إليه مقامه
مجاز مشهور
تأويل

Misconception: Interpretations like this are arbitrary and without linguistic basis.
Correction: They follow consistent rules of Arabic rhetoric and Quranic exegesis, as shown by the principle of 'ḥadhf al-muḍāf' and supporting examples.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The second interpretation establishes that 'Allah comes' is a metaphorical expression for the coming of His command, consistent with linguistic and exegetical norms.

Why God's 'Coming' in the Verse is a Threat
Imam Razi argues that the verse about God's coming must be interpreted as a threat, not literal presence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the verse 'Do they await but that Allah should come to them?' must be interpreted metaphorically as a threat, and not literally.

Core Idea
God's mere presence cannot be inherently threatening; so the verse must imply awe and terror.

Imam Razi presents a logical argument against literal interpretation of divine attributes. He examines the verse that mentions God's 'coming' and shows that literal presence would not necessarily convey threat, since presence can be either merciful or wrathful. Since the verse's context is clearly threatening, the intended meaning must be something else, namely the manifestation of divine awe. This short passage demonstrates how careful reasoning supports metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly, noting key terms like مجيء, حضور, تهديد. Try to grasp the argument structure before looking at the translation.
The text has some OCR uncertainty, but the main argument is clear.
2
Understand the logical conditional
Notice how Razi sets up a conditional: if coming and going could be literal, then mere presence would not be a cause for threat or deterrence—because presence could also bring honor.
This step is crucial for the argument's validity.
3
Identify the conclusion
Since the verse's purpose is threat (تهديد), we must imply the meaning of fear, awe, and terror. This removes the doubt and yields a sound interpretation.
Razi concludes that the verse must be interpreted metaphorically.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider how Razi uses logical necessity (الضرورة العقلية) to support scriptural interpretation. This is a key method in Islamic theology for dealing with anthropomorphic verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك بقوله : « هل ينظرون إلا أن يأتيهم الله ، ومن المعلوم : أن بتقدير أن يصح المجيء والذهاب على الله تعالى، لم يكن مجرد حضوره سببا للزجر والتهديد ، لأنه عند الحضور كما يزجر قوما ويعاقبهم ، فقد يشبب قوما ويكرمهم . فثبت أن مجرد الحضور ، لا يكون سببا للزجر والتهديد والوعيد ، ولما كان المقصود من الآية ، إنما هو التهديد ، وجب أن يضمر في الآية بجى [؟] الهيبة والقهر والتهديد . ومى [؟] أضمرنا ذلك ، زالت الشبهة بالكلبة [؟] . وهذا تأويل حسن موافق لنظم الآيه
That is by His saying, 'Do they await but that Allah should come to them?' And it is known: if it were possible that coming and going apply to Allah Almighty, His mere presence would not be a cause for deterrence and threat, because when present, just as He deters some people and punishes them, He may also encourage some people and honor them. So it is established that mere presence is not a cause for deterrence, threat, and warning. And since the intended purpose of the verse is only threat, it is necessary to imply in the verse [the meaning of] fear, awe, and threat. And if we imply that, the doubt is completely removed. And this is a good interpretation consistent with the structure of the verse.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is an argument for interpreting the verse 'Do they await but that Allah should come to them?' (2:210) metaphorically. The author begins by noting that if coming and going were attributed to God literally, mere presence could be either threatening or merciful, so it would not necessarily convey a threat. Since the verse's context is threatening, the intended meaning must be something else—namely, the awe and terror of God's manifestation. By implying this, the difficulty is resolved. This is a sound interpretation that fits the verse's wording.

Correction: Imam Razi shows that literal interpretation fails because God's mere presence would not necessarily produce the effect of threat; it could also produce mercy. Therefore, metaphorical interpretation is necessary.

D) Because the verse is about judgement day only.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that literal interpretation of divine attributes can lead to contradictions, so metaphorical interpretation is necessary when the literal meaning does not fit the context.

The Command of God: A Metaphorical Interpretation
Razi states the interpretation and the principle of deletion with examples and two evidences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's interpretation of the verse 'Allah comes' as 'the command of Allah comes' and the hermeneutical principle of deleting the mudaf.

Core Idea
When literal attribution is impossible, delete the mudaf and let the mudaf ilayh stand.

Imam Razi begins by proposing that the coming of God in the verse actually refers to the coming of God's command. He then lays down a fundamental principle of Quranic interpretation: if an action is attributed to something but the literal meaning is impossible, we must interpret it metaphorically. This lesson explains that principle and its supporting evidence from the Quran.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the key terms and the examples from other Quranic verses.
The passage uses a very condensed argumentative style.
2
Identify the hermeneutical principle
Find where Razi states that when literal attribution is impossible, ta'wil is necessary. Notice the examples: 'oppose Allah' means 'oppose His friends', and 'ask the village' means 'ask its people'.
This principle is central to many theological interpretations.
3
Examine the two evidences
The first evidence is from Surah an-Nahl, which explicitly says 'the command of your Lord'. The second is the definite article in 'the matter is decided', implying a previously mentioned command.
These evidences show consistency across Quranic verses.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that the intended meaning is the command of Allah coming, not Allah Himself. This avoids attributing spatial movement to God.
This preserves divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أن يكون المراد : هل ينظرون ، إلا أن يأتيهم أمر الله. ومدار الكلام فى هذا الباب : أنه تعالى إذا أضاف فعلا إلى شيء ، فإن كان ظاهر تلك الإضافة ممتنعاً ، فالواجب صرف ذلك الظاهر إلى التأويل . كما قال العلماء في قوله تعالى: «إن الذين يحادون الله (۱۷) والمراد : يحادون أولياءه ، وقد قال تعالى : ( واسئل القرية (۱۸) ، والمراد : أهل القرية . فكذا قوله تعالى : ويأتيهم الله (۱۹) ، أي يأتيهم أمر الله ، وليس فيه إلا حذف . المضاف ، وإقامة المضاف إليه مقامه ، وذلك مجاز مشهور . يقال : ضرب الأمير فلانا ، وأعطاه . والمراد : أنه أمر بذلك . و الذي يؤكد صحة هذا التأويل وجهان : الأول : إن قوله تعالى «يأتيهم الله، وقوله : دوجاء ربك (۲۰)، إخبار عن حال القيامة . ثم إن الله تعالى ذكر هذه الواقعة بعينها في سورة النحل فقال : « هل ينظرون إلا أن تأتيهم الملائكة أو يأتي أمر ربك (٢١) ، فصار هذا مفسرا لذلك المتشابه ، لأن كل هذه الآيات لما وردت في واقعة واحدة ، لم يبعد حمل بعضها على البعض . والثاني : إنه تعالى قال بعد هذه الآي : ، وقضى الأمر (۲۲) ، ولاشك أن الألف واللام المعهود السابق . وهذا يستدعى أن يكون قد جرى ذكره من قبل ذلك، حتى تكون الألف واللام إشارة إليه . وما ذاك إلا الذي أضمر ناه [؟] من أن قوله « يأتيهم الله ، أو يأتيهم (٢٣) أمر الله . فإن قيل : أمر الله - عندكم - : صفة قديمة. فالإتيان عليها : محال . قلنا الأمر فى اللغة له معنيان: أحدهما : الفعل . والثاني : الطريق . قال تعالى : دوما أمرنا إلا واحدة كلمى بالبصر (٢٤) ، وقال : « وما أمر فرعون برشيد (٢٥) ، في حل الأمر فى هذه الآية على الفعل وهو ما يليق بتلك المواقف من الأهول، وإظهار الآيات المهيبة ، وهذا هو التأويل (الأول) (٢٦) الذي ذكرناه . و أما إن حملنا الأمر ، على الأمر الذى هو ضد النهى . ففيه وجهان : الأول : أن يكون التقدير هو أن مناديا ينادي يوم القيامة : ألا إن الله يأمركم بكذا وبكذا . فيكون إتيان الأمر : هو وصول ذلك النداء ، إليهم . وقوله : « فى ظلل من الغمام ، أى مع ظلل . والتقدير : أن سماع ذلك النداء ووصول تلك الظلال : يكون (۲۷) في زمان واحد . الثاني : أن يكون المراد من إتيان أمر الله تعالى في ظلل : حصول
The second aspect: that the intended meaning is: 'Do they await but that the command of Allah should come to them?' And the core of the discussion in this chapter is: When Allah attributes an action to something, if the literal meaning of that attribution is impossible, then it is necessary to turn that literal meaning to interpretation (ta'wil). As the scholars said regarding His statement: 'Indeed, those who oppose Allah' (58:20) and the intended meaning is 'oppose His friends.' And He said: 'And ask the village' (12:82) and the intended meaning is 'the people of the village.' Similarly, His statement: 'and Allah comes to them' (2:210) means 'the command of Allah comes to them.' And this involves only the deletion of the thing added (mudaf) and placing the mudaf ilayh in its place, and that is a well-known metonymy. It is said: 'The amir struck so-and-so, and gave him,' and the intended meaning is that he ordered that. And two things confirm the correctness of this interpretation: First: His statement 'Allah comes to them' and 'your Lord comes' (89:22) is informing about the situation of the Resurrection. Then Allah mentioned this same event in Surah an-Nahl: 'Do they await but that the angels should come to them or the command of your Lord should come?' (16:33). So this explains that ambiguous (mutashabih) because when all these verses occur about one event, it is not unlikely that some are interpreted by others. Second: He said after these verses: 'and the matter is decided' (2:210). Undoubtedly the definite article refers to a previously mentioned matter. This requires that it has been mentioned before, so that the definite article refers to it. And that is nothing but what we implied from His statement 'Allah comes to them' or 'the command of Allah comes to them.' If it is said: 'The command of Allah, according to you, is an eternal attribute. Coming upon it is impossible.' We say: The word 'amr' in the language has two meanings: one: the act (fi'l). The second: the path (tariq?). He said: 'Our command is but one, like a glimpse of the eye' (54:50), and He said: 'And the command of Pharaoh was not right' (11:97). So we interpret the 'amr' in this verse as the act, which is the terrors befitting those situations and the display of awesome signs. This is the first interpretation we mentioned. And if we carry 'amr' upon the 'amr' that is the opposite of prohibition, then there are two aspects: First: The estimation is that a caller calls on the Day of Resurrection: 'Indeed, Allah commands you with such-and-such.' So the coming of the command is the arrival of that call to them. And His saying: 'in canopies of clouds' (2:210) means 'with canopies.' And the estimation: that hearing that call and arrival of those canopies occur at one time. Second: That the intended meaning of the coming of the command of Allah in canopies is: the occurrence of
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the second interpretation. Imam Razi first states that the intended meaning of 'God comes' is 'the command of God comes.' He lays down a general hermeneutical principle: when an action is attributed to something and the literal attribution is impossible, it must be interpreted metaphorically. He gives examples from the Quran: 'those who oppose Allah' means opposing His friends; 'ask the village' means ask its people. Similarly here, 'God comes' means His command comes, by deleting the mudaf (the word 'command') and putting the mudaf ilayh (the word 'God') in its place, a common figure of speech. Two evidences support this: (1) The parallel in Surah an-Nahl uses the phrase 'or the command of your Lord comes,' explaining the ambiguous verse. (2) The subsequent verse 'and the matter is decided' with a definite article implies a previously mentioned command, which is what we interpreted. Then an objection is raised: 'The command of God, according to you, is an eternal attribute; coming upon it is impossible.' The answer distinguishes two meanings of 'amr': act (fi'l) or command (as opposed to prohibition). If interpreted as act, it refers to the terrifying events of the Day of Judgment, which is consistent with the first interpretation. If interpreted as command (prohibition), then two sub-interpretations: either a caller announces God's command, with the clouds arriving at the same time, or the coming is the occurrence of something (the text cuts off here).

أمر الله
حذف المضاف
إقامة المضاف إليه مقامه
مجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that attributing an action to God is always literal, even when impossible.
Correction: Razi shows that when the literal meaning is impossible, we must interpret figuratively, as provided by examples and common speech.

Checkpoint

B) The verse says 'and your Lord comes' in Surah al-Fajr.
C) The word 'amr' always means act.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi interprets 'Allah comes' as 'the command of Allah comes' using a well-known figure of speech and provides two Quranic evidences.

?

Addressing the Objection: The Dual Meaning of 'Amr'
Razi addresses an objection and offers a resolution by distinguishing meanings of 'amr', then gives two sub-interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi responds to the objection that God's command is eternal and cannot come, by distinguishing between two meanings of 'amr' and providing further sub-interpretations.

Core Idea
The word 'amr' can mean both act and command—one temporal, one eternal.

Having established the interpretation of 'coming' as the coming of God's command, Imam Razi anticipates an objection: if God's command is eternal, how can it 'come'? He answers by showing that the Arabic word 'amr' has two meanings: act and command (opposite of prohibition). This lesson explores that answer and the sub-interpretations that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage beginning with 'فإن قيل'. Understand the objection that the command is an eternal attribute and thus cannot come.
The objection is based on a theological premise.
2
Distinguish the two meanings of 'amr'
Razi explains that 'amr' can mean 'act' (fi'l) or 'command' (the opposite of prohibition). The act meaning refers to events that occur temporally.
He cites Quranic verses where 'amr' means act.
3
Explore the first sub-interpretation
If 'amr' means command, then the coming is the arrival of a proclamation (a call) on the Day of Resurrection, with the clouds coming simultaneously.
This maintains the literal sense of 'coming' for the announcement.
4
Note the second sub-interpretation
The passage begins the second sub-interpretation but cuts off. It likely continues to explain the coming of the command in the context of 'canopies of clouds'.
The text is incomplete, but the main point is clear.

And if we carry 'amr' upon the 'amr' that is the opposite of prohibition, then there are two aspects: First: The estimation is that a caller calls on the Day of Resurrection: 'Indeed, Allah commands you with such-and-such.' So the coming of the command is the arrival of that call to them. And His saying: 'in canopies of clouds' (2:210) means 'with canopies.' And the estimation: that hearing that call and arrival of those canopies occur at one time. Second: That the intended meaning of the coming of the command of Allah in canopies is: the occurrence of
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the second interpretation. Imam Razi first states that the intended meaning of 'God comes' is 'the command of God comes.' He lays down a general hermeneutical principle: when an action is attributed to something and the literal attribution is impossible, it must be interpreted metaphorically. He gives examples from the Quran: 'those who oppose Allah' means opposing His friends; 'ask the village' means ask its people. Similarly here, 'God comes' means His command comes, by deleting the mudaf (the word 'command') and putting the mudaf ilayh (the word 'God') in its place, a common figure of speech. Two evidences support this: (1) The parallel in Surah an-Nahl uses the phrase 'or the command of your Lord comes,' explaining the ambiguous verse. (2) The subsequent verse 'and the matter is decided' with a definite article implies a previously mentioned command, which is what we interpreted. Then an objection is raised: 'The command of God, according to you, is an eternal attribute; coming upon it is impossible.' The answer distinguishes two meanings of 'amr': act (fi'l) or command (as opposed to prohibition). If interpreted as act, it refers to the terrifying events of the Day of Judgment, which is consistent with the first interpretation. If interpreted as command (prohibition), then two sub-interpretations: either a caller announces God's command, with the clouds arriving at the same time, or the coming is the occurrence of something (the text cuts off here).

Key Terms
صفة قديمة
فعل
طريق
ظلل الغمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'amr' only means 'command' and thus the verse is problematic.
Correction: Razi shows that 'amr' has multiple meanings, including 'act', which resolves the apparent contradiction.

Checkpoint
How does Razi respond to the objection that God's eternal command cannot come?
A) He denies that the command is eternal.
B) He says 'amr' can mean 'act' and thus can occur temporally.
C) He says the coming is metaphorical for knowledge.
D) He says the verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi resolves the objection by distinguishing two meanings of 'amr', showing that it can refer to temporal acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does Razi's distinction between 'act' and 'command' challenge you to be more humble about your assumptions? What would it take for you to adopt a posture of learning like Razi's objector, rather than insisting on your own understanding?

Consider a time when you clung to a literal reading of a verse without considering its linguistic depth. How might that have narrowed your understanding of God's transcendence? Will you now seek out the deeper meanings before forming conclusions? What is one verse you can examine with this new approach starting today?

The Second Argument: The Definite Article in 'Wa Quda al-Amr'
Al-Razi presents the second argument linking the definite article to a previous mention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses the definite article to argue for a metaphorical interpretation of Allah's coming.

Core Idea
The definite article always points to something previously mentioned—even when that mention is implicit.

In this lesson, we examine Al-Razi's second argument from the verse 'Wa quda al-amr' (And the matter was decided). Al-Razi points out that the definite article in 'al-amr' requires a previous mention, which he finds in the implied subject of the earlier clause. This linguistic observation strengthens the metaphorical reading of divine coming.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the flow of Al-Razi's reasoning.
The argument is concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the key linguistic claim
Al-Razi asserts that the definite article (alif and lam) in 'al-amr' indicates a previously known referent (al-mahud al-sabiq). Find this claim in the text.
This is a standard rule in Arabic grammar.
3
Connect to the broader interpretation
Ask yourself: what is the antecedent? Al-Razi says it is the implied 'coming of Allah' or 'command of Allah' from the earlier part of the verse. How does this support the metaphorical interpretation?
The argument links the two parts of the verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إنه تعالى قال بعد هذه الآي : وقضى الأمر ، ولاشك أن الألف واللام المعهود السابق . وهذا يستدعى أن يكون قد جرى ذكره من قبل ذلك، حتى تكون الألف واللام إشارة إليه . وما ذاك إلا الذي أضمر من أن قوله « يأتيهم الله ، أو يأتيهم أمر الله .
And the second: He, exalted, said after these verses: 'And the matter was decided' (Quran 2:210), and there is no doubt that the definite article (alif and lam) refers to a previously known thing. This requires that it have been mentioned before that, so that the definite article points to it. And that is nothing other than what is implied from His saying: 'That Allah comes to them, or that the command of Allah comes to them.'
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage is an argument (the second argument) from al-Razi in support of a particular interpretation of the verse 'Do they await but that Allah should come to them in the shadows of clouds?' (Quran 2:210). The argument focuses on the phrase 'Wa quda al-amr' (And the matter was decided) which appears later in the same verse. Al-Razi asserts that the definite article in 'al-amr' indicates a previously mentioned referent. He argues that this referent must be the 'amr Allah' (command of Allah) or 'Allah' Himself mentioned earlier in the same verse. Thus, the two parts of the verse are linked: the coming of Allah’s command (or of Allah) is the same as the decisive event. The argument follows al-Razi's earlier reasoning that the coming of Allah is to be interpreted metaphorically.

Correction: Al-Razi insists the definite article must refer to a specific previously mentioned referent, which he identifies as the implied coming of Allah or His command.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's argument demonstrates how linguistic precision in Quranic interpretation leads to affirming divine transcendence.
