Two Aspects of Interpreting 'Amr' as Command
Al-Razi gives two ways to interpret 'coming of the command' as a verbal command with clouds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two interpretative approaches to the phrase 'coming of the command of Allah' when 'amr' is taken as a verbal directive, and how they avoid anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
The 'command' can be a created herald's call or divine inscriptions in clouds.

Imam al-Razi continues his analysis of the verse about the coming of Allah's command. Assuming 'amr' means a spoken command (not an eternal attribute), he presents two ways to understand how that command arrives. Both interpretations preserve divine transcendence by explaining the phenomenon through created sounds or writings in clouds, which serve as signs for the Day of Judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noting the two explicit 'wajhan' (aspects). Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Pay attention to the sub-options in the second aspect.
2
Identify the two main interpretations
The first interpretation involves a herald announcing Allah's commands. The second interpretation splits into two possibilities: segmented sounds or visible inscriptions in the clouds.
Al-Razi uses 'aw' (or) to give alternatives.
3
Understand the purpose behind these interpretations
Al-Razi is reconciling the literal wording with the need to avoid attributing physical actions to Allah. The 'coming of the command' is a created event that signals the imminence of judgment.
Think about why he offers multiple interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و أما إن حملنا الأمر ، على الأمر الذى هو ضد النهى . ففيه وجهان : الأول : أن يكون التقدير هو أن مناديا ينادي يوم القيامة : ألا إن الله يأمركم بكذا وبكذا . فيكون إتيان الأمر : هو وصول ذلك النداء ، إليهم . وقوله : « فى ظلل من الغمام ، أى مع ظلل . والتقدير : أن سماع ذلك النداء ووصول تلك الظلال : يكون في زمان واحد . الثاني : أن يكون المراد من إتيان أمر الله تعالى في ظلل : حصول أصوات مقطعة مخصوصة في تلك الغمامات ، دالة على حكم الله تعالى على كل واحد ، مما يليق به من السعادة والشقاوة . أو يكون المراد : أنه تعالى خلق نقوشا منظومة فى ظلل من الغمام ، وتكون النقوش جلية ظاهرة ، لأجل شدة بياض ذلك الغمام، وسواد تلك الكتابة . وهى دالة على أحوال أهل الموقف في الوعد والوعيد وغيرهما . وتكون فائدة الظل من الغيام : أنه تعالى جعلها أمارة لما يريد إنزاله بالقوم فيعلمون : إن الأمر قد قرب وحضر .
And if we interpret 'amr' as the command that is the opposite of prohibition, then it has two aspects: The first: that the estimation is that a caller calls on the Day of Resurrection: 'Indeed, Allah commands you such and such.' So the coming of the command is the arrival of that call to them. And His saying: 'in the shadows of clouds' means 'with shadows.' The estimation: that hearing that call and the arrival of those shadows occur at one time. The second: that the meaning of the coming of the command of Allah, exalted, in shadows is the occurrence of specific segmented sounds in those clouds, indicating Allah's judgment upon each one, of what befits them of happiness and misery. Or it means that He, exalted, created arranged inscriptions in the shadows of clouds, and the inscriptions are clear and apparent, due to the intense whiteness of the clouds and the blackness of that writing. They indicate the states of the people of the standing in promise and threat and other things. And the benefit of the shadow from the clouds is that He, exalted, made them a sign of what He wishes to send down upon the people, so they know that the matter has approached and arrived.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents two alternative interpretations (wajhan) under the assumption that 'amr' in the verse means 'command' in the sense of a verbal directive (as opposed to prohibition). The first interpretation envisions a herald announcing Allah's commands on the Day of Resurrection; the 'coming of the command' is the arrival of this proclamation, and the 'shadows of clouds' accompany it simultaneously. The second interpretation offers two sub-options: either Allah creates distinct sounds within the clouds that convey His judgment of each person's fate, or He creates visible inscriptions (like writing) in the clouds – the white clouds contrasting with black writing – which display the rewards and punishments. In both sub-options, the shadows serve as a sign that the final reckoning is imminent. Al-Razi's approach here is to reconcile the literal wording with metaphorical understandings that avoid anthropomorphism while preserving the rhetorical impact of the Quranic description.

النداء
ظلل من الغمام
أصوات مقطعة
نقوش منظومة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'the coming of the command of Allah' must refer to His eternal attribute descending or moving.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that it can be understood as a created event—a herald's call or sounds/inscriptions in clouds—which preserves divine transcendence.

Checkpoint

B) As the arrival of a proclamation or sounds/writing in the clouds

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi offers two ways to understand the 'coming of the command' as a created phenomenon, preserving divine transcendence while respecting the Quranic wording.

The Third Interpretation: Omission for Emphasis
Imam Razi explains the third interpretation: omitted object for greater intimidation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi interprets Q. 2:210 by omitting the specific punishment to intensify the warning.

Core Idea
Omission multiplies terror by letting the imagination run wild.

Imam al-Razi presents his third interpretation of Q. 2:210, where the verb 'come' is retained but what Allah comes with is deliberately omitted. He argues that this omission makes the warning more terrifying than if the punishment were named. This lesson explores the logic behind this rhetorical strategy and its support from Quranic and linguistic examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: first Razi states the interpretation, then explains the effect, then gives examples.
2
Identify the rhetorical device
The key device is 'omission' (حذف). Razi says the verse deliberately omits what Allah comes with. Ask yourself: why would the Quran omit something important?
3
Understand the effect
Razi explains that naming the punishment confines the listener's mind, but omission scatters thoughts in every direction, making the threat more intense. Reflect on how this works psychologically.
4
Examine the examples
Razi gives three examples: Q. 59:2, Q. 16:26, and a common Arabic expression. Notice how each uses 'come' metaphorically and omits the specific calamity.
5
Summarize the principle
The principle is that apparent anthropomorphic expressions can employ omission and metaphor for rhetorical effect. This interpretation upholds divine transcendence while preserving the Quran's literary power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث فى التأويل : أن يكون المعنى : هل ينظرون إلا أن يأتيهم الله بما وعد من العذاب والحساب ؟ فحذف ما يأتي به تعويلا على الفهم. إذ لو ذكر ذلك العذاب الذى يأتيهم به ، لكان ذلك أسهل عليهم في باب الوعيد . و أما إذا لم يذكره كان أبلغ فى التهويل ، لأنه حينئذ تنقسم خواطرهم ، وتذهب أفكارهم في كل وجه . ومثله قوله تعالى: «فأتاهم الله من حيث لم يحتسبوا وقذف في قلوبهم الرعب ، يخربون بيوتهم بأيديهم ، وأيدى المؤمنين» والمعنى : وأتاهم الله بخذلانه من حيث لم يحتسبوا . وكذا قوله تعالى : «فأتى الله بنيانهم من القواعد» ويقال في الكلام المتعارف المشهور ، إذا سمع بولاية رجل : جاءنا فلان بجوره وظلمه . ولا شك أنه مجاز مشهور
The third interpretation: That the meaning is: 'Do they wait except that Allah should come to them with what He has promised of punishment and reckoning?' So He omitted what He comes with, relying on understanding. For if He had mentioned that punishment which He comes with, it would have been easier for them in the matter of warning. But when He did not mention it, it is more emphatic in intimidation, because then their thoughts scatter and go in every direction. An example of this is His statement: 'Then Allah came to them from where they did not expect and cast terror into their hearts, destroying their houses with their own hands and the hands of the believers.' The meaning: And Allah came to them with His abandonment from where they did not expect. Likewise His statement: 'Then Allah came to their building from the foundations.' It is said in common conventional speech, when one hears of a man's authority: 'So-and-so came to us with his injustice and oppression.' There is no doubt that it is a well-known metaphor.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the third interpretation of Quran 2:210 by Imam Razi. It is a definition and example of a specific rhetorical device: omission for emphasis. Razi explains that the verse deliberately omits the specific thing Allah comes with (punishment or reckoning) to make the threat more terrifying. If the punishment were named, the listener's mind would be confined to that; but by leaving it unspecified, the imagination runs wild, increasing intimidation. He supports this with two Quranic examples: Q. 59:2 (where Allah's coming is with abandonment and terror) and Q. 16:26 (where Allah comes to their building from the foundations). Both verses use the verb 'come' metaphorically to describe divine action in a way that avoids literal movement. Razi also cites a common Arabic expression ('He came with his injustice') as a conventional metaphor. The passage illustrates the principle that apparent anthropomorphic expressions in the Quran often employ omission and metaphor for rhetorical effect, and must be interpreted accordingly.

التأويل
الحذف
التهويل
المجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the verse implies God literally moves from place to place.
Correction: The third interpretation avoids literalism by taking 'coming' as coming with punishment, not physical movement. The omission and metaphor show it is a rhetorical expression.

Checkpoint

A) To make the verse shorter
B) To intensify the warning by scattering the listeners' thoughts
C) To avoid specifying the punishment

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi shows that omitting the specific punishment in Q. 2:210 makes the threat more terrifying by engaging the listener's imagination.

The Fourth Interpretation: 'Fi' as 'Bi'
Imam Razi interprets the verse by taking 'fi' in the sense of 'bi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how interpreting the preposition 'fi' as 'bi' in Quran 2:210 avoids anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Changing a preposition removes the image of God inside clouds.

This lesson explores the fourth interpretation offered by Imam Razi for the verse about Allah coming with shadows of clouds. By taking 'fi' (in) as 'bi' (with), the verse no longer implies that Allah is physically within the clouds. This grammatical move is a key tool in defending divine transcendence while remaining faithful to the Arabic text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the translation to understand the basic rephrasing.
Notice the notation: 'fi' in the sense of 'ba'.
2
Understand preposition substitution
Imam Razi states that prepositions can sometimes replace each other in Arabic. This linguistic principle is essential for this interpretation.
Think of other examples like 'I entered by the door' (ba) vs 'I entered in the house' (fi) – subtle shifts.
3
Reflect on the anthropomorphic implication
Without this interpretation, the verse might suggest God is physically inside clouds. The reinterpretation as 'with clouds' removes that image.
This preserves divine transcendence (tanzih) while accepting the literal wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع فى التأويل : أن تكون فى ، بمعنى الباء - وحروف الجر يقام بعضها مقام البعض - وتقديره : هل ينظرون أن يأتيهم الله بظلل من الغمام والملائكة، والمراد : أنه يأتيهم الله بالغمام مع الملائكة
The fourth interpretation: That 'fi' is in the sense of 'bi' – and prepositions are placed one in place of another – so its estimation is: 'Do they wait except that Allah should come to them with shadows of clouds and angels?' And the intended meaning is that He comes to them with clouds along with angels.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth interpretation of Quran 2:210. It is a grammatical reinterpretation where the preposition 'fi' (in) is taken to mean 'bi' (with). Imam Razi notes the principle that Arabic prepositions sometimes substitute for one another. By reading the verse as 'with shadows of clouds and angels' rather than 'in shadows', the anthropomorphic implication of God being physically inside the clouds is removed. The verse simply states that God accompanies the clouds and angels, which is understood metaphorically as a sign of divine power. This interpretation relies on a well-established linguistic flexibility in Arabic.

Key Terms
حروف الجر
في
الباء
ظلل الغمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse requires God to be physically inside the clouds.
Correction: The preposition 'fi' can mean 'with', so the verse means God brings clouds and angels with Him, not that He is inside them.

Checkpoint

B) Substitution of prepositions
C) Metaphorical extension of meaning
D) Allegorical interpretation of the entire verse

Key Takeaway
The preposition 'fi' can mean 'bi', allowing the verse to describe divine accompaniment rather than physical containment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Fifth Interpretation: The Verse Reports Jewish Beliefs
Razi argues the verse is a narrative about Jewish anthropomorphic expectations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi argues that Q. 2:210 merely reports Jewish expectations, not a real divine action, thereby preserving divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The verse reports what Jews expected, not what God does.

In this lesson, we encounter Imam Razi's fifth and strongest interpretation of the verse. He argues that the context of the surrounding verses shows the verse addresses the Jews, and thus it only narrates their false belief that God would come in clouds. This interpretation resolves the apparent conflict with divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the argument. Notice how Razi begins by asserting this is the strongest interpretation.
2
Identify the three parts of the argument
Razi's argument has three parts: (1) establishing the context (the verse addresses Jews), (2) claiming the verse is a narrative about their beliefs, (3) providing evidence from the incident with Moses. Keep an eye on these as you read the commentary.
3
Contrast with literalist reading
Compare Razi's interpretation with a literalist reading that would take the verse as describing an actual divine action. Ask yourself: How does Razi's context change the meaning?
This helps see the theological stakes.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that this interpretation removes the theological problem (ishkal) and is the definitive answer. Consider why he says it is 'relied-upon' (mu'tamad).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : - وهو أقوى من كل ما سبق – إنا ذكرنا في التفسير الكبير ، أن قوله تعالى : ( يا أيها الذين آمنوا . ادخلوا في السلم كافة ) إنما نزل فى حق اليهود. وعلى هذا التقدير يكون قوله تعالى : ( فإن زللتم من بعد ما جاءتكم البينات ) خطابا مع اليهود، فيكون قوله : ( هل ينظرون إلا أن يأتيهم الله في ظلل من الغمام ؟ ) حكاية عنهم . والمعنى : أنهم لا يقبلون دينكم ، إلا لأنهم ينتظرون أن يأتيهم الله في ظلل من الغمام . ومما يدل على أن المراد ذلك : أنهم فعلوا ذلك مع موسى عليه السلام ، فقالوا : « لن نؤمن لك ، حتى نرى الله جهرة » وإذا ثبت أن هذه الآية حكاية عن حال اليهود واعتقادهم ، لم يمتنع إجراء الآية على ظاهرها . وذلك لأن اليهود كانوا على دين التشبيه . وكانوا يجوّزون المجيء والذهاب على الله تعالى، وكانوا يقولون : إنه تعالى تجلى لموسى عليه السلام على الطور في ظلل من الغمام ، فظنوا مثل ذلك في زمان محمد عليه السلام . ومعلوم : أن مذهبهم ليس بحجة. و بالجملة : فإنه يدل على أن قوما ينتظرون أن يأتيهم الله . وليس في الآية دلالة على أن أولئك الأقوام محقون أو مبطلون . وعلى هذا التقدير زال الإشكال . وهذا هو الجواب المعتمد عن تمسكهم بالآية المذكورة في سورة الأنعام
The fifth interpretation – and it is stronger than all that preceded – we mentioned in al-Tafsir al-Kabir that His statement: 'O you who have believed, enter into Islam completely' was revealed concerning the Jews. On this estimation, His statement: 'But if you deviate after clear proofs have come to you' is addressed to the Jews. Thus His statement: 'Do they wait except that Allah should come to them in shadows of clouds?' is a narrative about them. The meaning: They do not accept your religion except because they await that Allah should come to them in shadows of clouds. Evidence that this is intended: They did that with Moses, peace be upon him, saying: 'We will not believe you until we see Allah plainly.' When it is established that this verse is a narrative about the state of the Jews and their belief, there is no objection to interpreting the verse according to its apparent meaning. That is because the Jews were upon the religion of anthropomorphism. They would permit coming and going for Allah, and they said that He manifested Himself to Moses on the mountain in shadows of clouds. So they thought the like of that in the time of Muhammad, peace be upon him. And it is known that their doctrine is not a proof. In summary: It indicates that a people are waiting for Allah to come to them. There is no indication in the verse that those people are right or wrong. On this estimation, the problem is removed. And this is the relied-upon answer against their adherence to the mentioned verse in Surat al-An'am.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth and strongest interpretation. It is a argumentative refutation of the literalist reading of Q. 2:210. Imam Razi argues that the verse is not a statement about God's action, but a report (hikaya) of what the Jews expected. He supports this by showing that the surrounding verses (Q. 2:208-209) address the Jews. The Jews, being anthropomorphists (mushabbiha), thought God would come in clouds as they believed He did for Moses. Therefore, the verse merely narrates their false expectation, not a real event. Since the Jews' doctrine is not a proof, the verse cannot be used to support divine movement. Razi concludes that this interpretation resolves the theological difficulty (ishkal) and is the definitive response to those who use the verse to argue for anthropomorphism.

التشبيه
الحكاية
الظاهر

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse describes an actual event of God coming in clouds.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the verse is a narrative about Jewish false beliefs, not a factual description of divine action.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth interpretation, who does Q. 2:210 describe?
A) The believers who await God's reward
B) The Jews who expected God to come in clouds
C) The polytheists who denied God
D) All people waiting for the Day of Judgment

Key Takeaway
The verse reports the Jews' false expectation of a physical divine appearance, not a real event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What does this lesson teach you about the danger of imposing your own expectations onto God's word? Will you commit to studying the context of difficult verses before forming a conclusion?
?
The Jews demanded to see God physically before believing. In what areas of your faith are you demanding God conform to your expectations rather than trusting His transcendence? How can you actively cultivate awe for a God who is beyond human limitations, instead of reducing Him to what you can imagine?
?

Would you be willing to examine your own assumptions this week and repent of any attempt to confine the Unconfined?

The Fifth Argument: Quoting Jewish Belief
Imam Razi presents the fifth argument that the verse is a quotation of Jewish belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's fifth and strongest argument that the verse about Allah coming in clouds is a quotation of Jewish belief and does not imply literalism.

Core Idea
The verse quotes Jewish expectation, not divine action.

This lesson presents Imam Razi's most powerful argument against those who take the verse 'Do they await but that Allah should come to them in clouds of shadow?' as literal. He explains that the verse was revealed about the Jews and reports their anthropomorphic anticipation, not a factual event. By understanding this, the apparent problem of God's coming is resolved.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the verse
Understand that the previous verses (2:208-209) address the Jews, so the coming verse is part of that address.
Razi mentions his Tafsir al-Kabir for this context.
2
Identify the argument's strength
Razi declares this the strongest of all previous arguments. Note his confidence.
3
Follow the reasoning
See how Razi links the verse to Jewish history: they demanded to see Allah plainly with Moses. Thus, their anticipation of Allah coming physically is consistent.
The verse is a report (hikaya) of their state.
4
Apply the conclusion
Once the verse is seen as a report of Jewish belief, it does not conflict with divine transcendence. The Jews' anthropomorphic doctrine is not a proof.
Literal wording is fine because it quotes them, not affirms it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : - وهو أقوى من كل ما سبق – إنا ذكرنا في التفسير الكبير ، أن قوله تعالى : ( يا أيها الذين آمنوا . ادخلوا في السلم كافة ، (۳۱) إنما نزل فى حق اليهود. وعلى هذا التقدير يكون قوله تعالى : فإن زللتم من بعد ما جاءتكم البينات (۳۲) خطابا مع اليهود، فيكون قوله : « هل ينظرون إلا أن يأتيهم الله في ظلل من الغمام ؟ (۳۳) حكاية عنهم . والمعنى : أنهم لا يقبلون دينكم ، إلا لأنهم ينتظرون أن يأتيهم الله في ظلل من الغمام . ومما يدل على أن المراد ذلك : أنهم فعلوا ذلك مع موسى عليه السلام ، فقالوا : « لن نؤمن لك ، حتى نرى الله جهرة (٣٤) وإذا ثبت أن هذه الآية حكاية عن حال اليهود واعتقادهم ، لم يمتنع إجراء الآية على ظاهرها . وذلك لأن اليهود كانوا على دين التشبيه . وكانوا يجوزون المجيء و الذهاب على الله تعالى، وكانوا يقولون : إنه تعالى تجلى لموسى عليه السلام على الطور في ظلل من الغمام ، فظنوا مثل ذلك في زمان محمد عليه السلام . ومعلوم : أن مذهبهم ليس بحجة . و بالجملة : فإنه يدل على أن قوما ينتظرون أن يأتيهم الله . وليس في الآية دلالة على أن أولئك الأقوام محقون أو مبطلون . وعلى هذا التقدير زال الإشكال . وهذا هو الجواب المعتمد عن تمسكهم بالآية المذكورة في سورة الأنعام (٣٥)
The fifth aspect - and it is stronger than all that preceded -: We mentioned in the Great Commentary that His statement, the Exalted, 'O you who have believed, enter into Islam completely' (2:208) was only revealed concerning the Jews. And on this estimation, His statement, the Exalted, 'But if you deviate after clear proofs have come to you' (2:209) is an address to the Jews, so His statement 'Do they await but that Allah should come to them in clouds of shadow?' (2:210) is a report about them. And the meaning is that they do not accept your religion except because they are waiting for Allah to come to them in clouds of shadow. And among the evidence that the intent is that is that they did that with Moses, peace be upon him, and said, 'We will not believe you until we see Allah plainly' (2:55). And if it is established that this verse is a report about the state of the Jews and their belief, then there is no impediment to applying the verse according to its literal wording. That is because the Jews were upon the religion of anthropomorphism. They permitted coming and going upon Allah, the Exalted, and they used to say that He, the Exalted, manifested Himself to Moses, peace be upon him, on the mountain in clouds of shadow, and they thought the like of that in the time of Muhammad, peace be upon him. And it is known that their doctrine is not an authority. In summary: it indicates that a group of people are waiting for Allah to come to them. And there is no indication in the verse that those groups are right or wrong. On this estimation, the difficulty is removed. And this is the relied-upon answer regarding their holding onto the mentioned verse in Surat al-An'am (6:158).
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a proof (specifically the fifth and strongest argument) that the verse 'Do they await but that Allah should come to them in clouds of shadow?' (Quran 2:210) does not imply a literal coming of God. The author, Imam Razi, begins by stating that this is the strongest of all previous arguments. He then explains that the verse was revealed concerning the Jews, based on his commentary on the preceding verses (2:208-209) which address the People of the Book. Thus, the phrase 'Do they await...' is reported as a statement of the Jews, not as a description of reality. The meaning is that they refuse to accept Islam because they expect God to come to them in a physical manner, as they did with Prophet Moses when they demanded to see God plainly. Imam Razi emphasizes that once it is established that this is a report about Jewish belief, the verse can be interpreted according to its literal wording because the Jews held an anthropomorphic doctrine (tashbih). They believed God can come and go, and they thought that God manifested Himself to Moses on the mountain in clouds. They expected the same in the time of Prophet Muhammad. However, the author points out that their belief is not a proof. He concludes that the verse only tells us that some people are waiting for God to come, but it does not indicate whether they are correct or not. This resolution solves the problem, and he presents it as the definitive answer to those who use this verse to argue for literalism.

Objection and Answer: Consistency with the Verse's Ending
Imam Razi answers an objection about the consistency of the interpretation with the verse's ending.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends his interpretation by showing that the verse's ending serves as a threat, not a contradiction.

Core Idea
The verse's ending is a threat, not a statement of God's literal coming.

Imam Razi faces an objection: if the verse reports Jewish belief, why does it end with 'And to Allah are returned all matters'? The answer lies in seeing that ending as a threat to the stubborn Jews. Let's examine the objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the opening 'If it is said...' to understand what the objector is challenging.
Notice the objector focuses on the verse's ending phrase.
2
Analyze the answer
Imam Razi responds by distinguishing between the reported statement of the Jews and God's own threat.
He does not reinterpret the ending but recontextualizes it.
3
Consider the method
Reflect on how this objection-and-answer reveals the importance of context in interpreting ambiguous verses.
This is a common method in kalam tafsir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : هذا التأويل كيف يتعلق بهذه الآية ، لأنه قال في آخرها : . وإلى الله ترجع الأمور ، (٣٦) ؟ قلنا : إنه تعالى حكى عنادهم وتوقيفهم قبول الدين الحق ، على الشرط الفاسد . تم ذكر بعده ما يجرى مجرى التهديد لهم ، فقال : « وإلى الله ترجع الأمور ،
If it is said: How does this interpretation relate to this verse, because He said at its end: 'And to Allah are returned all matters' (2:210)? We say: He, the Exalted, reported their stubbornness and their conditional acceptance of the true religion on a corrupt condition. Then He mentioned after that what has the effect of a threat to them, and He said: 'And to Allah are returned all matters'.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (qāla wa qulnā). The objector asks how Imam Razi's interpretation (that the verse reports Jewish belief) can be consistent with the end of the verse which says 'And to Allah are returned all matters'. The objector implies that the ending seems to affirm that God will come. The author answers that the first part of the verse reports their stubbornness and their conditional acceptance of Islam on a false condition. Then the ending is a threat: it means all affairs ultimately return to God, so they will be held accountable. Thus, the ending does not describe a literal coming but serves as a warning.

Key Terms
تأويل
تهديد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse's ending contradicts the interpretation that it reports Jewish belief, so the interpretation must be wrong.
Correction: The ending is a separate threat from God, not part of the Jewish statement, so there is no contradiction.

Checkpoint

B) It is a threat directed at the Jews
C) It is a general statement about the afterlife
D) It is an abrogated clause

Key Takeaway
The verse's ending does not describe divine action but serves as a threat to wrongdoers, resolving the apparent contradiction.

First Approach: Deletion of the Deleted
First approach: interpretations via deletion of the deleted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first interpretive approach to the verse 'وجاء ربك' which involves deletion of the deleted, and its three specific interpretations.

Core Idea
The verse's meaning can be unpacked by assuming an omitted word.

Imam Razi begins by classifying the interpretations of the verse 'وجاء ربك'. The first major approach is to assume that the verse involves deletion of the deleted (حذف المضاف). Under this approach, he lists three plausible meanings. This lesson explores each of these meanings and how they preserve divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first approach
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Imam Razi introduces the concept of deletion of the deleted.
2
Identify the three interpretations
Find the three interpretations listed under this approach. Each one substitutes a different implied word for 'your Lord'.
The interpretations are: (1) His command, (2) His overwhelming power, (3) manifestation of knowledge of Allah.
3
Understand the reasoning
For the second interpretation, note the analogy to a king's army. For the third, note how the manifestation of knowledge functions as a 'coming'.
These interpretations avoid attributing physical movement to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : وجاء ربك ، والملك صفا صفا (۳۷) فالكلام فيه أيضا على وجهين : الأول : أن تحمل هذه الآية على باب حذف المضاف . وعلى هذا الوجه ، ففى الآية وجوه : أحدها : وجاء أمر ربك بالمحاسبة والمجازاة . وثانيها : وجاء قهر ربك . كما يقال : جاءنا الملك القادر . إذا جاء عسكره . وثالثها : وجاء ظهور معرفة الله تعالى بالضرورة في ذلك اليوم . فصار ذلك جارياً مجرى مجيئه وظهوره . الوجه الثاني : إنا لا نحمل هذه الآية على حذف المضاف . ثم فيه وجهان : الأول : أن يكون المراد من هذه الآية : التمسك بظهور آيات الله تعالى ، وسر آثار قدرته وقهره وسلطانه . والمقصود : تمثيل تلك الحالة بحال الملك إذا حضر ، فإنه يظهر بمجرد حضوره من آثار الهيبة والسياسة، ما لا يظهر بحضور عساكره كلها .
As for His statement, the Exalted, 'And your Lord comes, and the angels in rows' (89:22), the discussion concerning it also has two aspects: The first: that this verse is interpreted according to the category of deletion of the deleted. According to this aspect, there are several faces in the verse: One of them: The command of your Lord comes for accounting and recompense. The second: The overwhelming power of your Lord comes, just as it is said: 'The powerful king came to us' when his army comes. The third: The manifestation of the knowledge of Allah, the Exalted, necessarily on that Day comes, so that becomes in the place of His coming and manifestation. The second aspect: We do not interpret this verse by deletion of the deleted. Then within it there are two faces: The first: That the intent of this verse is to adhere to the manifestation of the signs of Allah, the Exalted, and the secret of the traces of His power, overwhelming force, and sovereignty. And the purpose is to liken that situation to the state of a king when he appears, for by his mere presence, there appears from traces of awe and governance that which does not appear with the presence of all his armies.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of two major approaches to interpreting the verse 'And your Lord comes, and the angels in rows' (Quran 89:22). The author begins by stating there are two aspects (قوله فيه أيضا على وجهين). The first aspect is to interpret the verse as involving deletion of the deleted (حذف المضاف), meaning an implied word is omitted. Under this aspect, there are three possible interpretations: (1) the command of your Lord comes for accounting and recompense; (2) the overwhelming power (qahr) of your Lord comes, analogous to saying 'the powerful king came' when his army comes; (3) the manifestation of the knowledge of Allah necessarily on that Day comes, effectively serving as His coming and manifestation. The second aspect is to not assume deletion. Under this aspect, there are two faces, but only the first is given here: that the meaning is adhering to the manifestation of Allah's signs and the traces of His power, authority, and sovereignty. The purpose is to compare that situation to a king when he appears personally, because his mere presence produces awe and governance that his entire army's presence does not. The passage ends before presenting the second face of this second aspect.

Key Terms
حذف المضاف
أمر ربك
قهر ربك
ظهور معرفة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the verse must be taken literally, implying Allah physically moves or comes like a creature.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the verse can be understood through deletion of the deleted, substituting a related but non-anthropomorphic meaning.

Checkpoint
Under the first approach (deletion of the deleted), how many interpretations does Imam Razi provide for 'وجاء ربك'?
A) Two
B) Three
C) One
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The first approach interprets 'وجاء ربك' by assuming an omitted word, yielding three non-literal meanings that uphold divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Second Approach: No Deletion and the Analogy to a King
Second approach: interpretation without deletion and the analogy to a king.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second interpretive approach to the verse 'وجاء ربك' which does not assume deletion of the deleted, and its explanation through the analogy of a king.

Core Idea
The verse is best understood by comparing it to a king's awe-inspiring presence.

Imam Razi presents a second approach that does not rely on deletion of the deleted. Instead, it interprets the verse as emphasizing the manifestation of Allah's signs and power. The key concept is 'تمثيل' (likening) the situation to a king whose mere presence produces greater awe than his entire army. This lesson examines that analogy and its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the second approach
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that this approach rejects deletion and offers a different face.
2
Understand the first face
The first face of this approach interprets the verse as referring to the manifestation of Allah's signs and the traces of His power, overwhelming force, and sovereignty.
It is about the visible effects of divine attributes.
3
Analyze the analogy
The purpose is to liken (تمثيل) that situation to a king appearing personally. His mere presence produces awe and governance that even his entire army cannot achieve.
This shows that the verse is a metaphor for the overwhelming nature of divine manifestation on that Day.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : وجاء ربك ، والملك صفا صفا (۳۷) فالكلام فيه أيضا على وجهين : الأول : أن تحمل هذه الآية على باب حذف المضاف . وعلى هذا الوجه ، ففى الآية وجوه : أحدها : وجاء أمر ربك بالمحاسبة والمجازاة . وثانيها : وجاء قهر ربك . كما يقال : جاءنا الملك القادر . إذا جاء عسكره . وثالثها : وجاء ظهور معرفة الله تعالى بالضرورة في ذلك اليوم . فصار ذلك جارياً مجرى مجيئه وظهوره . الوجه الثاني : إنا لا نحمل هذه الآية على حذف المضاف . ثم فيه وجهان : الأول : أن يكون المراد من هذه الآية : التمسك بظهور آيات الله تعالى ، وسر آثار قدرته وقهره وسلطانه . والمقصود : تمثيل تلك الحالة بحال الملك إذا حضر ، فإنه يظهر بمجرد حضوره من آثار الهيبة والسياسة، ما لا يظهر بحضور عساكره كلها .
As for His statement, the Exalted, 'And your Lord comes, and the angels in rows' (89:22), the discussion concerning it also has two aspects: The first: that this verse is interpreted according to the category of deletion of the deleted. According to this aspect, there are several faces in the verse: One of them: The command of your Lord comes for accounting and recompense. The second: The overwhelming power of your Lord comes, just as it is said: 'The powerful king came to us' when his army comes. The third: The manifestation of the knowledge of Allah, the Exalted, necessarily on that Day comes, so that becomes in the place of His coming and manifestation. The second aspect: We do not interpret this verse by deletion of the deleted. Then within it there are two faces: The first: That the intent of this verse is to adhere to the manifestation of the signs of Allah, the Exalted, and the secret of the traces of His power, overwhelming force, and sovereignty. And the purpose is to liken that situation to the state of a king when he appears, for by his mere presence, there appears from traces of awe and governance that which does not appear with the presence of all his armies.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of two major approaches to interpreting the verse 'And your Lord comes, and the angels in rows' (Quran 89:22). The author begins by stating there are two aspects (قوله فيه أيضا على وجهين). The first aspect is to interpret the verse as involving deletion of the deleted (حذف المضاف), meaning an implied word is omitted. Under this aspect, there are three possible interpretations: (1) the command of your Lord comes for accounting and recompense; (2) the overwhelming power (qahr) of your Lord comes, analogous to saying 'the powerful king came' when his army comes; (3) the manifestation of the knowledge of Allah necessarily on that Day comes, effectively serving as His coming and manifestation. The second aspect is to not assume deletion. Under this aspect, there are two faces, but only the first is given here: that the meaning is adhering to the manifestation of Allah's signs and the traces of His power, authority, and sovereignty. The purpose is to compare that situation to a king when he appears personally, because his mere presence produces awe and governance that his entire army's presence does not. The passage ends before presenting the second face of this second aspect.

Key Terms
تمثيل
آيات الله
قدرته
قهره
سلطانه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To show that Allah physically appears

C) To prove that Allah has a body
D) To deny the resurrection

Key Takeaway
The second approach interprets 'وجاء ربك' without deletion, using an analogy to a king's presence to highlight the overwhelming manifestation of divine power and awe.

Interpretations of 'And your Lord comes'
Razi lists possible interpretations of 'وجاء ربك'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main interpretative approaches to the verse 'وجاء ربك' and the five specific interpretations Razi offers, demonstrating how Islamic theology maintains divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Razi shows that 'coming' in the Qur'an cannot mean physical movement.

This passage from Imam Razi's Asas al-Taqdis addresses a critical verse for divine transcendence: 'And your Lord comes...' This apparent anthropomorphism is systematically interpreted by Razi, who lists possible meanings under two broad approaches. This classification is a model of how kalam scholars handle ambiguous texts while upholding tanzih.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the structure: Razi introduces two main aspects, each with several interpretations.
The passage uses technical terms like حذف المضاف (deletion of the genitive).
2
Identify the two main aspects
The first aspect assumes deletion of a genitive (e.g., 'command' or 'coercion' is coming). The second aspect does not assume deletion but interprets 'Lord' metaphorically.
3
Learn the three interpretations under deletion
Under the first aspect, Razi gives three possibilities: (1) the command of your Lord comes, (2) the coercion of your Lord comes, (3) the manifestation of knowledge of Allah comes. Each avoids attributing physical movement to Allah.
The third interpretation likens occurrence of knowledge to 'coming'.
4
Learn the two interpretations without deletion
Under the second aspect, the verse is not about deletion: (1) it is a likening to a king's awe-inspiring presence, (2) 'Lord' (رب) may refer to a great angel who is a nourisher.
The second interpretation is more unconventional, suggesting a created being.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Ask: Why does Razi present these interpretations? The goal is to affirm that Allah does not move or change location, while accepting the Qur'anic text. Every interpretation here shields divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : وجاء ربك ، والملك صفا صفا فالكلام فيه أيضا على وجهين :
الأول : أن تحمل هذه الآية على باب حذف المضاف . وعلى هذا الوجه ، ففي الآية وجوه :
أحدها : وجاء أمر ربك بالمحاسبة والمجازاة .
وثانيها : وجاء قهر ربك . كما يقال : جاءنا الملك القادر . إذا جاء عسكره .
وثالثها : وجاء ظهور معرفة الله تعالى بالضرورة في ذلك اليوم . فصار ذلك جاريا مجرى مجيئه وظهوره .
الوجه الثاني : إنا لا نحمل هذه الآية على حذف المضاف . ثم فيه وجهان :
الأول : أن يكون المراد من هذه الآية : التمسك بظهور آيات الله تعالى ، وسر آثار قدرته وقهره وسلطانه . والمقصود : تمثيل تلك الحالة بحال الملك إذا حضر ، فإنه يظهر بمجرد حضوره من آثار الهيبة والسياسة، ما لا يظهر بحضور عساكره كلها .
الثاني : إن الرب هو المربى . فلعل ملكا عظيما هو أعظم الملائكة، كان مربيا للذي ، وكان هو المراد من قوله : وجاء ربك والملك صفا صفا
As for His saying, exalted: 'And your Lord comes, and the angels row upon row,' the discussion about it also has two aspects: First: that this verse is carried on the mode of deletion of the genitive. On this aspect, the verse has several interpretations: One: 'And the command of your Lord comes for reckoning and recompense.' Second: 'And the coercion of your Lord comes,' as it is said: 'The powerful king came to us' if his army came. Third: 'And the manifestation of knowledge of Allah the Exalted necessarily on that day came,' so that became akin to His coming and appearance. Second aspect: We do not carry this verse on deletion of the genitive. Then it has two interpretations: First: that the meaning of this verse is adherence to the appearance of Allah's signs, and the secret of the effects of His power, coercion, and sovereignty. The purpose is to liken that state to the state of a king when he presents himself, for he displays through his mere presence effects of awe and authority that are not displayed by the presence of all his armies. Second: that 'the Lord' means the nourisher. Perhaps a great angel, who is the greatest of angels, was the nourisher of [something], and he was intended by His saying: 'And your Lord comes, and the angels row upon row.'
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a classification of interpretations for the Qur'anic verse 'And your Lord comes' (Q. 89:22). Razi presents two main approaches. The first approach interprets the verse with a deleted genitive (حذف المضاف), meaning that something like 'command' or 'coercion' or 'manifestation' is implied as coming, not God Himself. Three specific interpretations are given: (1) the command of God comes for judgment, (2) the force of God's power comes, using an analogy of a king whose army arrives, (3) the manifestation of necessary knowledge of God on that day. The second approach does not assume deletion but interprets 'the Lord' metaphorically: either as a representation of God's overwhelming signs likened to a king's presence, or as referring to a great angel who is a nourisher (رب) in a subordinate sense.

تمثيل
ظهور آيات

Key Takeaway
Razi's treatment of this verse exemplifies the kalam method of reconciling scriptural language with rational principles of divine transcendence.

The Linguistic and Rational Meaning of Divine Descent (Nuzul)
Razi presents linguistic and rational arguments that the divine descent is not physical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi defends divine transcendence by showing that 'nuzul' can be used non-physically and that the hadith's purpose does not require physical movement.

Core Idea
Descent (nuzul) in Arabic does not require physical movement.

The hadith of Allah's descent to the lowest heaven is often cited by anthropomorphists. Imam al-Razi offers a two-pronged defense: first, a linguistic analysis showing that 'nuzul' is used in the Quran and Arabic for non-physical actions, and second, a rational argument that the stated purpose of the descent is either unachieved or unnecessary. This lesson walks through both arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice that Razi divides his discussion into two types: linguistic and rational.
The first type gives four examples; the second type is a conditional argument.
2
Understand the first type: linguistic
Razi gives four examples where 'nuzul' does not mean physical movement. For each, note how the context forces a non-physical interpretation.
Examples: cattle, tranquility, Quran, and al-Shafi'i's saying.
3
Understand the second type: rational
Razi considers the purpose of the descent: either for us to hear God's call (which doesn't happen) or for the call itself (which doesn't require descent). This shows that the hadith must be interpreted metaphorically.
This is a powerful argument by reduction.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
Think about why Razi goes to such lengths. The issue is central to tanzih (divine transcendence). This passage shows how classical scholars reconcile revealed texts with rational theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحديث المشتمل على النزول إلى السماء الدنيا . فالكلام عليه من نوعين :
الأول : بيان النزول وهو أن النزول قد يستعمل في غير الانتقال . وتقريره من وجوه :
أحدها : قوله تعالى : وأنزل لكم من الأنعام ثمانية أزواج ونحن نعلم بالضرورة : أن الجمل أو البقر ، ما نزل من السماء إلى الأرض ، على سبيل الانتقال . وقال الله تعالى : فأنزل الله سكينته على رسوله والانتقال على السكينة محال . وقال الله تعالى : نزل به الروح الأمين على قلبك والقرآن سواء قلنا : إنه عبارة عن صفة قديمة ، أو قلنا : إنه عبارة عن الحرف والصوت : فالانتقال عليه محال . وقال الشافعي المطلبي - رضى الله عنه : - دخلت مصر فلم يفهموا كلامي . فنزلت ثم نزلت ، ولم يكن المراد من هذا النزول : الانتقال .
الثاني : إنه إن كان المقصود من النزول من العرش إلى السماء الدنيا ، أن يسمع نداؤه، فهذا المقصود ما حصل . وإن كان المقصود مجرد النداء، سواء سمعناه أو لم نسمعه ، فهذا ما لا حاجة فيه إلى النزول من العرش .
As for the hadith that includes the descent to the lowest heaven, the discussion about it is of two types: First: an explanation of 'descent' (nuzul) – that descent may be used in other than physical movement. Its elaboration is from several angles: One: His saying, exalted: 'And He sent down for you of the cattle eight pairs' – and we know necessarily that the camel or cow did not descend from heaven to earth by way of physical movement. And Allah the Exalted said: 'Then Allah sent down His tranquility upon His Messenger' – and physical movement upon tranquility is impossible. And Allah the Exalted said: 'The Trustworthy Spirit has brought it down upon your heart' – and the Quran, whether we say it is an expression of an eternal attribute or we say it is an expression of letter and sound, physical movement upon it is impossible. And al-Shafi'i al-Muttalibi – may Allah be pleased with him – said: 'I entered Egypt and they did not understand my speech. So I descended, then I descended,' and he did not mean by this descent physical movement. Second: If the purpose of descending from the Throne to the lowest heaven was so that His call be heard, then this purpose was not achieved. And if the purpose was merely the call, whether we hear it or not, then there is no need for descent from the Throne.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage addresses the famous hadith about Allah's descent to the lowest heaven. Razi presents two independent arguments. The first argument is linguistic: the word 'nuzul' (descent) can be used in Arabic without implying physical movement. He provides four examples: (1) the Quranic verse about 'sending down' cattle, which clearly did not involve physical descent from heaven; (2) the sending down of tranquility (sakinah) upon the Prophet, which is an abstract concept; (3) the 'sending down' of the Quran by the Spirit, which is also not a physical movement regardless of one's view on the nature of the Quran; (4) an anecdote from Imam al-Shafi'i who used 'nazaltu' to mean 'I settled' or 'I alighted' in a city, not physical descent from a height. The second argument is rational: if the purpose of God's descent were for us to hear His call, then we do not actually hear it (since the hadith refers to the last third of the night, and we don't hear a call), so the purpose fails. If the purpose were simply the call itself regardless of hearing, then no descent is needed because God can call without moving. Thus the hadith must be interpreted metaphorically.

Key Terms
نزول
انتقال
سكينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith of descent implies that Allah physically moves or changes location.
Correction: As Razi shows, the word 'nuzul' can be used non-physically, and the purpose of the hadith does not require physical movement.

Checkpoint

B) Sending down tranquility
C) Sending down rain

Key Takeaway
Razi's treatment shows that the hadith of descent must be interpreted metaphorically to preserve divine transcendence without rejecting the text.

Allegorical Interpretation of 'And Your Lord Comes'
Razi proposes an allegorical interpretation of the verse 'And your Lord comes' by redefining 'rabb'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the linguistic meaning of 'rabb' to defend divine transcendence by interpreting the verse metaphorically.

Core Idea
The word 'rabb' can refer to a created being when it means 'nurturer'.

In this lesson, we examine a key argument from Imam Razi's Asas al-Taqdis. The verse 'And your Lord comes' might seem to imply bodily movement for God. Razi offers a linguistic and allegorical interpretation to preserve divine transcendence. He shows that the word 'rabb' here means 'nurturer', and that a great angel, as nurturer, is the intended referent.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Note that it begins with 'الثاني', indicating this is part of a series of arguments.
2
Understand the linguistic basis
Razi starts by defining 'rabb' as 'al-murabbi' (the nurturer). This is crucial because it opens the door for a created referent.
3
Apply to the verse
He then suggests that the 'lord' coming could be a great angel who nurtured something/someone. This removes any notion of Allah's physical movement.
The passage concludes that this angel is the intended meaning in the Quranic verse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن الرب هو المربى . فلعل ملكا عظيما هو أعظم الملائكة، كان مربيا للذي [؟]، وكان هو المراد من قوله : وجاء ربك والملك صفا صفا
Second: Indeed, 'al-rabb' means the nurturer. Perhaps a great angel, the greatest of the angels, was the nurturer of that one, and he is the intended meaning of His saying: 'And your Lord comes, and the angels, rank after rank.'
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of anthropomorphism. Razi proposes an alternative interpretation for the verse 'And your Lord comes' (Qur'an 89:22). He suggests that the word 'Lord' (rabb) here does not refer to God Himself, but rather to a created being, specifically a great angel who acts as a nurturer (murabbī). The phrase 'your Lord' could be honorific or metaphorical. Razi bases this on the linguistic meaning of 'rabb' as 'nurturer' or 'master', which can apply to angels. Thus, the verse does not imply God's physical movement.

Key Terms

المربى

المجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume the word 'rabb' always and exclusively refers to God.
Correction: In this context, 'rabb' means 'nurturer' and refers to a great angel, not God Himself.

Key Takeaway

Linguistic Proof that 'Descent' Can Be Metaphorical
Razi proves 'descent' is used metaphorically without spatial movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses linguistic evidence from the Qur'an and Arabic speech to show that the word 'descent' (nuzūl) can be used metaphorically, thereby defending divine transcendence against anthropomorphic interpretations.

Core Idea
'Descent' in Arabic often means something other than moving downward.

Before we can discuss the hadith about God's descent, we must first clarify what 'descent' itself means. Imam al-Razi demonstrates through Qur'anic verses and a report from al-Shafi'i that the word is frequently used in non-physical, metaphorical senses. This linguistic groundwork is essential for understanding how the attributes of Allah are to be interpreted without corporealism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi structures his argument with multiple examples.
The passage uses a repetitive pattern: 'God said...' and then explains why physical movement is impossible.
2
Identify each of the four examples
Razi gives four distinct proofs: 1) the verse about cattle, 2) the verse about tranquility, 3) the verse about the Spirit and the Qur'an, and 4) the report from al-Shafi'i. Find each one in the text.
3
Understand why each example works
For each example, ask: What is being 'sent down'? Why can't it physically descend? This reveals the metaphorical usage.
4
Connect to divine descent
Razi's goal is to show that when the Prophet ﷺ says 'Our Lord descends to the lowest heaven,' it can be understood metaphorically, without spatial movement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : بيان النزول وهو أن النزول قد يستعمل في غير الانتقال . وتقريره من وجوه : أحدها : قوله تعالى : وأنزل لكم من الأنعام ثمانية أزواج ونحن نعلم بالضرورة : أن الجمل أو البقر ، ما نزل من السماء إلى الأرض ، على سبيل الانتقال . وقال الله تعالى : فأنزل الله سكينته على رسوله والانتقال على السكينة محال . وقال الله تعالى : نزل به الروح الأمين على قلبك والقرآن سواء قلنا : إنه عبارة عن صفة قديمة ، أو قلنا : إنه عبارة عن الحرف والصوت : فالانتقال عليه محال . وقال الشافعي المطلبي - رضى الله عنه : - دخلت مصر فلم يفهموا كلامي . فنزلت تم نزلت ، ولم يكن المراد من هذا النزول : الانتقال
First: Explanation of 'descent' (al-nuzūl), and it is that 'descent' may be used without physical movement. Its elaboration is from aspects: One: His statement, exalted: 'And He sent down for you from the cattle eight pairs' and we know by necessity that the camel or the cow did not descend from the heaven to the earth by way of physical movement. And Allah said, exalted: 'Then Allah sent down His tranquility upon His Messenger' and physical movement upon tranquility is impossible. And Allah said, exalted: 'The Trustworthy Spirit descended with it upon your heart' — and the Qur'an, whether we say it is an expression of an eternal attribute or we say it is an expression of letters and sound, physical movement upon it is impossible. And al-Shafi'i al-Mutallibī — may Allah be pleased with him — said: 'I entered Egypt and they did not understand my speech, so I descended (nazaltu) then I descended (nazaltu),' and he did not mean by this descent physical movement.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic proof that the word 'descent' (nuzūl) is often used metaphorically, not referring to physical movement from a higher place. Razi provides multiple examples: First, the Qur'anic verse 'And He sent down for you from the cattle eight pairs' (Qur'an 39:6) is used for creation or provision, not literal descent. Second, 'sent down tranquility' (Qur'an 9:40) — tranquility is an accident, not a body. Third, 'the Spirit descended with it upon your heart' (Qur'an 26:193-194) — whether the Qur'an is an eternal attribute or created words, it is not a physical object. Finally, the reported speech of Imam al-Shafi'i about his descent to Egypt, where he used 'nazaltu' to mean arrival or settlement, not physical descent from a height. Thus, the hadith about God's descent to the lowest heaven can be understood metaphorically without spatial movement.

Key Terms
النزول
الانتقال
السكينة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: When the word 'descent' (nuzūl) is used for God in the hadith, it must be literal physical movement downward.
Correction: Razi shows that 'descent' is commonly used in Arabic for non-physical meanings, such as provision, bestowal, or arrival. Therefore, the hadith can be interpreted metaphorically without attributing spatial movement to Allah.

Checkpoint

A) Sending down cattle
B) Sending down tranquility
C) Sending down rain
D) Al-Shafi'i's descent into Egypt

Key Takeaway
The word 'descent' in Arabic is used metaphorically in the Qur'an and classical speech, so applying it to Allah does not necessitate spatial movement.

?

?

The Purpose Argument Against Literal Descent
Razi argues literal descent is pointless if God's purpose is to be heard or to call.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's reductio ad absurdum: if God's descent has a purpose, that purpose is either unachieved or unnecessary, making the act futile and unbefitting divine wisdom.

Core Idea
Literal descent serves no purpose, so it is absurd and unworthy of God.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a powerful logical argument against understanding divine descent literally. He considers the possible purposes of such an act and shows that each leads to absurdity. This argument relies on the principle that God's actions must be wise and purposeful. Let's examine the passage carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the two possible purposes
Razi considers two purposes for descent: to make His call heard, or merely to call. List them and note why each fails.
The first fails because the call is not heard; the second fails because descent is unnecessary.
3
Analyse the analogy
Razi gives an analogy of a man walking to call someone he knows will not hear. Understand how this mirrors the divine case and leads to absurdity.
The analogy shows the act is vain and like that of madmen.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that such an act does not befit God's wisdom. Consider how this argument supports the impossibility of literal descent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إنه إن كان المقصود من النزول من العرش إلى السماء الدنيا أن يسمع نداؤه، فهذا المقصود ما حصل . وإن كان المقصود مجرد النداء، سواء سمعناه أو لم نسمعه ، فهذا ما لاحاجة فيه إلى النزول من العرش إلى السماء الدنيا ، بل كان يمكنه أن ينادينا وهو على العرش . ومثاله : أن يريد من في المشرق إسماع من في المغرب ومناداته، فيتقدم إلى جهة المغرب، بأقدام معدودة. ثم يناديه، وهو يعلم أنه لا يسمعه البتة . فههنا تكون تلك الخطوات عملا باطلا، وعبثا فاسداً . فيكون كفعل المجانين . فعلينا : أن ذلك غير لائق بحكمة الله تعالى
Second: If the purpose of the descent from the Throne to the lower heaven is that His call be heard, then this purpose is not achieved. And if the purpose is merely the call, whether we hear it or not, then there is no need for the descent from the Throne to the lower heaven; rather, He could call us while He is upon the Throne. Its example: someone in the east wants to make someone in the west hear his call; he advances toward the west a few steps, then calls him, knowing that he will not hear at all. These steps become vain action and futile play, like the act of madmen. So we say: that is not befitting the wisdom of Allah, exalted.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum against the literal descent. Razi states a disjunction: if the purpose of descent is to make His call heard, then it fails because we do not hear it (as per the hadith? Actually the hadith says He descends and says 'Who asks of Me?' etc., but we do not necessarily hear it). If the purpose is just the act of calling, then descent is unnecessary since God can call from the Throne. He gives an analogy: a man who walks toward someone to call him, but knows the other will not hear, is acting absurdly. Such absurdity is beneath God's wisdom. Therefore, literal descent is impossible.

Key Terms
النزول
النداء
العبث
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's descent has a clear purpose that justifies literalism.
Correction: Razi shows that any proposed purpose (being heard or just calling) leads to absurdity, so literal descent cannot be meant.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the consequence if God descends to call but we do not hear?
A) It proves His power
B) It is a wise act
C) It would be vain like a madman's act
D) It is a mystery

Key Takeaway
Razi's reductio ad absurdum demonstrates that literal descent is incompatible with divine wisdom because it serves no rational purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Reflect on the analogy of the man walking to call someone who cannot hear. Have you ever performed an action you knew would be futile? What does that reveal about your own intentions and accountability?
?
If you believe God descends literally, how do you reconcile that with the problem of purpose raised by Razi? Does your belief rest on textual literalism or on a deeper understanding of divine wisdom?

Razi's Argument Against Divine Descent from Size
Razi argues from relative sizes of heavens against literal descent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a dilemma based on relative sizes to refute the literal descent of God.

Core Idea
If God descends, either parts interpenetrate or are annihilated — both impossible.

Razi presents a powerful argument against the anthropomorphist view that God literally descends to the lowest heaven. Using the believers' own cosmology of vast size differences, he constructs a dilemma: descent would require either interpenetration or annihilation of divine parts. Both options are rationally impossible for God. This lesson walks through that proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the cosmological premise
Read the passage's opening: each heaven is tiny compared to the one above, all heavens are tiny compared to the Footstool, and the Throne is far larger. The anthropomorphists say God fills the Throne and His foot rests on the Footstool.
This sets up the vast size difference between God and the lowest heaven.
2
Examine the first horn: interpenetration
If God descends into the tiny lowest heaven, either parts of His immense body must compress into each other (interpenetration). This would imply divisibility and that the whole world could fit into a mustard seed — an absurdity.
3
Examine the second horn: annihilation
The other possibility is that parts of God's body are annihilated (baliyat) during descent. This implies God is subject to non-existence and existence, which is impossible for the eternal Creator.
Both horns lead to impossibilities, so the premise (literal descent) is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن القوم رأوا أن كل سماء في مقابلة السماء التي فوقها، تكون كقطرة في بحر، وكدرهم في مفازة . ثم كل السموات في مقابلة الكرسي، كقطرة في البحر، والكرسي في مقابلة العرش كذلك، ثم يقولون: إن العرش مملوء منه، والكرسي موضع قدمه . فإذا نزل إلى السماء الدنيا، وهي في غاية الصغر، بالنسبة إلى ذلك الجسم العظيم، فإما أن يقال: إن أجزاء ذلك الجسم العظيم، يدخل بعضها في بعض، وذلك يوجب القول بأن تلك الأجزاء قابلة للتفرق والتمزق، ويوجب القول أيضا بتداخل الأجزاء بعضها في بعض، وذلك يقتضي جواز تداخل جملة العالم في خردلة واحدة، وهو محال . وإما أن يقال : إن تلك الأجزاء بليت عند النزول إلى السماء الدنيا، وذلك قول بأنه قابل للعدم والوجود . وذلك مما لا يقوله عاقل في صفة الإله تعالى . فيثبت بهذا البرهان القاهر : القول بالنزول على الوجه الذي قالوه باطل
Third: Those people hold that each heaven in comparison to the heaven above it is like a drop in the sea, and like a dirham in a desert. Then all the heavens in comparison to the Footstool (al-kursī) are like a drop in the sea, and the Footstool in comparison to the Throne (al-ʿarsh) is likewise. Then they say: the Throne is filled with Him, and the Footstool is the place of His foot. So when He descends to the lowest heaven, which is extremely small relative to that great body, then either it is said: parts of that great body enter into each other, which necessitates that those parts are subject to separation and tearing, and also necessitates interpenetration of parts into each other, which necessitates the possibility of the entire world interpenetrating into a single mustard seed, which is impossible. Or it is said: those parts perished (baliyat) upon descent to the lowest heaven, which is a statement that He is subject to non-existence and existence. That is something no rational person would say regarding the attribute of Allah, exalted. So by this overwhelming proof it is established that the statement of descent in the manner they said is false.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction based on the anthropomorphist's own cosmological assumptions. Razi reports the view that the heavens are vastly smaller than the Footstool (kursī), which is smaller than the Throne (ʿarsh), and that God's body fills the Throne. If this immense body descends into the tiny lowest heaven, two impossibilities arise: either the parts compress into each other, implying divisibility and interpenetration (which leads to absurdities like the entire world fitting into a mustard seed), or the parts are annihilated, implying change and potential non-existence — both impossible for God. Therefore, the literal descent is false.

Key Terms
العرش
الكرسي
السماء الدنيا
تداخل الأجزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can change size or location in a literal, physical sense.
Correction: Razi shows that if God were a physical body that descends, it would lead to either interpenetration or annihilation, both impossible for God. Hence, such a view is false.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what does the possibility of interpenetration of parts lead to?
A) God's omnipotence
B) The entire world fitting into a mustard seed
C) The heavens being equal
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes literal divine descent by showing it forces either interpenetration or annihilation of God's parts, both rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever pictured God as being in a specific place or moving from one location to another? After reading Razi's proof, are you willing to completely abandon any spatial image of God, even if it feels unsettling?
?
Razi calls his proof 'overwhelming' (qahir). Is your own conviction that God is free from physical limitations as strong and clear as this argument demands? What assumptions about God have you held that, upon reflection, might actually contradict His perfection?
?
If God cannot descend, change location, or have parts, then how does your heart truly imagine Him when you call upon Him in prayer? Are you relying on a mental image that this proof forces you to reject? Commit today to purifying your concept of God from any trace of physicality.

Spherical Earth and Perpetual Night Refute Descent
Razi argues the earth is spherical and night is perpetual, so descent would be continuous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi's argument from spherical earth and perpetual night challenges a literal interpretation of divine descent.

Core Idea
Perpetual night on a spherical earth makes a one-time descent impossible.

Razi uses a cosmological fact—the earth is a sphere—to show that night is always occurring somewhere. The hadith about descent during the last third of the night would therefore require God to be continuously descending, which is absurd. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the logical structure: premises followed by conclusion.
Notice that Razi states his premises as established facts.
2
Understand the premises
The first premise is that the earth is a sphere. The second is that night and day perpetually alternate on opposite halves. Connect these to the idea that night is always present somewhere.
3
Analyze the conclusion
If descent is tied to night, and night is perpetual, then descent would be continuous. Razi then says God would rotate with the sphere, implying impossibility. Ask yourself why this is absurd for the divine.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إنا قد دللنا على أن العالم كرة . وإذا كان كذلك ، وجب القطع بأنه أبدا يكون الحاصل في أحد نصفي الأرض هو الليل، وفي النصف الآخر هو النهار . فإذا وجب نزوله إلى السماء الدنيا في الليل - وقد دللنا على أن الليل حاصل أبدا - فهذا يقتضي أن يبقى أبدا في السماء الدنيا، إلا أنه يستدير على ظهر الفلك بحسب استدارة الفلك، وبحسب انتقال الليل
Fourth: We have demonstrated that the world is a sphere. If that is so, it must be definitively that at all times half of the earth has night and the other half has day. So if His descent to the lower heaven is obligated to occur at night—and we have demonstrated that night perpetually exists—then this requires that He remains forever in the lower heaven, except that He rotates on the back of the sphere according to the rotation of the sphere and according to the passage of night.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a cosmological proof against a nightly descent. Razi asserts the earth is spherical, meaning night and day are perpetual across the globe. If the hadith specifies that God descends during the last third of the night, then since night is always happening somewhere, God would have to be continuously descending, remaining in the lower heaven while rotating with the earth. This is absurd and contradicts the idea of a one-time nightly descent. Thus, the literal understanding is impossible.

Key Terms
كرة
الليل
النهار
استدارة الفلك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That night is a fixed period worldwide; that is, the entire earth experiences night at the same time.
Correction: Razi uses the spherical earth to show that night and day coexist perpetually, so night is always happening somewhere, making a single nightly descent impossible.

Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude from the earth being a sphere regarding the descent?
A) God descends in the east
B) God would have to remain perpetually in the lower heaven
C) The descent is at noon
D) The earth is flat

Key Takeaway
The literal understanding of a nightly descent is incompatible with the spherical nature of the earth, as it would require perpetual descent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever pictured God as moving from place to place like a physical being? How does Razi's argument unsettle that mental image, and what does it demand you change about how you think of God?
?

?
Are you willing to let go of a literal understanding of a beloved hadith if reason shows it contradicts God's majesty? What makes that surrender difficult for you personally?
