Third Proof Against Literal Descent: The Compelling Argument
Razi presents the third rational proof refuting literal nuzul via two impossible consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational proof against literal divine descent, which demonstrates that the literalist view leads to absurd consequences about God's nature.

Core Idea
Literal descent forces either interpenetration or disintegration — both impossible for Allah.

Imam Razi presents his third compelling proof against the literal understanding of divine descent. Building on the vast size differences between the heavens, Footstool, and Throne as described by the literalists themselves, he demonstrates that any literal descent would require either interpenetration of divine parts or their disintegration — both of which contradict the necessary attributes of God. This argument is a classic example of burhan al-qahir (the overwhelming proof).

Guided Path
1
Understand the Literalists' Claims
Review the size comparisons: each heaven is like a drop compared to the one above, all heavens like a drop compared to the Footstool, and the Footstool like a drop compared to the Throne. They also claim the Throne is filled with God and the Footstool is His footstool.
This sets the stage for the dilemma.
2
Identify the Dilemma
If God descends to the tiny lowest heaven, either His parts interpenetrate to fit, or they disintegrate. These are the only two logical possibilities.
3
Examine the First Horn: Interpenetration
Interpenetration implies divisibility and the absurd possibility that the entire world could fit into a mustard seed. This is impossible.
4
Examine the Second Horn: Disintegration
Disintegration implies change from existence to non-existence, meaning God is subject to potentiality and annihilation. This is also impossible for the Necessary Being.
5
Conclusion from the Proof
Since both alternatives are impossible, the literal descent cannot be true. This establishes bāṭil (false) the anthropomorphic claim.
This proof is called 'al-burhān al-qāhir' (the compelling proof).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن القوم رأوا أن كل سماء في مقابلة السماء التي فوقها تكون كقطرة في بحر، وكدرهم في مفازة. ثم كل السموات في مقابلة الكرسي كقطرة في البحر، والكرسي في مقابلة العرش كذلك، ثم يقولون: إن العرش مملوء منه، والكرسي موضع قدمه. فإذا نزل إلى السماء الدنيا، وهي في غاية الصغر بالنسبة إلى ذلك الجسم العظيم، فإما أن يقال: إن أجزاء ذلك الجسم العظيم يدخل بعضها في بعض، وذلك يوجب القول بأن تلك الأجزاء قابلة للتفرق والتمزق، ويوجب القول أيضا بتداخل الأجزاء بعضها في بعض، وذلك يقتضي جواز تداخل جملة العالم في خردلة واحدة، وهو محال. وإما أن يقال: إن تلك الأجزاء بليت عند النزول إلى السماء الدنيا، وذلك قول بأنه قابل للعدم والوجود. وذلك مما لا يقوله عاقل في صفة الإله تعالى. فيثبت بهذا البرهان القاهر: القول بالنزول على الوجه الذي قالوه باطل.
The third: That the people [i.e., the anthropomorphists] saw that each heaven in comparison to the heaven above it is like a drop in an ocean, and like a dirham in a desert. Then all the heavens in comparison to the Footstool (al-Kursi) are like a drop in the ocean, and the Footstool in comparison to the Throne (al-Arsh) is likewise. Then they say: The Throne is filled with Him, and the Footstool is the place of His foot. So when He descends to the lowest heaven, while it is extremely small relative to that great body, then either it is said that parts of that great body enter into one another, and that requires saying that those parts are susceptible to separation and tearing, and it also requires saying the interpenetration of parts into one another, and that necessitates the permissibility of the interpenetration of the entire world into one mustard seed, and that is impossible. Or it is said that those parts disintegrated upon descent to the lowest heaven, and that is a saying that He is susceptible to non-existence and existence. And that is something no rational person would say regarding the attribute of God Almighty. So by this compelling proof it is established that the saying of descent in the manner they said is false.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the third rational argument against a literal interpretation of divine descent (nuzul). It is a refutation of the anthropomorphic position. The argument begins by establishing the immense disparity in size between the heavens, the Footstool (al-Kursi), and the Throne (al-Arsh), based on the claims of the literalists themselves: each heaven is minuscule compared to the one above, all heavens are like a drop in the ocean compared to the Footstool, and the Footstool is similarly tiny relative to the Throne. They also assert that the Throne is 'filled' with God and the Footstool is the place of His foot. If God then descends to the lowest heaven (which is infinitesimally small relative to His supposed body), two impossible consequences follow. First, either parts of that immense body must interpenetrate to fit into the smaller space, implying divisibility and mutability—and this would allow the entire world to fit into a mustard seed, an absurdity. Second, or the parts disintegrate, which would imply that God is subject to annihilation and origination, which is impossible for the Necessary Being. Since both alternatives are impossible, the literal descent cannot be true. The proof is called 'al-burhan al-qahir' (the compelling proof).

Key Terms
نزول
كرسي
عرش
تداخل الأجزاء
البرهان القاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's infinite power allows Him to shrink or change form without contradiction.
Correction: The argument shows that shrinking implies interpenetration (divisibility) or disintegration (changeability), both of which are impossible for the Necessary Being, regardless of power.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, what two possibilities does the literal descent entail?
A) Either God's parts interpenetrate or they disintegrate.
B) Either God becomes small or He disappears.
C) Either God remains infinite or He becomes finite.
D) Either God moves or He stays still.

Key Takeaway
Literal divine descent is impossible because it would require either interpenetration or disintegration of God's essence, both of which are absurd for the Necessary Being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever imagined Allah as being 'up there' or 'coming down' in a physical sense, even unconsciously? How does this proof challenge that mental image, and what step will you take to purify your concept of Allah from any trace of physicality?
?
When you hear of Allah's attributes like 'descent', do you feel a tension between the literal meaning and transcendence? Which do you tend to lean toward, and how will you train yourself to submit to the rational proofs of the scholars rather than your imagination?
?
The proof here is called 'al-burhan al-qahir'—the compelling proof. Has the truth of Allah's transcendence ever compelled you to change a deeply held assumption? What assumption about God's nature are you still holding onto that you know rationally cannot be true? Commit to one concrete action today to align your belief with the truth.

The Spherical Earth Argument Against Literal Nuzul
Imam Razi presents the fourth rational proof refuting literal nuzul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the spherical earth and the perpetual cycle of night and day to refute the literal interpretation of divine descent (nuzul).

Core Idea
If night is always present somewhere, literal descent would be eternal and absurd.

Imam Razi now presents a powerful rational argument that uses the physical reality of a spherical earth. Because night and day continuously alternate across the globe, night never ceases to exist somewhere. This fact, combined with the hadith mentioning descent during the night, leads to a logical impossibility if taken literally. Read carefully how Razi turns this into a reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the full argument
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Razi builds on a previously established premise about the earth's shape.
2
Identify the key premises
The argument rests on three premises: (1) the world is spherical, (2) night and day perpetually alternate, so night always exists somewhere, (3) the hadith specifies descent at night.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
If descent occurs at night and night is perpetual, then descent must be perpetual. This would mean God constantly rotates with the celestial sphere as night moves across the earth.
4
Understand the reductio ad absurdum
Razi concludes by showing that this would make God analogous to a rotating celestial body, implying the sphere itself could be God—an absurdity no rational person accepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: إنا قد دللنا على أن العالم كرة. وإذا كان كذلك، وجب القطع بأنه أبدا يكون الحاصل في أحد نصفي الأرض هو الليل، وفي النصف الآخر هو النهار. فإذا وجب نزوله إلى السماء الدنيا في الليل - وقد دللنا على أن الليل حاصل أبدا - فهذا يقتضي أن يبقى أبدا في السماء الدنيا، إلا أنه يستدير على ظهر الفلك بحسب استدارة الفلك، وبحسب انتقال الليل من جانب من الأرض إلى جانب آخر. ولو جاز أن يكون الشيء المستدير مع الفلك أبدا إله العالم، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون إله العالم هو الفلك؟ ومعلوم أن ذلك لا يقوله عاقل.
The fourth: We have demonstrated that the world is a sphere. If that is so, it must be definitively concluded that at all times what is obtained in one half of the earth is night, and in the other half is day. So if His descent to the lowest heaven is incumbent during the night – and we have demonstrated that night is perpetual – then this requires that He remains perpetually in the lowest heaven, except that He rotates on the surface of the celestial sphere according to the rotation of the sphere and according to the transfer of night from one side of the earth to another. And if it is permissible for the thing that rotates with the sphere eternally to be the God of the world, then why is it not permissible that the God of the world be the celestial sphere itself? And it is known that no rational person would say that.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against the literal interpretation of nuzul. It builds on the established premise that the earth is spherical. Given that night and day perpetually alternate across the globe, night always exists on one half of the earth. The hadith specifies that God descends during the night. Since night is perpetual globally, this descent would have to be perpetual, meaning God remains in the lower heaven continuously. Moreover, He would have to rotate with the celestial sphere as night moves across the earth. This would make God akin to a moving celestial body. The argument then poses a reductio ad absurdum: if it is allowed that a rotating body accompanying the sphere perpetually could be the God of the world, then why not say that the celestial sphere itself is God? No rational person would accept that conclusion, so the literal descent is invalid.

Key Terms
كرة
فلك
ليل
نهار
استدارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Descent at night is a one-time event that happens only during a specific night.
Correction: Because night is perpetually present somewhere on a spherical earth, if descent is tied to night, it would be continuous, not a single event.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth argument conclude about literal descent?
A) It leads to God being perpetually in the lower heaven and rotating with the sphere, making Him like a celestial body.
B) It proves that descent occurs only at midnight.
C) It shows that God descends only to the highest heaven.
D) It confirms that the earth is stationary.

Key Takeaway
The perpetual nature of night on a spherical earth forces literal descent to be continuous and rotating, reducing it to an absurdity that no rational person accepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you had heard the hadith of descent without any rational explanation, would you have taken it literally? What does your answer reveal about your willingness to let reason shape your understanding of God?
?
The argument forces you to choose between a comforting literal reading and a transcendent understanding that protects God's majesty. Which do you actually prefer, and why? Are you willing to sacrifice emotional comfort for theological purity?
?
Razi uses astronomy to defend God's transcendence. Are you willing to engage deeply with science and reason to purify your faith, or do you prefer to avoid such challenges? What specific step can you take this week to confront a belief that might be anthropomorphic?

Ta'wil of Descent as Mercy: Three Reasons for the Last Third of the Night
Imam Razi presents the interpretative approach and three reasons for specifying the last third of the night

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the hadith of descent as referring to the descent of mercy and the three rational reasons for specifying the last third of the night.

Core Idea
Ta'wil of descent as mercy reveals divine wisdom and preserves transcendence.

Imam Razi now moves from refuting literal interpretations to offering a constructive interpretation (ta'wil). He explains that the descent in the hadith should be understood as the descent of God's mercy to the earth during the last third of the night. This lesson presents three reasons why this specific time is singled out for such mercy, supported by Quranic verses.

Guided Path
1
Understand the shift to ta'wil
Read the opening sentence where Razi introduces the second type of discussion: interpreting descent as descent of mercy. Notice that this is a constructive approach, not a refutation.
This sets the stage for a rational theological interpretation.
2
Examine the three reasons
Read the three numbered reasons carefully. For each, identify the core argument: sincerity of repentance, overcoming laziness for greater reward, and the need for additional incentive to worship at night.
Each reason builds on a psychological or spiritual insight.
3
Reflect on the Quranic support
Look at the two Quranic verses cited at the end. Ask yourself how they reinforce the idea that the pre-dawn hours are specially blessed for seeking forgiveness.
This shows that ta'wil is grounded in scripture, not mere speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثاني من الكلام في هذا الحديث: بناؤه على التأويل على سبيل التفصيل، وهو أن يحمل هذا النزول على نزول رحمته إلى الأرض في ذلك الوقت. والسبب في تخصيص ذلك الوقت بهذا الفعل وجوه:

الأول: إن التوبة التي يؤتى بها في قلب الليل: الظاهر أنها تكون عالية عن شوائب الدنيا، لأن الأغيار لا يطلعون عليها. فتكون أقرب إلى القبول.

الثاني: إن الغالب على الإنسان في قلب الليل الكسل والنوم والبطالة، فلولا الجد العظيم في طلب الدين، والرغبة الشديدة في تحققه، ما تحمل مشاق السهر، ولما أعرض عن اللذات الجسمانية، ومتى كان الجد والرغبة والإخلاص أتم وأكمل، كان الثواب أوفر.

الثالث: إن الليل وقت الكسل والفتور، فاحتيج في الترغيب في الاشتغال بالعبادة في الليل إلى مزيد أمور تؤثر في تحريك دواعي الاشتغال والتهجد، فيحسن أن الشارع إنما خص هذا الوقت بمثل هذا الكلام ليكون توفر الدواعي على التهجد أتم. فهذه الجهات الثلاثة تصلح أن تكون سببا لتخصيص الشرع هذا الوقت بهذا التشريف. ولأجلها قال الله تعالى: "وبالأسحار هم يستغفرون" وقال: "والمستغفرين بالأسحار".
The second type of discussion regarding this hadith: its foundation is on interpretation (ta'wil) in a detailed manner, which is that this descent is taken to mean the descent of His mercy to the earth at that time. The reason for specifying that time with this act are several aspects:

First: The repentance that is performed in the depth of the night – apparently it is elevated above the impurities of the world, because others are not aware of it. So it is closer to acceptance.

Second: The predominant state of a person in the depth of the night is laziness, sleep, and inactivity. Were it not for the great earnestness in seeking religion and the intense desire to realize it, he would not bear the hardships of staying awake, nor would he turn away from physical pleasures. And whenever earnestness, desire, and sincerity are more complete and perfect, the reward is greater.

Third: The night is a time of laziness and weakness, so there is need in encouraging engagement in worship at night for additional matters that influence the stirring of the motives for engagement and night prayer. Thus it is fitting that the Lawgiver particularly specified this time with such speech so that the abundance of motives for night prayer be more complete. So these three aspects are suitable to be a reason for the Law's specification of this time with this honor. And because of them Allah Almighty said: 'And in the hours of the early dawn they were asking forgiveness' and said: 'And those who ask forgiveness in the early hours of dawn.'
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage marks a shift from rational refutation to constructive interpretation (ta'wil). It presents the second approach to the hadith of descent: instead of taking it literally, the descent is understood as the descent of God's mercy at that specific time. The text then provides three reasons why the last third of the night (specifically, the pre-dawn hours) is singled out for this mercy. First, repentance during this time is more sincere because it is hidden from others, free from show, and thus closer to acceptance. Second, overcoming the natural inclination to sleep and laziness requires extraordinary earnestness and sincerity, which yield greater reward. Third, because the night inherently induces lethargy, additional incentive is needed to motivate worship; the hadith serves as that incentive, stirring the desire for night prayer (tahajjud). These three rational explanations justify the divine specification. The passage ends by citing Quranic verses (51:18 and 3:17) that commend seeking forgiveness at dawn, reinforcing the wisdom of this timing.

قلب الليل

Misconception: Some may think that ta'wil is arbitrary or that there is no rational basis for the specification of the last third of the night.
Correction: The text provides three rational reasons grounded in human psychology and scripture, showing that ta'wil is a reasoned theological method.

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is repentance in the depth of the night more likely to be accepted?
A) Because it is performed in a specific location.
B) Because it is concealed from others and free from worldly impurities.
C) Because the angels are present.
D) Because the night is short.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's ta'wil of descent as mercy is supported by three rational reasons and Quranic verses, showing that the last third of the night is a time of special mercy for sincere repentance and worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pray or repent in the night, is your heart truly hidden from others, or do you still seek validation? What would it take for you to make your worship purely for Allah alone?
?
The second reason highlights the struggle against laziness. Are you willing to sacrifice sleep and comfort to seek His mercy? What is the one change you can commit to this week to increase your night worship, even if only for a few minutes?
?
The hadith is meant to stir your motivation. Does the promise of special mercy in the last third of the night actually move you to wake up and pray? If not, what is holding you back, and how can you sincerely ask Allah to soften your heart?

Divine Transcendence

Ta'wil of Nuzul: The Descent of Mercy and Three Reasons
Imam Razi explains the allegorical interpretation and gives three reasons for the night's honor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam Razi interprets the descent (nuzul) in the hadith as the descent of mercy, and you will learn three reasons why the last third of the night is singled out for this mercy.

Core Idea
The descent is mercy, not movement: three reasons show God's wisdom in timing.

Imam al-Razi now turns to the second type of discussion about the famous hadith of divine descent. Rather than taking the word 'descent' literally, he insists it must be interpreted allegorically (ta'wil). He proposes that it refers to the descent of Allah's mercy to the earth at that specific time. Then he provides three interconnected reasons why this particular time — the last third of the night — is so special.

Guided Path
1
Understand the ta'wil principle
Read the opening statement: the descent is interpreted as the descent of mercy. This is the foundation of the entire discussion.
Notice that Imam Razi does not deny the hadith but reinterprets it.
2
Examine the first reason
Repentance at the heart of the night is pure and free from worldly impurities, making it more likely to be accepted.
The word 'heart of the night' emphasizes the depth of the night.
3
Examine the second reason
Staying awake despite laziness shows great earnestness and sincerity, so the reward is greater.
Sincerity and effort increase the value of worship.
4
Examine the third reason
The night is a time of lethargy, so extra encouragement is needed to motivate worship. God's speech provides that motivation.
This reason ties to the Quranic verses about seeking forgiveness at dawn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثانى من الكلام فى هذا الحديث : بناؤه على التأويل (٥٠) على سبيل التفصيل ، وهو أن يحمل هذا النزول على نزول رحمته إلى الأرض في ذلك الوقت . والسبب فى تخصيص ذلك الوقت بهذا الفعل وجوه :
الأول : إن التوبة التي يؤتى بها في قلب الليل : الظاهر أنها تكون عالية عن شوائب الدنيا ، لأن الأغيار لا يطلعون عليها . فتكون أقرب إلى القبول .
الثاني : إن الغالب على الإنسان في قلب الليل الكسل والنوم والبطالة، فلولا الجد العظيم في طلب الدين ، والرغبة الشديدة في تحققه ، ما تحمل مشاق السهر ، ولما أعرض عن اللذات الجسمانية ، ومتى كان الجد والرغبة والإخلاص ، أتم وأكمل ، كان الثواب أوفر .
الثالث : إن الليل وقت الكسل والفتور، فاحتيج في الترغيب في الاشتغال بالعبادة في الليل إلى مزيد أمور تؤثر فى تحريك دواعي الاشتغال والتهجد ، فيحسن أن الشارع إنما خص هذا الوقت بمثل هذا الكلام : ليكون توفر الدواعى على التهجد أتم . فهذه الجهات الثلاثة تصلح أن تكون سببا لتخصيص الشرع هذا الوقت بهذا التشريف . ولأجلها قال الله تعالى : ( وبالأسحار هم يستغفرون ) (٥٢) وقال : ( والمستغفرين بالأسحار ) (٥٣)
الوجه الرابع : إن جمعا من أشراف الملائكة ينزلون في ذلك الوقت بأمر الله تعالى كما يقال : بنى الأمير دارا ، وضرب دينارا . ومن ذهب إلى هذا التأويل : من يروى الخبر يضم إليها تحقيقا لهذا المعنى .
واعلم : أن تمام التقرير في تأويل هذا الخبر : أن من نزل من الملوك عند إنسان لإصلاح شأنه، والاهتمام بأمره . فإنه يكرمه جدا . بل يكون نزوله عنده مبالغة في إكرامه (٥٤) . ولما كان النزول موجبا للإكرام، أو موجبا له ، أطلق اسم النزول على الإكرام . وهذا أيضا هو المراد بقوله تعالى : ( وجاء ربك والملك صفا صفا ) (٥٥) وذلك أن الملك إذا جاء وحضر يفصل الخصومات ، عظم وقعه واشتدت هيبته . والله أعلم (٥٦)
The second type of speech concerning this hadith: building it upon allegorical interpretation (ta’wīl) (50) in detail, and it is that this ‘descent’ (nuzūl) be taken to mean the descent of His mercy to the earth at that time. The reason for specifying that time with this act are aspects: First: that repentance which is offered in the midst of the night: apparently it is free from the impurities of the world, because outsiders are not aware of it. So it is closer to acceptance. Second: that which predominates over man in the midst of the night is laziness, sleep, and idleness; were it not for the great earnestness in seeking the religion and the intense desire to achieve it, he would not bear the hardships of staying awake, nor would he turn away from physical pleasures; and whenever earnestness, desire, and sincerity are more complete and perfect, the reward is greater. Third: that the night is a time of laziness and lethargy, so there is a need in encouraging engagement in worship at night for additional things that influence the motivation for engagement and night prayer; so it is appropriate that the Lawgiver specifically designated this time with such speech, so that the abundance of motivations for night prayer is more complete. So these three aspects are suitable to be a cause for the Law specifying this time with this honor. And for their sake Allah the Exalted said: ‘And in the hours before dawn they would ask forgiveness’ (52) and said: ‘and those who ask forgiveness before dawn’ (53). The fourth aspect: that a group of the noble angels descend at that time by the command of Allah the Exalted, as it is said: ‘The prince built a house and struck a dinar.’ And whoever adopts this interpretation: the one who reports the narration includes it to realize this meaning. And know: that the complete exposition in the interpretation of this report is: that whoever descends from kings to a person to rectify his situation and attend to his affair, then he greatly honors him. Rather, his descending to him is an exaggeration in honoring him (54). And since descent necessitates honor, or is a cause for it, the name ‘descent’ was applied to honor. And this is also what is meant by His statement the Exalted: ‘And your Lord comes, and the angels, rank upon rank’ (55). And that is because when a king comes and is present to decide disputes, his impact is great and his awe intensifies. And Allah knows best (56).
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the second type of discussion concerning the hadith of divine descent (nuzūl). It bases the interpretation on ta’wīl (allegorical interpretation) in detail, meaning that the 'descent' mentioned in the hadith should be understood as the descent of Allah's mercy to the earth at that time (the last third of the night). The author then provides reasons for why this particular time is singled out for this act of mercy. He lists four aspects (wujūh). The first three are grouped together: First, repentance offered in the depths of the night is free from worldly impurities and unknown to others, making it more likely to be accepted. Second, since people are naturally lazy and sleepy at night, only genuine earnestness and desire for religion would motivate one to stay awake and abandon physical pleasures; the greater the sincerity, the greater the reward. Third, because the night is a time of lethargy, extra encouragement is needed to motivate worship, so it is fitting that the Lawgiver (al-Shāri‘) specifically mentioned such speech to boost motivation for night prayer. The author notes that these three aspects are suitable causes for this special honor, and supports them with Quranic verses about seeking forgiveness at dawn (al-āsḥār). Then he presents a fourth aspect: a group of noble angels descend at that time by Allah's command, using the analogy of a prince building a house or striking a coin (meaning the act is attributed to the one who commands it). Those who hold this interpretation include this in realizing the meaning. Finally, the author explains the complete exposition of the interpretation of this report (the hadith): when a king descends to a person to rectify his affairs and attend to him, he is honoring him greatly; descending is an exaggeration in honor. Since descent necessitates honor, the term 'descent' is applied to honor. He then links this to the Quranic verse 'And your Lord comes, and the angels, rank upon rank' (al-Fajr 89:22), explaining that when a king comes to settle disputes, his impact is great and his awe intensifies. The passage concludes with 'And Allah knows best.' Thus, the overall argument is that the 'descent' in the hadith is metaphorical, referring to the descent of mercy and the manifestation of divine honor, not a literal movement.

Key Terms
نزول
تأويل
رحمة
تهجد
الأسحار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith means Allah literally moves to the lowest heaven.
Correction: Imam Razi explains that nuzul is allegorical, referring to the descent of mercy, not a literal movement.

Checkpoint
According to the first three reasons, why is the last third of the night particularly suited for the descent of mercy?
A) Because people are more alert at that time.
B) Because repentance is pure and sincerity is greater.
C) Because angels descend at that time.
D) Because it is a time of darkness.

Key Takeaway
The descent in the hadith is the descent of mercy, and the night is chosen because repentance is purer, sincerity is greater, and extra motivation is needed for worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself lazy at night, do you truly believe that overcoming that laziness for worship is a sign of your sincerity? Or do you make excuses? Tonight, will you consciously choose to pray Tahajjud, knowing your effort is witnessed and valued by Allah?
?
The lesson teaches that repentance at night is purer because it is hidden. Is your repentance ever performed for show? Are you willing to repent in the secrecy of the night, where no one sees but Allah? What holds you back?
?
Imam Razi says the night is chosen to motivate worship. Have you ever felt that extra motivation from a hadith like this? Or do you read it and remain unmoved? What would it take for you to actually act on these three reasons tonight?

Divine Transcendence

The Fourth Reason: Descent of Angels and the Honor Metaphor
Imam Razi gives the fourth reason and concludes that descent metaphorically signifies honor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's fourth reason for the night's honor—the descent of noble angels—and his concluding analogy that 'descent' is a metaphor for honor, supported by the Quranic verse 'And your Lord comes.'

Core Idea
Descent of angels signals honor; the very word 'descent' is a metaphor for divine honor and awe.

Imam al-Razi now presents a fourth reason for the specification of the last third of the night. He notes that a group of noble angels descend at that time by Allah's command, using a common Arabic expression where an act is attributed to the one who commands it (like 'the prince built a house'). Then he elaborates on the complete exposition: when a king descends to attend to someone, it is an act of great honor. Thus, the word 'descent' is metaphorically applied to honor itself. He ties this to the Quranic verse 'And your Lord comes, and the angels, rank upon rank' (89:22), explaining that the coming of a king to settle disputes inspires awe.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth reason
A group of noble angels descend at that time by Allah's command. Notice the analogy: 'the prince built a house'.
This indicates that the act is attributed to the one who commands it.
2
Understand the complete exposition
When a king descends to attend to a person, it is a great honor. Descending itself is an exaggeration in honor.
Thus, the term 'descent' is applied to honor because it necessitates it.
3
Connect to the Quranic verse
The verse 'And your Lord comes' (89:22) is similarly metaphorical, referring to the manifestation of divine majesty and awe, just like a king's arrival to settle disputes.
This reinforces that 'coming' and 'descent' are not literal movements.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the fourth reason and the concluding analogy together show that the hadith must be understood metaphorically to preserve divine transcendence.
The key is that God's acts are described in terms of their effects (mercy, honor), not literal motion.

The Descent of Angels: A Metaphorical Interpretation
Imam Razi presents and interprets the fourth aspect regarding the descent of angels.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's metaphorical interpretation of the descent of angels as an expression of divine honor and care, rather than literal spatial movement.

Core Idea
The descent of angels symbolizes honor, not physical movement.

This lesson explores the fourth aspect presented by Imam Razi in interpreting the hadith about the descent of angels. Rather than taking it literally, Razi argues that the descent is a metaphor for the honor and attention bestowed by Allah. This interpretation preserves divine transcendence while making sense of the scriptural language.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once carefully. Notice how Imam Razi structures his argument with an analogy and Quranic support.
The passage includes some OCR uncertainties marked with [؟], but the meaning is clear.
2
Understand the analogy
Razi uses the example 'the prince built a house and struck a dinar' to show that actions can be metaphorical. Consider what this analogy implies about the descent of angels.
3
Connect descent to honor
Razi explains that when a king descends to help someone, it is a great honor. Thus, the term 'descent' is applied to honor itself. Trace this reasoning in the text.
4
See the Quranic support
He quotes the verse 'And your Lord comes, and the angels, rank upon rank' as another example where coming denotes majesty, not spatial movement.
This reinforces the metaphorical interpretation.
5
Reflect on the takeaway
Think about why this interpretation is important for divine transcendence. How does it avoid anthropomorphism?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : إن جمعا من أشراف الملائكة ينزلون في ذلك الوقت بأمر الله تعالى كما يقال : بنى الأمير دارا، وضرب دينارا . ومن ذهب إلى هذا التأويل : من يروى الخبر يضم هياء [؟] تحقيقا لهذا المعنى . واعلم : أن تمام التقرير في تأويل هذا الخبر : أن من نزل من الملوك عند إنسان لإصلاح شأنه، والاهتمام بأمره . فإنه يكرمه جدا . بل يكون نزوله عنده مبالغة في إكرامه . ولما كان النزول موجبا الإكرام، أو موجباله [؟]، أطلق اسم النزول على الإكرام . وهذا أيضا هو المراد بقوله تعالى : (وجاء ربك ، والملك ، صفا صفا) . وذلك أن الملك إذا جاء وحضر افصل [؟] الخصومات، عظم وقعه واشتدت هيبته . والله أعلم.
The fourth aspect: That a group of noble angels descend at that time by the command of Allah Almighty, as it is said: 'The prince built a house and struck a dinar.' And whoever goes to this interpretation: whoever narrates the report includes a form [؟] to realize this meaning. Know that the complete exposition in the interpretation of this report is that whoever among kings descends upon a person to set right his affair and attend to his matter, then he greatly honors him. Rather, his descent upon him is an exaggeration in honoring him. And since descent necessitates honor, or is a cause of it [؟], the name of descent was applied to honor. And this is also the intended meaning of His statement, the Exalted: 'And your Lord comes, and the angels, rank upon rank' (Surah al-Fajr 22). And that is because when a king comes and attends the settlement of disputes, his impact becomes great and his awe intensifies. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is an argument and interpretation (ta'wil) of a hadith mentioning the descent of angels. The author, Imam Razi, presents the fourth of several interpretative aspects. He likens the descent of angels to the metaphorical statement 'the prince built a house and struck a dinar,' which means he acted with authority and generosity, not literal building or striking. Similarly, the descent of angels is not a physical movement downward but a figurative expression of honor and attention from Allah. Razi explains that when a king descends upon someone to help, it signifies great honor; thus, the term 'descent' is extended to mean 'honor.' He then supports this by quoting the Quranic verse 'And your Lord comes, and the angels, rank upon rank,' interpreting that the coming of the Lord signifies majesty and awe, not spatial movement. The passage concludes with 'Allah knows best,' indicating the author's humility before divine knowledge.

Key Terms
تأويل
نزول
إكرام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith about angels descending implies literal spatial movement from heaven to earth.
Correction: Imam Razi argues that the descent is metaphorical, representing honor and care from Allah, not physical relocation.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's fourth aspect, how is the descent of angels interpreted?
A) As a literal physical descent from heaven to earth
B) As a metaphorical expression of honor and attention
C) As a descent only for punishment
D) As a descent only for recording deeds

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi interprets the descent of angels metaphorically as an honor from Allah, not a literal spatial descent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the hadith about angels descending, do you instinctively imagine a physical movement? How does Razi's metaphorical interpretation challenge you to think beyond literal images? Will you now train yourself to see the honor behind such descriptions?
?
The Quran says 'And your Lord comes' – have you ever truly felt the awe of that verse? How does Razi's explanation of a king's arrival help you grasp the majesty of Allah's coming without limiting Him to space? What specific step will you take this week to consciously remember that Allah's 'coming' is an act of honor, not spatial movement?
?
Razi uses an analogy from kings to explain honor. When you think of Allah's actions, do you tend to slip into human-like imagery? This lesson calls you to purify your concept of Allah. What false assumption about Allah's actions do you need to repent from today, and how will you replace it with a more transcendent understanding?

Interpreting the Vision of Allah Like the Moon
Imam Razi interprets the hadith about seeing Allah like the full moon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the hadith of seeing Allah like the full moon, distinguishing comparison of seeing from comparison of the seen.

Core Idea
The hadith compares seeing to seeing, not Allah to the moon.

This lesson examines a famous hadith about the vision of Allah in the afterlife. Imam Razi explains that the prophetic comparison is only about the clarity of sight, not about any similarity between Allah and the moon. He also clarifies the meanings of the words 'la tudamun' and 'la tudarun'. The interpretation protects divine transcendence while affirming the vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and translation
First, read the Arabic and the literal translation. Notice the key words: رؤية, تشبيه, لا تضامون, لا تضارون.
The hadith is well-known; focus on Razi's interpretation.
2
Identify the two comparisons
Razi distinguishes between comparing the act of seeing and comparing the object seen. Understand that the moon is not like Allah, but the clearness of seeing is similar.
3
Understand the word explanations
Learn what 'la tudamun' and 'la tudarun' mean: not crowding/harming. Also the variant 'tudamun' meaning injustice.
These explain the ease of the vision.
4
Reflect on the theological purpose
This interpretation guards against anthropomorphism (tashbih). The vision is real but not bodily or limited.
Connects to the broader theme of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال عليه السلام : سترون ربكم كماترون القمر ليلة البدر . لا نضامون في رؤيته ، وفي رواية : « لا تضارون، والتأويل : أن المقصود : تشبيه الرؤية بالرؤية، لا تشبيه المرئى بالمربى . ومعنى قوله « لا تضامون ، : أى لا ينضم بعضكم إلى بعض ، كما تتضمون فى رؤية الهلال رأس الشهر ، بل رؤية جهرة من غير تكلف لطلمبه [؟]، كما ترون البدر . وقوله : « لانضارون» أى لا يا تكم [؟] ضرر فى طلب رؤيته ، بل ترونه من غير تكلف الطلب . وما روى « تضامون ، مخففا . فالمراد منه : الضيم . أي لا يلحقكم فيه ضيم.
He (peace be upon him) said: You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon. You will not be crowded in seeing Him, and in a narration: 'you will not be harmed.' And the interpretation (ta'wil) is that the intended is a comparison of seeing to seeing, not a comparison of the seen to the seen. And the meaning of his saying 'you will not be crowded' is that you will not join one another, as you crowd in seeing the crescent at the beginning of the month, but rather a clear sight without effort in seeking it [؟], as you see the full moon. And his saying 'you will not be harmed' means that no harm comes to you [؟] in seeking to see Him, but you see Him without effort in seeking. And what is narrated as 'you will not be wronged' (in a lighter form), its intended meaning is injustice, i.e., no injustice will afflict you in it.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage interprets a famous hadith about the vision of Allah (ru'yat Allah) in the afterlife. The author clarifies two key points. First, the comparison 'as you see the moon' is only of the act of seeing (ru'ya), not the object seen (mar'i). The moon is a created object; Allah is not comparable to it. Second, the words 'la tudamun' and 'la tudarun' are explained: 'la tudamun' means you will not crowd each other, like for the crescent moon, but will see clearly; 'la tudarun' means you will not be harmed in seeking the vision. A variant 'tudamun' (with tashdid) is interpreted as 'daim' (injustice), meaning no injustice will occur. The interpretation guards against anthropomorphism and emphasizes the clarity and ease of the vision.

Key Terms
رؤية
تشبيه
تأويل
لا تضامون
لا تضارون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith compares Allah to the moon.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the comparison is only of the act of seeing, not of the object seen. Allah is not like the moon.

Checkpoint
In the hadith about seeing Allah like the moon, what does Imam Razi emphasize is being compared?
A) The object seen (Allah) to the moon
B) The act of seeing to the act of seeing
C) The angels' sight to human sight
D) The time of seeing to the night of the full moon

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi clarifies that the hadith compares the clarity of seeing, not the object seen, thereby upholding divine transcendence while affirming the vision of Allah.

Interpreting Allah's Emergence: Divine Transcendence Preserved
Imam Razi interprets a hadith about Allah's emergence on Friday for Paradise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets anthropomorphic hadith about Allah's emergence on Friday for Paradise dwellers, preserving divine transcendence (tanzih).

Core Idea
Divine 'emergence' is not movement but a created vision and mercy.

Imam Razi tackles another challenging hadith—that Allah 'emerges' every Friday for the people of Paradise. How can we understand such language without contradicting Allah's transcendence (tanzih)? Razi shows that even if the hadith is weak, its interpretation must uphold that Allah is not subject to movement, location, or change. Let's explore his careful ta'wil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the literal meaning of the hadith and Razi's comments.
Notice the hadith describes Allah 'emerging' on a mound of camphor—an anthropomorphic image.
2
Identify the interpretation of 'emergence'
Find how Razi interprets the word 'buruz' (emergence). He says it means Allah creates a vision (ru'ya) for them, not a literal movement.
Key phrase: 'فهو أن يخلق لهم رؤية متعلقة'
3
Identify the interpretation of 'closeness'
Find how Razi interprets 'qurb' (closeness). He says it means closeness in mercy (rahma), not spatial proximity, citing a well-known hadith.
This reinforces divine transcendence by avoiding corporealism.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider why Razi insists on interpreting these phrases figuratively. The principle of tanzih requires that any scriptural expression suggesting physicality be interpreted in a way that befits Allah's majesty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أيضاً عليه السلام : ( إن الله يبرز كل يوم جمعة لأهل الجنة على كتيب من كافور ، فكون [؟] في الغرب [؟] على تبكيرهم إلى الجمعة الا فسارعوا إلى الخيرات، واعلم : أنه قيل : إن هذا الخبر ضعيف . وإن صح . فالتأويل ( فيه (١)) : أن أهل الجنة يرون على مقادير أوقات الدنيا فيما سبق من أعمالهم الحسنة . وأما بروزه لأهل الجنة ـ وبذلك يتخيل لهم - فهو أن يخلق لهم رؤية متعلقة ، وهم على كثيب من كافور . وأما قربه منهم فمعناه : القرب بالرحمة كما قال : « من تقرب إلى شبراً تقربت إليه ذراعاً ، ويقال للفاسق : إنه بعيد من الله .
And he (peace be upon him) also said: 'Indeed, Allah emerges every Friday for the people of Paradise on a mound of camphor, and is [؟] in the west [؟] over their eagerness to attend Friday, so hasten to good deeds.' And know: it was said that this report is weak. But if it is authentic, then the interpretation (ta'wil) of it is that the people of Paradise see according to the measures of the times of the world regarding their previous good deeds. As for His emergence to the people of Paradise — and thereby He appears to them — it is that He creates for them a related vision, while they are on a mound of camphor. And as for His closeness to them, its meaning is closeness in mercy, as He said: 'Whoever draws near to Me by a handspan, I draw near to him by an arm's length,' and the wicked is said to be far from Allah.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage deals with another hadith about Allah manifesting (yabrazu) every Friday to the people of Paradise. The author first notes that the hadith is considered weak, but provides interpretation assuming it is authentic. The interpretation is that the people of Paradise see the rewards of their good deeds according to their measure in worldly time. The 'emergence' (buruz) is not physical but means Allah creates for them a vision (ru'ya) related to that state, while they are on a mound of camphor. The phrase 'closeness' (qurb) is interpreted as closeness in mercy (rahma), not spatial proximity, supported by another hadith about drawing near. This interpretation upholds divine transcendence (tanzih) by denying literal movement or spatial proximity.

Key Terms
بروز
تأويل
رؤية
قرب
رحمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith describes a literal physical emergence of Allah.
Correction: The author interprets it as creating a vision and mercy, not literal movement.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi interpret Allah's 'emergence' to the people of Paradise on Friday?
A) As a literal movement from one place to another
B) As a creation of a vision for them while they are on a mound of camphor
C) As a metaphorical reference to the angels' descent
D) As a purely figurative statement with no meaning

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi preserves divine transcendence by interpreting Allah's 'emergence' as a created vision and 'closeness' as mercy, not spatial movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a hadith that describes Allah in seemingly human terms, do you instinctively imagine a physical form, or do you stop to reflect on its deeper meaning? What does your response reveal about your understanding of divine transcendence?
?
The people of Paradise see according to the measure of their good deeds. Does your current striving for good deeds reflect a desire to see Allah's mercy in this life and the next? If not, what is holding you back?
?
Closeness to Allah is not a matter of location but of mercy. If you feel distant from Allah, is it because you have neglected the actions that draw His mercy? Will you commit today to one act that brings you nearer to Him in mercy?

Interpreting the Hadith of Seeing Allah Like the Moon
Razi interprets the hadith of seeing Allah like the moon, distinguishing vision from object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the famous hadith about seeing Allah like the moon, avoiding anthropomorphism by distinguishing the vision from the object seen.

Core Idea
The hadith compares vision to vision, not Allah to the moon.

Imam Razi engages with a well-known hadith that might seem to suggest Allah is visible like a physical object. He provides a careful interpretation that preserves divine transcendence by focusing on the clarity and ease of the vision, not on any likeness of Allah to the moon. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and explains the variant wordings in the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its translations
Begin by reading the Arabic hadith and its literal translation. Note the two variant wordings: 'la tudaamoon' and 'la tudaaroon'.
These variant readings are important for the interpretation.
2
Identify the core interpretation
The author states that the comparison is between vision and vision, not between the seen (Allah) and the seen (the moon). This is the key to avoiding anthropomorphism.
This is the central point of the lesson.
3
Understand the explanations of the variant wordings
Razi explains 'la tudaamoon' as not needing to crowd together like for the crescent moon, and 'la tudaaroon' as no harm or difficulty in seeing. Also note the third interpretation of 'tudaamoon' as oppression (daym).
Each wording is interpreted to emphasize ease and clarity.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why such interpretation is necessary. It prevents attributing physical characteristics to Allah while affirming the promised vision in the afterlife.
This connects to divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال عليه السلام : سترون ربكم كماترون القمر ليلة البدر . لا نضامون في رؤيته ، وفي رواية : « لا تضارون، والتأويل : أن المقصود : تشبيه الرؤية بالرؤية، لا تشبيه المرئى بالمربى . ومعنى قوله « لا تضامون ، : أى لا ينضم بعضكم إلى بعض ، كما تتضمون فى رؤية الهلال رأس الشهر ، بل رؤية جهرة من غير تكلف لطلبه ، كما ترون البدر . وقوله : « لا تضارون» أى لا ياتيكم ضرر فى طلب رؤيته ، بل ترونه من غير تكلف الطلب . وما روى « تضامون ، مخففا . فالمراد منه : الضيم . أي لا يلحقكم فيه ضيم
He (peace be upon him) said: 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon on the night of the full moon. You will not be crowded (la nudaamoon) in seeing Him.' And in another narration: 'you will not be harmed (la tudaaroon).' The interpretation: the intended meaning is comparing the vision to the vision, not comparing the seen to the seen. And the meaning of His saying 'la tudaamoon' is: you will not join one another, as you join one another in seeing the crescent at the beginning of the month, but rather a clear vision without the trouble of seeking it, as you see the full moon. And His saying 'la tudaaroon' means: no harm will come to you in seeking His vision, but you will see Him without the trouble of seeking. And what is narrated as 'tudaamoon' with lightening: its intended meaning is oppression (al-daym), i.e., no oppression will befall you in it.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation (ta'wil) of the famous hadith about seeing Allah on the Day of Judgment. The author begins by quoting the hadith with two variant wordings: 'la tudaamoon' (you will not be crowded) and 'la tudaaroon' (you will not be harmed). He then clarifies that the comparison of seeing Allah to seeing the moon is a comparison of the vision itself—its clarity and ease—not a comparison of the object seen to the moon. This avoids any anthropomorphism. He explains 'la tudaamoon' as meaning that people will not crowd together as they do when trying to sight the new moon; rather, the vision will be clear and without effort. For 'la tudaaroon', he interprets it as meaning no difficulty or harm will come to the viewer in seeking this vision. Finally, he notes that the reading 'tudaamoon' (with a lightened form) refers to 'daym' (oppression), meaning no injustice will occur in that vision. The overall interpretation preserves divine transcendence by emphasizing the nature of the vision rather than the physical likeness of Allah.

Key Terms
رؤية
تشبيه
تأويل
ضيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith implies Allah is visible like the moon in shape or location.
Correction: The author clarifies that the comparison is of the vision to seeing the full moon—clear and without effort—not a likeness of Allah to the moon.

Checkpoint
What is the correct interpretation of the hadith 'You will see your Lord as you see the moon'?
A) Allah resembles the moon in shape and light.
B) The vision of Allah is clear and without difficulty, like seeing the full moon.
C) Allah will appear in the form of the moon.
D) The hadith is to be taken literally without interpretation.

Key Takeaway
Razi's interpretation safeguards divine transcendence by showing the hadith speaks about the nature of the vision, not about Allah's form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine seeing Allah on the Day of Judgment, do you still picture something like the moon? How does Razi's clarification challenge that image, and what does that reveal about your own tendency to anthropomorphize?
?
Are you willing to let go of a literal, comfortable image of Allah in order to obey the command of tanzih? What attachment to imagining Allah do you need to surrender?
?
The hadith promises a vision that requires no effort. Do you live with an eager hope for that vision, or is your heart more attached to worldly sights? What would it cost you to long for Allah's vision above all else?

Divine Transcendence

The Ta'wil of Khalwah on Judgment Day
Razi interprets the hadith of the divine encounter metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the prophetic hadith about each person being alone with Allah on Judgment Day without an interpreter, and why this interpretation preserves divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Allah's 'aloneness' with each person is a metaphor for His direct, personal attention.

Imam Razi tackles a hadith that might suggest a literal, physical solitude with Allah on Judgment Day. He provides a ta'wil (interpretive explanation) that avoids any anthropomorphism or spatial limitation. In this passage, he explains that the 'khalwah' refers to Allah's singular focus on the one who turns to Him, and He enables each to hear His speech directly. This lesson unpacks that interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and Razi's response
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the hadith quote and Razi's 'then we say' which introduces his ta'wil.
The hadith is well-known; Razi's approach is to reinterpret it metaphorically.
2
Identify the two aspects of the ta'wil
Razi gives two points: (1) the aloneness means Allah's attention when one turns to Him; (2) Allah's power allows each to hear that He is not speaking with others. Locate these in the text.
The second point reinforces that Allah's speech is simultaneous to all.
3
Reflect on how this preserves divine transcendence
Consider why Razi avoids a literal reading. How does interpreting 'khalwah' as attention rather than physical solitude protect the belief that Allah is not confined to space or time?
This connects to the broader principle of tanzih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا ما روى أنه عليه السلام قال : « ما منكم من أحد إلا سيخلو به ربه يوم القيامة ويكله ، وليس بينه و بينه ترجمان ، فنقول . وجه التأويل فيه : ( أن (٢)) من أراد أن يتوجه إليه منهم ، فإنه يخلو به . فعبر به عنه . وأيضاً : لما كان قادراً ، أراد (۳) أن يسمع كل واحد ، أنه لا يتكلم مع غيره ( والله أعلم)(٤)
And also what is narrated that he (peace be upon him) said: 'There is not one of you but his Lord will be alone with him on the Day of Resurrection and will entrust him, and there is no interpreter between him and Him.' Then we say: The aspect of interpretation in it is that (2) whoever among them intends to turn to Him, then He is alone with him. So He expressed that by it. And also: since He is powerful, He intended (3) that each one hear that He does not speak with anyone else. (And Allah knows best) (4)
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage deals with the hadith that on the Day of Judgment, each person will be 'alone' with Allah without an interpreter. The author provides a ta'wil: the aloneness is not physical or spatial; rather, it means that when a person intends to turn to Allah, Allah directs His attention singularly to that person. The expression 'alone' is a metaphor for that direct attention. Additionally, because Allah is all-powerful, He can enable each individual to hear that He is not speaking with anyone else simultaneously, emphasizing the unique personal nature of the encounter. The interpretation avoids any notion of Allah being confined to a place or limited in His ability to attend to all simultaneously.

Key Terms
خلوة
ترجمان
توجه
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah will physically be alone with each person in a literal, spatial sense, implying a place or body for Allah.
Correction: Razi clarifies that 'khalwah' is metaphorical: it refers to Allah's direct, personal attention to the believer who turns to Him, not a physical seclusion. Allah is beyond space and time, and His addressing each person individually does not require spatial proximity.

Checkpoint
What does the 'aloneness' (khalwah) with Allah on Judgment Day mean according to Razi?
A) Allah physically isolates each person from others.
B) Each person sees Allah with their physical eyes.
C) When a person turns to Allah, Allah directs His attention solely to him.
D) Allah speaks to each person in a different language.

Key Takeaway
The 'aloneness' with Allah on Judgment Day is a metaphor for His direct, personal attention to each believer, preserving His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine standing before Allah on Judgment Day, do you envision a physical space or a direct, personal attention from Him? How does Razi's ta'wil challenge your mental image, and what does it demand from you regarding how you conceive of Allah's transcendence?
?
If the 'khalwah' is about Allah's undivided attention, does that change how you view your relationship with Him right now? Are you truly turning to Him alone in your prayers and du'a, or do you allow distractions?
?
The hadith says there is no interpreter between you and your Lord. How does this reality affect your hope and fear regarding meeting Him? Do you live as if you will face Him directly, with no mediator—and if so, what changes in your actions today?

Refuting Apparent Divisibility of Allah
Refutes verses and hadith that might imply Allah is divisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why verses and hadith about Allah's spirit do not imply divisibility, and how scholars interpret them to preserve Allah's transcendence.

Core Idea
Allah is indivisible; scriptural references to His spirit are honorific, not literal.

Some Quranic verses and hadith might appear to suggest that Allah has parts or is divisible. In this lesson, we examine these texts and learn the orthodox interpretation that affirms Allah's absolute oneness and transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter heading
The heading states the topic: apparent meanings that suggest Allah is divisible. Keep this in mind as you read.
The heading sets up the problem.
2
Identify the problematic texts
Note the three Quranic verses and the hadith. They all involve the word 'spirit' attributed to Allah.
The texts are listed as evidence for the apparent meaning.
3
Understand the interpretation
The author gives two interpretative moves: 1) attribution of spirit is honorific, not literal; 2) 'breathing' is a metaphor where the cause is expressed as the effect.
This resolves the apparent problem.
4
Reflect on the necessity
The author concludes this interpretation is necessary because Allah cannot be divisible. Consider why this is a cornerstone of Islamic theology.
The argument preserves tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الحادي عشر في الظواهر التي توهم كونه قابلا للتجزيء والتبعيض - تعالى الله عنه علوا كبيرا
أما الذي ورد منه في القرآن . فقوله تعالى في حق آدم عليه السلام : فإذا سويته ونفخت فيه من روحى ، وقال في مريم عليها السلام : ونفخنا فيها من روحنا ، وقوله تعالى في حق عيسى عليه السلام : وروح منه ،
وأما الخبر . فما روى أبو هريرة - رضى الله عنه ـ أنه قال : لما خلق الله آدم ، ونفخ فيه من روحه ، عطس آدم ، وشكر الله : فقال له ربه : يرحمك ربك ، ثم قال : هذا تحيتك ، وتحية ذرّيتك ، والتأويل: أن تقول: أما إضافة الروح إلى نفسه ، فهو إضافة التشريف . وأما النفخ فإنه عبر بالسبب عن المسبب . وهذا مما يحب المصير إليه ، لامتناع أن يكون تعالى قابلا للتجزىء والتبعيض .
Chapter Eleven: On the apparent meanings that suggest He is susceptible to division and partitioning—exalted is Allah above that, a great exaltation.
As for what has come in the Qur'an: His saying, exalted, concerning Adam, peace be upon him: "When I have fashioned him and breathed into him of My spirit" (Qur'an 15:29). And His saying concerning Mary, peace be upon her: "And We breathed into her of Our spirit" (Qur'an 21:91). And His saying, exalted, concerning Jesus, peace be upon him: "And a spirit from Him" (Qur'an 4:171).
As for the hadith: that which Abu Hurairah—may Allah be pleased with him—reported: he said: When Allah created Adam and breathed into him of His spirit, Adam sneezed and thanked Allah, so his Lord said to him: "May your Lord have mercy on you. Then He said: This is your greeting and the greeting of your offspring."
The interpretation: that you say: As for the attribution of the spirit to Himself, it is an attribution of honor. As for the breathing, He expressed the cause by the effect. And this is what must be resorted to, because it is impossible that the Exalted be susceptible to division and partitioning.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of apparent texts that might imply Allah is divisible. It begins with a chapter heading that states the topic: the apparent meanings that suggest Allah is subject to division and partitioning. Then it presents two categories of problematic texts: Qur'anic verses and a hadith. The verses are: 1) about breathing the spirit into Adam, 2) about breathing into Mary, 3) describing Jesus as 'a spirit from Him'. The hadith is about Allah creating Adam, breathing into him of His spirit, Adam sneezing and thanking, and Allah responding. The author then gives the interpretation: the addition of the spirit to Allah is an addition of honor (idafat al-tashrif), not a part. The 'breathing' is a metaphor where the cause (the spirit) is expressed by the effect (the breath). The author concludes that this interpretation is necessary because it is impossible for Allah to be divisible.

Key Terms
التجزيء
التبعيض
إضافة التشريف
النفخ
الروح
السبب
المسبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrases 'My spirit' and 'breathed into him of My spirit' imply that Allah has a spirit that is a part of Him, which can be separated or divided.
Correction: The attribution of 'spirit' to Allah is one of honor, not of part-whole composition. The 'breathing' is a metaphorical expression where the cause (the spirit being created) is expressed by the effect (breathing). Allah is completely indivisible and transcendent.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the correct interpretation of the phrase 'breathed into him of My spirit'?
A) Allah literally gave some of His own spirit to Adam.
B) The attribution of 'spirit' to Allah is one of honor, and 'breathing' is a metaphor for causation.
C) The spirit is a part of Allah that was transferred to Adam.
D) The verse refers to the Holy Spirit, i.e., the angel Gabriel.

Key Takeaway
The apparent texts about Allah's spirit are interpreted figuratively to maintain His absolute oneness and indivisibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses like 'He breathed into him of My spirit,' does your heart instinctively sense God's closeness, or do you realize the danger of imagining Allah as having parts? This lesson guards your faith from anthropomorphism. Pause now and say silently: 'Allah is one, indivisible, unlike anything in creation.'
?
The author insists that we must accept these interpretations because it is impossible for Allah to be divisible. Is there any area of your life where you subconsciously attribute human limitations or physicality to Allah? Repent from that thought right now and reaffirm His absolute transcendence.
?
This chapter is about guarding against apparent meanings that could lead to disbelief. In your daily recitation of the Qur'an, are you actively seeking to understand verses in a way that preserves Allah's majesty, or do you sometimes let your imagination wander? Commit from today to learn the correct 'aqeedah interpretations for every verse about Allah's attributes.

Refuting Divine Divisibility from Apparent Texts
Razi lists problematic texts and provides the correct interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets Quranic verses and a hadith that seem to imply God is divisible, preserving divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Apparent divisibility in texts is honorific, not literal.

Imam Razi addresses a critical challenge: how to understand texts that, taken literally, might suggest God is composed of parts. He cites specific verses and a hadith, then provides a consistent interpretive method that upholds divine unity. This lesson unpacks his approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the structure: first the problematic texts, then the interpretation.
Pay attention to the key terms: إضافة التشريف and النفخ.
2
Identify the problematic texts
List the three Quranic references and the hadith that seem to attribute a 'spirit' from God. Ask yourself: what could be misunderstood?
3
Understand the interpretation
Razi explains that 'spirit' being attributed to God is an honorific (تشريف) and that 'breathing' is a metaphor (سبب for مسبب). Grasp why this is necessary.
The key is that God cannot be divided.
4
Apply the principle
Consider any other text that might anthropomorphize God. How would Razi's principle of honorific attribution apply?
This connects to the broader theme of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الحادي عشر في الظواهر التي توهم كونه قابلا للتجزيء والتبعيض - تعالى الله عنه علوا كبيرا. أما الذي ورد منه في القرآن فقوله تعالى في حق آدم عليه السلام: "فإذا سويته ونفخت فيه من روحي"، وقال في مريم عليها السلام: "ونفخنا فيها من روحنا"، وقوله تعالى في حق عيسى عليه السلام: "وروح منه". وأما الخبر فما روى أبو هريرة - رضي الله عنه - أنه قال: لما خلق الله آدم، ونفخ فيه من روحه، عطس آدم، وشكر الله، فقال له ربه: يرحمك ربك، ثم قال: هذا تحيتك وتحية ذريتك. والتأويل: أن تقول: أما إضافة الروح إلى نفسه، فهو إضافة التشريف. وأما النفخ فإنه عبر بالسبب عن المسبب. وهذا مما يجب المصير إليه، لامتناع أن يكون تعالى قابلا للتجزىء والتبعيض.
Chapter eleven: On the apparent (implications) that suggest He is divisible and partitionable—exalted is God above that greatly. As for what came in the Quran, God says regarding Adam, peace be upon him: "When I have fashioned him and breathed into him of My spirit", and He says regarding Mary, peace be upon her: "And We breathed into her of Our spirit", and God says regarding Jesus, peace be upon him: "And a spirit from Him". As for the hadith, Abu Huraira, may God be pleased with him, narrated that he said: When God created Adam and breathed into him of His spirit, Adam sneezed and thanked God, so his Lord said to him: "Your Lord has mercy on you," then said: "This is your greeting and the greeting of your offspring." The interpretation: to say that the attribution of the spirit to Himself is an attribution of honor. As for the breathing, it is an expression of the cause for the effect. This is what must be resorted to, because it is impossible for the Exalted to be subject to division and partition.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the idea that God can be divided or partitioned based on apparent meanings of certain texts. Imam Razi first presents the problematic verses: the verse about Adam (15:29) where God says "breathed into him of My spirit", the verse about Mary (21:91) "breathed into her of Our spirit", and the verse about Jesus (4:171) "a spirit from Him". Then he presents a hadith from Abu Huraira describing Adam's creation and the greeting. He then gives the interpretation: when the spirit is attributed to God, it is an honorific attribution (إضافة التشريف), not a literal division. The "breathing" is a metaphorical expression where the cause (the breath) is used to refer to the effect (the spirit). This interpretation is necessary because God cannot be divisible or partitionable.

Key Terms

التجزيء
التبعيض
النفخ
الروح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'spirit of God' implies a part of God that was separated or breathed out.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the attribution is honorific, not literal, and the breathing is a metaphor for the effect (spirit) by its cause (divine command).

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, how should we interpret the attribution of 'spirit' to God in the Quran?
A) It means a part of God that is separated.
B) It is an honorific attribution.
C) It refers to the angel Gabriel.
D) It means God's physical breath.

Key Takeaway
The apparent texts suggesting God's divisibility must be interpreted honorifically to preserve God's transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'I breathed into him of My spirit' in the Quran, does your heart secretly imagine that you have a little piece of God inside you? This lesson calls that thought a dangerous misconception. Will you now train yourself to replace that feeling with humble gratitude for being honored, rather than pride for being divine?
?
You often greet others with the same greeting Adam received from his Lord. Do you say 'alhamdulillah' after sneezing with the awareness that it is a created response, not an echo of eternity? How does this lesson change the weight of that simple act?
?
If you are honest, do you sometimes find comfort in the idea that God's spirit is literally inside you because it makes you feel special? This lesson says that is an impossible belief about God. Are you willing to let go of that comfort and accept that your value comes solely from being a servant whom God chose to honor?

Answering the Objection from the Verse about Idols' Organs
Imam Razi answers an objection about God having limbs from a verse on idols.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that God has organs by reinterpreting the verse about idols as referring to the worshippers' physical capabilities.

Core Idea
The verse about idols criticizes worshippers, not God.

You might encounter an argument that since God criticizes idols for lacking organs, He must have organs Himself. This objection seems logical but misses the context. Imam Razi provides a clear answer by showing the verse actually addresses the worshippers' superiority over their idols.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the flow of objection and answer come through.
Notice the structure: objection first, then answer.
2
Identify the objection
Find the part where the critics argue that if God criticizes idols for lacking organs, He must have organs Himself. Understand why this seems valid.
3
Follow Razi's answer
Razi gives two points: the verse refers to the worshippers' organs, not God's, and the organs represent powers, not physical limbs. See how each point refutes the objection.
Focus on the logic: worshippers have organs, idols don't, so worshippers are superior — irrational to worship idols.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني عشر في الجواب عن استدلالهم بقوله تعالى "ألهم أرجل يمشون بها؟ أم لهم أيد يبطشون بها؟ أم لهم أعين يبصرون بها؟ أم لهم آذان يسمعون بها؟" من قالوا: فإنه تعالى عاب هذه الأصنام، وطعن في كونها آلهة، بناء على عدم هذه الأعضاء لها. فلو لم تكن هذه الأعضاء حاصلة لله تعالى، لتوجه الطعن هناك. وذلك باطل. والجواب عنه: أن يقال: المقصود بهذه الأيدي شيء آخر، سوى ما ذكرتم. وبيانه: هو أن الكفار الذين كانوا يعبدون الأصنام كانت لهم أرجل يمشون بها، وأيد يبطشون بها، وأعين يبصرون بها، وآذان يسمعون بها. ولأن المقصود من الرجل واليد والعين والأذن: هو هذه القوى المتحركة والمدركة. ولأن هذه الأعضاء كانت حاصلة لكم، وغير حاصلة لها. كنتم أشرف وأعلى منها. أيليق بالعقل إقدامكم على عبادتها؟ وبالله التوفيق.
Chapter twelve: On the answer to their argument using God's statement: "Or do they have legs with which they walk? Or do they have hands with which they strike? Or do they have eyes with which they see? Or do they have ears with which they hear?" They said: He, exalted, criticized these idols and impugned them being gods, based on the absence of these organs for them. So if these organs were not possessed by God, the criticism would be directed there. And that is false. The answer: it is to be said: the intended meaning of these 'hands' is something else besides what you mentioned. Its explanation: the disbelievers who used to worship idols had legs with which they walked, hands with which they struck, eyes with which they saw, and ears with which they heard. And because the intended meaning of leg, hand, eye, and ear is these moving and perceiving powers. And because these organs were present for you and absent for them, you are nobler and higher than them. Is it reasonable for you to proceed to worship them? And with God is success.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objection: In Surah Al-A'raf (7:195), God criticizes idols for lacking organs (legs, hands, eyes, ears). The critics argue that if God did not have these organs Himself, He would be subject to the same criticism. Razi's answer: The verse is not describing God's attributes but the attributes of the worshippers. The disbelievers had these organs, while the idols did not. Therefore, the worshipers are superior to the idols, and it is irrational to worship them. Moreover, the intended meaning of these terms (leg, hand, etc.) refers to powers (قوى) of movement and perception, not physical organs. Thus, no implication that God has physical limbs.

Key Terms
أرجل
أيد
أعين
آذان
قوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God criticizing idols for lacking organs implies He has organs.
Correction: Razi shows the criticism is about the worshippers having organs while idols do not, not about God's attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Razi's answer to the objection from the verse about idols?
A) God does have organs but they are unlike ours.
B) The verse is about the worshippers' organs, not God's.
C) The verse is metaphorical.
D) Idols actually have organs.

Key Takeaway
Razi's answer demonstrates that the verse does not imply God has organs; rather, it highlights the irrationality of worshipping powerless idols.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God, do you ever unconsciously imagine Him with human-like limitations? This lesson warns against that. What specific habit can you develop to remind yourself that God is beyond any physical description?
?
The disbelievers in the verse worshipped idols despite being nobler than them. Are there any attachments in your life—status, wealth, or relationships—that you elevate above God even though they are lesser? What is one concrete step you will take this week to redirect your heart's devotion to God alone?
?
Razi shows that a verse that seems to criticize God's nature actually criticizes the worshippers' folly. Have you ever misread a divine command or attribute because you focused on yourself rather than the message? How can you ensure you approach revelation with humility and correct understanding?
