Razi's Answer to the Objection from God's Limbs
Razi answers an objection based on Quranic critique of idols lacking limbs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the claim that God must have physical limbs based on Quranic criticism of idols.

Core Idea
The Quran criticizes idols for lacking powers, not body parts — God is free of limbs.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi tackles a subtle objection: if the Quran mocks idols for not having hands, feet, eyes, and ears, does that mean God must have them to avoid the same criticism? Razi dismantles this by showing that the Quranic critique is not about physical organs but about the capacities they represent. This lesson unpacks his precise answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Start by reading the objection that the Quranic verse criticizing idols for lacking limbs implies God must have limbs, otherwise He would be subject to the same criticism. This sets up the problem.
Notice how the objection uses a logical parallel: if absence of limbs is a flaw in idols, it would be a flaw in God too.
2
Identify Razi's Answer
Razi responds by stating that the intended meaning of 'hands, feet, eyes, ears' in the verse is something other than physical organs. He explains that the verse refers to the moving and perceiving powers (القوى المتحركة والمدركة) that the idols lack.
This reinterpretation is key: the critique is about capabilities, not body parts.
3
Understand the Reasoning
Razi argues that the disbelievers themselves possessed these powers (they could walk, strike, see, hear), yet they worshipped idols devoid of them. Thus, the Quranic rebuke is about the irrationality of worshipping beings inferior to oneself, not about God's attributes.
Razi concludes by asking: is it intellectually appropriate to worship such idols?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني عشر
في
الجواب عن استدلالهم بقوله تعالى ألهم أرجل يمشون بها؟ أم لهم أيد يبطشون بها؟ أم لهم أعين يبصرون بها؟ أم لهم آذان يسمعون بها؟
قالوا: فإنه تعالى عاب هذه الأصنام، وطعن في كونها آلهة، بناء على عدم هذه الأعضاء لها. فلو لم تكن هذه الأعضاء حاصلة لله تعالى، لتوجه الطعن هناك. وذلك باطل. والجواب عنه: أن يقال: المقصود بهذه الأيدي: شيء آخر، سوى ماذكرتم. وبيانه: هو أن الكفار الذين كانوا يعبدون الأصنام كانت لهم أرجل يمشون بها، وأيد يبطشون بها، وأعين يبصرون بها، وآذان يسمعون بها. ولأن المقصود من الرجل واليد والعين والأذن: هو هذه القوى المتحركة والمدركة. ولأن هذه الأعضاء كانت حاصلة لكم، وغير حاصلة لها. كنتم أشرف وأعلى منها. يليق بالعقل أقدامكم على عبادتها؟ وبالله التوفيق
The twelfth chapter: On the answer to their argument by His statement, the Exalted: 'Do they have feet with which they walk? Or do they have hands with which they strike? Or do they have eyes with which they see? Or do they have ears with which they hear?' They said: He, the Exalted, criticized these idols and impugned their being gods, based on the absence of these organs for them. So if these organs were not present for God, the Exalted, then the criticism would apply there. And that is false. And the answer to it is that it should be said: The intended meaning of these hands is something other than what you mentioned. And its explanation is that the disbelievers who used to worship idols had feet with which they walked, and hands with which they struck, and eyes with which they saw, and ears with which they heard. And because the intended meaning of 'foot', 'hand', 'eye', and 'ear' is these moving and perceiving powers. And because these organs were present for you and absent for the idols. You are nobler and higher than them. Is it intellectually appropriate for you to worship them? And with God is success.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer format. Razi presents an objection from those who argue that since the Quran criticizes idols for lacking limbs (feet, hands, eyes, ears), it would imply that God must have such limbs to avoid similar criticism. Razi refutes this by explaining that the Quranic critique is not about the physical absence of organs but about the absence of the capacities (power to move, perceive, etc.) that those organs symbolize. The disbelievers who worship idols possess these capacities themselves, yet they worship things devoid of them, which is intellectually disgraceful. Thus, the absence of literal, physical organs in God does not invite criticism.

Key Terms
الأعضاء
القوى المتحركة والمدركة
الأصنام
الجواب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must have physical limbs because the Quran criticizes idols for lacking them.
Correction: The Quran criticizes idols for lacking capacities (power to move, see, hear, etc.), not physical organs. God is free from bodily parts.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the actual intended meaning of 'hands, feet, eyes, ears' in the Quranic verse criticizing idols?
A) Physical limbs and organs
B) Moving and perceiving powers
C) Divine attributes of God
D) Tools for worship

Key Takeaway
Razi clarifies that the Quranic critique of idols targets their lack of powers, not physical limbs, thus removing any implication about God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quranic critique of idols, did you ever subconsciously imagine that God must have similar attributes? This lesson forces you to confront that assumption. Are you willing to submit your intellect to the reality that God is absolutely unlike His creation, even when your mind tries to draw parallels?
?
The disbelievers worshipped beings lower than themselves. In what areas of your life do you elevate created things—status, wealth, desires—above your relationship with Allah, even though you know they are inferior? How does Razi's argument expose the foolishness of that?
?
Razi reinterprets the verse to protect God's transcendence. Have you ever hesitated to accept a scholarly interpretation because it contradicted a surface-level understanding? Are you ready to trust the depth of Islamic scholarship over your own limited reading?

Proof that 'Face' Cannot Be a Physical Limb: The First Argument
Al-Razi argues that 'Face' in revelation cannot mean a physical limb, citing the verse 'Everything perishes except His Face.'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's first proof that the 'Face' of Allah mentioned in revelation cannot refer to a physical limb, based on the verse 'Everything perishes except His Face.'

Core Idea
If 'Face' were a limb, His Face alone would survive — an absurd conclusion.

Imam Al-Razi opens his argument against anthropomorphism by tackling the word 'Face' in the Quran and hadith. He asserts that interpreting it as a literal physical organ leads to an impossible scenario when we consider the verse 'Everything perishes except His Face.' Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then read the English translation. Notice the logical structure: an assertion followed by a proof introduced with 'The first: ...'
The passage is part of a longer argument, but this chunk contains a complete proof unit.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Al-Razi uses an if-then reasoning: If 'Face' means a physical organ, then from the verse 'Everything perishes except His Face,' it would follow that only the face survives while the rest of the body perishes. What else would perish according to this logic?
He specifically mentions the eye on the face would perish, while the face remains.
3
Understand why the conclusion is absurd
Consider the implications: if the face is a limb, it is part of a body. The verse would then imply that Allah has a body that mostly perishes, leaving only a face. This contradicts the Islamic belief that Allah is eternal and not composed of parts.
Al-Razi notes that some foolish people have actually accepted this absurdity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم: أنه لا يمكن أن يكون الوجه المذكور في هذه الآيات، وهذه الأخبار: هو الوجه. بمعنى العضو والجارحة ويدل عليه وجوه. الأول: قوله تعالى: "وكل شيء هالك إلا وجهه"، وذلك لأنه لو كان الوجه هو العضو المخصوص، لزم أن يفنى جميع الجسد والبدن، وأن تفنى العين التي على الوجه، وأن لا يبقى إلا مجرد الوجه: وقد التزم بعض حمقى
Know: It is impossible that the Face mentioned in these verses and these reports is the face in the sense of the organ and limb. Evidence for it is [from] aspects: The first: His statement, the Most High: 'Everything perishes except His Face.' That is because if the face were the specific organ, it would necessarily follow that the entire body and physique would perish, and that the eye that is on the face would perish, and that nothing would remain except the mere face. And some fools have affirmed [this].
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument against anthropomorphism. Al-Razi asserts categorically that 'the Face' (الوجه) mentioned in the Quranic verses and hadiths cannot be interpreted as a physical limb or organ. He then provides the first proof: the Quranic verse 'Every thing is perishable except His Face' (Quran 28:88). The argument proceeds by conditional: if 'Face' meant a literal body part like a physical face, then perishing would apply only to the rest of the body and the face would survive alone, which is absurd. Moreover, the eye on that face would perish while the face remains, which is impossible. Al-Razi notes that some foolish people have actually upheld this absurd position (that only the face remains). The passage is incomplete in this window, but the first proof establishes that interpreting 'Face' literally leads to an untenable conclusion, thereby supporting a metaphorical or non-corporeal understanding.

Key Terms
الوجه
العضو
الجارحة
كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Everything perishes except His Face' means that Allah has a literal face that never perishes, while the rest of Him perishes.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that if 'Face' were a physical limb, the verse would imply that Allah's body perishes except a part, which is absurd. The verse actually proves that 'Face' here means something other than a limb, such as His essence or His religion.

Al-Razi's first proof shows that taking 'Face' literally leads to an absurd physical scenario, thus 'Face' must be understood in a non-corporeal sense.

Refutation of Anthropomorphism Regarding the Face of Allah
Imam Razi refutes literalism of 'wajh' with four scriptural and rational proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that 'wajh' in the Quran cannot be taken literally as a physical organ, and you will know the four proofs he presents.

Core Idea
Allah's 'face' is not a limb—it is a metonym for His Essence and pleasure.

Imam Razi begins by stating categorically that the word 'face' (wajh) in the Quran cannot refer to a physical body part. He then provides four powerful proofs from scripture and reason to support this. Let's examine each proof to see how he systematically refutes anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow. Notice the clear structure: an initial assertion followed by four numbered proofs.
2
Examine the first proof
Understand how the verse 'Everything is perishing except His face' (28:88) is used. If 'face' were a literal organ, only that part would remain, which is absurd.
Some anthropomorphists actually accepted this absurdity.
3
Examine the second proof
Analyze the verse 'And there remains the face of your Lord, Possessor of Majesty and Honor' (55:27). Since majesty and honor describe the divine Essence, 'face' must be a metonym for the Essence.
4
Examine the third proof
Consider the rational argument from 'So wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah' (2:115). A physical face cannot be everywhere; that would entail one body in multiple places.
5
Examine the fourth proof
Look at verses about 'desiring His face' (18:28, 92:20). A literal reading implies desiring an eternal object, which is impossible. What is really desired is Allah's pleasure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم : أنه لا يمكن أن يكون الواجه المذكور في هذه الآيات ، وهذ الأخبار : هو الوجه . بمعنى العضو والجارحة ويدل عليه وجوه الأول . قوله تعالى : وكل شى هالك إلا وجهه ، (٢٤) وذلك لأنه لو كان الوجه هو العضو المخصوص ، لزم أن يفنى جميع الجسد والبدن ، وأن تفتى العين التي على الوجه ، وأن لا يبقى إلا مجرد الوجه : وقد التزم بعض حمقى المشبهة : ذلك . وهو جهل عظيم . الثاني : إن قوله تعالى: «و يبقى وجه ربك ذو الجلال والإكرام، (٢٥) ظاهرة : يقتضى وصف الوجه بالجلال والإكرام . ومعلوم : أن الموصوف بالجلال والإكرام : هو الله تعالى . وذلك يقتضى أن يكون الوجه ، كتابة عن الذات . الثالث : قوله تعالى : فأينما تولوا ، فثم وجه الله (٢٦) ، وليس المراد من الوجه ههنا : هو العضو المخصوص، فإنا ندرك بالحس : أن المعضو المسمى بالوجه ، غير موجود فى ( جميع (٢٧)) جوانب العالم. وأيضا : فلو حصل ذلك العضو في جميع الجوانب، لزم حصول الجسم الواحد دفعة واحدة ، فى أمكنة كثيرة ، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل . الرابع : إن قوله تعالى : « يريدون وجه (٢٨) وقوله : « إلا ابتغاء وجه ربه الأعلى (٢٩) ، لا يمكن حمل شيء منهما على الظاهر . لأن وجهه تعالى - على مذهبهم - قديم أزلي ، والقديم الأولى لا يراد . لأن الشيء الذي براد : معناه أنه يراد حصوله ودخوله في الوجود . وذلك في القديم الأزلي : . محال : وأيضا : فهؤلاء كانوا يعبدون الله تعالى ، وما كانوا يريدون وجه الله . كيف كان ؟ لأنه لو كان غضبانا عليهم فهم لا يريدونه . وإنما يريدون منه كونه راضيا عنهم ، وذلك يدل على أنه ليس المراد من الوجه في هذه الآيات : نفس الجارحة المخصوصة ، بل المراد منه شيء آخر وهو كونه تعالى واضيا منهم
Know: it is impossible that the 'face' mentioned in these verses and these reports be the face in the sense of the limb and the organ. This is indicated by several aspects. First: His saying, 'Everything is perishing except His face' (28:88); because if 'face' were the specific organ, it would necessitate that all of the body and frame perish, and that the eye which is on the face perish, and that nothing remain except mere face. Some fools of the anthropomorphists have adhered to that, and it is great ignorance. Second: His saying, 'And there remains the face of your Lord, Possessor of Majesty and Honor' (55:27); its apparent meaning requires describing the face with majesty and honor. It is known that the one described with majesty and honor is Allah Almighty. That necessitates that the face be a metonym for the Essence. Third: His saying, 'So wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah' (2:115); the intended meaning of 'face' here is not the specific organ, for we perceive by sense that the organ called 'face' does not exist in all directions of the world. Also, if that organ were to occur in all directions, it would necessitate the occurrence of one single body in many places at once, and that no rational person would say. Fourth: His saying, 'Desiring His face' (18:28) and His saying, 'Except seeking the face of his Lord, the Most High' (92:20); neither of them can be taken literally. Because His face—according to their school—is eternal and pre-eternal, and the eternal pre-eternal is not desired. For the thing that is desired means that its coming into existence and entry into being is desired. That is impossible in the case of the eternal pre-eternal. Also, these people used to worship Allah Almighty, and they were not desiring the face of Allah. How could it be? For if He were angry with them, they would not desire it; rather, they desire from Him that He be pleased with them. And this indicates that the intended meaning of 'face' in these verses is not the specific organ itself, but rather something else, namely that He Almighty be pleased with them.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of anthropomorphism (tashbih). Imam Razi states at the outset that it is impossible for the 'face' (al-wajh) mentioned in verses and hadiths to mean a physical limb or organ. He then provides four proofs to support this. First proof: from the verse 'Everything is perishing except His face' (28:88). If 'face' meant a literal body part, then only that part would remain while the rest of Allah's being would perish—an absurd conclusion that some foolish anthropomorphists actually accepted. Second proof: from the verse 'And there remains the face of your Lord, Possessor of Majesty and Honor' (55:27). Since majesty and honor are attributes of the divine Essence, the 'face' here must be a metonym (kinaya) for the Essence itself, not a separate organ. Third proof: from the verse 'So wherever you turn, there is the face of Allah' (2:115). Sensory experience tells us that a physical face cannot be present in every direction simultaneously. Moreover, if it were, that would require one body to be in multiple places at once—a rational impossibility. Fourth proof: from verses mentioning 'desiring His face' (18:28) and 'seeking the face of his Lord' (92:20). A literal reading would imply that believers seek an eternal, pre-eternal object that already exists—but seeking implies bringing something into existence, which cannot apply to the eternal. Additionally, what believers actually desire is Allah's pleasure and satisfaction, not a literal face. Therefore, 'face' in these verses must refer to something else—specifically, that Allah be pleased with them. The passage carefully follows a logical sequence, first establishing the impossibility of literalism, then offering scriptural and rational arguments.

Key Terms
الوجه (al-wajh)
التشبيه (tashbih)
التنزيه (tanzih)
القديم الأزلي (al-qadim al-azali)
كتابة (kinaya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'face' of Allah mentioned in the Quran is a physical organ like a human face.
Correction: Imam Razi proves that 'face' cannot be a physical organ through four arguments: it would imply only that organ remains, it is described with divine attributes, it cannot be physically everywhere, and it cannot be 'desired' in a literal sense. Instead, it refers to Allah's Essence or pleasure.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's first proof, why can 'face' in 'Everything is perishing except His face' (28:88) not mean a physical organ?
A) Because a physical face cannot be described with majesty and honor.
B) Because if it meant a physical organ, only that organ would remain, which is absurd.
C) Because sensory perception shows that Allah's face is everywhere.
D) Because seeking a physical face would imply desiring an eternal object.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that the 'face' of Allah in the Quran is never a physical limb but a metonym for His divine Essence or His pleasure and acceptance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Razi calls those who take 'face' literally 'fools' and their position 'great ignorance.' ?

The Face of Allah is a Metaphor for His Essence
Imam Razi presents two Quranic proofs that 'face' is not a limb, focusing on Quran 55:27 and 2:115.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the word 'face' in Quran 55:27 and 2:115 cannot refer to a physical limb and must be understood as a metaphor for Allah's Essence or omnipresence.

Core Idea
The Quran describes Allah's face with attributes that only belong to His Essence, so 'face' is a metaphor.

Imam Razi continues his refutation of anthropomorphism by presenting two Quranic proofs. He shows that describing Allah's face with majesty and honor, and stating that His face is everywhere, logically entails that 'face' is not a literal organ but a metaphorical expression for His Essence or omnipresence.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the second proof about Quran 55:27. Notice how the verse describes the face with majesty and honor, which are attributes of Allah Himself.
This proof focuses on the attribute of majesty.
2
Analyze the implication
Reflect on why describing the face with divine attributes forces it to be a metaphor for the Essence, not a literal limb.
3
Read the third proof
Read the third proof about Quran 2:115. Consider the logical and sensory arguments against a physical face being everywhere.
This proof uses the impossibility of one body being in many places.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : إن قوله تعالى: «و يبقى وجه ربك ذو الجلال والإكرام» ظاهرة: يقتضى وصف الوجه بالجلال والإكرام. ومعلوم: أن الموصوف بالجلال والإكرام: هو الله تعالى. وذلك يقتضى أن يكون الوجه، كناية عن الذات.
الثالث : قوله تعالى : فأينما تولوا، فثم وجه الله، وليس المراد من الوجه ههنا: هو العضو المخصوص، فإنا ندرك بالحس: أن العضو المسمى بالوجه، غير موجود في جميع جوانب العالم. وأيضا: فلو حصل ذلك العضو في جميع الجوانب، لزم حصول الجسم الواحد دفعة واحدة، في أمكنة كثيرة، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل.
الرابع : إن قوله تعالى: «يريدون وجهه» وقوله: «إلا ابتغاء وجه ربه الأعلى» لا يمكن حمل شيء منهما على الظاهر. لأن وجهه تعالى - على مذهبهم - قديم أزلي، والقديم الأزلي لا يراد. لأن الشيء الذي يراد: معناه أنه يراد حصوله ودخوله في الوجود. وذلك في القديم الأزلي: محال. وأيضا: فهؤلاء كانوا يعبدون الله تعالى، وما كانوا يريدون وجه الله. كيف كان؟ لأنه لو كان غضبانا عليهم فهم لا يريدونه. وإنما يريدون منه كونه راضيا عنهم، وذلك يدل على أنه ليس المراد من الوجه في هذه الآيات: نفس الجارحة المخصوصة، بل المراد منه شيء آخر وهو كونه تعالى راضيا منهم.
الخامس : الخبر الذي رويناه. وهو قوله عليه السلام: «أقرب ما تكون المرأة من وجه ربها، إذا كانت في قعر بيتها». ومعلوم: أنه لو كان المراد من الوجه: العضو المخصوص، لم يختلف الحال في القرب والبعد. بسبب أن تكون في بيتها أو لم تكن. أما إذا حملنا الوجه على الرضاء، استقام ذلك. فثبت بهذه الدلائل: أنه لا يمكن أن يكون للوجه المذكور في هذه الآيات والأخبار بمعنى العضو والجارحة.
Second: Indeed His statement, exalted be He, 'And there remains the Face of your Lord, Possessor of Majesty and Honor' (Quran 55:27) — its apparent meaning necessitates describing the face with majesty and honor. And it is known that the one described with majesty and honor is Allah, exalted be He. That necessitates that the face is a metaphor (kinayah) for the Essence (dhat).
Third: His statement, exalted be He, 'Wherever you turn, there is the Face of Allah' (Quran 2:115). And what is meant by 'face' here is not the specific organ, for we perceive by sense that the organ called the face does not exist in all directions of the world. Also, if that organ were present in all directions, it would necessitate the existence of one body simultaneously in many places, and no rational person would say that.
Fourth: Indeed His statement, exalted be He, 'Desiring His Face' (Quran 18:28) and His statement, 'Except seeking the Face of his Lord, the Most High' (Quran 92:20) cannot possibly be interpreted literally in either of them. Because His face, exalted be He, according to their school, is eternal and pre-eternal (qadim azali), and the eternal pre-eternal is not desired (yuradu). Because the thing that is desired means that its coming into existence and entry into being is desired. That is impossible for the eternal pre-eternal. Also, these people used to worship Allah, exalted be He, and they did not desire the face of Allah — how could that be? Because if He was angry with them, they would not desire it; rather they desired from Him that He be pleased with them. That indicates that what is meant by 'face' in these verses is not the specific organ itself, but something else, namely that He, exalted be He, is pleased with them.
Fifth: The report (khabar) that we narrated, which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'The closest a woman is to the face of her Lord is when she is in the depth of her house.' And it is known that if what is meant by 'face' were the specific organ, the situation would not differ in proximity and distance because she is in her house or not. But if we interpret 'face' as pleasure (rida'), that becomes consistent. So it is established by these proofs that it is not possible for the 'face' mentioned in these verses and reports to mean the limb and organ.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents four distinct proofs (second through fifth) that the word 'wajh' (face) in Quranic verses and a prophetic hadith cannot refer to a literal physical limb. The author, Imam Razi, is refuting anthropomorphism (tashbih).

The second proof: In Quran 55:27, Allah's face is described as possessing majesty and honor. Since these attributes belong to Allah Himself, the 'face' here must be a metaphor (kinayah) for the Essence (dhat).

The third proof: Quran 2:115 states that wherever one turns, there is the face of Allah. If ‘face’ were a physical organ, it would have to exist in every direction simultaneously, which is impossible for a single body. Moreover, sensory observation confirms that no such organ exists in all directions.

The fourth proof: Verses like 18:28 and 92:20 speak of 'desiring' Allah's face. But desire implies bringing something into existence, which is inapplicable to the eternal pre-eternal. Also, the context shows the believers desired Allah's pleasure, not a physical organ. Therefore, 'face' here means His pleasure (rida).

The fifth proof: The hadith states that a woman is closest to the face of her Lord when she is in the depth of her house. If 'face' were a physical limb, closeness would be spatial and unaffected by location. Interpreting it as 'pleasure' makes sense, as her seclusion increases divine pleasure. Thus all these proofs demonstrate that 'wajh' in such texts cannot mean a physical organ.

كناية
ذات
جارحة

Key Takeaway
The use of 'face' in Quran 55:27 and 2:115 cannot be literal; it is a metaphor for Allah's Essence and omnipresence.

?

The Face of Allah Means His Pleasure
Imam Razi presents two more proofs (fourth and fifth) showing that 'face' means Allah's pleasure, not a physical organ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the word 'face' in verses about desiring Allah's face and in the hadith about a woman's closeness must be interpreted as Allah's pleasure, not a physical organ.

Core Idea
Desiring Allah's face is nonsensical if it is a limb, but makes perfect sense as desiring His pleasure.

Imam Razi presents two more proofs that 'face' cannot be a physical organ. The first examines verses that speak of 'desiring Allah's face,' showing that desire applies to temporal things, not the eternal. The second is a hadith that links a woman's proximity to Allah's face to her being in her house, which only makes sense if 'face' means pleasure.

Guided Path
1
Read the fourth proof
Read the fourth proof about Quran 18:28 and 92:20. Notice the argument about desire and the eternal nature of Allah's face.
This proof uses logical impossibility of desiring the eternal.
2
Understand the context
Consider why the believers would desire Allah's pleasure, not a physical organ, and how that supports the metaphorical interpretation.
3
Read the fifth proof
Read the hadith about a woman's closeness to the face of her Lord. Reflect on why a literal interpretation fails and how interpreting as pleasure works.
This proof is from a prophetic hadith.

الرابع : إن قوله تعالى: «يريدون وجهه» وقوله: «إلا ابتغاء وجه ربه الأعلى» لا يمكن حمل شيء منهما على الظاهر. لأن وجهه تعالى - على مذهبهم - قديم أزلي، والقديم الأزلي لا يراد. لأن الشيء الذي يراد: معناه أنه يراد حصوله ودخوله في الوجود. وذلك في القديم الأزلي: محال. وأيضا: فهؤلاء كانوا يعبدون الله تعالى، وما كانوا يريدون وجه الله. كيف كان؟ لأنه لو كان غضبانا عليهم فهم لا يريدونه. وإنما يريدون منه كونه راضيا عنهم، وذلك يدل على أنه ليس المراد من الوجه في هذه الآيات: نفس الجارحة المخصوصة، بل المراد منه شيء آخر وهو كونه تعالى راضيا منهم.
الخامس : الخبر الذي رويناه. وهو قوله عليه السلام: «أقرب ما تكون المرأة من وجه ربها، إذا كانت في قعر بيتها». ومعلوم: أنه لو كان المراد من الوجه: العضو المخصوص، لم يختلف الحال في القرب والبعد. بسبب أن تكون في بيتها أو لم تكن. أما إذا حملنا الوجه على الرضاء، استقام ذلك. فثبت بهذه الدلائل: أنه لا يمكن أن يكون للوجه المذكور في هذه الآيات والأخبار بمعنى العضو والجارحة.
Second: Indeed His statement, exalted be He, 'And there remains the Face of your Lord, Possessor of Majesty and Honor' (Quran 55:27) — its apparent meaning necessitates describing the face with majesty and honor. And it is known that the one described with majesty and honor is Allah, exalted be He. That necessitates that the face is a metaphor (kinayah) for the Essence (dhat).
Third: His statement, exalted be He, 'Wherever you turn, there is the Face of Allah' (Quran 2:115). And what is meant by 'face' here is not the specific organ, for we perceive by sense that the organ called the face does not exist in all directions of the world. Also, if that organ were present in all directions, it would necessitate the existence of one body simultaneously in many places, and no rational person would say that.
Fourth: Indeed His statement, exalted be He, 'Desiring His Face' (Quran 18:28) and His statement, 'Except seeking the Face of his Lord, the Most High' (Quran 92:20) cannot possibly be interpreted literally in either of them. Because His face, exalted be He, according to their school, is eternal and pre-eternal (qadim azali), and the eternal pre-eternal is not desired (yuradu). Because the thing that is desired means that its coming into existence and entry into being is desired. That is impossible for the eternal pre-eternal. Also, these people used to worship Allah, exalted be He, and they did not desire the face of Allah — how could that be? Because if He was angry with them, they would not desire it; rather they desired from Him that He be pleased with them. That indicates that what is meant by 'face' in these verses is not the specific organ itself, but something else, namely that He, exalted be He, is pleased with them.
Fifth: The report (khabar) that we narrated, which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'The closest a woman is to the face of her Lord is when she is in the depth of her house.' And it is known that if what is meant by 'face' were the specific organ, the situation would not differ in proximity and distance because she is in her house or not. But if we interpret 'face' as pleasure (rida'), that becomes consistent. So it is established by these proofs that it is not possible for the 'face' mentioned in these verses and reports to mean the limb and organ.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents four distinct proofs (second through fifth) that the word 'wajh' (face) in Quranic verses and a prophetic hadith cannot refer to a literal physical limb. The author, Imam Razi, is refuting anthropomorphism (tashbih).

The second proof: In Quran 55:27, Allah's face is described as possessing majesty and honor. Since these attributes belong to Allah Himself, the 'face' here must be a metaphor (kinayah) for the Essence (dhat).

The third proof: Quran 2:115 states that wherever one turns, there is the face of Allah. If ‘face’ were a physical organ, it would have to exist in every direction simultaneously, which is impossible for a single body. Moreover, sensory observation confirms that no such organ exists in all directions.

The fourth proof: Verses like 18:28 and 92:20 speak of 'desiring' Allah's face. But desire implies bringing something into existence, which is inapplicable to the eternal pre-eternal. Also, the context shows the believers desired Allah's pleasure, not a physical organ. Therefore, 'face' here means His pleasure (rida).

The fifth proof: The hadith states that a woman is closest to the face of her Lord when she is in the depth of her house. If 'face' were a physical limb, closeness would be spatial and unaffected by location. Interpreting it as 'pleasure' makes sense, as her seclusion increases divine pleasure. Thus all these proofs demonstrate that 'wajh' in such texts cannot mean a physical organ.

Key Terms
وجه
رضا
كناية
جارحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi conclude about the meaning of 'face' in the hadith 'The closest a woman is to the face of her Lord is when she is in the depth of her house'?
A) It means a physical organ that is near.
B) It means Allah's pleasure.
C) It means the woman's own face.
D) It means the Kaaba.

Key Takeaway
The word 'face' in verses about desiring Allah's face and in the hadith is a metaphor for Allah's pleasure, not a literal body part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship like prayer or charity, do you stop to examine your intention—are you truly seeking Allah's pleasure (ridā) or just fulfilling a habit? What would change if you consciously oriented each action toward His face?
?
The hadith says a woman is closest to the face of her Lord when she is in the depth of her home. In a world full of distractions, what is the 'depth of your home'—the place or state where you can sincerely turn to Allah? Are you actually spending time there, or do you let other things crowd it out?
?
If Allah's 'face' truly means His pleasure, then what does it mean that you will see His face in the Hereafter (a belief held by Ahl al-Sunnah)? How does this shift your longing—are you yearning for a vision or for a relationship of infinite pleasure? And how does that shape your hope and fear right now?

Three Reasons Why 'Face' Is a Metaphor for the Essence
Razi enumerates three reasons for interpreting 'face' as a metaphor for the Essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and rational arguments that necessitate interpreting the word 'face' (wajh) as a metaphor for the divine Essence (dhat) in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Language itself shows that 'face' naturally stands for the whole essence.

Imam Razi has already established that 'face' in divine texts is not literal. Here he deepens the argument by explaining why it is necessary to interpret 'face' as a metaphor for the Essence. He presents three compelling reasons drawn from everyday language and human experience, showing that this metaphorical usage is not arbitrary but deeply rooted in linguistic convention and rational observation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that Razi previously argued that anthropomorphic terms in the Quran must be interpreted metaphorically. This lesson explains why 'face' specifically is taken as a metaphor for the Essence.
This lesson builds on the previous discussion of divine transcendence.
2
Read the passage in Arabic
Read the Arabic text slowly, noticing how Razi introduces each reason with 'الأول', 'الثاني', 'الثالث'. The third reason is cut off; observe the pattern.
Focus on the first two reasons since the third is incomplete.
3
Analyze the first reason
The face is the most visible part and distinguishes a person. It is used to refer to the whole person, just as a leader is called 'face of the people'. This linguistic custom supports the metaphor.
The word 'وجيه' (wajih) meaning 'chief' or 'distinguished' reinforces this.
4
Analyze the second reason
The purpose of a person is to manifest intellect and senses. The face is where these effects appear. Since the purpose is most evident in the face, the whole essence can be called by that part.
This reason appeals to teleology – the purpose of creation.
5
Reflect on the third reason (incomplete)
Although the text cuts off, the third reason likely relates to the face's beauty and subtlety. Consider how this might further justify the metaphor.
We can only speculate, but the pattern suggests it completes the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا، فنقول: لفظ الوجه قد يجعل كناية عن الذات تارة، وعن الرضى أخرى. أما الأول فنقول: السبب في وجوب جعل الوجه كناية عن الذات وجوه:
الأول: إن المرئى من الإنسان في أكثر الأوقات ليس إلا وجهه، وبوجهه يتميز ذلك الإنسان عن غيره. فالوجه كأنه هو العضو الذي به يتحقق وجود ذلك الإنسان، وبه يعرف كونه موجودا. ولما كان الأمر كذلك، لاجرم حسن جعل الوجه اسما لكل الذات. ومما يقوى ذلك: أن القوم إذا كان معهم إنسان يرتب أحوالهم ويقوم بإصلاح أمورهم، سموه وجه القوم ووجيههم. والسبب فيه: ما ذكرنا.
الثاني: إن المقصود من الإنسان ظهور آثار عقله وحسه وفهمه وفكره. ومعلوم: أن معدن هذه الأحوال هو الرأس، ومظهر آثار هذه القوى هو الوجه. ولما كان معظم المقصود من خلق الإنسان إنما يظهر في الوجه، لاجرم حسن إطلاق اسم الوجه على كل الذات.
الثالث: إن الوجه مخصوص بمزيد الحسن واللطافة، والتركيب
If you understand this, then we say: The word 'face' (wajh) may be used as a metaphor (kinayah) for the Essence (dhat) sometimes, and for pleasure (rida) at other times. As for the first, we say: The reason for the necessity of making 'face' a metaphor for the Essence has several aspects:
First: What is seen of a person most of the time is only his face, and by his face that person is distinguished from others. So the face is as if it is the organ by which that person's existence is actualized, and by which his existence is known. Since this is the case, it is fitting to make 'face' a name for the entire essence (dhat). And strengthening that is that when a people have a person who arranges their affairs and undertakes to reform their matters, they call him the 'face' of the people (wajh al-qawm) and their 'chief' (wajih). The reason for that is what we mentioned.
Second: The purpose of a person is the manifestation of the effects of his intellect, sense, understanding, and thought. And it is known that the source of these states is the head, and the manifestation of the effects of these faculties is the face. Since most of the purpose of creating a person appears in the face, it is fitting to use the name 'face' for the entire essence.
Third: The face is distinguished by extra beauty, subtlety, and composition...
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a further analysis by Imam Razi after establishing that 'face' in divine texts is not literal. Here he explains why it is necessary to interpret 'face' as kinayah for the Essence (dhat) in some contexts. He acknowledges that 'wajh' can be metaphor for either the Essence or for pleasure (rida), and then gives three reasons for the first usage.

First reason: The face is the most visible part of a person, by which he is recognized and distinguished. Therefore, it is natural to use 'face' to refer to the entire person. Likewise, a leader who manages a community's affairs is called 'the face of the people' (wajh al-qawm) or 'their distinguished one' (wajih). This linguistic custom supports the metaphorical extension.

Second reason: The purpose of human existence is to manifest intellect, sense, understanding, and thought. The head is the center of these faculties, and the face displays their effects. Since the face is the primary locus of this purpose, it is appropriate to call the whole essence by the name of that part through which its purpose is most evident.

Third reason (incomplete): The face is distinguished by extra beauty, subtlety, and composition. The text cuts off here, but the idea likely is that the face's special qualities make it a fitting symbol for the essence's perfection. All three reasons together justify the metaphorical reading in theological contexts.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the metaphorical interpretation of 'face' is arbitrary and not grounded in language.
Correction: Razi shows that the metaphor is based on well-established linguistic usage and rational principles, as evidenced by everyday speech and the purpose of human creation.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason Razi gives for calling the entire essence 'face'?
A) Because the face is the most beautiful part.
B) Because the face is the most visible part by which a person is recognized.
C) Because the face is the source of intellect.
D) Because the face is always exposed.

Key Takeaway
The metaphorical interpretation of 'face' as the divine Essence is not arbitrary but grounded in linguistic usage and human observation, as shown by three rational reasons.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'And there remains the Face of your Lord' (55:27), do you instinctively imagine a physical face, or do you let the Quran's own linguistic flexibility teach you that 'face' here points to His exalted essence? What would change in your awe of God if you truly internalised that His 'face' is not like ours?
?
Your own face is the part of you that others see most—what does it reveal about your purpose? If, as Razi argues, the face manifests intellect and thought, does your face reflect a mind occupied with the Divine, or does it show attachment to this world? How can you ensure your face becomes a sign of your devotion?
?
Is your conception of God shaped more by your imagination or by the disciplined understanding of scholars like Razi? Are you comfortable with the idea that God's attributes, such as 'face,' are beyond literal human similarity, yet you still pray to Him as if He hears you? What does that tension demand of your faith?

Hadith Proof: 'Wajh' as Pleasure, Not Limb
Imam Razi cites hadith to prove 'wajh' cannot mean a physical organ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a hadith about a woman's closeness to God's face proves that 'wajh' cannot mean a physical organ but rather God's pleasure.

Core Idea
A woman's location affects closeness to God's face, so 'face' cannot be a body part.

Imam Razi presents a fifth proof from a prophetic hadith to argue that the word 'wajh' (face) in the Quran and hadith does not refer to a physical limb. The hadith states that a woman is closest to the face of her Lord when she is in the depth of her house. This lesson examines the logical implication: if 'face' were a physical organ, physical proximity would be constant regardless of location, but the hadith emphasizes that seclusion brings closeness. Thus, 'wajh' must be interpreted as God's pleasure or mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its translation
Read the Arabic hadith carefully and then the literal translation. Notice the key phrase 'the face of her Lord'.
The hadith is well-known and highlights a spiritual closeness.
2
Analyze the logical argument
Imam Razi points out that if 'face' meant a physical limb, then being in the house or not would not make a difference to closeness. But the hadith says the woman is closest when in her house. Therefore, 'face' must have a non-literal meaning.
This is a classic demonstration of how figurative interpretation is necessary to avoid contradiction.
3
Understand the conclusion
Razi concludes that 'face' in these texts cannot mean a physical organ. Instead, it must mean God's pleasure or mercy, which is attained through piety and seclusion.
This proof is part of a larger series of arguments for divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : الخبر الذى رويناه . وهو قوله عليه السلام : « أقرب ما تكون المرأة من وجه ربها ، إذا كانت في قعر بيتها، ومعلوم : أنه لو كان المراد من الوجه : العضو المخصوص ، لم يختلف الحال في القرب والبعد . بسبب أن تكون في بيتها أو لم تكن . أما إذا حملنا الوجه على الرضاء ، استقام ذلك . فثبت بهذه الدلائل : أنه لا يمكن أن يكون لوجه المذكور فى هذه الآيات والأخبار بمعنى العضو والجارحة .
The fifth: the report that we narrated. And it is his saying, peace be upon him: 'The closest the woman is to the face of her Lord is when she is in the depth of her house.' And it is known: if what was meant by 'the face' were the specific organ, the state of closeness and distance would not differ because of her being in her house or not. But if we interpret the face as pleasure, that is correct. So it is established by these proofs that it is impossible for 'the face' mentioned in these verses and reports to mean the physical organ and limb.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof in a series that the word 'wajh' (face) in religious texts cannot refer to a physical body part. Imam Razi cites a hadith where the Prophet says a woman is closest to her Lord's face when she is in the depths of her home. The argument is: if 'face' meant a physical organ, then being in the house or not would not affect closeness to that organ. But the hadith indicates that her location (being secluded in her house) brings her closer to God's face. Therefore, 'face' here must mean God's pleasure or mercy, which is attained through piety and seclusion. This proof demonstrates that interpreting 'wajh' literally leads to contradiction, so it must be metaphorical.

Key Terms
الوجه (al-wajh)
الرضاء (al-rida)
العضو المخصوص (al-‘udw al-makhsūs)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'wajh' in divine texts always refers to a physical face or organ.
Correction: This hadith shows that 'wajh' must mean pleasure because if it were a physical organ, physical proximity would be constant regardless of location, but the hadith indicates that seclusion increases closeness.

Checkpoint

A) It means a physical organ.

C) It means the essence of God.
D) It means the woman's face.

Key Takeaway
The hadith about the woman's closeness to God's face proves that 'wajh' cannot be a physical organ because location would not affect proximity to a physical face; it must mean God's pleasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you think of being 'close to God', do you imagine a physical closeness or something else? How does this hadith challenge your default assumption? What concrete action will you take today to seek what this hadith identifies as true nearness to God?
?
If the woman's closeness to God's 'face' depends on her staying at home, what does that imply about the means to achieve God's pleasure in your own life? Are you actively pursuing actions that bring you closer to God's pleasure, or are you relying on empty claims?
?
This hadith shows that even a prophetic statement can be misinterpreted if taken literally. Are you willing to re-examine your understanding of any other divine attributes? What would it cost you to let go of a literal interpretation if the evidence points otherwise?

Understanding 'Wajh' as Pleasure in the Quran
Imam Razi explains the metaphor of 'wajh' for pleasure and distinguishes its meanings in Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi justifies the metaphorical use of 'wajh' for divine pleasure and how he applies it to specific Quranic verses.

Core Idea
When a person's face turns, it shows inclination—thus 'wajh' can symbolize pleasure.

Imam Razi continues his analysis of the word 'wajh' (face) in the Quran. He has already shown that 'wajh' can refer to the essence. Now he demonstrates that in certain verses it refers to God's pleasure, based on the natural human association between turning one’s face and inclination of the heart. This lesson explores his reasoning and its application.

Guided Path
1
Understand the metaphorical basis
Read the passage's opening explanation: when a person's heart inclines toward something, he turns his face toward it. This makes 'wajh' a natural metaphor for pleasure. Recognize the logical connection between turning the face and being pleased.
This is a kalam technique of grounding metaphorical language in observable human behavior.
2
Distinguish between essence and pleasure
Imam Razi classifies Quranic verses: some use 'wajh' to mean the divine essence (e.g., 'Everything is perishing except His face'), others to mean God's pleasure (e.g., 'for the face of God'). Note the two categories and the reasoning for each.
The distinction resolves apparent anthropomorphism.
3
Apply the interpretation to verses and hadith
The passage ends by stating that the same interpretation applies to the hadiths mentioned earlier. Reflect on how this unified approach maintains divine transcendence while honoring the Quranic text.
This step connects the lesson to broader theological concerns.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما بيان السبب في جواز جعل لفظ الوجه كناية عن الرضى : فهو أن الإنسان إذا مال قلبه إلى الشيء ، أقبل بوجهه عليه ، وإذا كره شيئاً أعرض بوجهه عنه . فلما كان إقبال الإنسان بوجهه عليه من لوازم كونه مائلا إليه ، لاجرم حسن جعل لفظ الوجه كناية عن الرضى . إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة ، فنقول : أما قوله تعالى : {كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه} وقوله : {ويبقى وجه ربك} ، فالمراد منه : الذات . والمقصود من ذكره : التأكيد والمبالغة ، فإنه يقال : وجه هذا الأمر : كذا وكذا ، ووجه هذا الدليل : هو كذا و كذا ، والمراد منه : هو نفس ذلك الشيء ، ونفس ذلك الدليل . فكذا هذا . وأما قوله تعالى : {فثم وجه الله} ، {إنما نطعمكم لوجه الله} ، {إلا ابتغاء وجه ربه الأعلى}، فالمراد من الكل : رضى الله تعالى . وهكذا القول في تلك الأحاديث . وبالله التوفيق.
As for the explanation of the reason for the permissibility of making the word 'wajh' a metaphor for pleasure: it is that when a person's heart inclines toward something, he turns his face toward it, and when he dislikes something, he turns his face away from it. Since a person's turning his face toward something is among the necessary concomitants of his being inclined toward it, it is fitting to make the word 'wajh' a metaphor for pleasure. If you have understood this premise, we say: As for His saying, the Most High: {Everything is perishing except His face} and His saying: {And the face of your Lord will remain}, what is meant by it is the essence. And the purpose of mentioning it is emphasis and exaggeration, for it is said: 'the face of this matter is such and such', and 'the face of this proof is such and such', and what is meant by it is the thing itself and the proof itself. So likewise here. And as for His saying, the Most High: {Then wherever you turn, there is the face of God}, {We feed you only for the face of God}, {Except seeking the face of his Lord, the Most High}, what is meant in all of them is the pleasure of God, the Most High. And the same applies to those hadiths. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage provides the basis for the second metaphorical use of 'wajh': pleasure. The reason is that a person turns their face toward what they desire and away from what they dislike, so the face indicates inclination. Thus 'wajh' can stand for pleasure or approval. Imam Razi then applies this to specific Quranic verses: In verses like 'Everything is perishing except His face' and 'The face of your Lord will remain', the meaning is the divine essence, used for emphasis. In verses like 'Wherever you turn, there is the face of God', 'We feed you only for the face of God', and 'Seeking the face of his Lord', the meaning is God's pleasure. This distinction resolves apparent anthropomorphism. The same interpretation applies to the hadiths mentioned.

Key Terms
الرضى (al-riḍā)
كناية (kināyah)
الوجه (al-wajh)
المبالغة (al-mubālaghah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All occurrences of 'wajh' in the Quran refer to the divine essence.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that in some verses, 'wajh' means God's pleasure, based on the metaphorical link.

Checkpoint
How does Imam Razi interpret the phrase 'فثم وجه الله' (Then wherever you turn, there is the face of God)?
A) It means the physical face of God.
B) It means the divine essence.
C) It means God's pleasure.
D) It means the direction of prayer.

Key Takeaway
The word 'wajh' in the Quran can refer to either the divine essence or God's pleasure, depending on context, and Imam Razi explains the metaphor based on human behavior.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'We feed you only for the face of God' (76:9), do you truly examine your intentions to ensure every act is solely for His pleasure, not for recognition or reward? Pause now and write one action today that you will purify for His face alone.
?
Razi explains that turning the face is a sign of the heart's inclination. Look at your daily preoccupations—where does your face naturally turn? Does it match what you claim to love most? Identify one distraction you will turn away from today for Allah's sake.
?
The word 'wajh' can mean both Allah's essence and His pleasure. When you yearn to see His face (as in Paradise), are you primarily seeking the vision or the pleasure of His closeness? How does this distinction reshape your longing? Commit to a dua asking for His pleasure above all.

Necessity of Figurative Interpretation for God's 'Eye'
Imam Razi argues for the necessity of figurative interpretation of Quranic verses about God's 'eye'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that Quranic references to God's 'eye' must be interpreted figuratively as intense care and protection, not literally.

Core Idea
Literal interpretation of God's 'eye' leads to absurdity; ta'wil is rationally necessary.

Imam Razi tackles a key theological challenge: how to understand Quranic verses that attribute an 'eye' to God. He begins by citing common usage that already points to a figurative meaning. Then he presents three compelling reasons why taking these verses literally is impossible. This passage lays the foundation for the method of ta'wil in defending divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text carefully, then the full translation. Notice the structure: evidence from usage, then three numbered reasons, then conclusion.
The passage is dense but logically organized.
2
Understand the three rational objections
Identify each of the three reasons Imam Razi gives against literal interpretation. Think about why each one makes literal sense impossible.
The third reason about multiple eyes is particularly striking.
3
Grasp the necessity of ta'wil
See how the author concludes that ta'wil is unavoidable. Note that he doesn't just say 'interpret figuratively' but provides a justification for the specific metaphor of care.
The metaphor is based on the connection between looking and caring.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider how this argument fits into the overall project of preserving God's transcendence. Why is it important to reinterpret anthropomorphic verses?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أيضا على إثبات العين لله تعالى: ما روي في الدعوات: «احفظنا بعينك التي لا تنام»، وأيضا يقال في العرف: عين الله عليك.
واعلم أن النصوص من القرآن لا يمكن إجراؤها على ظاهرها لوجوه:
الأول: إن ظاهر قوله تعالى: (ولتصنع على عيني) يقتضي أن يكون موسى عليه السلام مستقرا على تلك العين ملتصقا بها مستعليا عليها، وذلك لا يقوله عاقل.
الثاني: إن قوله تعالى: (واصنع الفلك بأعيننا) يقتضي أن تكون تلك الأعين آلة تلك الصنعة.
الثالث: إن إثبات الأعين في الوجه الواحد قبيح.
فثبت أنه لابد من المصير إلى التأويل، وذلك هو أن تحمل هذه الألفاظ على شدة العناية والحراسة. والوجه في حسن هذا المجاز: أن من عظمت عنايته بشيء وميله إليه ورغبته فيه كان كثير النظر إليه. فجعل لفظ العين التي هي آلة ذلك النظر كناية عن شدة العناية.
Also regarding proving the 'eye' of God Almighty: it is narrated in the supplications: 'Guard us with Your eye that does not sleep,' and it is also said customarily: 'the eye of God is upon you.' And know that the texts of the Quran cannot be taken according to their apparent meanings, for reasons: First: The apparent meaning of His statement (And that you be formed under My eye) requires that Moses, peace be upon him, be settled upon that eye, attached to it, and elevated over it, which no rational person would say. Second: His statement (And build the ark under Our eyes) requires that those eyes be the tool of that construction. Third: Affirming multiple eyes in one face is repugnant. So it is established that it is necessary to resort to figurative interpretation (ta'wil), and that is to interpret these expressions as indicating intense care and protection. The aspect of the goodness of this metaphor is that one whose care for something is great, and his inclination and desire towards it, he frequently looks at it. So the word 'eye,' which is the instrument of that looking, is made a metaphor for intense care.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of figurative interpretation (ta'wil) of Quranic verses that attribute an 'eye' (عين) to God. It begins by citing two pieces of evidence from established usage: a hadith in supplications asking for protection by God's 'eye that does not sleep', and the common Arabic expression 'the eye of God is upon you'. These show that 'eye' is conventionally understood as care and protection, not a physical organ. Then the author lists three rational arguments why the literal interpretation is impossible. First, the verse about Moses (20:39) 'formed under My eye' would imply Moses was physically located on God's eye, which is absurd. Second, the verse about Noah (11:37) 'build the ark under Our eyes' would mean the eyes themselves were the tools of construction, which is nonsensical. Third, affirming multiple eyes (plural 'أعيننا') in a single face is repulsive to reason. Therefore, one must resort to ta'wil: interpreting these expressions as indicating intense care (عناية) and protection. The metaphor is justified because a person who cares deeply for something looks at it frequently; thus the word 'eye', the instrument of looking, becomes a metaphor for intense care.

Key Terms
التأويل
المجاز
العناية
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Figurative interpretation of Quranic anthropomorphic verses is arbitrary and unnecessary.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that literal interpretation leads to rational absurdities, making ta'wil a logical necessity. The metaphor of 'eye' as care is grounded in the natural connection between looking and caring.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Because literal reading of God's 'eye' leads to rationally impossible conclusions, ta'wil is required, interpreting 'eye' as a metaphor for God's intense care and protection.

Resolving a Problematic Hadith about the Dajjal
Imam Razi critiques a problematic hadith about the Dajjal and defends it by suggesting missing context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi defends a problematic hadith by positing missing context, using the analogy of Aisha's critique of Ibn Umar's narration.

Core Idea
Problematic hadith can be reconciled by assuming missing context, not rejecting the text.

Imam Razi presents a challenging hadith that seems to compare God to the Dajjal. Rather than rejecting it, he offers a principle: the speech might have been preceded by other words that clarify the intended meaning. To illustrate, he invokes a famous incident involving Aisha and Ibn Umar. This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument unfold.
Pay attention to how Razi introduces the problem and then offers a solution.
2
Identify the problem and solution
The problem is that the hadith's apparent meaning is far-fetched (بعيد). Razi's solution is to assume the speech was preceded (مسبوق) by other words that would clarify the context.
3
Understand the analogy
Razi compares this to the famous incident where Aisha criticized Ibn Umar's narration about the dead being punished by weeping, arguing missing context. Recognize how this analogy strengthens his point.
This analogy shows that the method of assuming missing context is precedent in early Islamic scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما هذا الخبر الذي رويته فمشكل، لأن ظاهره يقتضي أن المطلوب إظهار الفرق بين الإله تعالى وبين الدجال يكون الدجال أعور وكون الله تعالى ليس بأعور، وذلك بعيد. وخير الواحد إذا بلغ هذه الدرجة أن يعتقد أن الكلام كان مسبوقا بكلام آخر لو ذكر لزال هذا الإشكال. أليس راوي هذا الحديث هو ابن عمر؟ ومن المشهور أن ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما لما روى قوله: إن الميت ليعذب ببكاء أهله، طعنت عائشة رضي الله عنها فيه وذكرت أن هذا الكلام من الرسول كان مسبوقا بكلام آخر واحتجت على ذلك بقوله تعالى: (ولا تزر وازرة وزر أخرى)، لو حكي لزال هذا الإشكال، فكذا ههنا. إنه من البعد صدور مثل هذا الكلام عن الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم الذي اصطفاه الله تعالى لرسالته وأمره ببيان شريعته. وبالله التوفيق.
As for this report that you narrated, it is problematic, because its apparent meaning requires that the intended thing is to show the difference between the divine God and the Dajjal: that the Dajjal is one-eyed and that God is not one-eyed, and that is far-fetched. And the best of one, when it reaches this degree, is to believe that the speech was preceded by another speech which, if mentioned, would remove this difficulty. Is not the narrator of this hadith Ibn Umar? And it is well-known that Ibn Umar, may God be pleased with him, when he narrated his statement: 'Verily the dead person is punished by the weeping of his family,' Aisha, may God be pleased with her, criticized it and mentioned that this speech from the Messenger was preceded by another speech, and she argued for that by His statement: (And no bearer of burdens bears the burden of another), if it had been mentioned, this difficulty would have been removed. So too here. It is far-fetched that such speech would issue from the Messenger, peace be upon him, whom God chose for His message and commanded to explain His law. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection concerning a problematic hadith that seems to compare God and the Dajjal by stating that the Dajjal is one-eyed while God is not one-eyed. The author finds this implication far-fetched (بعيد). He suggests the best course is to assume that the hadith was preceded (مسبوق) by another part of speech that would clarify the context and remove the difficulty. He then draws an analogy with a famous incident: Ibn Umar narrated a hadith that 'the dead person is punished by the weeping of his family'. Aisha criticized this narration, arguing that the Prophet's statement was preceded by another context (perhaps referring to a non-believer who was already being punished), and she supported her argument with Quran 35:18 ('no bearer of burdens bears the burden of another'). Had that preceding context been reported, the apparent contradiction would be resolved. Similarly, the hadith about the Dajjal likely had a missing preamble that would explain the intended distinction without implying any imperfection in God. The author concludes that it is very unlikely that the Prophet, whom God chose and commanded to explain His law, would utter such a problematic statement without clarifying context.

الإشكال
المسبوق بكلام آخر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this hadith is weak or fabricated and must be rejected.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the hadith can be reconciled by assuming missing context, following the precedent of Aisha's approach, without needing to reject the text outright.

Key Takeaway

Interpreting 'Nafs al-Rahman' in Hadith
Imam Razi explains the meaning of 'nafs al-Rahman' in two hadiths about wind.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the phrase 'nafs al-Rahman' in prophetic hadiths using linguistic derivation to preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
"Nafs al-Rahman" means relief from Allah, not a literal breath.

This lesson explores Imam Razi's interpretation of two hadiths that mention "the breath of the Most Merciful." Rather than taking these literally, Razi shows how the word "nafs" is derived from a root meaning relief and alleviation. Understanding this demonstrates the importance of ta'wil in defending God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadiths and identify the problematic term
Read the two hadiths carefully and notice the phrase 'nafs al-Rahman'. Ask yourself what might be problematic about it from a theological perspective.
This sets up the need for interpretation (ta'wil).
2
Understand linguistic derivation
Learn how Imam Razi traces 'nafs' to the root meaning 'to relieve' (nafas). Compare with the Arabic phrase 'nafastu 'an fulan' meaning 'I brought him relief'.
This is the key move in Razi's interpretation.
3
Connect wind to relief from discomfort
Reflect on how pleasant wind removes discomfort, and how the Prophet's experience of wind from Yemen was associated with support and victory.
Razi uses this to show that 'nafs' here means relief, not breath.
4
See how this upholds divine transcendence
Realize that by interpreting 'nafs' as relief, we avoid attributing a bodily attribute to Allah. This is a classic example of ta'wil in action.
This connects to the broader theme of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس عشر. هذا اللفظ غير وارد في القرآن، لكنه ورد عن النبي أنه قال: لا تسبوا الريح، فإنها من نفس الرحمن، وقال أيضا: إني لأجد نفس الرحمن من جانب اليمن، والتأويل: إنه مأخوذ من قوله: نفست عن فلان أى فرجت عنه ونفس الله عن فلان، أى فرج عنه، والريح إذا كانت طيبة، فقد زالت هذه المكاره، فلما وجدها من قبل اليمن فقد حصل المقصود. فالمقرون بالمكروه، مكروه، والمقرون بالمحبوب، محبوب. فلما وجد النبي النصرة من قبل اليمن، فقد وجد التنفس من المكروهات من ذلك الجانب، فلا جرم صدق قوله: إني لأجد نفس الرحمن من قبل اليمن، ولهذا قال النبي: الإيمان يمان والحكمة يمانية، وهذا هو المراد من قوله: إن الريح من نفس الرحمن، أي هي مما جعل الله فيها من التفريج والتنفيس. وبالله التوفيق.
Chapter Fifteen. This wording does not occur in the Quran, but it is reported from the Prophet that he said: 'Do not revile the wind, for it is from the breath (nafs) of the Most Merciful,' and he also said: 'Indeed I find the breath (nafs) of the Most Merciful from the direction of Yemen.' The interpretation: it is taken from his saying: 'I relieved (nafastu) so-and-so,' meaning I brought him relief, and 'Allah relieved (nafasa) so-and-so,' meaning He brought him relief. And the wind, when it is pleasant, these discomforts have gone. So when he found it from the direction of Yemen, the purpose was achieved. For what is associated with something disliked is disliked, and what is associated with something loved is loved. So when the Prophet found support from the direction of Yemen, he found relief from discomforts from that side. Hence his saying is true: 'Indeed I find the breath (nafs) of the Most Merciful from the direction of Yemen.' And for this reason the Prophet said: 'Faith is Yemeni and wisdom is Yemeni.' And this is the intended meaning of his saying: 'Indeed the wind is from the breath (nafs) of the Most Merciful,' i.e., it is something in which Allah has placed relief and alleviation. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation (ta'wil) of two hadiths: 'Do not revile the wind, for it is from the breath (nafs) of the Most Merciful' and 'I find the breath of the Most Merciful from the direction of Yemen.' The author (Imam Razi) explains that the word 'nafs' here does not indicate a literal divine attribute like breath, but is derived from the linguistic root meaning 'relief' (tafrij). He connects the pleasant wind from Yemen to the Prophet's victory and support from that direction. The interpretation preserves divine transcendence by avoiding anthropomorphism. The author also cites the hadith 'Faith is Yemeni and wisdom is Yemeni' to reinforce the connection. Thus, 'nafs al-Rahman' means the relief sent by Allah, not a literal breath.

لا تسبوا الريح
التفريج والتنفيس
التأويل

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Imam Razi explains that 'nafs' here is derived from the root meaning relief and alleviation, so it means the relief sent by Allah, preserving His transcendence and avoiding anthropomorphism.

C) As a reference to the angel of the wind.

Key Takeaway

Interpretation of the Hadith 'Nafs al-Rahman'
Imam Razi explains the linguistic interpretation of 'nafs' in hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the phrase 'nafs al-Rahman' in the hadith as a metaphor for divine relief, avoiding anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
In the hadith, 'nafs' means relief, not a literal breath of God.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses a potential misunderstanding: the word 'nafs' (soul/breath) when attributed to God in certain hadiths. He argues that it should be understood linguistically as 'relief' or 'comfort', not as a literal attribute. This interpretation safeguards God's transcendence while honoring the Prophetic words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the overall argument. Note that the word 'nafs' does not appear in the Quran, only in hadith.
2
Identify the linguistic root
Razi explains that 'nafs' comes from the root meaning 'to relieve' (nafastu 'an fulan). See how this root applies to the context of wind and victory from Yemen.
The root 'n-f-s' has connotations of relief and comfort.
3
Understand the application
Razi connects the linguistic meaning to the hadiths: the pleasant wind from Yemen brings relief, and the Prophet's victory from Yemen is a form of divine relief.
4
Reflect on the refutation of literalism
Consider how this interpretation refutes the idea that God has a literal breath or soul. The passage uses linguistic analysis to preserve tanzih (transcendence).
This is a classic kalam approach to problematic hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس عشر
في النفس
هذا اللفظ غير وارد في القرآن. لكنه ورد عن النبي أنه قال: «لا تسبوا الريح، فإنها من نفس الرحمن»، وقال أيضا: «إني لأجد نفس الرحمن من جانب اليمن». والتأويل: إنه مأخوذ من قوله: نفست عن فلان أي فرجت عنه، ونفس الله عن فلان أي فرج عنه. والريح إذا كانت طيبة، فقد زالت هذه المكاره. فلما وجدها من قبل اليمن فقد حصل المقصود. فالمقرون بالمكروه مكروه، والمقرون بالمحبوب محبوب. فلما وجد النبي النصرة من قبل اليمن، فقد وجد التنفس من المكروهات من ذلك الجانب، فلا جرم صدق قوله: «إني لأجد نفس الرحمن من قبل اليمن». ولهذا قال النبي: «الإيمان يمان والحكمة يمانية». وهذا هو المراد من قوله: إن الريح من نفس الرحمن، أي هي مما جعل الله فيها من التفريج والتنفيس. وبالله التوفيق.
Chapter Fifteen. On the 'nafs' (breath/soul). This word does not occur in the Qur'an. But it is related from the Prophet that he said: 'Do not revile the wind, for it is from the breath of the Merciful (nafs al-Rahman).' And he also said: 'Indeed I find the breath of the Merciful from the direction of Yemen.' The interpretation: it is taken from his saying: 'Nafastu 'an fulan' meaning I relieved him, and 'nafsa Allahu 'an fulan' meaning He relieved him. And the wind, when it is pleasant, these hardships have gone away. So when he found it from the direction of Yemen, the purpose was achieved. For that which is accompanied by hardship is disliked, and that which is accompanied by what is beloved is beloved. So when the Prophet found victory from the direction of Yemen, he found relief from hardships from that side. Hence his saying is true: 'I find the breath of the Merciful from the direction of Yemen.' And for this reason the Prophet said: 'Faith is Yemeni and wisdom is Yemeni.' And this is the intended meaning of his saying: 'The wind is from the breath of the Merciful,' i.e., it is something in which God has placed relief and comfort. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation of two hadiths that mention 'nafs al-Rahman' (the breath of the Merciful). Imam Razi begins by noting that the word 'nafs' (breath/soul) does not appear in the Qur'an, but it is found in the hadith. He then cites the hadiths: one prohibits reviling the wind because it is from the breath of the Merciful, and another states that the Prophet finds the breath of the Merciful from Yemen. To avoid a literal anthropomorphic interpretation, Razi provides a linguistic analysis: the root 'n-f-s' in Arabic can mean 'to relieve' or 'to comfort,' as in 'nafastu 'an fulan' (I relieved someone). Thus, 'nafs Allah' means God's relief. He applies this to the hadiths: when a pleasant wind comes from Yemen, it brings relief from hardships. The Prophet experienced victory from Yemen, which was a form of relief. Therefore, the hadiths refer to a metaphorical 'breath' meaning divine comfort and relief, not a physical attribute. The passage concludes by connecting this to the Prophet's saying that faith and wisdom are Yemeni, emphasizing the positive connotation.

نفس (nafs)
نفس الرحمن (nafs al-Rahman)
التفريج (al-tafrij)
التنفيس (al-tanfis)

Misconception: One might think 'nafs' in hadith implies a literal breath or soul for God.
Correction: Razi clarifies that 'nafs' is derived from the root meaning 'to relieve', so 'nafs al-Rahman' means the relief of the Merciful, not a literal breath.

Key Takeaway
The hadith phrase 'nafs al-Rahman' means divine relief, not a literal attribute, preserving God's transcendence.

God's Hand Above Their Hands: Power Not Limb
Razi interprets 'yad' in 48:10 as power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets 'Yad Allah fawqa aydihim' as a metaphor for God's dominant power.

Core Idea
God's 'hand' over ours means His power prevails.

Imam Razi now applies the principle that 'yad' in the Quran can be metaphorical. The first verse he addresses is from Surah al-Fath (48:10), where believers pledge allegiance. Razi explains that 'yad' here refers to God's power (qudrah) that dominates all created power, not a literal limb.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Notice how Razi states the interpretation directly without extensive proof.
He assumes the earlier principle that 'yad' can be metaphorical.
2
Understand the meaning
What does Razi mean by 'power dominating'? Reflect on the idea that God's power is superior to all created powers.
3
Connect to the overarching lesson
This verse is one example of how Razi consistently interprets 'yad' as a metaphor for attributes like power or blessing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة . فنقول : أما قوله تعالى : « يد الله فوق أيديهم (1)، فالمعنى : إن قدرة الله تعالى غالبة على قدرة الخلق . وأما قوله ما تلى حكاية عن اليهود - أنهم قالوا : د يد الله مغلولة (١٠) - فاليد ههنا بمعنى النعمة. والدليل عليه : أن اليهود إما أن يقال : إنهم مقرون بإثبات الخالق ، أو يقال : بأنهم منكرون له. فإن أقروا به ، امتنع أن نقول : إن خالق العالم جعل مغلولاً مقيداً (۱۱) . فإن ذلك لا يقوله عاقل . وإن أنكروه لم يكن للقول بكونه مخلولا فائدة . فثبت : أن المراد : أنهم كانوا يعتقدون أن نعم الله تعالى محبوسة عن الخلق ، ممنوعة عنهم، فصارت هذه الآية من أقوى الدلائل على أن لفظة اليد قد يراد بها النعمة .
Now that you know this introduction, we say: As for His saying, exalted: 'The hand of God is above their hands' (48:10), the meaning is that the power of God, exalted, dominates the power of creation. And as for His saying, which was recited as a story about the Jews – that they said: 'The hand of God is fettered' (5:64) – the 'hand' here means blessing (ni'mah). The proof for that is that the Jews: either it is said that they affirm the existence of the Creator, or it is said that they deny Him. If they affirm Him, it is impossible to say that the Creator of the world is fettered and bound, for no rational person would say that. And if they deny Him, there would be no benefit in saying that He is fettered. So it is established that the intended meaning is that they believed that the blessings of God, exalted, are withheld from creation, prevented from them. Thus this verse became one of the strongest proofs that the word 'hand' may be intended as blessing.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a verse-by-verse application of the metaphorical framework. The author first interprets 'Yad Allah fawqa aydihim' (48:10) as God's power (qudrah) overcoming the power of creatures. He does not provide a lengthy proof here, simply stating the meaning. Then he turns to the Jewish saying 'Yad Allah maghlulah' (5:64). He argues that the word 'hand' here must mean blessing (ni'mah), not a literal limb, by a two-part dilemma. If the Jews were theists, they could not mean a literal fettered hand for God because that is absurd. If they were atheists, calling God 'fettered' would have no point. So the only sensible interpretation is that they meant God's blessings are withheld. Thus this verse itself demonstrates that 'hand' can mean blessing. The author concludes that this is a strong proof for the metaphorical usage.

قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) God's literal hand is above human hands.
B) God's power dominates the power of creation.
C) God's hand is a metaphor for His essence.
D) The verse refers to a physical pledge.

Key Takeaway
Razi interprets 'yad' in 48:10 as a metaphor for God's dominant power, avoiding any literal hand.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
Do you ever act as if your success depends solely on your effort, forgetting that God's power is above yours? What practical step can you take today to acknowledge His supremacy in your life?

The Fettered Hand: Blessing Not Limb
Razi interprets 'yad' in 5:64 as blessing via a dilemma proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses a dilemma to prove that 'yad Allah maghlulah' means God's blessings are withheld.

Core Idea
God's hand being 'fettered' refers to His blessings being withheld.

The second verse comes from Surah al-Ma'idah (5:64), where the Jews are quoted as saying 'God's hand is fettered.' Razi presents a brilliant rational argument that the word 'hand' here must mean blessing (ni'mah), as a literal interpretation would be absurd whether the Jews believed in God or not.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the entire argument. Notice the two-part dilemma.
The proof is structured as 'either the Jews were theists or atheists.'
2
Understand the two scenarios
First, if the Jews affirmed a Creator, attributing literal fetters to God is impossible. Second, if they denied God, calling Him fettered has no point.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Since both literal readings fail, the word 'hand' must mean blessing, and 'fettered' means withheld.
Thus this verse becomes a strong proof that 'yad' can mean blessing.
