Razi's Two Proofs for the Metaphorical Meaning of 'Bal Yadahu Mabsutatani'
Razi gives two proofs that 'both His hands are outstretched' means abundant blessing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two proofs that the verse 'Bal yadahu mabsutatani' metaphorically refers to God's abundant blessings, not literal hands.

Core Idea
Context and transcendental purity force a metaphorical reading of God's 'hands.'

Imam Razi continues his method of interpreting anthropomorphic verses metaphorically. Here he tackles the phrase 'both His hands are outstretched' from Surah Al-Ma'idah, offering two compelling proofs that the intended meaning is the abundance of divine blessings. The first relies on the verse's context as a reply to the Jews' accusation, and the second on the absurdity of a literal reading.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the two proofs Razi presents.
This is a standard method for engaging with kalam texts.
2
Analyze the first proof
Examine the first proof based on contextual coherence. Understand how the reply must match the question about blessing.
The Jews' statement 'God's hand is fettered' was already interpreted as withholding blessings.
3
Analyze the second proof
Examine the reductio ad absurdum argument. See how literal interpretation leads to an impossible anthropomorphic image (spastic hands).
This is a key tool in kalam for rejecting literalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : « بل يداه مبسوطتان (۱۲) فالمراد منه أيضاً : النعمة . ويدل عليه وجهان : الأول : إن هذا ورد في معرض الجواب عن قول اليهود : « يد مغلولة ، ولما بينا بالدليل : أن قولهم « يد الله مغلولة ، ايس معناه الغل والحبس، بل معناه : احتباس نعم الله تعالى عنهم ، وجب أن يكون قوله : د بل يداه مبسوطتان ، عبارة عن كثرة نعم الله تعالى وشمولها للخلق حتى يكون الجواب مطابقاً للسؤال . والثاني : إن قوله : « بل يداه مبسوطتان ، لو حملناه على ظاهره ، لزم كون يديه مبسوطين ، مثل يد صاحب التشنيج (۱۳) ـ تعالى الله عنه - فثبت : أن المراد منه : إفاضة النعم .
And as for His saying, exalted: 'Nay, both His hands are outstretched' (5:64), the intended meaning is also blessing. Two points indicate this. The first: this came in the context of a response to the saying of the Jews: 'The hand is fettered.' And since we proved by evidence that their saying 'The hand of God is fettered' does not mean the fetter and confinement, but rather means the withholding of God's blessings from them, then His saying 'Nay, both His hands are outstretched' must be an expression of the abundance of God's blessings and their encompassing creation, so that the response matches the question. The second: His saying 'Nay, both His hands are outstretched' – if we took it literally, it would require that His two hands are outstretched like the hand of a person with a spastic condition (tashannuj) – exalted is God above that – so it is established that the intended meaning is the outpouring of blessings.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is a proof structure for interpreting 'Bal yadahu mabsutatani' (5:64) as metaphorically referring to blessing. The author gives two proofs. First, contextual coherence: the verse is a reply to the Jews who said 'God's hand is fettered,' which we already established means His blessings are withheld. So the reply 'both hands outstretched' must logically mean blessings are abundant and widespread. Second, reduction to absurdity: if taken literally, the verse would picture God with two outstretched hands, like a person suffering from a condition that causes limb extension (tashannuj). Since this is impossible for God, the literal meaning is false, so it must be metaphorical – specifically, the outpouring of blessings. This second proof relies on the principle that literal interpretation of divine attributes leads to anthropomorphism, which must be avoided.

Key Terms
يداه مبسوطتان
النعمة
التشنيج
إفاضة النعم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'yad' in this verse must be taken literally as a body part.
Correction: Razi shows that context (reply to Jews) and rational necessity (avoiding absurd anthropomorphism) rule out literalism.

Checkpoint
What does Razi give as a second proof that 'Bal yadahu mabsutatani' is metaphorical?
A) The verse is about charity
B) Literal interpretation would imply God has spastic hands
C) The Jews do not believe in God
D) The word 'yad' never means hand

Key Takeaway
Razi's dual proof method—contextual coherence and reductio ad absurdum—demonstrates that 'both hands outstretched' signifies the outpouring of divine blessings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses that describe God in physical terms, do you feel a tension in your heart? Are you quick to accept a metaphorical meaning, or do you cling to a literal image? How does Razi's method challenge you to let go of anthropomorphic ideas and trust God's transcendence?

Have you ever felt that God's generosity is 'fettered' in your life—that He is not giving you what you need? In light of this lesson, will you choose to see God's 'outstretched hands' of blessing even when you feel disappointed? What would change in your relationship with Him if you did?
?
The reductio ad absurdum argument exposes the absurdity of taking 'outstretched hands' literally. Are there any attributes of God that you still imagine in a human-like way? What would it mean for your worship to truly affirm that God is nothing like His creation? Will you ask Him tonight to purify your understanding of His perfection?

Interpreting 'Hand' (Yad) in the Quran: Power and Blessing
Razi interprets 'yad' in three Quranic verses as power or blessing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the word 'yad' (hand) in three Quranic verses to mean either power (qudrah) or blessing (ni'mah), based on rational and contextual evidence.

Core Idea
Allah's 'hand' is never literal; it signifies His power or His blessing.

Imam Razi builds on an earlier premise to explain the true meaning of 'yad' in verses that might be misunderstood. He shows that a literal reading leads to absurdities, while the figurative meanings of power and blessing fit the context perfectly. This lesson walks through his arguments for three key verses.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The passage begins by referencing a previously established premise. Though not quoted, it sets the stage for applying a metaphorical interpretation of 'yad'.
2
Analyze the first verse: power
Read Razi's interpretation of 'yad Allah fawqa aydihim' (Q. 48:10). He says it means Allah's power prevails.
The verse is often cited in a pledge of allegiance context.
3
Analyze the second verse: blessing
Examine the Jews' statement 'yad Allah maghlulah' (Q. 5:64). Razi argues 'hand' here means blessing, not a literal fettered hand, using a rational proof.
He uses a dilemma: if Jews affirm God, they can't say He is fettered; if they deny Him, the claim is pointless.
4
Analyze the third verse: blessing again
Look at 'bal yadahu mabsutatani' (also Q. 5:64). Razi presents two proofs that it means abundant blessings.
The first proof is contextual correspondence; the second is the absurdity of literalism.
5
Synthesize the lesson
Step back and see how Razi consistently defends divine transcendence by interpreting 'yad' metaphorically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة. فنقول: أما قوله تعالى: «يد الله فوق أيديهم» فالمعنى: إن قدرة الله تعالى غالبة على قدرة الخلق. وأما قوله ما تلى حكاية عن اليهود أنهم قالوا: «يد الله مغلولة» فاليد ههنا بمعنى النعمة. والدليل عليه: أن اليهود إما أن يقال: إنهم مقرون بإثبات الخالق، أو يقال: بأنهم منكرون له. فإن أقروا به، امتنع أن نقول: إن خالق العالم جعل مغلولا مقيدا. فإن ذلك لا يقوله عاقل. وإن أنكروه لم يكن للقول بكونه مغلولا فائدة. فثبت: أن المراد: أنهم كانوا يعتقدون أن نعم الله تعالى محبوسة عن الخلق، ممنوعة عنهم، فصارت هذه الآية من أقوى الدلائل على أن لفظة اليد قد يراد بها النعمة. وأما قوله تعالى: «بل يداه مبسوطتان» فالمراد منه أيضا: النعمة. ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول: إن هذا ورد في معرض الجواب عن قول اليهود: «يد مغلولة»، ولما بينا بالدليل: أن قولهم «يد الله مغلولة» ليس معناه الغل والحبس، بل معناه: احتباس نعم الله تعالى عنهم، وجب أن يكون قوله: «بل يداه مبسوطتان» عبارة عن كثرة نعم الله تعالى وشمولها للخلق حتى يكون الجواب مطابقا للسؤال. والثاني: إن قوله: «بل يداه مبسوطتان» لو حملناه على ظاهره، لزم كون يديه مبسوطين، مثل يد صاحب التشنيج - تعالى الله عنه - فثبت: أن المراد منه: إفاضة النعم.
Once you have understood this premise, we say: As for His statement, the Exalted: 'The hand of Allah is above their hands' – the meaning is: the power of Allah, the Exalted, prevails over the power of creation. As for His statement which follows as a narration from the Jews that they said: 'The hand of Allah is fettered' – so 'hand' here is in the sense of blessing. The evidence for it is that the Jews: either it is said that they affirm the existence of the Creator, or it is said that they deny Him. If they affirm Him, it is impossible that we say: the Creator of the world is made fettered, bound. For no rational person would say that. And if they deny Him, there is no benefit in saying that He is fettered. So it is established that the intended meaning is that they believed that the blessings of Allah, the Exalted, are withheld from creation, prevented from them. Thus this verse becomes among the strongest evidences that the word 'hand' can be intended to mean blessing. And as for His statement, the Exalted: 'Nay, both His hands are outstretched' – the intended meaning from it is also blessing. Two aspects indicate it: The first: This came in the context of a response to the statement of the Jews: 'Hand is fettered' – and when we demonstrated through evidence that their statement 'The hand of Allah is fettered' is not its meaning shackling and confinement, but its meaning is the withholding of the blessings of Allah, the Exalted, from them, then it is necessary that His statement: 'Nay, both His hands are outstretched' be an expression of the abundance of the blessings of Allah, the Exalted, and their encompassment of creation so that the response corresponds to the question. The second: His statement: 'Nay, both His hands are outstretched' if we were to take it upon its apparent meaning, it would necessitate that His two hands be outstretched like the hand of one suffering from tetanus – exalted is Allah above that. So it is established that the intended meaning from it is the outpouring of blessings.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This passage is an interpretative argument. The author builds on a previously established premise (muqaddimah) to explain the meaning of 'yad' in three Quranic verses. First, he interprets 'yad Allah fawqa aydihim' (Q. 48:10) as meaning that Allah's power (qudrah) prevails over the power of creatures. Second, he addresses the Jews' statement 'yad Allah maghlulah' (Q. 5:64) and argues that 'hand' here means blessing (ni'mah), not a literal hand. He provides a rational proof: if the Jews affirm God, they cannot claim He is literally fettered; if they deny God, the claim is meaningless. Thus 'hand' must refer to blessings withheld. Third, he interprets 'bal yadahu mabsutatani' (Q. 5:64) also as blessing, with two proofs. The first proof is contextual: the verse responds to the Jews' claim of a 'fettered hand', so 'outstretched hands' must mean abundant blessings. The second proof is absurdity: taking 'outstretched hands' literally implies a physical deformity (like tetanus), which is impossible for Allah. The conclusion is that 'yad' metaphorically denotes the outpouring of blessings.

قدرة
نعمة
مقدمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'yad' (hand) in the Quran always refers to a literal, physical hand when attributed to Allah.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that in certain verses, 'yad' must be interpreted metaphorically as power or blessing, based on rational necessity and contextual coherence. A literal reading leads to theologically unacceptable conclusions.

Checkpoint

B) Blessing (ni'mah)
C) A literal hand
D) Both power and blessing

Key Takeaway
The word 'yad' in the Quran, when attributed to Allah, must be interpreted metaphorically to uphold divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Will you commit now to checking your own interpretations against the principles of tanzih before you recite or hear these verses?

Rebuttal of Special Honor Argument
Razi refutes the first literalist argument using Quranic counterexample and metaphor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that the phrase 'with My two hands' in Adam's creation implies special honor over other creatures by providing a counterexample and a metaphorical interpretation.

Core Idea
God's care, not literal hands, is the real meaning of 'with My two hands'.

Imam Razi addresses those who take the phrase 'with My two hands' literally to mean physical hands. Their first argument is that this phrase gives Adam special honor over other creatures. But Razi points out that the Quran also describes animals as created 'with Our hands', which would make them even more honored if taken literally. He then offers a metaphorical interpretation: 'two hands' signifies intense divine care.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Razi structures his rebuttal.
The passage is dense but follows a clear logical flow.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent argues that 'with My two hands' proves special honor for Adam. Find that claim in the text.
3
Understand Razi's counterexample
Razi cites the creation of animals with 'Our hands' from the Quran. How does that undermine the opponent's logic?
If literal hands mean honor, animals would be more honored.
4
Grasp the metaphorical explanation
Razi explains that 'two hands' is like a human saying 'I did it with my own hand' to emphasize care. This is the key insight.
The metaphor highlights God's special attention to Adam's creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن القول الأول : أما ما تمسكوا به أولا: فهو أنه لو كان تخليق آدم باليدين يوجب مزيد الاصطفاء، لكان تخليق البهائم والأنعام بالأيدي، يوجب رجحانها على آدم، في هذه الاصطفاء لقوله تعالى في صفة تخليقها: {مما عملت أيدينا أنعامًا} فهم لها ما ليكون، ثم نقول: لم لا يجوز أن يكون معنى قوله تعالى: {خلقت بيدي}، هو بيان الكثرة عناية الله تعالى في إيجاده وتكوينه. فإن الإنسان إذا أراد المبالغة في إصلاح بعض المهمات، وفي تكميله، فقد يقول: هذا الشيء أعملته بيدي. ومن المعلوم أن التخليق بغير هذا النوع من العناية، ما كان حاصلا في حق غير آدم عليه السلام. والجواب عما تمسكوا به ثانياً: إن التثنية لا تدل على حصول العدد، بدليل قوله تعالى: {فقدموا بين يدي نجواكم صدقة} وقوله: {يدي رحمته}. والجواب عما تمسكوا به ثالثاً: إن التخصيص بالذكر هنا، لم يدل على نفي حكمه عما عداه؛ لكنا بينا أن التخليق باليدين: عبارة من التخليق المخصوص بمزيد الكرامات والتشريفات. وهذا المجموع ما كان حاصلا في حق غير آدم عليه السلام.
And the answer to the first statement: As for what they clung to firstly, it is that if the creation of Adam with the two hands necessitated an increase of preference, then the creation of beasts and cattle with the hands would necessitate their superiority over Adam in this preference, because of His saying, may He be exalted, describing their creation: {From what Our hands have made} cattle, and they are submissive to them. Then we say: Why is it not permissible that the meaning of His saying, may He be exalted: {I created with My two hands} is the expression of the abundance of Allah's care in bringing him into existence and forming him? For when a person intends to exaggerate in rectifying some important matters and perfecting them, he may say: 'I have worked this thing with my own hand.' And it is known that creation without this type of care was not obtained in the case of other than Adam, peace be upon him. And the answer to what they clung to secondly: That the dual form does not indicate occurrence of number, as evidenced by His saying, may He be exalted: {And put forward before your hands (baina yaday) of your charity} and His saying: {Hands (yaday) of His mercy}. And the answer to what they clung to thirdly: That specification in mention here does not necessitate negation of its ruling from what is other than it; for we have shown that creation with the two hands is an expression of creation specifically characterized by extra honors and dignities. And this totality was not obtained in the case of other than Adam, peace be upon him.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage contains Imam Razi's rebuttal of three arguments put forward by those who insist on a literal interpretation of the 'two hands' in the creation of Adam. The author addresses each argument in order. First, he refutes the claim that the mention of 'two hands' indicates a special honor for Adam over other creatures by pointing out that animals are also described as created 'with Our hands' in the Quran, which if taken literally would give them even greater honor. He then offers an alternative interpretation: the phrase 'with My two hands' is a metaphor for God's intense care and attention in creating Adam, similar to a human saying 'I did this with my own hand' to emphasize personal effort. Second, he addresses the argument that the dual form implies two literal hands. He cites Quranic examples where 'yaday' (two hands) appears but clearly does not mean two hands, such as 'between your hands' (meaning 'before') and 'hands of His mercy'. Thus, the dual does not necessarily indicate number. Third, he refutes the claim that specifying Adam for creation 'with hands' excludes others, explaining that the specification is for a particular type of creation characterized by extra honor, which was not given to others. Thus, he defends the metaphorical interpretation while preserving the honor of Adam.

اصطفاء
عناية
تخليق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'with My two hands' in the Quran always refers to literal physical hands.
Correction: Imam Razi shows through Quranic counterexample and metaphorical interpretation that it signifies God's special care, not literal hands.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, what does the phrase 'with My two hands' in the creation of Adam primarily indicate?
A) That Allah has two physical hands
B) That Adam is honored more than animals
C) The abundance of Allah's care and attention in creating Adam
D) That creation of Adam required two agents

Key Takeaway
Razi demonstrates that 'two hands' is a metaphor for God's special care, not a literal attribute.

Rebuttal of Dual Form Argument
Razi refutes the argument from dual form using Quranic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dual form 'yadayn' does not necessarily indicate two literal hands, as shown by Imam Razi's Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
The dual form can be metaphorical; it does not always indicate number.

The second argument for literal hands relies on the dual form of the word 'hand' in the verse. Opponents claim that the dual number indicates two hands. Razi refutes this by citing other Quranic verses where 'yaday' appears but cannot mean two literal hands, such as 'between your hands' (i.e. before) and 'hands of His mercy'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the short Arabic passage and its translation. Note Razi's concise refutation.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent argues that the dual form 'yadayn' in 'bicideyya' proves two hands. Understand this claim.
3
Examine Razi's evidence
Razi cites two Quranic examples where 'yaday' appears: 'baina yaday' (between your hands) and 'yaday rahmatihi' (hands of His mercy). How do these show that the dual does not always mean two?
In both cases, the meaning is figurative, not literal two hands.
4
Draw the conclusion
Conclude that the dual form alone does not prove literal hands; context determines meaning.

Rebuttal of Specification Argument
Razi refutes the argument from specification using the concept of special creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why specifying Adam for creation 'with two hands' does not negate the possibility of a metaphorical interpretation for others.

Core Idea
Specification in the Quran indicates a special type of creation, not literal hands.

The third literalist argument claims that because the Quran specifically mentions Adam being created 'with My two hands', this implies a unique status that cannot be applied to others. Razi responds that specification does not negate the same ruling from others; rather, the phrase refers to a particular type of creation characterized by extra honors, which indeed was not given to any other creature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read Razi's third rebuttal. It is short but decisive.
2
Identify the opponent's argument
The opponent argues that the specification of Adam for 'two hands' proves that others are excluded from that attribute.
3
Understand Razi's response
Razi explains that specification does not negate the ruling from others; rather, the phrase is about a special type of creation with extra honors that only Adam received.
The 'two hands' is a description of the kind of creation, not a literal attribute.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that this special creation was not obtained by anyone else, preserving Adam's honor without literalism.

Interpreting Hadiths About God's Hand
Razi interprets hadiths about God's hand metaphorically

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets hadiths that mention God's hand as metaphorical, preserving divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God's hand in hadiths is a metaphor for honor, power, or mercy, not a limb.

Imam Razi addresses several prophetic hadiths that describe God having a 'hand.' To uphold God's transcendence and avoid anthropomorphism, he explains that these expressions must be understood metaphorically. In this passage, he lists the hadiths, gives interpretations, and provides rational proofs for why a literal reading is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadiths and their interpretations
First, read through the hadiths Razi cites and his metaphorical explanations for each. Notice how 'hand' is interpreted differently based on context.
Razi does not change the meaning of the hadiths; he uncovers their intended meaning.
2
Examine the rational proofs
Razi gives two arguments from the charity hadith to show a literal hand is impossible. Identify these arguments and understand their logic.
The first argument relies on observable reality; the second on textual consistency.
3
Reflect on the principle of ta'wil
Consider why metaphorical interpretation is necessary to maintain God's transcendence and avoid contradicting reason and other texts.
This connects to the broader Islamic theological commitment to tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الأحاديث، فنقول: أما قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: خلق آدم بيده، وكتب التوراة بيده، فذلك حق يدل على أن المراد: التخصيص بمزيد الكرامات. وكذا قوله: كتب بيده على نفسه أن رحمتي سبقت غضبي، وأما قوله: إن الله يفتح أبواب السماء في ثلث الليل الباقي فيبسط يده، فالمراد: إفاضة النعمة، وإيصال الرحمة والمغفرة إلى المحتاجين. وأما قوله: الصدقة تقع في يد الرحمن، فالمراد منه: شدة العناية بقبول تلك الصدقات، وتكثير الثواب عليها. وكذا المراد بقوله عليه السلام: خمر طينة آدم بيده، وأما قوله عليه السلام: «والذي نفسي بيده»، فالمراد باليد هنا: القدرة. والذي يدل على أن هذه الألفاظ يجب تأويلها: أن قوله الصدقة تقع في يد الرحمن، ليس المراد منه اليد بمعنى العضو والجارحة. ويدل عليه وجوه: الأول: إنا نشاهد أن تلك الصدقة ما وقعت إلا في يد الفقير. فالقول بأنها وقعت في يد أخرى، في عضو مركب من الأجزاء والأبعاض، مع أنا لا نراها ولا نحس بها، تشكيك في الضروريات. الثاني: هذا يقتضي أن تكون يد الله ظرفاً لصدقات العباد. وذلك على خلاف ظاهر قوله تعالى: {بل يداه مبسوطتان}.
As for the hadiths, we say: As for his saying, may God’s peace and blessings be upon him: 'He created Adam with His hand, and wrote the Torah with His hand,' that is true and indicates that what is meant is specification with extra honors. Likewise his saying: 'He wrote with His hand upon Himself that My mercy precedes My anger.' And as for his saying: 'Indeed God opens the gates of heaven in the last third of the night and spreads out His hand,' what is meant is the outpouring of grace, and conveying mercy and forgiveness to the needy. And as for his saying: 'Charity falls into the hand of the Merciful,' what is meant from it is the intensity of care in accepting those charities and multiplying the reward upon them. And likewise what is meant by his saying, peace be upon him: 'He kneaded the clay of Adam with His hand.' And as for his saying, peace be upon him: 'By Whose hand my soul is,' what is meant by 'hand' here is power. And what indicates that these expressions must be interpreted is that his saying 'Charity falls into the hand of the Merciful' is not intended by it the hand in the sense of a limb and organ. And it is indicated by two aspects: The first: Indeed we witness that that charity fell only into the hand of the poor. So the statement that it fell into another hand, a limb composed of parts and members, while we do not see it nor perceive it, is casting doubt on self-evident matters. The second: This would necessitate that the hand of God be a container for the charities of the servants. And that is contrary to the apparent meaning of His saying, may He be exalted: {But both His hands are outstretched}.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation of several prophetic hadiths that mention God's 'hand'. Imam Razi shows that they must be understood metaphorically. He begins by listing various hadiths: 'He created Adam with His hand,' 'He wrote the Torah with His hand,' 'He wrote with His hand upon Himself that My mercy precedes My anger,' 'He spreads out His hand in the last third of the night,' 'Charity falls into the hand of the Merciful,' 'He kneaded the clay of Adam with His hand,' and 'By Whose hand my soul is.' For each, he provides a metaphorical explanation: 'hand' refers to honor, care, power, or mercy depending on context. He then gives two rational proofs from the hadith about charity. First, we observe that charity physically falls into the hand of the poor person, not into some unseen limb of God. Claiming it falls into an invisible physical hand contradicts what we witness directly, undermining self-evident knowledge. Second, if God's hand were a literal limb, it would become a container for charity, which contradicts the Quranic verse 'But both His hands are outstretched' which implies giving, not containing. Thus, these texts must be interpreted metaphorically to preserve God's transcendence.

Key Terms
يد
تأويل
الصدقة
قدرة
رحمت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that hadiths mentioning God's hand must be taken literally as a physical limb.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that reason and other texts require metaphorical interpretation to avoid contradicting empirical observation and to uphold God's transcendence.

Checkpoint
What is the first rational argument Imam Razi gives to show that 'hand' in the hadith about charity is not literal?
A) The word 'hand' in Arabic always means power
B) The charity is seen to fall into the poor person's hand, not into an invisible limb
C) The Quran says God has no hands
D) The hadith is weak

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that hadiths mentioning God's hand must be interpreted metaphorically to preserve God's transcendence and avoid contradiction with reason and scripture.

?

If you truly internalized that God Himself receives your charity with intense care and multiplies the reward, how would your attitude towards giving change? What specific change in your giving habits will you commit to starting this week?

Refuting Literal Hands: Three Arguments
Imam Razi refutes literal hands with three rational arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the scriptural references to Allah's hands cannot be taken literally, and the three specific rational arguments he provides.

Core Idea
Allah's hands are not literal limbs; the Quranic and hadith language is metaphorical.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents the anthropomorphist evidence and then dismantles it with three powerful arguments. Before we read, recall that earlier discussions have already established that Allah is not a body. Now Razi shows that even if we consider the scriptural texts alone, a literal reading leads to absurd consequences. Let us follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Review the anthropomorphist evidence
Read the Quranic verses and the hadith that literalists use. Notice how Razi presents them neutrally before refuting.
The hadith is from Ibn Khuzaymah; note its key elements: clenched hands, choosing right hand, both hands are right.
2
Examine the first argument
The hadith says both of His hands are right. If literal limbs, having two right hands is an ugly distortion. Understand why this impossibility forces a metaphorical reading.
3
Study the second and third arguments
Second argument: if one hand were insufficient, that implies deficiency; if sufficient, the other is redundant. Third argument: the interaction resembles children playing, unbecoming of Allah. See how each leads to absurdity.
Razi concludes: the only valid interpretation is metaphorical, meaning extreme care and protection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثامن عشر في ما تمسكوا به في إثبات اليدين لله عز وجل. احتجوا بالقرآن والأخبار. أما القرآن: فقوله تعالى: (ما منعك أن تسجد لما خلقت بيدي) وقوله تعالى: (بل يداه مبسوطتان). وأما الخبر: فما روى ابن خزيمة عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم: «لما خلق الله آدم ونفخ فيه عطس. فقال: الحمد لله، فحمد الله بإذن الله. فقال له ربه: يرحمك ربك يا آدم. ثم قال له: يا آدم اذهب إلى الملائكة فقل: السلام عليكم. فلما ذهب وقال قالوا: عليكم السلام ورحمة الله. ثم رجع إلى ربه. فقال: هذه تحيتك وتحية بنيك. فقال الله تعالى - ويداه مقبوضتان - اختر أيهما شئت. فقال: اخترت يمين ربي - وكلتا يديه يمين مباركة - ثم بسطها فإذا فيها آدم وذريته. فقال: أي رب ما هؤلاء؟ فقال: هؤلاء ذريتك، فإذا كل إنسان مكتوب عمره بين عينيه». واعلم أن هذا الحديث طويل ومقصودنا هنا هذا القدر. وقد عرفت أنه لا يمكن حمل لفظ اليد في حق الله تعالى على الجارحة. وتدل ههنا وجوه أخرى: الأول: إن ظاهر الحديث يدل على أن كلتا يديه يمين. واليدين بمعنى الجارحة إذا كانت كلتاهما يمينا كان ذلك في غاية القبح وتشويه الخلقة - تعالى الله عن ذلك علوا كبيرا. والثاني: إن إحدى اليدين إذا كانت غير وافية بالعمل كانت ناقصة وجل إلهنا عنه، وإن كانت وافية بالعمل كانت ناقصة وذلك يوجب نقصانا في الصورة. والثالث: إن ظاهر الخبر الذي رويناه يدل على أنه كان يلعب مع آدم عليه السلام كما يلعب الصبيان بعضهم مع بعض، حين يقبضون أيديهم على الزوج والفرد. والصبيان إذا فعلوا ذلك ضربهم المعلم وأدبهم. فكيف ينسب ذلك إلى رب العالمين وأحكم الحاكمين؟ فثبت أنه يجب حمل ذلك على المبالغة في الحفظ والحراسة وشدة العناية. وبالله التوفيق.
The eighteenth chapter: Concerning what they adhere to in proving two hands for Allah, Mighty and Majestic. They argued with the Qur'an and the reports. As for the Qur'an: His statement, the Exalted: 'What prevented you from prostrating to what I created with My two hands?' and His statement, the Exalted: 'Nay, both His hands are outstretched.' As for the report: Ibn Khuzaymah narrated from Abu Hurayrah, may Allah be pleased with him, who said: The Messenger of Allah, peace and blessings be upon him and his family, said: 'When Allah created Adam and breathed into him, he sneezed. He said: 'Praise be to Allah,' so he praised Allah by Allah's permission. His Lord said to him: 'May your Lord have mercy on you, O Adam.' Then He said to him: 'O Adam, go to the angels and say: Peace be upon you.' When he went and said, they said: 'Peace be upon you and the mercy of Allah.' Then he returned to his Lord. He said: 'This is your greeting and the greeting of your children.' Then Allah, the Exalted - and His hands were clenched - said: 'Choose whichever you wish.' He said: 'I choose the right hand of my Lord - and both of His hands are a blessed right hand.' Then He spread them, and lo, there were Adam and his offspring. He said: 'O Lord, who are these?' He said: 'These are your offspring, and behold, every human has his lifespan written between his eyes.' Know that this hadith is long, and our purpose here is this amount. And you have known that it is impossible to apply the word 'hand' to Allah, the Exalted, in the sense of a limb. And there are other proofs here: The first: The apparent meaning of the hadith indicates that both of His hands are right. If 'two hands' in the sense of a limb, when both are right, that would be extremely ugly and a distortion of creation - exalted is Allah above that, a great exaltation. The second: If one of the two hands is insufficient for action, it would be deficient, and our God is exalted above that; and if it is sufficient for action, it would be deficient, and that necessitates a deficiency in the form. The third: The apparent meaning of the report we narrated indicates that He was playing with Adam, peace be upon him, as children play with each other when they clench their hands on even and odd. When children do that, the teacher hits them and disciplines them. So how can that be attributed to the Lord of the worlds and the most just of judges? So it is established that this must be interpreted metaphorically as extreme preservation, protection, and intense care. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the literal interpretation of Allah's hands. The author, Imam Razi, first presents the evidence that anthropomorphists use to assert that Allah has literal hands: two Quranic verses (38:75 and 5:64) and a hadith from Ibn Khuzaymah about Adam's creation. He then states that it is already known (from previous discussion) that the word 'hand' cannot be applied to Allah as a physical limb (جارحة). He then provides three additional arguments (وجوه) to further demonstrate the impossibility of a literal reading.

The first argument: The hadith itself states that both of His hands are right hands (يمين). If 'hands' were limbs, having both be right would be monstrously ugly, which is impossible for Allah.

The second argument: If one hand were insufficient for action, that would imply deficiency; if it were sufficient, then the other would be redundant, also implying deficiency. Either way, a literal interpretation leads to an imperfection in form, which is impossible for Allah.

The third argument: The apparent meaning of the hadith depicts Allah playing a guessing game with Adam, like children do. Such behavior is inappropriate for Allah, the most wise. Therefore, the only valid interpretation is that 'hands' here is a metaphor (مبالغة) for extreme care, protection, and attention.

Thus, the author concludes that the scriptural evidence must be interpreted metaphorically, not literally.

Key Terms
يد
جارحة
يمين
مبالغة
تشويه الخلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah has literal physical hands like humans because the Quran and hadith mention 'hands'.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that a literal reading leads to absurd consequences (ugly distortion, deficiency, unbecoming behavior), so the texts must be interpreted metaphorically as referring to Allah's care and power.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument in this passage, what does the hadith's description of Allah's interaction with Adam resemble?
A) A king commanding his subjects
B) Children playing a game with clenched fists
C) A teacher testing a student
D) A father blessing his son

Key Takeaway
The scriptural references to Allah's hands must be interpreted metaphorically to avoid absurdities that contradict divine perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah as having a body or limbs, even in a vague way? What will you do from now on to guard your mind against such thoughts whenever you read verses or hadith about Allah's attributes?
?
When you hear someone describe Allah in human terms, do you feel uncomfortable enough to correct them, or do you stay silent to avoid conflict? How will you balance kindness with the duty to protect Allah's transcendence after learning Razi's arguments?
?
If you knew that a literal belief in Allah's hands leads to attributing deformity or deficiency to Allah, would you still hold onto that belief out of attachment to the apparent meaning of scripture? What step will you take today to embrace the metaphorical interpretation that the scholars have agreed upon?

First Argument: Both Hands Right Entails Deformity
First argument from the hadith 'both hands are right'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpreting 'hand' as a literal limb leads to the absurd consequence of both hands being 'right' hands, implying deformity, which is impossible for Allah.

Core Idea
A literal 'right hand' for both hands would be a deformity, impossible for Allah.

Imam Razi opens his first rational argument by citing a hadith that describes both of Allah's hands as 'right' (yamin). If these were literal limbs, both being right would be an extreme ugliness and deformity of creation. Since Allah is absolutely perfect, such a description cannot be literal; it must be metaphorical.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully. Note the strong language: 'utmost ugliness and deformity of creation'.
The passage begins by restating that 'hand' cannot be a literal limb.
2
Understand the argument
The hadith says 'both His hands are right'. Literally, a right hand is one of two complementary types; having both right would be a deformity.
This shows that literalism leads to absurdity.
3
Reflect on divine perfection
Allah is exalted above any imperfection. Therefore, the literal meaning is impossible, and 'hand' must be interpreted metaphorically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عرفت : أنه لا يمكن حمل لفظ اليد فى حق الله تعالى على الجارحة . وتدل ههنا وجوه أخرى :
فالأول : إن ظاهر الحديث يدل على أن كلتا يديه يمين . واليدين بمعنى الجارحة ، إذا كانت كلتاهما يمينا ، كان ذلك في غاية القبح ، وتشويه الخلقة - تعالى الله عن ذلك علوا كبيرا -
والثاني : إن إحدى اليدين إذا كانت غير وافية بالعمل ، كانت ناقصة وجل إلهنا عنه ، و إن كانت وافية بالعمل كانت ناقصة) (۸) وذلك يوجب نقصانا في الصورة .
والثالث : إن ظاهر الخبر الذي رويناه ، يدل على أنه كان يلعب مع آدم (علیه السلام) (۹) كما يلعب الصبيان بعضهم مع بعض، حين(١٠) يقبضون أيديهم على الزوج والفرد . والصبيان إذا فعلوا ذلك ضربهم المعلم و أدبهم . فكيف ينسب ذلك إلى رب العالمين ، وأحكم الحاكمين ؟ فثبت : أنه بيجب حمل ذلك على المبالغة (۱۱) في الحفظ والحراسة ، وشدة العناية
) وبالله التوفيق )
You have known: It is impossible to apply the word 'hand' (yad) in the case of Allah Most High to a limb (jarḥah). And other proofs indicate here: The first: The apparent meaning of the hadith indicates that both His hands are right (yamin). If both hands, in the sense of a limb, were both right, that would be utmost ugliness and deformity of creation—exalted is Allah above that with great exaltation. The second: If one of the two hands were insufficient for the work, it would be deficient—and may our God be exalted above that—and if it were sufficient for the work, it would be deficient? (8) and that necessitates a deficiency in the form. The third: The apparent meaning of the report we narrated indicates that He was playing with Adam (peace be upon him) (9) as boys play with each other, when (10) they grasp their hands on the odd and even. And if boys do that, the teacher strikes and disciplines them. So how can that be attributed to the Lord of the worlds and the wisest of judges? So it is established that it must be interpreted as hyperbole (11) in preservation and guarding and intensity of care. And with Allah is success.

Commentary
This passage presents three rational arguments that the 'hand' (yad) attributed to Allah cannot be a literal physical limb. The first argument: The hadith says both of Allah's hands are 'right' (yamin). If they were literal hands, having both be right would be a deformity, which is impossible for Allah. The second argument: If one hand were insufficient for work, it would be deficient; if it were sufficient, the other would be superfluous, also implying deficiency—both are impossible. The third argument: The hadith about playing with Adam as children play would imply unbecoming behavior; such play is punished in children, so it cannot be attributed to Allah. Therefore, the hand must be interpreted metaphorically as power or care.

Second Argument: Either Deficiency or Superfluity
Second argument: dilemma of insufficiency or superfluity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument: if Allah's hand were a literal limb, either one hand would be insufficient (deficiency) or both would be sufficient yet one would be superfluous (also deficiency), both impossible for Allah.

Core Idea
Literal hands lead to either insufficiency or redundancy, both implying deficiency.

Imam Razi's second argument examines the functional necessity of two hands. If one hand were insufficient for the work, it would be deficient. If one hand were sufficient, the other hand would be useless, also implying deficiency. Both scenarios are impossible for Allah, so the hand must be metaphorical.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the two conditional scenarios: insufficiency or sufficiency.
2
Analyze the dilemma
If one hand is not enough for the work, that hand is deficient. If one hand is enough, the other is superfluous, which also implies deficiency in the form.
Either way, deficiency arises.
3
Connect to divine transcendence
Allah is free from any deficiency. Therefore, literal hand is impossible.

Source Text

Third Argument: Playing with Adam Would Be Unbecoming
Third argument: hadith about playing with Adam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument: the hadith about Allah playing with Adam like children is impossible literally, as such behavior is reprimanded in children, so it must be metaphorical.

Core Idea
Literal play attributed to Allah is as unbecoming as children's play that gets punished.

Imam Razi's third argument cites a narration describing Allah playing with Adam, grasping hands over odd and even numbers, as children do. If taken literally, this would imply frivolity, which is punished in children. Since Allah is the wisest of judges, such an action cannot be literal. Therefore, it must be interpreted as hyperbole for protection and care.

Guided Path
1
Read the third argument
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the comparison to children's play and the teacher's discipline.
2
Understand the analogy
The hadith describes Allah playing with Adam as children play. Children who do such play get disciplined by the teacher.
3
Apply the principle of divine perfection
Attributing such play to Allah would imply behavior that is reprimanded in humans, which is impossible. So the hadith must be interpreted metaphorically as hyperbole for preservation.
The conclusion is that 'hand' here means protection and care.

Source Text

Interpreting 'Yamin' as Power and Strength
Imam Razi explains that 'yamin' (right hand) is a metaphor for divine power and strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'yamin' (right hand) in revelation is a metaphorical expression for divine power and strength, not a literal body part.

Core Idea
'Yamin' (right hand) is a metaphor for power, not a body part.

Imam Razi addresses a common misunderstanding: when the Quran and Hadith mention Allah's 'right hand,' they do not imply a physical hand. Instead, 'yamin' is an Arabic expression for power and strength. In this lesson, we will examine his linguistic and textual evidence for this interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic source passage and its English translation. Notice the hadith about charity and Razi's commentary.
Pay attention to the structure: hadith, claim, evidence, application.
2
Understand the core claim and linguistic evidence
Focus on Razi's statement: 'al-yamin is an expression for power and strength.' Then review the evidence: the right side is called 'yamin' because it is stronger, and even the opposite can be called 'yamin' because it strengthens resolve.
This linguistic analysis shows the metaphorical basis.
3
Apply to Quranic verses and hadith
See how Razi applies this interpretation to two Quranic verses: 'the heavens are folded in His right hand' and 'We would have seized him by the right hand.' Understand the two possible interpretations for the second verse.
The principle extends to all similar reports (akhbar).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : روى ابن خزيمة في كتابه عن النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم قال : « أن أحدكم يتصدق بالتمرة من كسب طيب ـ ولا يقبل الله إلا طيبا - فيجعلها في يده اليمين ، ثم يربيها كما يربى أحدكم فلوه و فصيله ، حتى يصير مثل أحد »
واعلم : أن اليمين عبارة عن القوة والقدرة . والدليل عليه : أنه الجانب الأيمن ، باليمين ، لأنه أقوى الجانبين ، وسمى الخلف ، باليمين، لأنه يقوى عزم الإنسان على الفعل أو الترك. قال الشاعر :
إذا ما راية رفعت لمجــــــد
تلقاها عرابة باليمين
إذا عرفت هذا ( فنقول ): ظهر الوجه فى قوله تعالى : والسموات مطويات بيمينه ، أما قوله تعالى : لأخذنا منه باليمين ، فالمراد منه . يمين المأخوذ ( أى ثم أخذنا بيمين) ذلك الإنسان وهو كايقال : أخذت بيمين الصبي، وذهبت به إلى المكتب، وإن كان المراد يمين الآخذ فالمراد منه : القوة والقدرة وإذا عرفت ذلك فى الآية ، فاعرف مثله في الأخبار .
The fourth: Ibn Khuzayma narrated in his book from the Prophet (peace be upon him and his family) that he said: 'One of you gives charity with a date from good earning – and Allah does not accept except what is good – then He places it in His right hand, then He nurtures it as one of you nurtures his colt and his foal, until it becomes like [Mount] Uhud.' And know: that 'al-yamin' (the right hand) is an expression for power and strength. The evidence for it: that the right side is called 'yamin' because it is the stronger of the two sides, and the opposite is called 'yamin' because it strengthens a person's resolve to act or refrain. The poet said: 'When a banner is raised for glory, 'Araba receives it with his right hand.' If you know this, then we say: The meaning appears in His statement: 'And the heavens are folded in His right hand.' As for His statement: 'We would have seized him by the right hand,' the intended meaning is the right hand of the one seized, (i.e., We would have taken his right hand), and it is as if one says: I took the boy's right hand and took him to school. And if the intended meaning is the right hand of the taker, then its meaning is power and strength. And if you know that regarding the verse, then know the like of it in the reports (akhbar).
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage is a theological commentary on the term 'yamin' (right hand) as it appears in hadith and Quran. Imam Razi explains that 'yamin' is not a literal hand but an expression for divine power and strength (quwwa wa qudra). He first quotes a hadith from Ibn Khuzayma about a charity being placed in Allah's right hand, then provides the interpretive principle: the right side is called 'yamin' because it is stronger, and even the opposite side can be called 'yamin' metaphorically when it strengthens resolve. He then applies this to Quranic verses: 'the heavens are folded in His right hand' means by His power, and 'We would have seized him by the right hand' is interpreted either as seizing the person's actual right hand (like taking a boy by the hand) or as a metaphor for power. The conclusion is that all such expressions in verses and reports are to be understood metaphorically, not literally.

القوة
القدرة
الجانب الأيمن
التأويل

Correction: Imam Razi demonstrates that 'yamin' is a metaphor for power and strength, based on linguistic analysis and textual interpretation.

D) A physical right side

Key Takeaway
The term 'yamin' in reference to Allah always functions as a metaphor for divine power and strength, never as a literal body part.

Hadith of the 'Palm of the Merciful': Weak and Metaphorical
Imam Razi discusses the hadith about 'palm of the Most Merciful' and declares it weak and metaphorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi critically evaluates a hadith about divine attributes, demonstrating that it is weak in transmission and must be interpreted metaphorically.

Core Idea
The hadith about Allah's 'palm' is weak and metaphorical, never literal.

Imam al-Razi addresses a hadith that mentions the 'palm' (kaff) of the Most Merciful, a term not found in the Quran. By examining its chains of transmission and the uncertainty of the narrator, he shows the hadith is weak. Even if authentic, it must be understood as a metaphor for divine care, not a literal hand. This lesson walks through his method.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and note its variants
Read the two versions of the hadith from Ibn Khuzayma, noticing the narrator's uncertainty between 'hand' and 'palm'.
The term 'kaff' does not appear in the Quran.
2
Analyze the weakness
Examine how Abu Hurayra's uncertainty and the mawquf chain (stopped at a companion) indicate the hadith is weak.
A mawquf hadith is not a prophetic statement.
3
Understand the metaphorical interpretation
See how even if the hadith were authentic, it must be taken as a metaphor (kinaya) for God's intense care, upholding tanzih.
This is consistent with other metaphorical interpretations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل العشرون فى الكف
هذا اللفظ غير وارد في القرآن . لكنه مذكور في الخبر . روى ابن خزيمة في كتابه الذي سماه بـ « التوحيد ، عن أبى هريرة ( رضى الله عنه ) عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال : « من تصدق بصدقة من كسب طيب - ولا يقيل الله إلا طيبا ، ولا يصعد إلى السماء إلا الطيب - تقع في كف الرحمن غير بيها كما يربى أحدكم فصيله ، حتى أن التمرة لتعود مثل الجبل العظيم، وروى هذا الحديث برواية أخرى عن أبي هريرة . وفيه: إن الرجل ليتصدق باللقمة. فتربو فى يد الله تعالى ، أو قال : « فى كف الله تعالى ، حتى يكون مثل الجبل . فتصدقوا
واعلم : أن هذا يدل على أن أبا هريرة كان مترددا في أنه هل سمع لفظ اليد أو لفظ الكف ؟ ويمكن أن يقال : سمعهما معاً في مجلسين مختلفين . وروى ابن خزيمة فى آخر هذا الباب ، عن أبي الحباب . أنه سمع أبا هريرة يذكر هذا الحديث موقوفا . فثبت بطريق الضعف : هذا الحديث . وبتقدير الصحة : فهو كناية عن زيادة الاهتمام بذلك الفعل ، وقوة العناية به كما تقدم مثله فى سائر الألفاظ - ( وبالله التوفيق )
Chapter Twenty: On the Palm (al-Kaff). This term does not occur in the Quran, but it is mentioned in the report (al-khabar). Ibn Khuzayma narrated in his book which he named 'al-Tawhid' from Abu Hurayra (may Allah be pleased with him) from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Whoever gives charity from good earning – and Allah does not accept except what is good, and only what is good ascends to heaven – it falls into the palm of the Most Merciful, not... (unclear) as one of you nurtures his foal, until the date becomes like a great mountain.' And this hadith was narrated with another narration from Abu Hurayra, in which: 'A man gives a morsel in charity, then it grows in the hand of Allah Almighty,' or he said: 'in the palm of Allah Almighty, until it becomes like a mountain. So give charity.' And know: this indicates that Abu Hurayra was uncertain whether he had heard the word 'hand' (yad) or the word 'palm' (kaff). And it can be said: he heard both in two different gatherings. And Ibn Khuzayma narrated at the end of this chapter from Abu al-Habbab that he heard Abu Hurayra mentioning this hadith as suspended (mawquf). So by way of weakness, this hadith is established. And assuming its authenticity, it is a metaphor (kinaya) for increased care for that action and strong concern, as has preceded similarly in other terms. (And with Allah is success.)
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a critical evaluation of a hadith mentioning the 'palm' (kaff) of the Most Merciful. Imam Razi first notes that this word does not appear in the Quran but only in a report. He quotes the hadith from Ibn Khuzayma's 'al-Tawhid' about charity falling into the palm of the Most Merciful and growing. Then he observes that Abu Hurayra was uncertain (mutaraddidan) whether he heard 'hand' (yad) or 'palm' (kaff), suggesting a variant. Moreover, another chain from Abu al-Habbab reports it as a suspended (mawquf) saying of Abu Hurayra, not a prophetic statement. Therefore, the hadith is weak (da'if) in terms of transmission. Even if one assumes its authenticity (bi-taqdiri al-sihha), it must be interpreted as a metaphor (kinaya) for God's intense care and concern for the charitable act, like the previous interpretations of similar terms. The passage thus upholds the principle of tanzih (transcendence) by rejecting literalism.

Key Terms
الكف
الخبر
الضعف
الموقوف
الكناية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think the hadith about Allah's 'palm' is authentic and means Allah literally has a palm like a human.
Correction: Imam Razi demonstrates the hadith is weak due to transmission issues and, even if authentic, it must be understood metaphorically as divine care, not a physical limb.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that a hadith about Allah's 'palm' is weak due to transmission issues, and if authentic, it must be interpreted metaphorically to uphold divine transcendence.

Interpreting the 'Palm' in Hadith: Metaphor over Literalism
Imam Razi discusses the hadith about the palm of the All-Merciful and interprets it figuratively.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the hadith about the 'palm' of Allah as a metaphor for divine care, not a literal attribute.

Core Idea
The 'palm' in the hadith is a metaphor for Allah's care, not a literal hand.

This lesson examines a hadith that seems to attribute a physical palm to Allah. Imam Razi shows that even if the hadith is authentic, it must be understood metaphorically, in line with divine transcendence. He also notes the weak chain and the narrator's uncertainty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Imam Razi structures his argument: he first cites the hadith, then discusses its chain and variations, and finally offers the interpretation.
Pay attention to the two versions: one uses 'palm' (الكف) and the other 'hand' (اليد).
2
Identify the hadith and its versions
Imam Razi presents two narrations: one with 'palm' and one with 'hand'. He notes that Abu Hurayrah was uncertain which word he heard. This uncertainty is important for the figurative interpretation.
The narrator's doubt suggests that the exact wording is not crucial; the meaning is what matters.
3
Examine Razi's critique of the chain
Razi points out that the hadith is also narrated as 'mawquf' (stopped at a Companion), which weakens its chain. Even if authentic, he says it must be interpreted figuratively.
This shows the importance of hadith criticism in theological discussions.
4
Understand the metaphorical interpretation
Razi explains that 'palm' or 'hand' is a metaphor (كناية) for Allah's increased care and attention towards charity. He refers to similar figurative interpretations for other anthropomorphic expressions.
This preserves divine transcendence (tanzih) while affirming the hadith's message.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل العشرون فى الكف. هذا اللفظ غير وارد في القرآن . لكنه مذكور في الخبر . روى ابن خزيمة في كتابه الذي سماه بـ « التوحيد ، عن أبى هريرة ( رضى الله عنه (۱)) عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم (٢) أنه قال : « من تصدق بصدقة من كسب طيب - ولا يقيل الله إلا طيبا ، ولا يصعد إلى السماء إلا الطيب - تقع في كف الرحمن غير بيها كما يربى أحدكم فصيله ، حتى أن التمرة لتعود مثل الجبل العظيم، وروى هذا الحديث برواية أخرى عن أبي هريرة . وفيه: إن الرجل ليتصدق باللقمة. فتربو فى يد الله تعالى ، أو قال : « فى كف الله تعالى ، حتى يكون مثل الجبل . فتصدقوا (۳) واعلم : أن هذا يدل على أن أبا هريرة كان مترددا في أنه هل سمع لفظ اليد أو لفظ الكف ؟ ويمكن أن يقال : سمعهما معاً في مجلسين مختلفين . وروى ابن خزيمة فى آخر هذا الباب ، عن أبي الحباب (٤) . أنه سمع أبا هريرة يذكر هذا الحديث موقوفا . فثبت بطريق الضعف : هذا الحديث . وبتقدير الصحة : فهو كناية عن زيادة الاهتمام بذلك الفعل ، وقوة العناية به كما تقدم مثله فى سائر الألفاظ - ( وبالله التوفيق )(٥)
Chapter Twenty: On 'Al-Kaff' (The Palm). This word does not occur in the Qur'an, but it is mentioned in the report. Ibn Khuzaymah narrated in his book titled 'At-Tawhid' from Abu Hurayrah (may Allah be pleased with him) from the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Whoever gives charity from good earnings – and Allah does not accept except good, and nothing ascends to heaven except good – it falls into the palm of the All-Merciful, and He raises it just as one of you raises his foal, until even a date becomes like a great mountain.' And this hadith was narrated by another chain from Abu Hurayrah, and in it: 'A man gives a morsel in charity, and it grows in the hand of Allah Almighty,' or he said: 'in the palm of Allah Almighty until it becomes like a mountain. So give charity.' Know that this indicates that Abu Hurayrah was uncertain whether he heard the word 'hand' or the word 'palm.' It may be said that he heard both at two different gatherings. Ibn Khuzaymah also narrated at the end of this chapter from Abu al-Habbab that he heard Abu Hurayrah mention this hadith as a suspended (mawquf) narration. So this hadith is established by a weak chain. And if it were authentic, it is a metaphor for the increase of concern for that action and the strength of care for it, as similar examples have preceded in other expressions. (And with Allah is the granting of success).
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is an interpretative analysis of a hadith that uses the term 'palm' (الكف) in reference to Allah. Imam Razi begins by noting that the word 'palm' does not appear in the Qur'an but is found in a prophetic report. He then cites a hadith from Abu Hurayrah: charity given from good earnings falls into the palm of the All-Merciful and grows until a date becomes like a mountain. Another version mentions 'hand' (يد) instead of 'palm.' Imam Razi observes that Abu Hurayrah was uncertain which word he heard, suggesting he might have heard both at different occasions. He notes that Ibn Khuzaymah also narrated this hadith as suspended (mawquf, i.e., attributed to a Companion rather than the Prophet), which weakens its chain. Nevertheless, if the hadith is deemed authentic, Imam Razi explains that it is a metaphor (كناية) for Allah's intensified care and attention towards the act of charity, not a literal hand or palm. He refers to similar figurative interpretations he has given earlier for other anthropomorphic expressions. Thus, the passage defends divine transcendence by rejecting literalism.

Interpreting Allah's Forearm as Perfect Power
Imam Razi interprets a hadith about Allah's forearm as a metaphor for perfect power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the hadith about Allah's forearm as a metaphor for His perfect power, avoiding literal anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Allah's 'forearm' is a metaphor for His perfect power, not a literal body part.

Imam Razi addresses a hadith that could be misinterpreted as anthropomorphism. He explains that the term 'forearm' is not literal but a figure of speech for divine power. This lesson explores his reasoning and its parallel in the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the hadith and the Qur'anic verse quoted.
The passage is brief but contains two key references.
2
Identify the hadith and the verse
The hadith says 'The forearm of Allah is stronger than your forearm.' The verse is from Surah Adh-Dhariyat: 'Indeed, Allah is the Provider, the Possessor of strength, the Firm.' Compare them.
3
Understand the interpretative rule
Imam Razi says 'if this hadith is authentic, it is interpreted as perfect power.' He uses a conditional phrase, showing caution, and then gives a metaphorical interpretation.
This is a standard mutakallim approach to ambiguous texts.
4
Reflect on the importance of transcendence
Why does Imam Razi avoid a literal reading? Because Allah is beyond physical attributes. This upholds tanzih (divine transcendence).
This connects to a core principle of kalam.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الحادي والعشرون في الساعد. وذكر في حديث (۱) آخر طويل : د ساعد الله : أشـد من ساعدك. قال الداعي إلى الله المصنف رضى الله عنه) (۲) : إذا صح هذا الحديث في حمول على كمال القدرة . ونظيره : قوله تعالى : ( إن الله هو الرزاق ذو القوة المتين (۳)
Chapter Twenty-One: On 'As-Sa'id' (The Forearm). And it is mentioned in another long hadith: 'The forearm of Allah is stronger than your forearm.' The caller to Allah, the author (may Allah be pleased with him) said: If this hadith is authentic, it is interpreted as perfect power. And its parallel is His statement: 'Indeed, Allah is the Provider, the Possessor of strength, the Firm.' (Qur'an 51:58)
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a brief interpretative note on a hadith that mentions the 'forearm' (الساعد) of Allah. Imam Razi quotes the hadith: 'The forearm of Allah is stronger than your forearm.' He then states that if this hadith is authentic, it should be understood as a reference to perfect power (كمال القدرة). He draws a parallel with the Qur'anic verse from Surah Adh-Dhariyat (51:58): 'Indeed, Allah is the Provider, the Possessor of strength, the Firm.' This confirms the metaphorical interpretation: just as the verse attributes strength to Allah without literal body parts, the hadith's mention of 'forearm' is a figure of speech for His power. Thus, Imam Razi upholds divine transcendence by avoiding literal anthropomorphism.

Interpreting the Forearm Hadith as Perfect Power
Imam Razi interprets the hadith of God's forearm as perfect power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the hadith of God's forearm as a metaphor for divine perfect power, and why this interpretation is necessary to preserve God's transcendence.

Core Idea
The forearm is a symbol of power, not a literal limb.

Imam Razi addresses a hadith that seems to attribute a physical limb to God. He argues that if the hadith is authentic, it must be understood not literally but as an expression of God's perfect power. This lesson explores his reasoning and the Quranic support he provides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to familiarize yourself with the text. Notice the structure: a hadith quote followed by the author's commentary.
2
Identify the hadith and the author's comment
Find the hadith about God's forearm and Imam Razi's ruling. Observe that he conditions his interpretation on the hadith's authenticity.
3
Understand the interpretative move
Notice how Imam Razi shifts from a literal understanding to a metaphorical one: he interprets 'forearm' as 'perfect power'. This is a key principle of tawhid.
4
Connect to Quranic evidence
Examine the Quranic verse he cites (Q 51:58) as a parallel. Reflect on how the verse uses 'power' and 'strength' to describe God, supporting the metaphorical reading.

الفصل الحادي والعشرون
في الساعد
وذكر في حديث آخر طويل: ساعد الله أشد من ساعدك
قال الداعي إلى الله المصنف رضى الله عنه: إذا صح هذا الحديث فيحمل على كمال القدرة. ونظيره: قوله تعالى: "إن الله هو الرزاق ذو القوة المتين"
Chapter Twenty-One: On the Forearm. And it is mentioned in another long hadith: 'God's forearm is stronger than your forearm.' The caller to God, the author (may God be pleased with him) said: If this hadith is authentic, it is interpreted as perfect power. And its parallel is His saying (Exalted): 'Indeed, God is the Provider, the Possessor of Power, the Strong.'
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents a chapter on the attribute of 'al-sā'id' (forearm). The author begins by citing a hadith that states 'God's forearm is stronger than your forearm.' He then provides his own commentary: if this hadith is authenticated, it must be interpreted metaphorically as referring to God's perfect power (al-qudra al-kāmila). To support this interpretation, he quotes the Quranic verse from Surah al-Dhariyat (51:58): 'Indeed, God is the Provider, the Possessor of Power, the Strong.' Thus, the forearm is not a physical limb but a symbol of divine strength and capability.

Misconception: God has a physical forearm like humans.
Correction: The hadith is metaphorical; 'forearm' signifies God's perfect power, not a literal body part.

Hearts Between God's Fingers: Hadiths on Divine Control
Imam Razi presents two hadiths about hearts between God's fingers and the scale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the prophetic reports that describe hearts being between God's fingers and the theological significance of such language.

Core Idea
God's fingers in hadith are not literal; they symbolize His absolute control.

Imam Razi begins the chapter on 'al-usbu' (finger) by noting that this term does not appear in the Quran but is found in prophetic traditions. He then cites two hadiths where the Prophet (peace be upon him) describes hearts being between two fingers of God, and also mentions the scale in God's hands. This lesson examines these reports and their implications for understanding divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to understand the flow of the chapter.
Notice that the author first clarifies that 'finger' is not in the Quran.
2
Identify the two hadiths
Look for the two distinct hadith reports: one from al-Qushayri and Muslim, and another from Sharh al-Sunnah. Compare their wording.
Both mention the Prophet's supplication 'O Turner of hearts'.
3
Reflect on the scale report
The passage ends with a report about the scale (mīzān) being in God's hands. Consider how this relates to the theme of divine control.
4
Consider the theological point
Think about why the author emphasizes that 'finger' is not in the Quran. How does this shape the interpretation of these hadiths?
This is a key point in Asas al-Taqdis's approach to allegorizing anthropomorphic attributes.

الفصل الثاني والعشرون
في الأصبع
هذه اللفظة غير مذكورة في القرآن. لكنها مذكورة في الأخبار: فالخبر الأول: روى القشيرى عن مسلم بن الحجاج عن أنس بن مالك - رضي الله عنه قال: كان النبي يكثر أن يقول: يا مقلب القلوب ثبت قلبي على دينك، قالوا: يا رسول الله آمنا بك وبما جئت به فهل تخاف علينا؟ فقال: القلوب بين إصبعين من أصابع الله تعالى يقلبها كيف شاء.
الخبر الثاني: ما روى صاحب شرح السنة في باب قوله تعالى: "ونقلب أفئدتهم وأبصارهم" أن النبي قال: وما من قلب إلا وهو بين إصبعين من أصابع رب العالمين، إذا شاء أن يقيمه أقامه، وإذا شاء أن يزيغه أزاغه. قال: وكان النبي يقول: يا مقلب القلوب ثبت قلبي على دينك. والميزان بين يدي الرحمن يرفع أقواماً، ويضع آخرين إلى يوم القيامة.
Chapter Twenty-Two: On the Finger. This word is not mentioned in the Quran, but it is mentioned in the reports. The first report: al-Qushayri narrated from Muslim ibn al-Hajjaj from Anas ibn Malik (may God be pleased with him) who said: The Prophet used to say often: 'O Turner of hearts, make my heart firm upon Your religion.' They said: 'O Messenger of God, we have believed in You and in what You have brought, so do You fear for us?' He said: 'Hearts are between two fingers of the fingers of God Almighty; He turns them as He wills.' The second report: What the author of Sharh al-Sunnah narrated in the chapter on His saying (Exalted): 'And We turn their hearts and their sights' that the Prophet said: 'There is no heart except that it is between two fingers of the fingers of the Lord of the Worlds; if He wills to make it upright, He makes it upright, and if He wills to make it deviate, He makes it deviate.' He said: The Prophet used to say: 'O Turner of hearts, make my heart firm upon Your religion.' And the scale is in the hands of the Most Merciful; He raises some people and lowers others until the Day of Resurrection.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the chapter on 'al-usbu' (finger). The author first notes that the word 'finger' does not occur in the Quran but is found in prophetic reports (akhbār). He then cites two (or three) hadiths. The first hadith, transmitted via al-Qushayri and Muslim, mentions the Prophet's frequent supplication 'O Turner of hearts' and the reply that hearts are between two fingers of God, turned as He wills. The second hadith, from the author of Sharh al-Sunnah, similarly states that every heart is between two fingers of the Lord, and God makes it upright or deviant as He wills. The Prophet's same supplication is repeated. A third report is added about the scale (mīzān) being in God's hands, by which He raises and lowers people until the Day of Judgment. The author does not provide his own interpretation in this excerpt, but the context of the book (Asas al-Taqdis) is to allegorize such attributes.

الأصبع (finger)
القلب (heart)
التقليب (turning/overturning)
الميزان (scale)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadiths prove God has literal physical fingers like human digits.
Correction: Imam Razi and the Asas al-Taqdis tradition interpret such attributes metaphorically to uphold divine transcendence; the word 'finger' is not even in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The hadiths about God's fingers are not in the Quran and require careful interpretation to avoid anthropomorphism.
