The Hadith Reports on God's Finger
Razi narrates three hadith reports mentioning God's finger.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be familiar with the three authentic hadith reports that mention the finger of Allah and their significance.

Core Idea
The word 'finger' appears in hadith, not the Quran, indicating a need for interpretation.

Imam Razi presents three hadith reports that mention God's finger. These reports come from reliable sources and are used by anthropomorphists to argue for a literal interpretation. However, Razi will later show that a literal reading leads to contradictions. In this lesson, we examine the reports themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the first hadith from Muslim
The first report narrates the Prophet's frequent supplication 'O Turner of hearts' and his response that hearts are between two fingers of Allah.
This hadith emphasizes God's control over hearts.
2
Read the second hadith from Sharh al-Sunna
Similar to the first, but adds detail: every heart is between two fingers, and God turns it as He wills.
3
Read the third hadith from Ibn Khuzayma
A man from the People of the Book asks about God carrying creation on one finger. The Prophet laughs in affirmation.
This report is linked to Quran 39:67.
4
Observe the patterns
Notice that in all three, the finger is associated with control, not physicality.
This sets the stage for metaphorical interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني والعشرون
في الأصبع

هذه اللفظة غير مذكورة في القرآن. لكنها مذكورة في الأخبار: فالخبر الأول: روى القشيري عن مسلم بن الحجاج عن أنس بن مالك - رضي الله عنه - قال: كان النبي يكثر أن يقول: يا مقلب القلوب ثبت قلبي على دينك، قالوا: يا رسول الله آمنا بك وبما جئت به، فهل تخاف علينا؟ فقال: «القلوب بين إصبعين من أصابع الله تعالى يقلبها كيف شاء».
الخبر الثاني: روى صاحب شرح السنة في باب قوله تعالى: ونقلب أفئدتهم وأبصارهم، أن النبي قال: «وما من قلب إلا وهو بين إصبعين من أصابع رب العالمين، إذا شاء أن يقيمه أقامه، وإذا شاء أن يزيغه أزاغه». قال: وكان النبي يقول: «يا مقلب القلوب ثبت قلبي على دينك» - «والميزان بين يدي الرحمن يرفع أقواماً، ويضع آخرين إلى يوم القيامة».
والخبر الثالث: روى ابن خزيمة في كتابه عن علقمة عن عبد الله بن مسعود - رضي الله عنه - قال: أتى النبي رجل من أهل الكتاب، فقال: يا أبا القاسم، أبلغك أن الله يحمل الخلائق على إصبع، والسماوات على إصبع، والأرضين على إصبع، والشجر على إصبع، والثرى على إصبع؟ قال: فضحك النبي حتى بدت نواجذه. فأنزل الله تعالى: وما قدروا الله حق قدره إلى آخر الآية. ثم ذكر ابن خزيمة هذا الحديث برواية أخرى عن عبد الله بإسناد حسن، وقال: فضحك النبي تعجباً وتصديقاً له.
واعلم: أنه ليس المراد من الإصبع: العضو الجسماني. ويدل عليه وجوه:
الأول: إنه يلزم أن يكون الله تعالى بحسب كل قلب: إصبعان، أو يلزم أن يكون الله إصبعان فقط، وهما حاصلان في بطان كل إنسان، حتى يكون الجسم الواحد حاصلاً في أمكنة كثيرة. وذلك كله سخيف وباطل.
الثاني: إنه يلزم أن يكون إصبعاه في أجوافنا، مع أنه تعالى على العرش عند المجسمة - وذلك أيضاً محال.
الثالث: إنه يقتضي أن لا يصح منه التصرف إلا بالأصابع وهو عجز.
Chapter Twenty-Two: On the Finger. This word is not mentioned in the Qur'an, but it is mentioned in the reports. The first report: al-Qushayri narrated from Muslim ibn al-Hajjaj from Anas ibn Malik - may Allah be pleased with him - who said: The Prophet used to frequently say: 'O Turner of hearts, make my heart firm upon Your religion.' They said: 'O Messenger of Allah, we have believed in you and what you have brought. Do you fear for us?' He said: 'Hearts lie between two fingers of the fingers of Allah Almighty; He turns them as He wills.' The second report: The author of Sharh al-Sunna narrated in the chapter on His saying: 'And We turn their hearts and their sights' that the Prophet said: 'There is no heart but it lies between two fingers of the fingers of the Lord of the worlds; if He wills to make it stand, He makes it stand, and if He wills to make it deviate, He makes it deviate.' He said: And the Prophet used to say: 'O Turner of hearts, make my heart firm upon Your religion' - and 'The scale is before the Merciful; He raises some people and lowers others until the Day of Resurrection.' The third report: Ibn Khuzayma narrated in his book from 'Alqama from 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud - may Allah be pleased with him - who said: A man from the People of the Book came to the Prophet and said: 'O Abu al-Qasim, has it reached you that Allah carries the creations on one finger, the heavens on one finger, the earths on one finger, the trees on one finger, and the dust on one finger?' He said: Then the Prophet laughed until his molars appeared. So Allah Almighty revealed: 'And they did not measure Allah with His true measure' to the end of the verse. Then Ibn Khuzayma mentioned this hadith with another chain from 'Abdullah with a good chain, and said: The Prophet laughed in wonder and affirmation of it. Know that the intended meaning of 'finger' is not the bodily organ. Evidence for this are aspects: First: It would necessitate that Allah Almighty has two fingers for every heart, or that Allah has only two fingers, and they are present in the interior of every person, so that one body exists in many places. All that is absurd and false. Second: It would necessitate that His two fingers are inside our bodies, while He is above the Throne according to the anthropomorphists - and that is also impossible. Third: It would imply that He cannot act except with fingers, which is powerlessness.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a combined presentation of evidence and rational interpretation (ta'wil). Razi first notes that the word 'finger' (al-isba') does not occur in the Qur'an but appears in hadith. He then narrates three hadith reports that mention God's 'fingers'. The first report from Muslim describes the Prophet saying 'hearts lie between two fingers of the fingers of Allah'. The second, from the author of Sharh al-Sunna, similarly states that every heart is between two fingers of the Lord, and the Prophet supplicates for steadfastness. The third report from Ibn Khuzayma involves a man from the People of the Book asking about God carrying all creation on one finger, and the Prophet laughs in affirmation, after which the verse 'They did not measure Allah with His true measure' is cited. After presenting these reports, Razi asserts that 'finger' is not meant as a physical bodily organ (al-'udw al-jismani). He provides three rational proofs (wujuh). First: if every heart has two fingers, then either God has infinite fingers or only two fingers present in everyone's interior, implying one body in many places—absurd. Second: it would require God's fingers to be inside our bodies while He is above the Throne (a point against the anthropomorphists, al-mujassima). Third: it would imply that God cannot act without fingers, which implies powerlessness ('ajz). Thus, the term must be interpreted metaphorically. This passage is an example of the kalam method of defending divine transcendence (tanzih) by rational argument against anthropomorphic literalism.

Key Terms
إصبع (finger)
أخبار (reports)
قلب (heart)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith should be taken literally because they come from authentic sources.
Correction: Razi does not deny authenticity; he affirms the reports but argues for metaphorical interpretation based on rational proofs.

Checkpoint
Where does the word 'finger' (al-isba') appear in Islamic scripture according to Razi?
A) In the Quran
B) In the hadith reports only
C) In both Quran and hadith
D) In the Torah

Key Takeaway
The three hadith reports use the word 'finger' in contexts of divine control, not physical description.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Prophet's supplication 'يا مقلب القلوب ثبت قلبي على دينك', do you genuinely feel that your heart's stability depends entirely on Allah's will, or do you subconsciously rely on your own efforts? What would change if you truly internalized that your heart is between two fingers of Allah?
?
This passage shows that the Prophet laughed in affirmation of a statement that could be misinterpreted as anthropomorphism. Are there beliefs you hold about Allah that you have not critically examined, accepting them because they feel familiar? Will you commit to studying the methodology of ta'wil so that your understanding of Allah is free from human forms?
?
The word 'finger' is not in the Quran, only in hadith. Have you ever elevated a hadith interpretation to the level of Quranic certainty without considering the scholarly tradition of metaphorical explanation? What specific step will you take today to ensure your concept of Allah is based on the Quran's own descriptions rather than sensational reports?

Rational Proofs Against Literal Finger
Razi gives three rational proofs for metaphorical interpretation of 'finger'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's three rational arguments that prove 'finger' must be interpreted metaphorically, not as a physical organ.

Core Idea
Literal reading of 'finger' leads to absurdities that contradict divine majesty.

After citing the hadith reports, Razi argues definitively that 'finger' cannot mean a bodily organ. He presents three rational proofs that expose the logical contradictions inherent in a literal interpretation. These proofs are classic examples of kalam reasoning defending tanzih (divine transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
If fingers are physical, then either God has two fingers for every heart (infinite fingers) or only two fingers present everywhere—both absurd.
This proof relies on the impossibility of one body in many places.
2
Read the second proof
Physical fingers would be inside our bodies, contradicting the anthropomorphists' own claim that God is above the Throne.
This is a reductio ad absurdum against the mujassima.
3
Read the third proof
If God needs fingers to act, He would be powerless without them, which is impossible for the Almighty.
This proof appeals to God's omnipotence.
4
Synthesize the arguments
Notice that each proof targets a different aspect: multiplicity, contradiction with Throne, and divine power.

Evidence for this are aspects: First: It would necessitate that Allah Almighty has two fingers for every heart, or that Allah has only two fingers, and they are present in the interior of every person, so that one body exists in many places. All that is absurd and false. Second: It would necessitate that His two fingers are inside our bodies, while He is above the Throne according to the anthropomorphists - and that is also impossible. Third: It would imply that He cannot act except with fingers, which is powerlessness.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a combined presentation of evidence and rational interpretation (ta'wil). Razi first notes that the word 'finger' (al-isba') does not occur in the Qur'an but appears in hadith. He then narrates three hadith reports that mention God's 'fingers'. The first report from Muslim describes the Prophet saying 'hearts lie between two fingers of the fingers of Allah'. The second, from the author of Sharh al-Sunna, similarly states that every heart is between two fingers of the Lord, and the Prophet supplicates for steadfastness. The third report from Ibn Khuzayma involves a man from the People of the Book asking about God carrying all creation on one finger, and the Prophet laughs in affirmation, after which the verse 'They did not measure Allah with His true measure' is cited. After presenting these reports, Razi asserts that 'finger' is not meant as a physical bodily organ (al-'udw al-jismani). He provides three rational proofs (wujuh). First: if every heart has two fingers, then either God has infinite fingers or only two fingers present in everyone's interior, implying one body in many places—absurd. Second: it would require God's fingers to be inside our bodies while He is above the Throne (a point against the anthropomorphists, al-mujassima). Third: it would imply that God cannot act without fingers, which implies powerlessness ('ajz). Thus, the term must be interpreted metaphorically. This passage is an example of the kalam method of defending divine transcendence (tanzih) by rational argument against anthropomorphic literalism.

Key Terms
عضو جسماني (bodily organ)
محال (impossible)
عجز (powerlessness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the hadith must be taken literally because they are authentic.
Correction: Razi argues that literal interpretation leads to absurdities, so metaphorical interpretation is necessary.

Checkpoint
What is the third rational proof against literal finger?
A) It requires multiple bodies in one place
B) It contradicts God being above the Throne
C) It implies God cannot act without fingers, which is powerlessness
D) It contradicts the Quran

Key Takeaway
The three rational proofs show that a literal reading of 'finger' leads to logical contradictions, so metaphorical interpretation is necessary to uphold divine transcendence.

Three Rational Proofs Against a Literal Finger
Imam Razi presents three logical proofs against literal interpretation of 'finger'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the literal interpretation of 'finger' in the hadith is rationally impossible, based on three logical arguments from Imam Razi.

Core Idea
God's transcendence forbids any literal bodily interpretation of anthropomorphic hadith.

This passage from Imam Razi's Asas al-Taqdis addresses a hadith where the Prophet is asked about God carrying creation on a finger. Razi clarifies that 'finger' is not a bodily limb, and he provides three rational proofs demonstrating the absurdities of a literal reading. These proofs protect the doctrine of divine transcendence (tanzih) against anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and context
Begin by reading the narration from Ibn Mas'ud. Note the question from the People of the Book and the Prophet's reaction. This sets the stage for Razi's rational analysis.
The Prophet laughed in amazement and affirmation, but the meaning is not literal.
2
Understand Razi's thesis
Razi states clearly that 'finger' does not mean a bodily organ. This is the claim he will prove with three arguments.
This is the central point to remember as you study the proofs.
3
Examine the first proof: spatial absurdity
If God had two literal fingers, they would have to be in every person's body, leading to the absurdity of one body being in many places simultaneously.
This proof uses the principle that a physical object cannot be in multiple locations at once.
4
Examine the second proof: contradiction with the throne
Literal fingers would be inside our physical bodies, but the throne verse places God above the throne. That is impossible.
This highlights the inconsistency between a literal reading and other scriptural statements.
5
Examine the third proof: implication of need
Needing fingers to act implies incapacity and need, which are impossible for Allah, who is perfect and self-sufficient.
This proof appeals to the attribute of divine perfection.

والخبر الثالث : روى ابن خزيمة فى كتابه عن علقمة عن عبد الله بن مسعود - رضى الله عنه - قال : أتى النبي رجل من أهل الكتاب . فقال : يا أبا القاسم ، أبلغك أن الله تعالى يحمل الخلائق على إصبع ، والسموات على إصبع ، والأرضين على إصبع ، والشجر على إصبع ، والثرى على إصبع ؟ قال : فضحك النبي حتى بدت نواجذه . فأنزل الله تعالى : ( وما قدروا الله حق قدره ) إلى آخر الآية . ثم ذكر ابن خزيمة هذا الحديث برواية أخرى عن عبد الله بإسناد حسن وقال : فضحك النبي تعجبا وتصديقا له . واعلم : أنه ليس المراد من الإصبع : العضو الجسماني . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنه يلزم أن يكون الله تعالى بحسب كل قلب إصبعان، أو يلزم أن يكون الله إصبعان فقط وهما حاصلان في بدن كل إنسان ، حتى يكون الجسم الواحد حاصلا في أمكنة كثيرة . وذلك كله سخيف وباطل . الثاني : إنه يلزم أن يكون إصبعاه في أجوافنا، مع أنه تعالى على العرش عند المجسمة - وذلك أيضا محال . الثالث : إنه يقتضى أن لا يصح منه التصرف إلا بالأصابع وهو عجز وحاجة وذلك على الله تعالى محال .
And the third report: Ibn Khuzayma narrated in his book from 'Alqama from 'Abdullah ibn Mas'ud - may Allah be pleased with him - who said: A man from the People of the Book came to the Prophet and said: O Abu al-Qasim, has it reached you that Allah - exalted is He - carries the creations on a finger, and the heavens on a finger, and the earths on a finger, and the trees on a finger, and the dust on a finger? He said: So the Prophet laughed until his molars appeared. Then Allah - exalted is He - revealed: 'And they did not measure Allah with His true measure' to the end of the verse. Then Ibn Khuzayma mentioned this hadith by another chain from 'Abdullah with a good chain and said: So the Prophet laughed in amazement and affirmation of him. And know: that what is meant by 'finger' is not the bodily organ. And there are proofs for that: First: It would necessitate that according to every heart Allah has two fingers, or it would necessitate that Allah has only two fingers and they are present in the body of every human, such that the one body is present in many places. All that is foolish and false. Second: It would necessitate that His two fingers are in our insides, while He - exalted is He - is above the throne according to the anthropomorphists - and that is also impossible. Third: It would necessitate that He cannot act except with fingers, and that is incapacity and need, and that is impossible for Allah - exalted is He.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure: Imam Razi first relates a hadith where a man asks the Prophet about God carrying creation on a finger, and the Prophet laughs and a verse is revealed. Then Razi states that 'finger' does not mean a bodily limb, and he gives three rational proofs. The first proof: If God had a finger in every direction, either He would have infinite fingers or only two fingers in every person's body, leading to absurdity. The second proof: Literally, the fingers would be inside us, contradicting the throne verse. The third proof: Needing fingers implies incapacity, which is impossible for God. Each proof shows the impossibility of a literal interpretation.

The Heart Between Two Fingers: Secret of Qadar
Razi explains the hadith of the heart between two fingers as a metaphor for divine decree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's metaphorical interpretation of the hadith 'The heart of the believer is between two of the fingers of the Merciful' and its implications for the doctrine of divine decree (qadar).

Core Idea
The heart's motives are in God's hands, like an object held between two fingers.

Imam Razi tackles a famous hadith that could be misinterpreted as anthropomorphism. He offers a subtle interpretation that reveals a profound secret about divine decree. In this lesson, we will follow his reasoning step by step, from the metaphor of the fingers to the infinite regress argument, and see how this hadith encapsulates the deepest principle of qadar.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Razi's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense argumentative style characteristic of kalam.
2
Understand the metaphor of the finger
Razi first explains that the literal attribution of fingers to God is impossible. The correct interpretation is that a finger symbolizes God's complete power over something, with no resistance.
This sets the stage for the hidden meaning.
3
Trace the argument about the heart
Razi argues that the heart is always in one of two states: inclined to action or not. The motive for action comes from God alone; otherwise, an infinite regress would occur. Therefore, the heart lies between these two divinely created states.
This is the core of the secret.
4
Realize the secret of qadar
Razi concludes that the two 'fingers' represent God's creation of motives for action and inaction. This is the greatest secret and the noblest principle in the matter of divine decree.
Connect this back to the hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاجة وذلك على الله تعالى محال. والتأويل الصحيح فيه: إن الشيء الذي يأخذه الإنسان بأصابعه يكون مقدور قدرته ومحل تصرفه على وجه السهولة، من غير ممانعة أصلا. ولما كانت الإصبع سبباً لهذه المسكنة والقدرة، جعل لفظ الإصبع كناية عن تلك القدرة الكاملة. إذا عرفت هذه المقدمة فنقول: أما الحديث الأول ففيه سر لطيف. وذلك لأن المتصرف في البدن هو القلب. والقلب لا ينفك عن الفعل وعن الترك. والفعل موقوف على حصول الدواعي إلى الفعل، والترك موقوف على عدم حصول تلك الدواعي. ولا خروج عن هاتين الحالتين، لأن الخروج عن طرفي النقيض محال. ثم إن حصول الداعي إلى الفعل من الله تعالى، ولا حصول له من العبد. وإلا لافتقر العبد في تحصيل ذلك الداعي إلى داع آخر، ويلزم التسلسل وهو محال. فثبت: أن القلب واقع بين هاتين الحالتين. فإن حصل فيه ما يدعوه إلى الفعل، أقدم على الفعل. وإن لم يحصل فيه ذلك، بقى على الترك. فحصول هاتين الحالتين في قلوب المؤمنين للفعل والترك، كالإصبعين المؤثرين في تقليب الأشياء. وتقليب القلب بسبب هاتين الداعيتين، يشبه تقليب الشيء المأخوذ بالإصبعين من حال إلى حال. وكما أن الإنسان يتصرف في الشيء المأخوذ بإصبعه بتلك الأصابع، فالحق سبحانه وتعالى يتصرف في قلوب عباده بواسطة خلق تلك الدواعي. وهذه النكتة هي السر الأعظم، والقانون الأشرف في مسألة القضاء والقدر. وقد عبر النبي بهذه اللفظة الوجيزة، والنكتة اللطيفة، عن هذا السر اللطيف.
And this is impossible for God, may He be exalted. The correct interpretation of it is that a thing which a person takes with his fingers is under the power of his ability and the place of his disposal with ease, without any hindrance at all. And since the finger is a cause for this subjugation and power, the word 'finger' was made a metaphor for that complete power. Once you understand this premise, we say: As for the first hadith, it contains a subtle secret. That is because the one who disposes in the body is the heart. The heart is never free from action or inaction. Action depends on the occurrence of motives to action, and inaction depends on the non-occurrence of those motives. There is no exit from these two states, because exiting from both extremes is impossible. Then, the occurrence of the motive to action is from God, and there is no occurrence of it from the servant. Otherwise, the servant would need another motive to obtain that motive, leading to infinite regress, which is impossible. So it is established that the heart lies between these two states. If there occurs in it what calls it to action, it proceeds to action. If that does not occur in it, it remains in inaction. So the occurrence of these two states in the hearts of the believers for action and inaction is like the two fingers that effectively turn things over. And the turning of the heart due to these two motives resembles the turning of a thing taken with two fingers from one state to another. Just as a person disposes of a thing taken with his fingers by means of those fingers, so the Truth, glorified and exalted, disposes in the hearts of His servants by means of creating those motives. This subtle point is the greatest secret and the noblest principle in the matter of divine decree and predestination (al-qada' wa'l-qadr). And the Prophet expressed this subtle secret with this concise expression and delightful subtlety.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an interpretation of the hadith that the heart of the believer is between two of God's fingers. Razi begins by stating that a literal attribution of fingers to God is impossible. The correct interpretation is metaphorical: just as a person can easily manipulate an object held between his fingers, the 'finger' symbolizes God's complete power over something, with no resistance. Applying this to the first hadith, Razi explains that the heart is the locus of decision in the body. It is always in one of two states: inclined to action or not. The motive to act comes from God alone; if it came from the servant, an infinite regress would follow. Therefore, God's creation of the motive in the heart is like a finger turning an object. The two 'fingers' represent the two states of motive presence and absence. This is the profound secret of qadar, expressed succinctly by the Prophet.

قضاء
قدر
داعٍ
قلب
إصبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God has literal fingers that physically turn the human heart.
Correction: Razi explains that 'finger' is a metaphor for God's complete power, and the two fingers represent the creation of motives for action or inaction in the heart.

Checkpoint

A) God's literal hand
B) The presence and absence of motives to action in the heart
C) The two angels recording deeds
D) The two extremes of free will and compulsion

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that the hadith of the heart between two fingers metaphorically means that God alone creates the motives for action and inaction in the heart, revealing the secret of divine decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The Prophet expressed this profound secret in a single phrase. Have you ever reflected on your own heart's fluctuations and seen the hand of God behind them? Today, will you surrender the illusion of self-sufficiency and ask Him to guide your heart to what pleases Him?

Refuting the Literalist Hadith on Divine Fingers
The author refutes a hadith report cited by anthropomorphists and interprets divine fingers metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith about God's fingers cannot be taken literally and how it should be interpreted metaphorically as a sign of God's power.

Core Idea
Divine attributes must be interpreted metaphorically when literal reading contradicts transcendence.

Imam Razi presents a double-layered refutation of a hadith that anthropomorphists use to ascribe body parts to God. He first questions the reliability of the report and then shows that even if authentic, the wording is a metaphor for God's effortless power. This lesson examines his two arguments and how they protect tanzih (God's transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage with translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the two distinct arguments introduced by 'الوجه الأول' and 'الوجه الثاني'.
2
Analyze the first argument: authority of the report
The author argues the speech is of Jewish origin, not a proof. Also, the Prophet's laughter was out of contempt, not approval. Consider why mere assumption (zann) is insufficient.
3
Examine the second argument: metaphorical interpretation
If the hadith is authentic, 'fingers' are a metaphor for absolute control. Understand the analogy: holding something with two fingers denotes ease of handling.
4
Connect to broader theology
See how this defense preserves God's transcendence (tanzih) and rejects anthropomorphism. Note the use of Quranic verse 39:67 to reinforce the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و مما يدل على أن المراد ما ذكرناه: ما روينا في الخبر أنه ما كان كثيراً ما يقول: «اللهم ثبت قلبي على دينك»، وأما الخبر الذي رواه عبد الله عن اليهود، فالكلام فيه من وجهين: الأول: إن هذا الكلام كلام اليهود فلا يكون حجة. ولعل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ضحك عند هذا الكلام استخفافاً به، فإن الإنسان العاقل إذا سمع كلاماً، قد يضحك عليه استخفافاً به. بقي أن يقال: أن عبد الله نقل أنه ضحك في كلامه تصديقاً له. إلا أنا نقول: هذا تمسك بمجرد الظن، فلا يكون حجة أصلاً، ثم إنه معارض بما روى في الخبر أنه قرأ عند ذلك قوله تعالى: (وما قدروا الله حق قدره)، وهذا مشعر بأنه عليه السلام كان منكراً للكلام. الوجه الثاني: إنه إن صح هذا الخبر فهو محمول على كونه تعالى قادراً على التصرف في هذه الأجسام العظيمة، بقدرة لا يدفعها دافع، ولا يعارضها مانع. وذلك أنا بينا أن الشيء الذي يأخذه الإنسان بإصبعيه يكون قادراً على التصرف فيه على أسهل الوجوه، فكان ذلك الإصبع هنا لتعريف كمال قدرة الله تعالى، ونفاذ تصرفه في هذه الأجسام العظيمة. ونظيره قولهم في وصف فعل من الأفعال بالسهولة واليسر: هذا العمل في كفه، بل على رأس إصبعه، والمراد: ما ذكرنا. وبالله التوفيق.
And from what indicates that the intended meaning is what we mentioned: what we have narrated in the hadith that he [the Prophet] used to often say: 'O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion.' As for the hadith that 'Abd Allah reported from the Jews, the discussion regarding it is from two aspects: First: Indeed this speech is the speech of the Jews, so it is not a proof. Perhaps the Prophet, peace be upon him, laughed at this speech out of contempt for it, for indeed an intelligent person, when he hears speech, may laugh at it out of contempt for it. It remains to be said that 'Abd Allah reported that he laughed at his speech in affirmation of it. However, we say: This is adherence to mere assumption, so it is not a proof at all, then it is contradicted by what is narrated in the hadith that he recited at that time the saying of the Exalted: 'And they did not estimate Allah with His true estimation' (Qur'an 39:67), and this indicates that he, peace be upon him, was denying the speech. The second aspect: If this hadith is authentic, it is interpreted as meaning that He, the Exalted, is able to dispose of these great bodies with a power that no repeller repels nor any hinderer opposes. And that is because we have shown that the thing that a person takes with his two fingers is able to dispose of it in the easiest of ways; so that finger here is for defining the perfection of the power of Allah, the Exalted, and the penetration of His disposal in these great bodies. And its analogy is their saying in describing an action as easy and simple: 'This work is in his palm, or even on the tip of his finger.' And the intended meaning is what we mentioned. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument defending the metaphorical interpretation of a hadith that ascribes 'fingers' to God. The author begins by citing a hadith where the Prophet used to supplicate 'O Allah, make my heart firm upon Your religion' – this serves as a background. Then he addresses a hadith reported by 'Abd Allah (Ibn Salam or another) about the Prophet laughing at the words of the Jews. The author divides the response into two aspects (wajhān). First: The words in question are the words of the Jews, so they cannot be used as evidence for divine attributes. The Prophet's laughter was out of contempt for their foolish speech, not affirmation. The author anticipates an objection: but 'Abd Allah transmitted that the Prophet laughed in affirmation. The author refutes this by saying it is mere speculation (zann) and not proof, and it is contradicted by another report where the Prophet recited 'They did not estimate Allah with His true estimation' (Qur'an 39:67), indicating his rejection of the Jews' anthropomorphic talk. Second: Even if the hadith were authentic, it must be interpreted metaphorically. The mention of 'taking with two fingers' is a figure of speech to express God's effortless power over the entire universe. The author explains that when a person holds something with two fingers, he can handle it with ease. This simile is used to convey the perfection of God's power. This is analogous to common Arabic expressions: 'This work is in his palm' or 'on the tip of his finger,' meaning it is easy for him. So the hadith's mention of 'fingers' is not literal but a metaphor for divine omnipotence. The passage concludes with 'And with Allah is success.'

القدرة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's laughter at the Jews' statement shows he approved of their anthropomorphic description of God.
Correction: The author argues the laughter was out of contempt (istikhfaf), not affirmation, and it is supported by the Prophet reciting Quran 39:67 which denies that anyone can fully comprehend God's power.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the primary reason the hadith of 'Abd Allah is not a valid proof for anthropomorphism?

B) The speech quoted is from the Jews, not the Prophet, so it cannot be used as evidence for divine attributes.
C) The Prophet laughed because he was pleased with the statement.
D) 'Abd Allah was not a reliable narrator.

Key Takeaway
The hadith about God's 'fingers' must be understood metaphorically as a description of His absolute power, not as a literal body part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you willing to submit to the scholarly interpretation that sees it as a metaphor for power, even if it challenges your initial understanding? Examine whether you prioritize God's transcendence over your own imaginings.
?
The author dismisses the claim that the Prophet's laughter affirmed the Jews' statement as 'mere assumption.' Reflect on your own life: are there beliefs you hold about Allah based on assumptions or weak evidence rather than clear revelation and sound reasoning? Commit today to re-evaluating one such belief.
?
The Prophet recited 'They did not estimate Allah with His true estimation' (39:67) when confronted with an anthropomorphic statement. When you encounter descriptions of Allah that seem human-like, does that verse come to your mind as a corrective? Or do you allow your mind to form images? Ask yourself: what is one step you can take to train your heart to think of Allah with absolute transcendence?

Interpreting the Hadith of the Prophet's Fingertips
Razi interprets a hadith metaphorically to preserve divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the hadith about the Prophet feeling the coolness of God's fingertips as a metaphor for divine care, preserving God's transcendence.

Core Idea
God's 'cool fingertips' are a metaphor for divine care, not a physical touch.

In this chapter, Imam Razi tackles a hadith that might appear to ascribe physicality to God. He explains that the words 'fingerprints' and 'coolness' are not to be taken literally. Instead, they are Arabic idioms expressing care and comfort. Let's see how he uses analogy and language to defend divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and identify key words
Read the hadith carefully. Notice the words 'anāmil' (fingertips) and 'bard' (coolness). The author points out that these words are not in the Qur'an but come from prophetic tradition.
This sets the stage for the need for interpretation.
2
Understand the king analogy
The author compares the hadith to a request made to a great king: 'Place your hand on his head.' This is an idiom for granting care and favor. Similarly, God's 'placing His hand' on the Prophet means directing care to him.
The analogy shows how metaphorical language about God is common in human expression.
3
Interpret 'coolness' as comfort
The author explains that in Arabic idiom, 'coolness' (bard) is used to express comfort and pleasure. For example, they pray 'May Allah cool down those abodes.' Thus, 'feeling the coolness of His fingertips' means experiencing the effect of divine care.
This linguistic insight is key to understanding the metaphor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثالث والعشرون في الأنامل. هذه اللفظة غير واردة في القرآن ولكنها واردة في الخبر. وهو ما روى عن النبي أنه قال: وضع يده على كتفي (وفي رواية: وضع كفه على كتفي) فوجدت برد أنامله فعلمت ما كان وما يكون. والتأويل: مثل أن يقال للملك الكبير: ضع يدك على رأس فلان. والمراد: اصرف عنايتك إليه. فقوله: «وضع يده على كتفي»، معناه: صرف العناية إلي. وقوله: «فوجدت برد أنامله»، معناه: وجدت أثر تلك العناية، فإن العرب تعبر عن وجدان الراحة واللذة، بوجدان البرد. وإذا أرادوا الدعاء قالوا: يرد الله تلك الديار.
Chapter Twenty-Three: On the Fingertips. This word does not occur in the Qur'an, but it occurs in the hadith. It is what is narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'He placed His hand on my shoulder (and in a narration: He placed His palm on my shoulder) and I felt the coolness of His fingertips, and I knew what has been and what will be.' The interpretation is: It is like when it is said to a great king: 'Place your hand on the head of so-and-so,' and the intended meaning is: 'Direct your care to him.' So his saying 'He placed His hand on my shoulder' means: He directed care to me. And his saying 'I felt the coolness of His fingertips' means: I felt the effect of that care. For the Arabs express the feeling of comfort and pleasure by the feeling of coolness. And when they intend to supplicate, they say: 'May Allah cool down those abodes.'
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a brief chapter on the interpretation of the word 'anāmil' (fingertips) which appears in a hadith but not in the Qur'an. The author first notes that this word occurs only in the hadith. He then cites the famous hadith where the Prophet says that God placed His hand on his shoulder and he felt the coolness of His fingertips, which gave him knowledge of past and future. The author provides a metaphorical interpretation (ta'wīl): this is analogous to a great king being told 'place your hand on the head of so-and-so' as a request to grant care and favor. So 'placing the hand on the shoulder' means directing divine care to the Prophet. Likewise, 'feeling the coolness of the fingertips' means experiencing the effect of that care. The author explains that in Arabic idiom, coolness (bard) is used to express comfort and pleasure. For example, when Arabs pray for someone, they say 'May Allah cool down those abodes,' meaning may He grant them comfort and happiness. Thus, the hadith is not literal; it conveys the profound divine care bestowed upon the Prophet, not a physical touch.

البرد (coolness)
العناية (care)
التأويل (interpretation)
الكف (palm)

Correction: The author explains it is a metaphor for divine care, not a physical description.

Key Takeaway
The hadith of the Prophet feeling God's cool fingertips is a metaphor for divine care, not a literal description of God.

The Metaphorical Interpretation of 'Anamil' in Hadith
Imam Razi provides a metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) of the hadith mentioning fingertips.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the hadith about Allah's fingertips as a metaphor for divine care and attention, thereby preserving divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Fingertips in the hadith are not literal but a metaphor for divine care.

This passage from Imam Razi's work addresses a hadith that might seem to attribute body parts to Allah. Razi demonstrates the method of ta'wil (allegorical interpretation) to reconcile such texts with the principle of tanzih (divine transcendence). We'll see how he uses linguistic and cultural context to explain the meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Familiarize yourself with the Arabic text and its literal meaning. Consider the structure: Razi first notes the word is not Quranic, then cites the hadith, then provides interpretation.
Pay attention to the phrase 'مثل أن يقال' which introduces the analogy.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice that Razi emphasizes the word 'anamil' does not occur in the Quran but in hadith, setting the stage for interpretation. This shows he distinguishes between sources requiring different levels of interpretation.
3
Understand the metaphorical explanation
Razi compares the hadith to saying 'place your hand' to a king meaning 'direct your care.' This clarifies that 'hand' and 'fingertips' are not literal body parts but symbols of attention and care.
The analogy with a great king helps illustrate the metaphorical usage.
4
Examine the linguistic evidence
Razi cites Arab usage of coolness to express comfort and pleasure, showing that 'coolness of fingertips' indicates the pleasant effect of divine care. He also references the supplication 'May Allah cool those abodes' to reinforce this.
This step grounds the interpretation in Arabic linguistic conventions.
5
Reflect on the purpose
Consider how this interpretation preserves Allah's transcendence while affirming the Prophet's knowledge. Ta'wil allows understanding without attributing physicality to Allah.
This is a key principle in Ash'ari theology.

Chapter Twenty-Three on 'Anamil (Fingertips). This word does not occur in the Qur’an, but it occurs in the hadith. It is what was narrated from the Prophet that he said: 'He placed His hand on my shoulder (and in another narration: He placed His palm on my shoulder) and I felt the coolness of His fingertips, so I knew what was and what will be.' The interpretation (ta'wil) is: (like it is said) to a great king: 'Place your hand on the head of so-and-so', meaning: direct your care to him. So his saying: 'He placed His hand on my shoulder' means: He directed care to me. And his saying: 'I felt the coolness of His fingertips' means: I felt the effect of that care. For the Arabs express the feeling of comfort and pleasure by feeling coolness. And when they intend supplication they say: 'May Allah cool those abodes.' And with Allah is success.

Commentary
This passage is an example of ta'wil (interpretation) of a hadith that seems to attribute body parts to Allah. Imam Razi first notes that the word 'anamil' (fingertips) does not appear in the Qur'an but occurs in a hadith. He then cites the hadith in which the Prophet reports feeling the coolness of fingertips on his shoulder, and through that he gained knowledge of past and future. The author then provides the metaphorical interpretation: similar to how one says to a great king 'place your hand on so-and-so's head' meaning 'direct your care to him,' so the Prophet's words mean divine attention and care. The phrase 'May Allah cool those abodes' in supplication further illustrates this usage. This interpretation prevents literal anthropomorphism.

Interpreting 'Janb' in the Quran: Metaphor for Revelation
Imam Razi explains the metaphorical meaning of 'janb' in a Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi interprets the word 'janb' (side) in the Quranic verse as a metaphor for revelation, and the linguistic reasoning behind that interpretation.

Core Idea
Janb' in the Quran does not mean a literal side but refers to revelation and sincere devotion.

In this passage, Imam Razi tackles a potentially problematic verse where the word 'side' is attributed to Allah. Rather than accepting a literal reading that would imply a body part, he explains through linguistic metaphor that 'janb' here signifies revelation and the act of separating oneself from everything but Allah. This lesson will walk through his reasoning and show how metaphorical interpretation protects divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the interpretation
Start by reading the Arabic verse and Imam Razi's immediate claim that the intended meaning of 'janb' is 'al-wahy' (revelation). Notice how he jumps straight to the metaphorical meaning.
The verse is from Surat Az-Zumar (39:56).
2
Understand the linguistic reason
Razi explains that the side of a thing is what separates it from others. Similarly, acting with sincerity separates one's action from anything other than Allah. So performing an act 'in the side of Allah' means doing it purely for His sake, following His revelation.
This reasoning is based on the root meaning of 'janb' as separation.
3
Recognize the validity of this metaphor
Razi concludes that this metaphorical usage is well-known and conventional, thus a valid interpretation. This method avoids attributing literal body parts to Allah.
This is a common ta'wil (interpretive) method in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الرابع والعشرون فى الجنب قال الله تعالى: يا حسرتا على ما فرطت في جنب الله. واعلم: أن المراد ههنا من الجنب: الوحى. والسبب في حسن هذا المجاز: أن جنب الشيء إنما يسمى جنبا لأنه به يصير ذلك الشيء مجانبا لغيره. فمن أتى بعمل على سبيل الإخلاص فى حق الله تعالى، فقد جانب فى ذلك العمل غير الله. فيصح أن يقال: إنه أتى بذلك العمل فى جنب الله. وهذه الاستعارة معروفة معتادة في العرف. وبالله التوفيق.
Chapter Twenty-Four on 'Janb' (Side). Allah Most High says: 'Alas for me that I fell short in the side of Allah' (Az-Zumar 39:56). Know that what is meant here by 'side' is revelation (al-wahy). The reason for the excellence of this metaphor is that the side of a thing is called a side because by it that thing becomes separated from other things. So whoever performs an act with sincerity towards Allah, then he has separated in that act from other than Allah. Thus it is correct to say: He performed that act in the side of Allah. And this metaphor is well-known and customary in convention. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a ta'wil of the Qur'anic verse 'يا حسرتا على ما فرطت في جنب الله'. Imam Razi first cites the verse and then states that the intended meaning of 'janb' (side) here is 'al-wahy' (revelation). He explains the linguistic reason for this metaphor: the side of an object is what makes it separate from other things. Similarly, a person who performs an action purely for Allah has separated that action from anything other than Allah. Therefore, it is correct to say that such an action is done 'in the side of Allah,' meaning for His sake or in accordance with His revelation. The author concludes that this metaphor is well-known and customary, thus valid. This interpretation avoids attributing a literal side to Allah.

Key Terms
الجنب
الوحى
المجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'janb' in the verse refers to a literal side of Allah, indicating He has a body.
Correction: Imam Razi clarifies that 'janb' is metaphorical, meaning revelation or sincere devotion, and does not imply a physical attribute.

Checkpoint

B) Revelation (الوحى)
C) The Garden of Allah
D) The mercy of Allah

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi interprets 'janb' in the Quran as a metaphor for revelation, based on the linguistic concept of separation, thereby upholding Allah's transcendence.

The Metaphorical Meaning of 'Janb' in the Qur'an
Imam Razi explains the intended meaning of 'janb' as revelation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the metaphorical interpretation of 'janb' (side) in the Qur'an as explained by Imam Razi, and why it cannot be taken literally.

Core Idea
'Janb' does not mean God's side; it means devotion that separates from other than God.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses a Quranic verse that seems to attribute a 'side' to Allah. He clarifies that the intended meaning is metaphorical, referring to revelation and sincere devotion. This lesson will explain his reasoning and the linguistic basis for this metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and the passage
Begin by reading the Quranic verse 'O regret over what I neglected in the side of God' and then the full passage by Imam Razi. Notice how he immediately defines the key term.
The verse is from Surah Az-Zumar (39:56).
2
Identify the metaphorical meaning
Imam Razi explicitly states that the intended meaning of 'janb' here is 'revelation' (al-wahy). This is the core claim: it is not a literal side.
3
Understand the rationale
Examine the reasoning: the 'side' of a thing is what makes it separate from others. Similarly, performing an action sincerely for God means separating oneself from other than God. Thus, such an action is done 'in the side of God'.
This is a linguistic metaphor based on the concept of separation.
4
Appreciate the custom
Imam Razi concludes that this metaphor is well-known and customary in Arabic. Recognize that this interpretation preserves divine transcendence by avoiding anthropomorphism.

قال الله تعالى ( يا حسرتا على ما فرطت في جنب الله ). واعلم : أن المراد ههنا من الجنب : الوحى. والسبب في حسن هذا المجاز : أن جنب الشيء إنما يسمى جنبا لأنه به يصير ذلك الشيء مجانبا لغيره . فمن أتى بعمل على سبيل الإخلاص فى حق الله تعالى ، فقد جانب فى ذلك العمل غير الله . فيصح أن يقال : إنه أتى بذلك العمل فى جنب الله . وهذه الاستعارة معروفة معتادة في العرف وبالله التوفيق

And the reason for the goodness of this metaphor is that the side of a thing is called 'side' because by it that thing becomes separate (mujanib) from others. So whoever performs an action with sincerity in the right of God Almighty, then he has separated himself in that action from other than God. Thus it is correct to say: 'He performed that action in the side of God.' And this metaphor is well-known and customary in usage. And with God is success.

He provides the rationale for this metaphorical usage: just as the side of a thing makes it separate from others, performing an action purely for God separates one from other than God. Therefore, such an action can be attributed to 'the side of God' in the sense of devotion to God. The author concludes that this metaphor is well-recognized and customary in the Arabic language.

Refuting a Literal Divine Shin
Imam Razi refutes proof-texts for a literal divine shin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the anthropomorphic interpretation of 'saq' (shin) in the Qur'an and hadith using linguistic and rational arguments.

Core Idea
A shin that is 'uncovered' does not prove God has a literal shin.

Imam Razi confronts a common anthropomorphic claim: that God has a shin because the Qur'an says 'the shin shall be uncovered' and a hadith mentions our Lord uncovering His shin. In this passage, he exposes the weakness of both proof-texts, showing that the language itself does not attribute a shin to God and that the very idea is rationally deficient. Let's examine his two arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the evidence presented by opponents
First, read the Qur'anic verse (68:42) and the hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri. Notice that the opponents take these as literal proof that God has a shin.
The hadith uses the phrase 'Our Lord will uncover His shin.'
2
Identify Razi's first argument: linguistic
Razi points out that the Qur'anic verse uses the passive voice 'يكشف عن ساق' (the shin is uncovered), without specifying who uncovers it. So it does not directly say God uncovers a shin.
This is a grammatical argument about the verb form.
3
Identify Razi's second argument: rational
Razi argues that a single shin is a deficiency for any living being, and God is exalted above deficiency. This is a rational refutation.
Think: why would a single shin be a deficiency? (It implies bodily limitation.)
4
Understand the incomplete nature of the passage
Note that the passage cuts off after the second argument. Perhaps Razi had more objections. But these two are sufficient to undermine the literalist claim.
The source note says the refutation is incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس والعشرون في الساق
احتجوا على الساق بالقرآن والخير .
أما القرآن : فقوله تعالى : ( يوم يكشف عن ساق ويدعون إلى السجود )
وأما الخبر : فقد روى صاحب شرح السنة (رحمه الله) في قوله تعالى: إن زلزلة الساعة شيء عظيم ، عن أبي سعيد الخدري ـ رضى الله عنه- أنه قال : سمعت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : « يكشف ربنا عن ساقه ، فيسجد له كل مؤمن ومؤمنة ، ويبقى من كان يسجد في الدنيا رياء وسمعة . فيذهب ليسجد له ، فيعود على ظهره طبقا ،
واعلم : أنه لا حجة للقوم في هذه الآية ، وفي هذا الخير . ويدل عليه وجوه :
الأول : إنه ليس فى الآيه أن الله تعالى : يكشف عن ساق ، بلفظ مالم يسم فاعله
والثانى : إن إثبات الساق الواحد للحيوان نقص . وتعالى الله عنه
Chapter Twenty-Five: On the Shin. They [the anthropomorphists] adduced evidence for the shin from the Qur'an and the hadith. As for the Qur'an: His saying, Exalted is He: 'On the Day when the shin shall be uncovered and they are called to prostrate.' As for the hadith: The author of Sharh al-Sunnah (may God have mercy on him) narrated regarding His saying, Exalted is He: 'Indeed the earthquake of the Hour is a tremendous thing,' from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri (may God be pleased with him) that he said: I heard the Prophet (peace be upon him) say: 'Our Lord will uncover His shin, and every believing man and woman will prostrate to Him, and there will remain those who used to prostrate in the world out of hypocrisy and show. So he will go to prostrate to Him, but his back will become one plate.' And know: that there is no proof for the people [the anthropomorphists] in this verse and in this hadith. This is indicated by several aspects: The first: That in the verse there is no statement that God Almighty uncovers a shin; it is expressed in the passive voice (ma lam yusamma fa'iluhu) [i.e., 'is uncovered']. The second: That affirming a single shin for an animal is a deficiency; and God is exalted above that.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of anthropomorphic interpretations of the term 'saq' (shin). The opponents first present their evidence: a Qur'anic verse from Al-Qalam (68:42) and a hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri. Then Imam Razi states that these texts do not constitute proof. He then gives two arguments. The first argument is linguistic: the verse uses the passive voice 'يكشف عن ساق' (the shin is uncovered) without specifying the agent, so it does not necessarily attribute the uncovering to God. The second argument is rational: affirming a single shin for an animal would be a deficiency, and God is far above that. The passage ends here, but the author likely had more aspects.

Key Terms
الساق (al-saq)
يكشف عن ساق (yukshafu 'an saq)
بلفظ مالم يسم فاعله (bi-lafz ma lam yusamma fa'iluhu)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Qur'anic verse and hadith literally describe God having a shin that He uncovers.
Correction: The verse uses the passive voice and does not specify the agent, so it does not attribute a shin to God. Moreover, a single shin implies deficiency, which is impossible for God.

Checkpoint
What is Imam Razi's first argument against the literal interpretation of the verse 'يوم يكشف عن ساق'?
A) The verse is metaphorical
B) The verse does not specify the agent of uncovering
C) The hadith is weak
D) God has no body parts

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the literal shin by showing the verse uses the passive voice and that a single shin implies deficiency, both incompatible with divine transcendence.

?
Imam Razi argues that a single shin implies deficiency. Have you ever considered that attributing human limbs to God actually degrades your concept of Him? How will you ensure that your worship reflects a God utterly beyond physical description?
?

Refutation of Literal Interpretation of 'Saaq' (Shin)
Imam Razi refutes literal interpretation of 'saaq' with three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's three objections against the literal interpretation of 'saaq' (shin) and his alternative metaphorical interpretation.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphic interpretations of Allah are refuted by reason and language.

In this passage, Imam Razi addresses a critical issue in theological hermeneutics: how to interpret verses that seem to attribute physical limbs to Allah. The opponents cite a Qur'anic verse and a hadith to argue that Allah has a shin. Razi systematically dismantles this claim using three rational objections and then provides the correct figurative meaning. This lesson traces his argumentation step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponents' evidence
Identify the Qur'anic verse (Q. 68:42) and the hadith that the opponents use to claim Allah has a shin.
The hadith is from Sharh al-Sunnah.
2
Understand Razi's refutation statement
Notice how Razi declares that there is no proof in these texts, then introduces three objections (wujuh).
3
Examine the three objections
Study each objection: (1) passive voice, (2) single shin implies deficiency, (3) worldly purpose of uncovering shin.
These are listed sequentially.
4
Learn Razi's positive interpretation
Razi explains that 'saaq' means the severity of the Day of Resurrection, using the idiom 'the war stood on its shin.'
This preserves divine transcendence.
5
Reflect on the method
Consider how Razi combines linguistic analysis and rational argument to refute anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس والعشرون
في
الساق
احتجوا على الساق بالقرآن والخبر.
أما القرآن: فقوله تعالى: {يوم يكشف عن ساق ويدعون إلى السجود} (القلم 42).
وأما الخبر: فقد روى صاحب شرح السنة (رحمه الله) في قوله تعالى: {إن زلزلة الساعة شيء عظيم} (الحج 1)، عن أبي سعيد الخدري رضي الله عنه أنه قال: سمعت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول: «يكشف ربنا عن ساقه، فيسجد له كل مؤمن ومؤمنة، ويبقى من كان يسجد في الدنيا رياء وسمعة، فيذهب ليسجد له، فيعود ظهره طبقاً واحداً».
واعلم: أنه لا حجة للقوم في هذه الآية وفي هذا الخبر. ويدل عليه وجوه:
الأول: إنه ليس في الآية أن الله تعالى يكشف عن ساق، بلفظ لم يسم فاعله.
والثاني: إن إثبات الساق الواحد للحيوان نقص. وتعالى الله عنه.
والثالث: إن الكشف عن الساق إنما يكون عند الاحتزاز عن تلوث الثوب بشيء محذور - وجل إله العالم عنه - بل نقول: المراد بالساق: شدة أهوال القيامة. يقال: قامت الحرب على ساقها، أي شدتها. فقوله: يكشف عن ساق، أي عن شدة القيامة وعن أهوالها وأنواع عذابها. وأضافه إلى نفسه لأنها شدة لا يقدر عليها إلا الله تعالى.
Chapter Twenty-Five: On the Shin (Saaq). They adduced evidence for the shin from the Qur'an and the hadith. As for the Qur'an: His statement, exalted is He: {On the Day when the shin shall be uncovered and they shall be called to prostrate} (al-Qalam 42). As for the hadith: The author of Sharh al-Sunnah (may Allah have mercy on him) narrated regarding His statement, exalted is He: {Indeed, the convulsion of the Hour is a tremendous thing} (al-Hajj 1), from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, may Allah be pleased with him, that he said: I heard the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, say: 'Our Lord will uncover His shin, and every believing man and believing woman will prostrate to Him, and there will remain those who used to prostrate in the world out of hypocrisy and reputation, so he will go to prostrate to Him, but his back will become a single layer.' Know: There is no proof for these people in this verse and in this hadith. This is indicated by the following aspects: First: In the verse it is not (stated) that Allah, exalted is He, uncovers a shin, in the passive voice. Second: Affirming a single shin for an animal is a deficiency - and Allah is exalted above that. Third: Uncovering the shin is only done when guarding against the garment being soiled by something forbidden - and the Lord of the worlds is too exalted for that. Rather, we say: What is meant by 'al-saaq' is the severity of the terrors of the Day of Resurrection. It is said: 'The war stood on its shin,' meaning its severity. So His statement, 'He uncovers a shin,' means concerning the severity of the Resurrection and its terrors and types of punishment. And He attributed it to Himself because it is a severity which no one except Allah is capable of.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a refutation. Imam Razi first presents the opponents' evidence: (1) the Qur'anic verse from Surah al-Qalam that mentions 'the day when the shin shall be uncovered,' and (2) a prophetic hadith narrated in Sharh al-Sunnah in which the Prophet says that on the Day of Resurrection, Allah will uncover His shin, prompting the believers to prostrate while hypocrites cannot. Razi then states that neither the verse nor the hadith provides proof for a literal shin for Allah. He gives three objections (wujuh) in sequence:

First objection: The Qur'anic verse is in the passive voice: 'yukashafu 'an saaq' (the shin shall be uncovered). It does not say that Allah uncovers His own shin. Thus, the literal attribution of a shin to Allah is not asserted by the verse.

Second objection: If we take the shin literally as a limb, attributing a single shin to a living being implies deficiency, because a normal animal has two shins. Such deficiency cannot be attributed to Allah, who is transcendent above all imperfection.

Third objection: In human experience, uncovering the shin is done to avoid soiling one's garment with something forbidden. Allah is far above such considerations. Therefore, the literal meaning is impossible. Razi then provides the correct figurative interpretation: 'saaq' here means the severity of the terrors of the Day of Resurrection, as in the Arabic idiom 'the war stood on its shin' meaning it became intense. So 'uncovering the shin' means 'revealing the severity' of that Day. The attribution to Allah emphasizes that such severity is uniquely within His power.

Razi thus argues that the correct interpretation is metaphorical, not literal.

Key Terms
الساق (al-saaq)
كشف عن ساق (kashf 'an saaq)
الحجة (al-hujja)
الوجوه (al-wujuh)
شدة أهوال القيامة (shiddat ahwal al-qiyama)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith literally proves that Allah has a physical shin.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the hadith must be interpreted figuratively; 'saaq' means the severity of the Day of Resurrection, not a limb.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection (al-wajh al-thani) raised by Imam Razi against the literal interpretation of 'saaq'?
A) The verse is in the passive voice.
B) A single shin implies deficiency.
C) Uncovering the shin is done to avoid soiling garments.
D) The hadith is weak.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi demonstrates that rational and linguistic analysis requires interpreting 'saaq' as a metaphor for severity, not a physical limb.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the hadith about Allah uncovering His shin, what was your immediate feeling? Did you find it difficult to accept the figurative explanation because it felt like you were 'explaining away' a clear text? How does this lesson challenge you to trust the scholarly methodology of interpreting ambiguous verses?

Think of a time when you insisted on a literal understanding of a religious text because it seemed simpler or more direct. After this lesson, are you willing to reconsider that position? What would it cost you to submit to the reasoned interpretation of scholars like Imam Razi?

The third objection argues that literal shin implies deficiency. Have you ever unconsciously attributed human limitations to Allah in your imagination? How can you actively train your mind to think of Allah as absolutely transcendent, beyond all physical concepts, starting from now?

Interpreting Kashf 'an Saq as Severity of Resurrection
Al-Razi gives a metaphorical interpretation of 'kashf 'an saq'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi interprets the Qur'anic phrase 'kashf 'an saq' metaphorically to safeguard divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The shin is not a divine limb but an idiom for overwhelming severity.

This passage addresses one of the most famous anthropomorphic verses in the Qur'an. Al-Razi, ever the rational theologian, provides a sophisticated linguistic reinterpretation. Instead of accepting a literal reading that would imply a body part for God, he shifts the meaning to the intense terrors of the Day of Resurrection, using a well-known Arabic idiom.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice how the passage begins with a rejection.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
The passage has a clear structure: first rejecting a literal meaning, then proposing a metaphorical interpretation. Identify these two sections.
3
Understand the linguistic proof
Al-Razi uses the idiom 'qamat al-harb 'ala saqiha' to justify the metaphorical meaning of 'saq' as severity. Reflect on how this idiom supports his interpretation.
This is a key element of his tawil method.
4
Appreciate the theological motive
Consider why Al-Razi insists on a metaphorical reading: to avoid attributing a physical shin to Allah and thus preserve divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : إن الكشف عن الساق ، إنما يكون عند الاحتزاز عن تلوث الثوب بشيء محذوور - وجل إله العالم عنه ـ بل نقول : المراد بالساق : شدة أهوال القيامة . يقال : قامت الحرب على ساقها . أى شدتها . فقوله : . يكشف عن ساق ، أى عن شدة القيامة ، وعن أهوالها وأنواع عذابها . وأضافه إلى نفسه لأنها شدة لا يقدر عليها إلا الله تعالى .
Third: That uncovering the shin (saq) is only done when avoiding the soiling of the garment by something forbidden (makhdhoor) - and exalted is the God of the worlds above that. Rather, we say: What is meant by 'shin' is the severity of the terrors of the Day of Resurrection. It is said: 'The war stood on its shin,' meaning its severity. So His saying, 'uncovers a shin,' means about the severity of the Resurrection, and about its terrors and types of its torture. And He attributed it to Himself because it is a severity that no one can bear except Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage provides a metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) of the Qur'anic phrase 'yawma yukshafu 'an saq' (the day the shin is uncovered). The author first dismisses any literal understanding—specifically, the idea that uncovering the shin is done to avoid soiling one's garment—as impossible for God. He then argues that 'saq' is an idiom for severity, citing the Arabic expression 'qamat al-harb 'ala saqiha' (the war stood on its shin), meaning the war became intense. Thus, 'uncovers a shin' refers to the unveiling of the extreme horrors of the Day of Judgment. God ascribes the action to Himself to emphasize that only He can bring about and endure that severity.

Correction: Al-Razi argues that 'saq' is a metaphor for severity, as in the Arabic idiom 'qamat al-harb 'ala saqiha' (the war stood on its shin), meaning the war became intense. Thus the verse refers to the unveiling of the intense terrors of the Resurrection, not a divine body part.

The Hadith of the Foot: Divine Transcendence and Anthropomorphic Language
Al-Razi presents a hadith about God placing His foot in Hell.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadith about God placing His foot in Hell and how al-Razi uses it to affirm divine transcendence, avoiding literal anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
God does not have a physical foot; the hadith must be interpreted metaphorically.

In this lesson, we examine a famous hadith in which the Garden and the Fire dispute, and the Fire is filled only when God places His foot in it. Al-Razi presents this hadith as a starting point for discussing anthropomorphic expressions in Islamic tradition. The passage sets the stage for a metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) that upholds divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the narrative structure: the dispute, God's response, and the key anthropomorphic element.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Hurairah and is considered authentic by Bukhari and Muslim.
2
Identify the anthropomorphic element
Locate the phrase 'until Allah places His foot in it'. This is the central expression that requires interpretation.
The word for foot here is 'rajul' (رجل), not 'qadam' (قدم), though both are used in similar contexts.
3
Understand the context
The hadith occurs in a chapter titled 'On the Foot and the Leg', indicating al-Razi's intent to address such expressions systematically.
The preceding chapters likely discussed other anthropomorphic terms.
4
Consider the need for tawil
Reflect on why literal interpretation is problematic for divine transcendence. How do scholars explain God's 'foot' metaphorically?
Common interpretations include power, control, or a sign of might.
5
Summarize the theological takeaway
Write a short summary of how this hadith is used to affirm tanzih (transcendence) while acknowledging the scriptural text.

Source Text

الفصل السادس والعشرون في الرجل والقدم. أما الرجل. فقد روى صاحب شرح السنة رحمه الله في آخر كتابه عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه قال: قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: تحاجت الجنة والنار. فقالت النار: أوثرت بالمتكبرين والمتجبرين، وقالت الجنة: فمالي لا يدخلني إلا أضعفاء المسلمين وسقطهم. فقال تعالى للجنة: إنما أنت رحمتي أرحم بك من أشاء من عبادي، وقال للنار: إنما أنت عذابي أعذب بك من أشاء من عبادي. ولكل واحدة منكما ملؤها. فأما النار فلا تمتلئ حتى يضع الله تعالى فيها رجله، فتقول: قط قط. فهناك تمتلئ ويزوى بعضها إلى بعض. ولا يظلم الله أحداً خلقه. وأما الجنة فإن الله ينشئ لها خلقاً. قال صاحب شرح السنة رحمه الله: هذا حديث متفق على صحته، أخرجه الشيخان.
Chapter Twenty-Six: On the Foot and the Leg. As for the foot: The author of Sharh al-Sunnah (may Allah have mercy on him) narrated in the last part of his book, on the authority of Abu Hurairah (may Allah be pleased with him), who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'The Garden and the Fire disputed. The Fire said: I have been favored with the arrogant and the tyrants. The Garden said: Why is it that only the weak and the lowly of Muslims enter me? So Allah Almighty said to the Garden: You are My mercy; I show mercy through you to whom I will of My servants. And He said to the Fire: You are My punishment; I punish through you whom I will of My servants. And each of you will be filled. As for the Fire, it will not be filled until Allah Almighty places His foot in it, whereupon it will say: Enough, enough. Then it will be filled and its parts will be drawn close together. And Allah does not wrong any of His creation. As for the Garden, Allah will create new people for it.' The author of Sharh al-Sunnah said: This hadith is agreed upon as authentic; al-Bukhari and Muslim narrated it.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on the interpretation of anthropomorphic terms 'rajul' (foot) and 'qadam' (leg) found in hadith. It presents a well-known hadith in which the Fire and the Garden dispute over who enters them. The Fire complains it receives the arrogant, while the Garden complains it receives only the weak. God assigns each its role: the Garden as mercy, the Fire as punishment. The key anthropomorphic element is that the Fire will not be filled until God places His foot in it, at which point it says 'Enough' and is filled. The author then notes the hadith's authenticity, citing agreement of Bukhari and Muslim. The passage sets up the need for a metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) to avoid attributing a physical limb to God.

First Objection: Rational Impossibility of Debate Between Inanimate Objects
Imam Razi provides the first proof against literal interpretation of the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the hadith about Paradise and Hell debating cannot be taken literally, based on logical and theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Inanimate objects cannot debate; even if made alive, they would not argue with their just Creator.

Imam Razi presents the first objection against the literal interpretation of the hadith. He points out that Paradise and Hell are inanimate, so debate is impossible. Then he anticipates a counter-argument and refutes it with two compelling points about divine justice and wisdom. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its literal translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let the original words and the literal meaning sink in before analyzing them.
Notice the structure: objection, anticipated response, two refutations.
2
Identify the main objection and the anticipated response
What is the initial argument? (Paradise and Hell are inanimate so can't debate.) What counter-argument does Imam Razi address? (God could make them alive.)
3
Understand the first refutation
If God made them alive and they know their Lord, they would recognize all His actions as just, so debate would be impossible. Why? Because they would see nothing to contest.
This refutation is based on divine justice (adl).
4
Understand the second refutation
If God foresaw that making them alive would lead to a debate that only ends when He puts His foot in Hell, His wisdom would prevent creating life to avoid this unnecessary tribulation.
This refutation is based on divine wisdom (hikma).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: إن الجنة والنار جمادان فكيف يتصور منهما المحاجة والمخاصمة؟ فإن قالوا: إن الله تعالى يجعلهما من الأحياء فنقول: إذا حصلت هذه الحالة وعرفا ربهما امتنعت حصول هذه المحاجة لأنهما يعرفان أن كل ما يفعله إله العالم فهو عدل وصواب وعلى هذا التقدير لا تبقى تلك المحاجة. وأيضا: إذا علم الله أنه إذا خلق الحياة فيهما فإنهما يقدمان على المحاجة ولا تنقطع تلك المحاجة إلا إذا وضع قدمه في النار كان يجب أن لا يخلق الحياة فيهما لئلا تحصل هذه الفتنة.
First: Paradise and Hell are inanimate objects, so how can debate and argument be imagined from them? If they say: God Almighty makes them living beings, we say: When this state occurs and they know their Lord, this debate becomes impossible, because they know that everything that the Lord of the worlds does is just and correct, and on this basis that debate would not remain. Also: If God knew that when He creates life in them they will initiate the debate, and that debate will not end except by placing His foot in Hell, then He should not have created life in them in order to avoid this tribulation.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is the first objection (al-wajh al-awwal) arguing that the hadith about Paradise and Hell debating cannot be taken literally. Imam Razi begins by pointing out that Paradise and Hell are inanimate objects (jamadan), so it is impossible to imagine them engaging in a rational debate (muhajja wa-mukhasama). He then anticipates an objection: opponents might say that God could make them living beings. He responds with two arguments. First, if they were made alive and knew God, they would recognize that all God's actions are just and correct, and therefore they would not argue – the debate would not occur. Second, if God knew in advance that making them alive would lead to a debate that only ends by placing His foot in Hell, then His wisdom would require that He not create life in them to avoid this unnecessary disturbance. Both arguments show the irrationality of taking the hadith literally.

Key Terms
جمادان
المحاجة
المخاصمة
يجعلهما من الأحياء
فتنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power can make anything possible, so literal debate is possible
Correction: The objection shows it is logically impossible even for God, because if made alive they would not argue due to knowledge of divine justice.

Checkpoint

A) They would still argue
B) They would not argue because they know God's justice
C) They would argue until God places His foot
D) They would become inanimate again

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's first objection uses the inanimate nature of Paradise and Hell, combined with divine justice and foreknowledge, to argue against literal interpretation.

The Foot in Hell: A Logical Dilemma
Razi presents a logical dilemma and responds to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses a logical dilemma to refute the literal interpretation of God having a physical foot, and how he responds to a potential counterargument.

Core Idea
A literal foot in Hell leads to an absurd contradiction: either the fire goes out or God burns.

Imam Razi presents the third proof against literalism by considering the implications of God placing His foot in Hellfire. He forces a dilemma: either the fire is extinguished (ending eternal punishment) or it burns God’s foot (impossible). He then anticipates an objection and gives a pointed response, though the text is incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the English translation. Let the logical flow emerge naturally.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Notice the two outcomes when God places His foot in the fire: either the fire is extinguished or it remains. Both lead to impossibility.
The first horn ends punishment (unacceptable), the second burns God's foot (impossible).
3
Understand the counterargument and response
Read the objection and Razi's reply. The objection suggests God could protect Himself, but Razi asks why He would need to if He has power.
The response is incomplete but the implication is that the literal interpretation is unnecessary.
4
Reflect on the theological lesson
Consider why this argument is important: it shows that literalism leads to absurdity, reinforcing God's transcendence.
This connects to the broader theme of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث: إنه إذا كان له رجل فالظاهر أنه لا يضع تلك الرجل في النار لأنه لو وضع رجله في النار وانطفئت النار فقد زال العذاب أهل النار وهو غير جائز وإن بقيت النار مشتعلة لزم وقوع الاحتراق في تلك الرجل وتعالى الله عن ذلك علوا كبيرا. فإن قالوا: لم لا يجوز أن يبقى الاحتراق في أبدان الكفار ويصون نفسه عن النار والاحتراق؟ فنقول: إذا قدر على ذلك فلم لم يقدر؟
Third: If He has a foot, it is apparent that He would not place that foot in the fire, because if He placed His foot in the fire and the fire was extinguished, then the punishment of the people of Hell would cease – and that is not permissible – and if the fire remained blazing, then burning would necessarily occur to that foot – and exalted is God above that greatly. If they say: Why is it not permissible that burning remains in the bodies of the disbelievers while He protects Himself from the fire and burning? We say: If He is able to do that, then why is He not able?
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is the third objection against the literal interpretation, along with a response to a possible counterargument. Imam Razi presents a logical dilemma: if God has a foot and places it in Hellfire, two outcomes are possible. First, the fire might be extinguished, which would mean the punishment of the disbelievers ends – an impossibility because Hell is eternal. Second, the fire might remain blazing, which would then burn God's foot – and this is also impossible because God is transcendent above physical harm. He then anticipates an objection: opponents might say that God could keep the disbelievers burning while protecting Himself from the fire. Imam Razi responds by asking why, if God is able to do that, He is not able to simply fill Hell without placing His foot? The response is cut off in the original, but the implication is that the literal interpretation leads to an unnecessary absurdity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God can protect Himself from fire, making the literal interpretation plausible.
Correction: Imam Razi's response shows that if God can protect Himself, then the literal foot is unnecessary; the entire scenario is absurd, reinforcing divine transcendence.

The literal interpretation of God having a foot leads to an unavoidable contradiction, demonstrating the necessity of divine transcendence.
