First Argument: Inanimate Objects Cannot Argue
Al-Razi presents the first argument against literal interpretation of hadith about Paradise and Hellfire arguing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Razi argues that Paradise and Hellfire cannot be taken literally because they are inanimate, and how he refutes the objection that God could make them alive.

Core Idea
Inanimate objects cannot argue — hadith about their disputes must be interpreted metaphorically.

Al-Razi begins his refutation of anthropomorphic hadith by presenting a rational argument. The first point is simple yet powerful: Paradise and Hellfire are inanimate objects, so how could they possibly argue? This lesson unpacks that argument and the response to a possible objection. Prepare to see why literal reading collapses under rational scrutiny.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow.
Notice the concise style of Al-Razi's argument.
2
Identify the core premise
The argument rests on the premise that Paradise and Hellfire are inanimate (جمادان). Understand why this makes literal argument impossible.
The word جمادان is key.
3
Follow the objection and rebuttal
Al-Razi anticipates an objection: God could make them alive. Then he gives two rebuttals. Trace the logic of each.
The first rebuttal: if alive, they'd know God's justice. The second: God's foreknowledge prevents the scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذه الأحاديث لا يمكن إجراؤها على ظاهرها. ويدل عليه وجوه: الأول: إن الجنة والنار جمادان. فكيف يتصور منهما المحاجة والمخاصمة ؟ فإن قالوا : إن الله تعالى يجعلهما من الأحياء ، فنقول : إذا حصلت هذه الحالة ، وعرفا ربهما ، امتنعت حصول هذه المحاجة . لأنهما يعرفان أن كل ما يفعله إله العالم فهو عدل وصواب . وعلى هذا التقدير لا تبقى تلك المحاجة . وأيضا : إذا علم الله أنه إذا خلق الحياة فيهما ، فإنهما يقدمان على المحاجة ، ولا تنقطع تلك المحاجة إلا إذا وضع قدمه في النار. كان يجب أن لا يخلق الحياة فيهما ، لئلا تحصل هذه الفتنة
Know that these hadiths cannot be taken upon their apparent meanings. And this is indicated by several points: The first: Paradise and Hellfire are inanimate objects. So how can arguing and disputing be imagined from them? If they say: God most high makes them alive, we say: When this state occurs, and they know their Lord, the occurrence of this argument becomes impossible. Because they know that everything the God of the world does is justice and correctness. On this assumption, that argument does not remain. Also: If God knows that if He creates life in them, they will proceed to argue, and that argument will not cease unless He places His foot in the Fire, then He should not have created life in them, lest this tribulation occur.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is an introduction and the first of several arguments refuting the literal reading of a hadith. The author begins by stating that these hadiths cannot be taken literally. He then presents the first argument: Paradise and Hellfire are inanimate objects (جمادان), so how could they possibly engage in argument and dispute? This is a rational impossibility. To a hypothetical objection that God could make them alive, the author responds that if they were made alive and came to know their Lord, they would recognize that everything God does is just and correct, so they would not argue. Furthermore, if God knew in advance that creating life in them would lead to argument that only ends when He places His foot in the Fire, He would avoid creating that life to prevent the tribulation. Thus the argument demonstrates the absurdity of taking the hadith literally.

محاجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God literally has a foot that He places in Hellfire.
Correction: The hadith cannot be taken literally because Paradise and Hellfire are inanimate and cannot argue; the mention of 'foot' is metaphorical or must be interpreted in a way befitting God's transcendence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The literal interpretation of hadith about Paradise and Hellfire arguing is rationally impossible because they are inanimate; even if God made them alive, they would not argue, and God's foreknowledge would prevent such a scenario.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
God's foreknowledge would prevent a scenario that leads to endless argument. Consider a situation in your life where you persist in a dispute or argument. How does this teaching about God's wisdom in preventing futile argument challenge you to stop arguing and submit to God's wisdom?

Second Argument: Implies Ignorance of Numbers
Al-Razi argues the hadith implies God did not know the number of inhabitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's second argument against the anthropomorphic hadith, which shows that it implies God's ignorance of the number of inhabitants.

Core Idea
A hadith that forces God to create extra creation contradicts His omniscience.

Al-Razi presents his second argument, building on the first. He points out that if the hadith is taken literally, it implies that God did not know the exact number of people destined for reward and punishment. As a result, He created Paradise and Hellfire with extra space, and then had to create additional beings and place His foot in the Fire to fill it. This reasoning demonstrates the absurdity and incompatibility with divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the phrase 'فلا جرم' which indicates a necessary consequence.
2
Identify the implication
Al-Razi claims that the hadith implies God was not aware of the number of people. Find the exact wording for this implication.
3
Trace the consequences
Follow the logical chain: because God did not know the number, He created extra space, then needed to fill it with extra creation and His foot.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the theological problem
Consider why this is a problem for those who take the hadith literally. How does it contradict God's attribute of perfect knowledge?
Al-Razi is using logic to defend divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إن هذا الحديث يقتضى أنه تعالى ما كان عالما بمقدار أهل الثواب والعقاب ، فلا جرم خلق الله الجنة والنار ، أوسع من قدر الحاجة ولا جرم احتاج إلى أن يخلق للجنة خلقا آخر ، وأن قدمه في النار .
And the second: This hadith implies that He, exalted, was not aware of the number of the people of reward and punishment. Therefore He created Paradise and Hellfire more spacious than the necessary capacity, and therefore He needed to create another creation for Paradise and that His foot is in the Fire.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument: the hadith implies that God was not aware of the exact number of people destined for reward and punishment. Consequently, He created Paradise and Hellfire with extra space beyond what was needed, and then had to create additional beings to fill Paradise and to place His foot in Hellfire to extinguish it. This is incompatible with God's perfect knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: Al-Razi's argument shows that God's knowledge is perfect and eternal; He knows the exact numbers, so there is no need for additional creation or 'foot' to fill extra space.

Checkpoint

The Dilemma of God's Foot in the Fire
Al-Razi presents the third argument against God having a foot.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Razi's third argument against anthropomorphism, which shows the absurdity of God having a foot by analyzing the logical consequences of placing it in the Fire.

Core Idea
If God has a foot, placing it in the Fire leads to an impossible dilemma.

Al-Razi continues his logical critique of anthropomorphic interpretations. The third argument focuses on the hypothetical scenario of God placing His foot in Hellfire. By examining the necessary consequences, he forces the literalist into a contradiction that can only be resolved by rejecting the premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhetorical structure come through.
Notice the dilemma: either the Fire is extinguished or it remains.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Al-Razi presents two possibilities if God places His foot in the Fire. Find each one and understand why each is impossible.
If extinguished, punishment ceases (impermissible). If burning continues, God's foot would burn (impossible).
3
Examine the objection and response
The opponent objects that God could protect His foot while the Fire burns others. Al-Razi counters with a question that reveals the absurdity.
If God can protect, why not simply remove the empty places in Hell so no need to place a foot?
4
Reflect on the argument's purpose
This argument aims to show that the literal interpretation leads to logical contradictions, thus reinforcing God's transcendence.
The key is that God is not subject to physical limitations or harm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : إنه إذا كان له رجل . فالظاهر أنه لا يضع تلك الرجل في النار ، لأنه لو وضع رجله في النار ، وانطفئت النار ، فقد زال العذاب أهل النار - وهو غير جائز - وإن بقيت النار مشتعلة ، لزم وقوع الاحتراق في تلك الرجل - وتعالى الله عن ذلك علوا كبيرا - فإن قالوا : لم لا يجوز أن يبقى الاحتراق فى أبدان الكفار ، ويصون نفسه عن النار والاحتراق ؟ فنقول : إذا قدر على ذلك فلم لم يقدر على أن ينفي المواضع الخالية عن جهنم ، حتى لا تصير ملجأ إلى وضع القدم فيها .
And the third: If He has a foot, then apparently He would not place that foot in the Fire, because if He placed His foot in the Fire and the Fire were extinguished, then the punishment of the people of the Fire would cease – which is impermissible – and if the Fire remains blazing, then burning would occur on that foot – and exalted is God above that greatly. If they say: Why is it not permissible that burning remains in the bodies of the disbelievers while He protects Himself from the Fire and burning? We say: If He has power over that, why was He not able to remove the empty spaces from Hellfire so that it would not become a resort to placing the foot in it?
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument. If we assume God has a foot (as the literal reading suggests), then placing it in the Fire leads to a dilemma: either the Fire is extinguished, which would end the punishment of the disbelievers (impermissible), or the Fire remains lit, which would burn God's foot (impossible for God). To the objection that God could protect His foot while the Fire burns the disbelievers, the author responds: if God can do that, why can He not simply remove the empty spaces in Hellfire so that He does not need to place His foot at all? This highlights the absurdity of the literal interpretation.

انطفاء
احتراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God could be subject to physical harm or that literal descriptions of God's body parts are unproblematic.
Correction: This argument shows that taking literal descriptions leads to logical absurdities, so they must be interpreted metaphorically to preserve God's transcendence.

Checkpoint

A) The Fire gets hotter or colder.
B) The Fire is extinguished or it burns the foot.
C) The Fire disappears or the foot stays intact.
D) The Fire doubles in size or the foot shrinks.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's third argument demonstrates that the literal interpretation of God having a foot leads to a logical contradiction, thereby affirming God's transcendence over physical attributes.

The Fire Demands Only Sinners
Razi argues that the Fire's demand is exclusively for sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Razi's fourth argument that the Fire's demand 'Is there any more?' is specifically for sinners, making the hadith's foot placement an incoherent response.

Core Idea
The Fire's demand is for sinners, not for God's foot.

Imam al-Razi presents a focused logical critique. The hadith says the Fire will ask 'Is there any more?' and Allah will place His foot, causing the Fire to be satisfied. But Razi points out that the Fire's demand is for those deserving punishment, not for an object. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: 'طلب' (demand), 'مزيد' (more), 'مستحق العذاب' (those deserving punishment).
The passage is very short but dense.
2
Identify the key premise
The passage states that the Fire's demand is only for those who deserve punishment. This is the crucial premise.
Without this premise, the argument collapses.
3
Understand the logical consequence
If there are no more sinners, then the demand is not about an object like a foot. Placing the foot does not answer the demand, so the hadith scenario becomes incoherent.
This shows how Razi uses the premise to critique the literal understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إن النار إنما تطلب بقولها : هل من مزيد ؟ : الذين يستحقون العذاب . ولذلك إذا لم يكن مستحقاً للعذاب ، لم يكن وضع تلك الرجل جواباً عن قولها : « هل من مزيد ، ؟ تلك المطالبة ،
The fourth: The Fire only demands by its saying 'Is there any more?' those who deserve punishment. Therefore if there is no one deserving punishment, placing that foot is not a response to its saying 'Is there any more?' that demand.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument. The Fire's demand 'Is there any more?' (هل من مزيد) is for those who deserve punishment. If there are no more sinners to be punished, then placing the foot does not satisfy that demand. The hadith suggests the foot is placed to stop the demand, but the demand is for sinners, not for the foot. Thus the scenario is logically incoherent.

Key Terms
طلب
مزيد
مستحق العذاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Fire's demand is for any object to fill it.
Correction: The demand is specifically for those deserving punishment, not for objects like God's foot.

Checkpoint

A) More fuel.
B) More people deserving punishment.
C) God's foot.
D) More space.

Key Takeaway
The Fire's demand is for sinners, so placing a foot does not satisfy it.

The Dilemma of Silencing Hellfire
Al-Razi presents a dilemma against literalism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi uses a logical dilemma to critique anthropomorphic interpretations of a hadith about God's foot.

Core Idea
A logical dilemma exposes the absurdity of attributing physical limbs to God.

Al-Razi presents a clever argument against the literal reading of a hadith that mentions God placing His foot in Hellfire. He poses a dilemma: either God can silence Hellfire or He cannot. Both options lead to conclusions incompatible with divine perfection. This illustrates the necessity of metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of the dilemma.
2
Identify the two horns
Find the two conditional statements: 'If He can silence...' and 'If He cannot silence...'. Each is a horn of the dilemma.
3
Understand each horn's implication
First horn: if God can silence, why doesn't He? This suggests He is not doing what is best. Second horn: if He cannot, then the hadith implies impotence and cowardice – both impossible for God.
Think about why each conclusion is problematic.
4
See why literalism fails
Combine the two horns: if either leads to absurdity, the literal meaning cannot be correct. Therefore, ta'wil (metaphorical interpretation) is needed.
This argument defends tanzih (transcendence).
5
Reflect on broader significance
This dilemma is a model for refuting anthropomorphic readings. It shows that logical reasoning is essential in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : إنه تعالى إن قدر على إسكات جهنم فلم يسكت ؟ وإن لم يقدر على إسكاتها فهذا يوهم أنه تعالى جعل رجل نفسه فداء لغيره . وذلك لا يفعله إلا أعجز الناس وأجبنهم .
The fifth: If He, exalted, had power to silence Hellfire, why did He not silence it? And if He does not have power to silence it, then this implies that He made His own foot a ransom for others. And that is not done except by the most incapable and most cowardly of people.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument. It poses a dilemma: if God has the power to silence Hellfire, why does He not silence it directly instead of placing His foot? If He does not have the power to silence it, then the hadith suggests that God sacrifices His foot as a ransom for others, which is characteristic of incapacity and cowardice – attributes not befitting God. Thus the literal reading is untenable.

Key Terms
إسكات
فداء
أعجز
أجبن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think God's foot is a literal physical limb needed to calm Hellfire.
Correction: Al-Razi's dilemma shows that such literalism leads to absurd conclusions (divine impotence or cowardice), so the hadith must be interpreted metaphorically as an expression of God's power.

Checkpoint
What is the dilemma posed in the fifth argument?
A) Either God is just or unjust.
B) Either God can silence the Fire (and does not) or cannot (implying impotence).
C) Either God created the Fire or not.
D) Either the Fire is hot or cold.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's dilemma forces rejection of literal interpretation of God's foot, defending divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you believe God is all-powerful and merciful, does the eternal existence of Hellfire ever make you question His power or mercy? How does Al-Razi's dilemma force you to reconcile God's omnipotence with the reality of Hell? Will you simply accept the mystery, or will you seek a deeper understanding that preserves both His power and justice?
?
When you imagine God 'placing His foot' in Hell, do you feel drawn to a physical, comforting image of God? Al-Razi calls that image absurd and even blasphemous. Are you willing to surrender that comfortable picture for a more transcendent, less anthropomorphic God? What would that cost you personally?
?
Al-Razi says that taking the hadith literally makes God seem like 'the most incapable and cowardly of people.' Have you ever defended a literal interpretation of a scriptural passage simply because it was familiar, without considering how it might diminish God's majesty? Will you now investigate your own assumptions and be willing to reject interpretations that reduce God's perfection?

The Qur'an Contradicts the Hadith on Hellfire
Al-Razi cites Qur'anic verses that contradict the hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses Qur'anic verses to refute a problematic hadith about Hellfire being filled from God's foot.

Core Idea
The Qur'an itself refutes the hadith about God's foot filling Hellfire.

Al-Razi now turns to a straightforward scriptural argument. He cites two Qur'anic verses that explicitly state who will fill Hellfire—Iblis and his followers, or all of jinn and mankind. These verses directly contradict the hadith's claim that Hellfire is filled by God's foot. Let's examine this argument and its implications for evaluating weak reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for Al-Razi's concise style. Note the key phrases: 'نص القرآن' and 'لأملأن جهنم'.
This is a short but powerful argument.
2
Understand the two verses
Identify the two Qur'anic verses Al-Razi quotes. The first addresses Iblis (from Surah Sad), the second addresses all of jinn and mankind (from Surah Hud).
Both verses use the same verb 'la-amla'anna' (I will surely fill).
3
See the contradiction
The hadith says Hellfire is filled from God's foot; the Qur'an says it is filled from specific created beings. Recognize the clear contradiction.
This shows the hadith cannot be authentic if it contradicts the Qur'an.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Al-Razi concludes that these reports (al-akhbar) are very weak (da'ifa jiddan). Understand that this is a scriptural refutation, not just rational.
This is a sample of how kalam scholars used the Qur'an to reject weak hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : إن نص القرآن يدل على أن جهنم تمتلىء من المكانين . قال الله تعالى : ( لأملأن جهنم منك ، ومن تبعك منهم أجمعين ) وقال: ( لأملأن جهنم من الجنة والناس أجمعين ) وهذا على خلاف قولهم إن جهنم تمتلىء من رجل الله - تعالى الله عنه علواً كبيراً ـ فثبت بهذه الوجوه: أن هذه الأخبار ضعيفة جداً .
The sixth: The text of the Qur'an indicates that Hellfire will be filled from two sources. God most high said: 'I will surely fill Hellfire with you and with those who follow you among them all' and He said: 'I will surely fill Hellfire with jinn and people all together.' And this contradicts their saying that Hellfire is filled from the foot of God – exalted is God above that greatly. Thus it is established by these points that these reports are very weak.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument, which is scriptural. The Qur'an explicitly states that Hellfire will be filled with specific groups: Iblis and his followers (in Surah Sad), and all of jinn and mankind (in Surah Hud). This directly contradicts the hadith's claim that Hellfire is filled by God's foot. The author then concludes that these reports (al-akhbar) are very weak (da'ifa jiddan).

Key Terms

تملئة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all hadith about the afterlife are as authoritative as the Qur'an.
Correction: This argument shows that if a hadith directly contradicts the Qur'an, it is considered very weak and rejected.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A hadith that directly contradicts the clear text of the Qur'an must be rejected as very weak.

Three Rational Objections to Literalistic Hadith Interpretation
Imam al-Razi's fourth to sixth objections against literal Hell hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three reasoned arguments from Imam al-Razi against taking hadith about Hell and God's foot literally, and why they support metaphorical interpretation.

Core Idea
Literal readings of divine attributes can lead to absurdities about God's power.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi continues his critique of anthropomorphic interpretations. After earlier points, he now presents three additional objections focusing on the internal logic of the hadith. These objections highlight how a literal reading leads to theological absurdities, reinforcing the necessity of metaphorical interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Razi structures his objections with numbered points.
The passage begins with a sentence that sets the context: negating empty places in Hell.
2
Identify the three objections
The passage contains three distinct objections (fourth, fifth, sixth). For each, identify the core argument and the specific absurdity it highlights.
The fourth objection focuses on the Fire's demand 'Is there any more?'
3
Examine the dilemma in the fifth objection
The fifth objection presents a dilemma: if God can silence Hell and does not, why not? If He cannot, that implies weakness. Consider why this is a powerful argument against literalism.
This argument uses human analogy to expose an absurdity.
4
Reflect on the Qur'anic basis of the sixth objection
The sixth objection quotes the Qur'an to show that Hell is filled from jinn and humans, not from God's foot. Notice how al-Razi uses scripture to refute a literal reading.
This shows that the hadith conflict with explicit Qur'anic statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على أن ينفى المواضع الخاليه عن جهنم ، حتى لا تصير ملجأ إلى وضع القدم فيها . الرابع : إن النار إنما تطلب بقولها : هل من مزيد ؟ : الذين يستحقون العذاب . ولذلك إذا لم يكن مستحقاً للعذاب ، لم يكن وضع تلك الرجل جواباً عن قولها : « هل من مزيد ، ؟ تلك المطالبة ، الخامس : إنه تعالى إن قدر على إسكات جهنم عن فلم يسكت ؟ وإن لم يقدر على إسكانها فهذا يوهم أنه تعالى جعل رجل نفسه فداء لغيره . وذلك لا يفعال إلا أعجز الناس وأجبنهم . السادس : إن نص القرآن يدل على أن جهنم تمتلىء من المكانين . قال الله تعالى : ( لأملان جهنم منك ، ومن تبعك منهم أجمعين ) وقال: «لا ملان جهم من الجنة والناس أجمعين، وهذا على خلاف قولهم إن جهنم تمتلىء من رجل الله - تعالى الله عنه علواً كبيراً ـ فثبت بهذه الوجوه: أن هذه الأخبار ضعيفة جداً .
That it negates the empty places of Hell, so that they do not become a refuge for placing the foot in them. Fourth: The Fire only seeks by its saying 'Is there any more?' those who deserve the punishment. Therefore, if there is no one deserving punishment, placing that foot is not an answer to its saying 'Is there any more?' of that demand. Fifth: If He, exalted, is able to silence Hell about [it] and did not silence it? And if He is not able to silence it, then this suggests that He, exalted, made His own foot a ransom for others. And that is not done except by the weakest of people and the most cowardly. Sixth: The text of the Qur'an indicates that Hell is filled from two places. Allah said: 'I will surely fill Hell with you and with those who follow you, all together.' And He said: 'I will surely fill Hell with jinn and people, all together.' And this is contrary to their saying that Hell is filled from the foot of Allah – exalted is Allah above that greatly. So it is established by these points that these reports are very weak.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments (the fourth, fifth, and sixth) against the literal interpretation of hadith that speak of Hell being filled from Allah's foot. The fourth objection argues that the Fire's request 'Is there any more?' is addressed to those deserving punishment; if there are none, placing a foot does not answer that demand. The fifth objection points out a dilemma: if Allah is able to silence Hell but does not, why not? And if He is unable, that implies He made His own foot a ransom, which is an act of the weakest and most cowardly. The sixth objection cites the Qur'an: verses state that Hell will be filled with jinn and humans, not from Allah's foot. The conclusion is that these reports are very weak.

تأويل (interpretation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objections are against the hadith themselves, rejecting them entirely.
Correction: The objections target only the literal reading; Imam al-Razi later allows metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil), preserving the validity of the reports when understood figuratively.

Key Takeaway
Literal readings of hadith about divine attributes can lead to theological contradictions that are resolved through metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil).

Metaphorical Interpretation of Anthropomorphic Hadith
Argues that even if authentic, such hadith can be interpreted metaphorically, giving examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim scholars use metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) to reconcile anthropomorphic hadith with divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Metaphorical interpretation is a natural, everyday use of language, not a forced distortion.

This passage tackles a crucial question: what do we do with hadith that seem to describe Allah in human terms? The author, Imam Razi, argues that even if such narrations are authentic, they can be understood metaphorically. He provides concrete examples from common speech and a prophetic hadith to show that metaphorical interpretation is a natural and valid approach. This lesson unpacks his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses a conditional: 'if we assume authenticity'.
2
Identify the metaphorical examples
The passage gives two types of examples: one from everyday speech about resolving disputes, and one from a specific hadith. Find each and note how they illustrate metaphorical usage.
The first example shows that 'placing a foot' is a common metaphor for involvement.
3
Understand the hadith interpretation
The hadith about Allah reclining is interpreted as a metaphor for the completion of an action. Make sure you see how the author connects the metaphor to the meaning of 'finishing' a task.
The Prophet used this expression to symbolize completion, not to describe a physical act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نقول : إن قلنا بتقدير صحة هذه الألفاظ فهى محتملة للتأويل . فإن من سعى لإزالة خصومة ، وتسكين فتنة ، صح أن يقال: إن فلانا وضع رجله في هذه الواقعة ، ووضع قدمه فيها . ويقال في المجاز المتعارف الظاهر : لك قدم مبارك ، وضع قدمك فيها حتى يصلح ويزول الشر. فهذا مجاز سانغ . وحمل اللفظ عليه محتمل . ومن هذا الباب : ماروى أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : « لما قضى الله بين خلقه استلقى على قفاه . ثم وضع إحدى رجليه على الأخرى ، ثم قال : لا ينبغي لأحد أن يفعل مثل هذا، والتأويل : أن المباشر اعمل إذا أتمة استلقى على قفاه . فعيز التي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن تتميم الأمر بهذه العبارة . وكذا القول في وضع إحدى الرجلين على الأخرى ) وبالله التوفيق
Then we say: If we assume the authenticity of these expressions, they are susceptible to interpretation. For whoever strives to remove a dispute and calm a disturbance, it is correct to say: So-and-so placed his foot in this incident, and placed his foot in it. And it is said in common apparent metaphor: You have a blessed foot, place your foot in it so that it may improve and evil may go away. So this is a permissible metaphor. And carrying the expression upon it is possible. And from this category is what is narrated that the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'When Allah finished judging between His creation, He reclined on His back, then placed one of His legs over the other, then said: It is not befitting for anyone to do like this.' And the interpretation: The direct agent of the work, when he finishes it, reclines on his back. So the Prophet, peace be upon him, expressed the completion of the matter by this phrase. And likewise the saying about placing one leg over the other. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage discusses the metaphorical interpretation of anthropomorphic expressions in hadith. The author first states that even if we grant the authenticity of such expressions (like 'foot of Allah'), they can be interpreted metaphorically. He gives a human example: when someone resolves a dispute, it is common to say 'he placed his foot in this incident' – this is a permissible metaphor (مجاز سانغ). Similarly, the phrase 'you have a blessed foot' is used metaphorically for bringing good. Then he connects this to a specific hadith: the Prophet narrated that after finishing judgment, Allah reclined and placed one leg over the other. The interpretation given is that this is a metaphor for the completion of the work (the 'direct agent' when finished reclines). Thus, the Prophet used this expression to indicate completion. The same applies to the hadith about placing one leg over the other. The passage concludes with 'With Allah is success.'

Key Terms
مجاز
تأويل
رجل
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think metaphorical interpretation of hadith is a far-fetched or arbitrary method to avoid literalism.
Correction: The author shows that metaphorical usage is natural and widespread in everyday Arabic, making ta'wil a legitimate and reasonable approach.

Checkpoint

A) It shows God's exhaustion
B) It indicates the completion of an action
C) It proves God has a body
D) It is a literal description of judgment

Key Takeaway
Metaphorical interpretation is a natural and valid method to interpret anthropomorphic hadith while upholding divine transcendence.

Metaphorical Interpretation of the Hadith of Reclining
Al-Razi explains that the hadith about God reclining is a permissible metaphor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi interprets the hadith about God reclining as a permissible metaphor signifying completion, preserving divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The hadith of reclining is a metaphor for completion, not a literal description of God.

This lesson examines a specific hadith that seems to attribute physical actions to God. Imam al-Razi clarifies that the Prophet used metaphorical language to describe the completion of God's judgment. By understanding this, we see how Muslim theologians protect God's transcendence while affirming the scriptural text.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the key terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage begins with 'فهذا مجاز سانغ' which sets the tone.
2
Identify key terms
Locate the terms مجاز (metaphor), تأويل (interpretation), استلقاء (reclining), and تتميم الأمر (completion of the affair). Understand their roles.
3
Understand the author's argument
Al-Razi states the hadith is a permissible metaphor. He explains that the reclining gesture is an expression for completing a task, not a literal action of God.
The Prophet used a human analogy to convey the end of God's judgment.
4
Connect to broader principle
This is an example of ta'wil (metaphorical interpretation) used to reconcile scriptural expressions with divine transcendence.
It shows how theologians handle anthropomorphic descriptions.
5
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that the hadith describes a literal physical act. Al-Razi's interpretation corrects this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشر. فهذا مجاز سانغ. وحمل اللفظ عليه محتمل. ومن هذا الباب: ما روى أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال: «لما قضى الله بين خلقه استلقى على قفاه، ثم وضع إحدى رجليه على الأخرى، ثم قال: لا ينبغي لأحد أن يفعل مثل هذا». والتأويل: أن المباشر للعمل إذا أتمه استلقى على قفاه، فعبر النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم عن تتميم الأمر بهذه العبارة. وكذا القول في وضع إحدى الرجلين على الأخرى. وبالله التوفيق.
This is a permissible metaphor. Interpreting the word in this way is plausible. From this category is what is narrated that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'When God finished judging between His creation, He reclined on His back, then placed one of His legs over the other, then said: None should do like this.' The interpretation: the one directly engaged in the work, when he completes it, reclines on his back. So the Prophet (peace be upon him) expressed the completion of the affair with this phrase. The same applies to placing one leg over the other. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of a metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) of a hadith that seems to attribute physical actions to God. The author begins by stating that the hadith is a 'permissible metaphor' (majaz sa'igh), meaning it is acceptable to interpret the words figuratively. He then explains the meaning of the hadith: when the Prophet said 'When God finished judging... he reclined...', the intended meaning is that the one who completes a task (i.e., God) metaphorically 'reclines upon completing it,' just as a person might physically recline after finishing hard work. The Prophet used this expression to convey the completion of God's judgment. Similarly, the placement of one leg over the other is metaphorical. The author emphasizes that this interpretation is plausible and preserves God's transcendence.

Key Takeaway

Why Laughter Cannot Be Attributed to God
Al-Razi states that laughter is impossible for God and provides a Qur'anic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Razi argues that the attribute of laughter cannot be literally attributed to God, based on the Qur'anic verse (53:43) and the principle of divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God causes laughter, but does not laugh — the Qur'an proves it.

This lesson tackles a subtle theological issue: how to understand hadith that describe God laughing. Al-Razi, following the Ash'ari tradition, demonstrates that such descriptions cannot be taken literally. He cites a key Qur'anic verse to show that God's action is to cause laughter and weeping, not to experience them Himself. This is a lesson in interpreting scripture according to what befits God's majesty.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice that Al-Razi first presents two hadith reports where God is described as laughing, then states his main claim.
The second proof is incomplete in the OCR, so focus on the first proof.
2
Identify Al-Razi's main claim
Find the sentence where Al-Razi states that the reality of laughter is impossible (محال) with respect to God. This is the thesis he will prove.
The phrase is 'أن حقيقة الضحك على الله تعالى محال'.
3
Examine the first proof
Look at the Qur'anic verse (53:43) and Al-Razi's interpretation. He argues that the verse says God makes laugh and makes weep, not that He laughs and weeps. The action befitting God is to cause, not to experience.
This is a classic tanzih argument: attributes must be understood in a way that suits divine perfection.
4
Consider the second proof (incomplete)
The text begins a second proof but cuts off. Think about what additional argument Al-Razi might have made, possibly from another verse or rational consideration.
The second proof starts with 'والثاني : إن' but is not available.
5
Reflect on the principle
Ask yourself: How does this argument illustrate the broader principle that God's attributes must be interpreted according to what befits His majesty?
This connects to the concept of tanzih (transcendence) in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل السابع والعشرون في الضحك
هذا الوصف لم يرد في القرآن، لكنه ورد في الخبر. روى صاحب شرح السنة - رحمه الله - في باب آخر من يخرج من النار، عن ابن مسعود - رضي الله عنه - حديثاً طويلاً في صفة من أخرجه الله بفضله من النار. قال: «فيسمع أصوات أهل الجنة فيقول: أي رب أدخلنيها. فيقول الله: يا ابن آدم، أيرضيك أن أعطيك الدنيا ومثلها؟ فيقول: أي رب، أتستهزئ مني وأنت رب العالمين؟ فضحك ابن مسعود، وقال: ألا تسألوني أضحك؟ فقالوا: مم تضحك؟ فقال: هكذا ضحك رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقالوا: ومم يضحك رسول الله؟ فقال: من ضحك رب العالمين. فيقول الله: إني لا أستهزئ بك، وأنا على ما أشاء قدير». وذكر أيضاً في أول هذا الباب حديثاً طويلاً عن أبي هريرة - رضي الله عنه - إلى أن قال: «ثم يقول: يا رب أدخلني الجنة. فيقول الله: أو لست قد زعمت أن لا تسألني غيره؟ ويلك يا ابن آدم، ما أغدرك! فيقول: يا رب لا تجعلني أشقى خلقك. فلا يزال يدعو حتى يضحك. فإذا ضحك منه، أذن الله له بالدخول في الجنة».
واعلم: أن حقيقة الضحك على الله تعالى محال. ويدل عليه وجوه: الأول: قوله تعالى: (وأنه هو أضحك وأبكى) يبين أن اللائق به أن يضحك ويبكي. فأما الضحك والبكاء فلا يليقان به. والثاني: إن [؟]
Chapter Twenty-Seven: On Laughter. This attribute does not occur in the Qur'an, but it occurs in the report. The author of Sharh al-Sunnah - may God have mercy on him - narrated in the chapter on 'The Last to Come Out of the Fire' from Ibn Mas'ud - may God be pleased with him - a long hadith describing one whom God brings out of the Fire by His grace. He said: 'He hears the voices of the people of Paradise and says: O Lord, admit me to it. God says: O son of Adam, would it please you that I give you the world and the like of it? He says: O Lord, are You mocking me while You are the Lord of the worlds? Ibn Mas'ud laughed and said: Will you not ask me why I laugh? They said: What makes you laugh? He said: Thus laughed the Messenger of God (peace be upon him). They said: And what makes the Messenger of God laugh? He said: At the laughter of the Lord of the worlds. God says: I am not mocking you, and I am able to do what I will.' He also mentioned at the beginning of this chapter a long hadith from Abu Hurayra - may God be pleased with him - up to his saying: 'Then he says: O Lord, admit me to Paradise. God says: Did you not claim that you would ask Me for nothing else? Woe to you, O son of Adam, how treacherous you are! He says: O Lord, do not make me the most wretched of Your creation. He keeps supplicating until He laughs. When He laughs at him, He permits him to enter Paradise.' Know that the reality of laughter is impossible with respect to God Most High. Evidence for that includes: First: His saying (And that He is the One who makes laugh and makes weep) indicates that what befits Him is that He makes laugh and makes weep. As for laughter and weeping themselves, they are not befitting to Him. Second: For [؟]
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is an argument that the attribute of laughter (al-dahk) cannot be attributed to God in a literal sense. The author begins by noting that this attribute does not appear in the Qur'an but only in hadith. He then reports two hadiths: one from Ibn Mas'ud and one from Abu Hurayra, in which God is described as laughing. After presenting the scriptural evidence, the author states categorically: 'Know that the reality of laughter is impossible with respect to God Most High.' He then provides evidence (wujuh). The first proof is from the Qur'anic verse (53:43): 'And that He is the One who makes laugh and makes weep.' The author argues that this verse shows that the action appropriate (al-la'iq) to God is to cause laughter and weeping, not to laugh and weep Himself. Laughter and weeping are creaturely acts and do not befit the divine majesty. The second proof is begun but cut off in the available text. The overall argument is that the hadith about God laughing must be interpreted metaphorically.

Key Terms
الضحك
البكاء
اللائق
أضحك وأبكى
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that hadith describing God laughing mean He literally laughs like a human.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the Qur'an attributes causing laughter to God, not the act of laughing. The hadith must be interpreted metaphorically to preserve divine transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof in the passage, why is literal laughter impossible for God?
A) Because the Qur'an explicitly denies it
B) Because the Qur'an says God makes laugh and weep, not that He laughs
C) Because laughter is a sign of weakness
D) Because only humans laugh

Key Takeaway
Literal laughter is impossible for God because the Qur'an shows His action is to cause laughter and weeping, not to experience them.

?
The author insists that what befits God is to cause laughter, not to laugh. How does this distinction affect the way you understand God's interaction with His creation? Does it make Him seem more distant or more majestic? How does that change your relationship with Him?

The Impossibility of Literal Divine Laughter and Four Proofs
Razi proves the impossibility of literal divine laughter with four arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why literal laughter cannot be attributed to God, based on four rational and scriptural proofs from Imam Razi.

Core Idea
God causes laughter, but He does not laugh—He is beyond physical and emotional change.

A common misconception is that since God is described as causing laughter, He must also laugh. However, Imam Razi clarifies that causing and experiencing are entirely different. In this passage, he presents four compelling proofs—from the Quran, from the nature of laughter, from logical consistency, and from the cause of laughter—to show that literal divine laughter is impossible. Let us examine each proof carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of Razi's argument. Notice that he starts with a firm assertion and then provides four distinct proofs.
Don't worry about details yet; just absorb the structure.
2
Analyze each proof individually
Go through each of the four proofs one by one. For each, identify the core logic: what is the premise and how does it lead to the conclusion?
Use the guided breakdown to help you see the structure.
3
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Consider the misconception that causing laughter implies laughing. How does the first proof directly address this? Why is the distinction between causing and experiencing important in Islamic theology?
This distinction is key to understanding divine transcendence.
4
Connect the proofs to the overall claim
After studying all four proofs, ask yourself: how do they collectively show that literal laughter is impossible for God? Does each proof target a different aspect (Quran, physicality, consistency, causation)?
This helps you see the comprehensiveness of Razi's argument.
5
Test your understanding
Complete the checkpoint quiz and the activity. If you can explain each proof in your own words, you have understood the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أن حقيقة الضحك على الله تعالى محال . ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : قوله تعالى : ( وأنه هو أضحك وأبكي ) يبين أن اللائق به أن يضحك ويبكي . فأما الضحك والبكاء ، فلا يليقان به . والثاني : إن الضحك سنح يحصل فى جلد الوجه ، مع حصول الفرح في القلب وهو على الله تعالى محال . والثالث : لو جاز الضحك عليه ، لجاز البكاء عليه . وقد التزمه بعض الحمقى، وزعم : أنه بكى على أهل طوفان نوح عليه السلام . وهذا جهل شديد . فإنه تعالى هو الذي خلق الطوفان . فإن كرهه فلم خلقه ؟ وإن لم يكرهه ، فلم ينكر عليه ؟ الرابع : إن الضحك إنما يتولد من التعجب ، والتعجب حالة تحصل للإنسان عند الجهل بالسبب. وذلك في حق عالم الغيب والشهادة محال .
Know that the reality of laughter with respect to God Almighty is impossible. This is indicated by several aspects: First: His statement, 'And that it is He who causes laughter and weeping' (53:43) shows that what is appropriate to Him is to cause laughter and weeping. As for laughter and weeping themselves, they are not appropriate to Him. Second: Laughter occurs with wrinkling in the skin of the face, along with the occurrence of joy in the heart, and this is impossible with respect to God Almighty. Third: If laughter were permissible for Him, then weeping would also be permissible for Him. And some fools have committed to this and claimed that He wept over the people of the flood of Noah, peace be upon him. This is severe ignorance. For He, exalted, is the one who created the flood. If He disliked it, then why did He create it? And if He did not dislike it, then why condemn it? Fourth: Laughter is only generated from wonder, and wonder is a state that occurs in a person due to ignorance of the cause. That is impossible in the case of the Knower of the unseen and the seen.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlal) that literal laughter cannot be attributed to God. The author first states the claim, then provides four proofs. The first proof is from the Qur'an: God ascribes to Himself 'causing laughter and weeping' (أضحك وأبكي), not laughing and weeping. This shows that He creates these actions in creatures, not that He Himself experiences them. The second proof is that laughter is a physical phenomenon involving facial skin and emotional joy, both of which are impossible for God. The third proof is that if laughter were possible, weeping would also be possible, and some foolishly claimed God wept over the flood, which is absurd because God created the flood. The fourth proof is that laughter arises from wonder, which stems from ignorance, impossible for the All-Knowing. Thus all four proofs show the impossibility of literal divine laughter.

Key Terms
ضحك
استحالة
تعجب
جهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because the Qur'an says 'He causes laughter,' God must also laugh.
Correction: The verse actually distinguishes between causing and experiencing; God causes laughter in others, but does not laugh Himself.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the four proofs Razi gives for the impossibility of literal divine laughter?
A) The Qur'anic verse distinguishes causing laughter from laughing.
B) Laughter involves physical changes in the face.
C) Laughter is a form of worship and God is beyond worship.
D) Laughter arises from wonder, which implies ignorance.

Key Takeaway
Literal divine laughter is impossible because it implies physical change, emotional flux, or ignorance—all incompatible with God's perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever pictured God feeling joy or sorrow like you do? How does Razi's argument that laughter involves physical change and ignorance force you to reconsider those mental images? What will you do when such images arise again?
?
Razi calls those who believed God wept over the flood 'fools' because He created it. Have you ever felt that God should feel sorry for your suffering, as if He didn't plan it? How would you correct that thought using the distinction between causing and experiencing?
?
If laughter stems from wonder and ignorance, and God is all-knowing, then every time you laugh, you are reminded of your own limitation. Does that humble you or make you uncomfortable? Will you let this truth deepen your awe of God's perfect knowledge?

Three Ta'wil for Divine Laughter and a Grammatical Suggestion
Razi enumerates three ta'wil for divine laughter and proposes a grammatical misreading in another hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three metaphorical interpretations Razi offers to reconcile hadith on divine laughter with divine transcendence, and a possible grammatical misreading in a related hadith.

Core Idea
Hadith about divine laughter can be interpreted metaphorically through grammar, simile, and metaphor.

Imam al-Razi, a master of both kalam and Arabic language, approaches hadith that seem to attribute anthropomorphic qualities to Allah with careful linguistic analysis. In this passage, he presents three distinct ta'wil (interpretive strategies) for a hadith mentioning 'laughter of the Lord,' and then addresses a specific case from Abu Huraira by suggesting a possible grammatical misreading. This lesson will walk through each interpretation and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first ta'wil
The first interpretation is grammatical: the verbal noun 'laughter' can be attributed to God as the cause, not the agent. Read the passage and note how Sanusi explains the attribution of masdar to subject vs object.
This interpretation removes direct attribution of laughter to Allah.
2
Understand the second ta'wil
The second interpretation is a hypothetical simile: if God were like kings, the statement would be causing laughter, but God is not like that. This is a concession to the listener's understanding.
3
Understand the third ta'wil
The third interpretation treats 'laughter' as a metaphor (isti'ara) for divine satisfaction and permission. This is a well-known rhetorical device.
This is the most common ta'wil among later scholars.
4
Consider the i'rab error suggestion
Razi suggests that in the hadith of Abu Huraira, a grammatical error (i'rab) may have occurred. The correct reading should be causative: 'God makes the angels laugh.' Evidence is the disagreement between Abu Huraira and Abu Sa'id about the reward amount, showing textual imprecision.
This shows that hadith texts can have variants that change the meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا ثبت هذا ، فنقول : وجه التأويل فيه من وجوه : أحدها : إن المصدر كما يحسن إضافته إلى المفعول ، فكذلك يحسن إضافته إلى الفاعل. فقوله: « ضحكت من ضحك الرب، أى من الضحك الحاصل في ذاتي ، بسبب أن الرب خلق ذلك الضحك . الثاني : أن يكون المراد : أنه تعالى لو كان ممن يضحك كالملوك ، كان هذا القول مضحكاله . الثالث : أن عمل الضحك على حصول الرضى والإذن، وهذا نوع مشهور من الاستعارة . و أما حديث أبي هريرة - رضي الله عنه أن العبد يقول : وهو لا تجعلني أشقى خلقك ، فيضحك الله منه ، فيجوز أن يكون قد وقع الغلط في الإعراب . وكان الحق : ( فيضحك الله منه ، أي يضحك الله الملائكة من ذلك القول . والذي يدل على أن ما ذكرناه ، محتمل : أن أبا هريرة ، وأبا سعيد الخدرى رضى الله عنهما اختلفا في قدر عطية ذلك الرجل . فقال أبو سعيد : يعطيه الله ذلك المطلوب ، وعشرة أمثاله . وقال أبو هريرة: يعطيه الله ذلك، ومثله معه وهذا الاختلاف بينهما في الحديث مذكور في كل كتب الأحاديث . ولما لم يضبط هذا الموضع من الخبر تجوز عدم الضبط في ذلك الإعراب ) وبالله التوفيق )
If this is established, then we say: The interpretation (ta'wil) of it is in several ways: One: Just as a verbal noun (masdar) can be properly attributed to the object, so also it can be properly attributed to the subject. So his statement, 'I laughed from the laughter of the Lord' means from the laughter that occurred in my essence, because the Lord created that laughter. Second: It could mean that if He, exalted, were someone who laughs like kings, this statement would be what made him laugh. Third: The effect of laughter is the occurrence of satisfaction and permission, and this is a well-known type of metaphor (isti'ara). As for the hadith of Abu Huraira, may God be pleased with him, that the servant says, 'My Lord, do not make me the most miserable of Your creation,' and God laughs at him, it is possible that a grammatical error occurred in the inflection (i'rab). The correct would be: 'God causes [the angels] to laugh at him,' i.e., God makes the angels laugh at that statement. What indicates that what we have mentioned is plausible is that Abu Huraira and Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, may God be pleased with both, disagreed about the amount of the gift to that man. Abu Sa'id said: God gives him that which he sought and ten times its like. Abu Huraira said: God gives him that and its like with it. This disagreement between them regarding the hadith is mentioned in all books of hadith. Since this part of the report was not precisely recorded, the lack of precision in that inflection (i'rab) is acceptable. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage provides interpretive solutions for hadith that appear to attribute laughter to God. The author first gives three metaphorical interpretations. The first is grammatical: the verbal noun 'laughter' can be attributed to God as the cause (subject) or as the object, so 'laughter of the Lord' means laughter caused by God. The second interpretation suggests a hypothetical if God were like kings, but the statement is just causing laughter. The third is a metaphor: laughter indicates divine pleasure and permission. Then the author addresses a specific hadith from Abu Huraira where God is said to laugh at a servant's plea. He suggests a possible grammatical error (i'rab): the verb should be read as causative, meaning God makes the angels laugh, not that God Himself laughs. Evidence for this possibility is a disagreement between Abu Huraira and Abu Sa'id al-Khudri about details of the same hadith, showing that the wording might not be precisely preserved. Thus the author upholds divine transcendence while accepting the possibility of textual variants.

Key Terms
تأويل
استعارة
إضافة
إعراب
مصدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that a single hadith with a problematic wording must be taken literally rather than considering alternative readings or interpretations.
Correction: Razi shows that linguistic analysis, metaphor, and even textual variants can resolve apparent anthropomorphisms without rejecting the hadith.

Checkpoint
What is the third interpretation (ta'wil) Razi suggests for hadith mentioning divine laughter?
A) It is a grammatical error in the verbal noun attribution.
B) It is a metaphor for divine satisfaction and permission.
C) It means God laughs like kings.
D) It refers to the angels laughing.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Razi demonstrates that apparent anthropomorphic descriptions in hadith can be reconciled with divine transcendence through linguistic analysis and the recognition of textual variants.

Negating Attributes: When and How
Razi discusses when and how negation of attributes is permissible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why merely negating an attribute from Allah can be problematic and learn the proper way to negate attributes based on Razi's methodology.

Core Idea
Negating attributes requires careful explanation to avoid denying what is befitting of Allah.

Razi addresses a subtle but critical methodological question: when we say an attribute does not apply to Allah, can we just use the negative form? He shows that simple negation can mislead, because it might imply that the attribute in its praiseworthy sense is being denied. Instead, we must clarify that the attribute in its human, limited sense is impossible for Allah. Quranic verses that appear as negations are actually negating the possibility of such descriptions being valid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note Razi's question and the two positions he presents.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice Razi's distinction between mere negation (which can mislead) and negation that clarifies impossibility. Find the examples he gives from the Quran.
Pay attention to the phrase 'نفي صحة الاتصاف'.
3
Apply the principle
Consider why saying 'Allah does not sleep' is different from saying 'Allah is not modest' without explanation. How does Razi's rule help us?
Think about the difference between negating an attribute and negating the possibility of description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: إن الذي لا يجوز على الله تعالى من جنس هذه الأوصاف. فهل يجوز ذكره على سبيل النفي عن الله تعالى؟ قال بعضهم: إنه لا يجوز إطلاق هذه الألفاظ على طريقة النفي، بل يجب أن يقال: إنه تعالى لا يوصف بها. فأما أن يقال: إنه لا يستحي ويطلق ذلك في حال. لأنه يوهم نفي ما يجوز عليه. وما ذكره الله تعالى في كتابه من قوله: لا تأخذه سنة ولا نوم، ولم يلد ولم يولد، فهو وإن كان في صورة النفي، لكنه ليس في الحقيقة. بل المراد منه: نفي صحة الاتصاف. وكذا قوله: ما كان الله أن يتخذ من ولد، وقوله: ما اتخذ الله من ولد، وقوله: وهو يطعم ولا يطعم. وليس كل ما ورد في القرآن إطلاقه، جاز أن يطلق في المخاطبات، بل الحق: أنه لا يجوز إطلاق ذلك إلا مع بيان أنه محال ممتنع في حق الله تعالى.
And the second: that which is not permissible for Allah Almighty of this kind of attributes. So is it permissible to mention it by way of negation (nafy) from Allah Almighty? Some said: It is not permissible to utter these expressions in the manner of negation; rather, it must be said: He, exalted, is not described by them. As for saying: He is not modest and uttering that unconditionally? Because it gives the impression of negating something that may be attributable to Him. And what Allah Almighty mentioned in His Book, such as His saying: 'Slumber does not overtake Him nor sleep' (2:255), and 'He did not beget nor was He begotten' (112:3), even though it is in the form of negation, it is not in reality [mere negation]. Rather, what is meant by it is negation of the validity of being described [by such things]. And similarly His saying: 'It is not for Allah to take a son' (19:35), and His saying: 'Allah has not taken a son' (23:91), and His saying: 'And He feeds but is not fed' (6:14). And not everything that occurs in the Qur'an as an utterance may be uttered in discourse [freely]; rather, the truth is that it is not permissible to utter it except with the clarification that it is impossible and prevented with respect to Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents the second interpretation regarding attributes that are not permissible for Allah. Razi asks: can we use these expressions in a negative form—e.g., 'Allah is not modest'? Some scholars said it is not permissible to use such negations unconditionally, because it might imply that the attribute in its true sense (like haya' as a praiseworthy quality) is being denied of Allah, which is not intended. Instead, one should say 'Allah is not described by such [human] attributes.' Razi then notes that certain Quranic negations (like 'Slumber does not overtake Him', 'He did not beget') appear in the form of negation, but their real purpose is not mere negation; they negate the very possibility of such descriptions being valid for Allah. He adds that not every expression found in the Quran may be freely used in human discourse; rather, such negations may only be uttered with the clarification that the attribute is impossible and prohibited (muhal mumtani') in relation to Allah. This guards against a potential misunderstanding that would negate attributes that are actually befitting of Allah.

Key Terms
نفي
إطلاق
محال ممتنع
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simply saying 'Allah is not X' is sufficient negation.
Correction: Razi teaches that such negation can imply denial of a praiseworthy quality; one must clarify that the attribute in its human sense is impossible for Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why is it problematic to say 'Allah is not modest' without clarification?
A) Because it is a lie
B) Because it might imply negation of a quality worthy of Allah
C) Because it contradicts the Quran
D) Because it is disrespectful

Key Takeaway
When negating attributes from Allah, one must clarify that the human-limited form of the attribute is impossible, not that the praiseworthy sense is denied.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah's transcendence, have you ever inadvertently described His perfect attributes in terms that imply human limitations? How can Razi's teaching guard your tongue so that every negation you utter is accompanied by the recognition that His perfection is beyond compare?
?
Razi warns that not every Quranic expression can be casually repeated. Have you ever quoted a verse or phrase about Allah without fully understanding its context and implications? What step will you take today to ensure that your words about Allah are always precise and reverent?
?
How often do you catch yourself thinking that Allah is 'free from' something in a way that might still carry a human conceptual framework? What would it look like for you to actively clarify in your heart, as Razi instructs, that what you are negating is only the limited human form, not the absolute perfection?

First Three Quranic Proofs for Direction
Razi enumerates the first three Quranic proofs for direction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three categories of Quranic verses that anthropomorphists use to argue that Allah has a direction.

Core Idea
Anthropomorphists cite istiwa', 'above,' and 'highness' verses to claim Allah has direction.

Imam Razi begins the final chapter by laying out the evidence that those who affirm a direction for Allah rely on. He identifies four sources: the Quran, prophetic reports, rational arguments, and combined arguments. He then starts detailing the Quranic proofs, which he says number ten aspects. In this passage, we see the first three: verses about Allah's 'istiwa'' over the Throne, verses using the word 'fawq' (above), and verses using words of highness like 'aliyy' and 'a'laa'. It is important to note that Razi is reporting these claims, not endorsing them—he will later rebut them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Razi introduces four types of evidence before focusing on the Quranic ten aspects.
The passage is a straightforward enumeration of the first three Quranic proof categories.
2
Identify the three Quranic proof categories
List the three categories: (1) verses containing 'istiwa'' over the Throne, (2) verses containing 'fawq' (above), (3) verses containing 'uluww' (highness).
Each category is introduced with 'التمسك بـ'.
3
Understand the examples given
For each category, notice the specific verses Razi quotes. For 'fawq' he gives three verses; for 'uluww' he gives four verses and a hadith.
These are the actual proof texts used by anthropomorphists.
4
Reflect on the author’s stance
Recognize that Razi is neutrally reporting the arguments of his opponents. He does not comment on their validity here.
This is crucial for understanding the rest of the chapter.
5
Check your understanding
Take the multiple-choice question to confirm you caught the number of aspects mentioned overall.
The answer is ten (عشرة).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثلاثون في ما يتمسكون به في إثبات الجهة لله تعالى تمسكوا في ذلك بالقرآن والأخبار والمعقول والوجوه المركبة من السمع والعقل. أما القرآن: فمن عشرة أوجه: الأول: التمسك بالآيات السنة الواردة بلفظ الاستواء على العرش. الثاني: التمسك بالآيات المشتملة على لفظ الفوق. فقد قال تعالى: وهو القاهر فوق عباده وهو الحكيم الخبير، وقال: وهو القاهر فوق عباده ويرسل عليكم حفظة، وقال: يخافون ربهم من فوقهم. الثالث: الآيات المشتملة على لفظ العلو. كقوله تعالى: وهو العلي العظيم، وقوله تعالى: وهو العلي الكبير، وقوله: سبح اسم ربك الأعلى، وقوله: إلا ابتغاء وجه ربه الأعلى، وأيضا: تواتر النقل في قوله تعالى: سبحان الأعلى.
Chapter Thirty: On what they cling to in proving direction for Allah Almighty. They cling in that to the Quran, the reports, the rational, and the combined aspects from transmitted and rational. As for the Quran: it is from ten aspects: The first: clinging to the six verses that contain the expression of istiwa' over the Throne. The second: clinging to the verses that contain the expression of 'above'. He said, the Exalted: 'And He is the Subduer above His servants, and He is the All-Wise, the All-Aware,' and He said: 'And He is the Subduer above His servants, and He sends over you guardians,' and He said: 'They fear their Lord from above them.' The third: the verses that contain the expression of highness, such as His saying, the Exalted: 'And He is the Most High, the Most Great,' and His saying: 'And He is the Most High, the Great,' and His saying: 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High,' and His saying: 'But only seeking the Face of his Lord, the Most High,' and also: the widely transmitted report of His saying: 'Glory be to the Most High.'
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the thirtieth chapter, which is structured as an enumeration of the proofs that the anthropomorphists (those who affirm a direction for Allah) use to support their position. Imam Razi first states that these people cling to four types of evidence: the Quran, the reports (hadith), rational arguments, and combined arguments from transmitted and rational sources. Then he begins detailing the Quranic evidence, which he says consists of ten aspects. In this excerpt, he covers the first three aspects. The first is clinging to the six verses that use the expression 'istiwa' over the Throne' (e.g., 'Then He rose over the Throne'). The second is clinging to verses using the word 'fawq' (above), such as 'He is the Subduer above His servants' and 'they fear their Lord from above them.' The third is clinging to verses using the word 'uluww' (highness), such as 'He is the Most High, the Most Great' and 'Glorify the name of your Lord, the Most High.' The author does not comment here on these claims; he is simply reporting them as the basis for the discussion that follows in later pages.

Key Terms
جهة (direction)
الاستواء (istiwa')
الفوق (fawq)
العلو ('uluww)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That these verses literally prove a physical direction for Allah (as the anthropomorphists claim).
Correction: Imam Razi is reporting the anthropomorphists' arguments, not affirming them. The verses are open to interpretation and are often interpreted metaphorically by mainstream Sunni scholars.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, into how many categories of Quranic evidence do the anthropomorphists divide their proof?
A) Three
B) Six
C) Ten
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Anthropomorphists base their claim that Allah has a direction on three types of Quranic verses: those about istiwa', those using the word 'above', and those using words of highness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verses of istiwa' or 'above,' do you find yourself tempted to visualize Allah in a specific place? What does that temptation reveal about your reliance on your imagination rather than on revelation?
?
The anthropomorphists cling to these verses as proof of direction. What attachments in your own life—whether to a particular opinion, practice, or person—might you be clinging to in a way that detracts from your understanding of Allah's absolute transcendence?
?
This lesson shows that some people derive physical direction from words like 'above' and 'high.' Is there any area where you have insisted on a literal interpretation of scripture without considering the deeper principles of tanzih? How can you train yourself to submit to Allah's own description of Himself without adding human limitations?
